Getting Started
Introduction
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Explain the purpose of sanctions.
• Identify who imposes sanctions.
• Describe the types of sanctions and their effects on behavior.
• Describe the permissible activities and delisting process under sanctions.
• Discuss the consequences of noncompliance with sanctions.

The Purpose of
Sanctions
Introduction
Setup: Drafting a proposal
You work as a Policy Analyst for the Ministry of Foreign Affairs of a member
state serving on the United Nations Security Council (UNSC). International aid
agencies have requested that your government assess the potential
humanitarian consequences that new multilateral sanctions could have on a
civilian population. You are asked to draft a proposal that will be presented to
the UNSC.
You conduct research to find a past example of when the UNSC has
unintentionally harmed a civilian population by imposing multilateral sanctions.
You find a good example: North Korea. The sanctions against North Korea are
designed to prevent the government from developing weapons of mass
destruction. The goal is to promote peace, security, and stability by restricting
North Korea’s access to the global financial system and supply chains.
However, your research indicates that these sanctions have had a negative
effect on North Korean civilians.
For example, food shortages have worsened because it is more difficult to
import fertilizer and agricultural equipment. In addition, you find that basic
humanitarian services for vulnerable populations are generally prohibited
without a license. Even with a license, critical services such as food, medicine,
clean water distribution, sanitation, and other health-related services are
severely restricted.
You try to determine the cause. Some evidence shows that North Korea’s own
border closure has caused delays. Other evidence suggests that the relevant
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 6
UN sanctions committee has extremely slow authorization procedures that
are required before certain goods can be sent into the country. Some sources
claim that a lack of funding is to blame for the problems.
Your government has made commitments to support ongoing humanitarian
efforts related to international aid projects. However, your government is
behind on its goals and you do not wish to draw attention to this. You are
under pressure to draft your proposal. Based on what you have found, you are
not sure what you should present.
What would you do?
What decision should you make?
• Address licensing issues.
• Focus on border closures.
• Expand your research.
• Highlight the lack of funding.
Feedback
The best response is to expand your research. You should review other
multilateral sanctions regimes so you can make a more detailed assessment
of potential unintended consequences. This will help you identify the steps
that could be taken to mitigate risks before adopting multilateral sanctions.
Focusing on border closures is not the best response. While sanctions do not
specifically target borders, it is an important topic to address. However,
focusing exclusively on border closures would leave out other relevant topics
and would fail to properly address the humanitarian issues.
Addressing licenses exclusively is not the best response. Your research
indicates that critical humanitarian services, such as food, medicine, and other
health services often remain severely restricted even with a license. There
must be other contributing factors.
Highlighting the lack of funding is not the best response. While this might be a
cause of unintended consequences for North Korea, there are other reasons
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 7
that contribute to the lack of humanitarian aid. A balanced report would need
to include other possible explanations. It should also include findings among
other sanctioned countries in addition to North Korea.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Define sanctions.
• Explain the purpose of sanctions.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 8
What are sanctions?
Sanctions
Sanctions are political decisions made as part of diplomatic efforts by countries,
regional organizations, and international bodies. Sanctions are primarily a
foreign policy and security tool.
So, what exactly are sanctions? Broadly speaking, sanctions are restrictive
measures that can be taken against individuals, organizations, vessels,
jurisdictions, and even whole countries. There are several different types of
sanctions.
Sanctions can include travel bans and other diplomatic restrictions. However,
when sanctions are discussed in the news, and sanctions compliance issues are
raised, they are primarily referring to financial, economic, and trade sanctions.
At their core, sanctions are intended to affect behavioral change through
deterrence, prevention, or punishment.
In some form or another, all the other purposes of sanctions contribute to this
end. The ultimate objective of a sanction varies according to the situation. In the
majority of cases, the sanctions' legal framework contains information
explaining why they were imposed and specifies their aim. Review boards and
committees are often established to monitor the implementation of sanctions.
Sanctions are an effective option for policymakers as a way of changing a
target’s actions and behavior without resorting to military action. They are often
viewed as a lower-cost, lower-risk intervention that sits between diplomacy
and war. Sanctions have increasingly become a defining feature of the Western
response to geopolitical challenges and international security threats. On
occasion, sanctions are utilized while military options are evaluated.
Sanctions are often referred to as “sanctions regimes,” that is, a set of sanctions
that have a common theme. Sanctions regimes are either referred to by their
issuer or the intended purpose of the set of sanctions. Examples include the
“Office of Foreign Assets Control or OFAC sanctions regime” and the “North
Korea sanctions regime.”
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 9
History of sanctions
The methods used to enforce sanctions have evolved significantly over the
past few decades. However, the fundamental nature of sanctions has
remained the same. One of the first recorded instances of sanctions dates to
the 5th century BCE. At that time, Athens imposed economic sanctions on the
citizens of the town of Megara, who were banned from accessing markets in
the empire.
Historically, sanctions have taken the form of governments choosing to block,
or embargo, trade intended for another nation. Sanctions methods began to
evolve to their current state near the end of the 19th century.
The League of Nations, which was a predecessor to the United Nations, came
into existence after World War I. It worked to impose boycotts, embargoes,
and other restrictive measures against target nations as an alternative to
military action. The League of Nations’ work represented the beginning of
multilateral sanctions, which is when multiple countries work together to
impose sanctions. The United Nations was formed in 1945, following the
conclusion of World War II. At that time, sanctions were formally recognized
within the Charter of the United Nations as a foreign policy tool.
While the US continues leading the world with the most sanctions, Western
Europe, particularly the United Kingdom, began playing a more active role in
recent years. As technology and banking evolved in the late 20th century,
sanctions became easier to enforce. They also began to be used by more
governments worldwide.
Sanctions have evolved considerably since then and continue to evolve at a
rapid pace.
Examples of sanctions include:
• Sanctions to prevent or restrict the financing of terrorist activities by
groups such as ISIL and al-Qaeda
• Asset freezing, which can be used to reduce a regime's access to funds
when it is repressing a civic population, such as in Syria
• Sanctions to discourage the development of weapons of mass destruction
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 10
Why sanctions are imposed
Sanctions: National and international
security
According to the United Nations, sanctions are used to promote international
peace and security. They do this by attempting to change behavior or foreign
policy through deterrence, prevention, and punishment. Sanctions can be
used in response to national security concerns and to address specific issues,
such as human rights abuses. Sanctions can support many specific objectives
or causes.
National and international sanctions are applied to achieve several objectives.
• In some cases, the threat of sanctions is used to prevent the outbreak of
armed conflict, by placing increasing financial constraints on the
aggressor. Additionally, sanctions can be used as a punishment for military
action.
• Sanctions can be used to prevent the proliferation of weapons of mass
destruction, or WMD, such as the sanctions against the Democratic
People’s Republic of Korea related to nuclear weapons.
• Sanctions can also be used to prevent and disrupt the activities of terrorist
organizations. In 1999, the United Nations Security Council, UNSC,
Resolution 1267 established a sanctions regime that targeted individuals
and entities affiliated with al-Qaeda and the Taliban. Subsequent UNSC
resolutions were expanded to counter the financing of terrorism.
• Sanctions can promote democratic values when used to target officials
and others who undermine democracy. They can also block the
exportation of equipment that can be used for internal repression.
• Sanctions can be used as punishment for human rights violations.
• Sanctions also can be used in response to cyberattacks by both state and
non-state actors.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 11
• The sanctions tool can be used to freeze and return resources that have
been misappropriated by corrupt leaders or officials.
• Sanctions target criminals and transnational organized crime groups by
reducing their flow of funds and disrupting their criminal activity.
• In addition, sanctions can disrupt illicit trafficking networks, such as the
illegal wildlife trade and illicit drug trafficking trade.
Protecting the financial system from
international criminals
Sanctions are tools used by governments and international organizations to
protect the financial system from international criminals engaging in activities
such as money laundering, terrorist financing, and corruption. Sanctions can
disrupt the flow of funds to terrorist organizations and transnational organized
crime groups, making it challenging for them to operate. The USA PATRIOT
Act of 2001 strengthens the financial system's defenses against these threats
and includes provisions related to interbank accounts. An interbank account is
an account held by a financial institution at another financial institution to
facilitate customer transactions.
Sanctions can freeze assets, restrict financial transactions, and impose
penalties on those participating in money laundering activities. Financial
institutions are required to implement robust AML programs that detect and
report suspicious transactions to relevant authorities.
Sanctions can also target individuals, organizations, and jurisdictions that
provide financial support to terrorist groups. Financial institutions are required
to screen transactions against lists of sanctioned individuals and entities to
identify any involvement in terrorist financing.
Sanctions are designed to target corrupt officials, organizations, and
jurisdictions involved in corrupt practices. They serve as a deterrent and help
prevent the movement of illicit funds through the international financial
system.
The 2001 USA PATRIOT Act includes provisions related to interbank accounts,
including due diligence procedures to detect and prevent money laundering
and terrorist financing. The Act also empowers regulatory authorities to
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 12
access information about interbank accounts and transactions to ensure
compliance with AFC regulations. International cooperation is essential to
implement these measures effectively. Many jurisdictions and international
organizations, such as the UN and the Financial Action Task Force, work
together to develop and enforce sanctions regimes and AFC standards.
Sanctions as punishment for military action
By definition, sanctions are often used as an alternative to military aggression.
They can be used as a form of economic and political pressure to deter and
punish military aggression by sovereign states. Sanctions used to punish
military aggression are meant to hold the aggressor accountable for their
actions and as a sign of the international community’s disapproval. These
sanctions can remain in place until the aggressor complies with international
demands or withdraws from the occupied territory. Economic sanctions are
also used to deter military action. The threat to the economy is often enough
to deter the threat.
The international community or individual jurisdictions implement these
sanctions to promote international peace and security. However, sanctions
are not always applied uniformly or consistently in response to military action.
Examples include the sanctions on Iraq following the invasion of Kuwait in
1990, and the ongoing international embargo against Northern Cyprus since
1983.
When military aggression against sovereign states occurs, additional or new
sanctions might be applied against the instigator to stop the aggression.
Sanctions bring a threat of economic and political isolation that often
discourages military aggression.
Financial sanctions used as punishment are meant to weaken the aggressor’s
economic resources and can deprive the aggressor of critical technologies
and necessary goods. However, it is important to note that military actions do
not always result in sanctions, making each case a complex and context-
dependent international relations matter.
After Iraq invaded Kuwait in 1990, the UN Security Council imposed a
comprehensive set of economic sanctions against Iraq. The sanctions
included restrictions on oil exports, arms trade, and financial transactions.
These sanctions severely disrupted Iraq's economy. The purpose of these
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 13
sanctions was to punish Iraq for the invasion and to deter further military
aggression. In addition, Iraq was ordered to pay Kuwait reparations, disclose
and eliminate any weapons of mass destruction, and was pressured to
withdraw from Kuwait.
Northern Cyprus, under the name Turkish Republic of Northern Cyprus,
declared independence from the Island Republic of Cyprus in 1983. The
international community, led by the UN, imposed a long-standing embargo on
Northern Cyprus as punishment for the unilateral declaration of
independence, and to further deter military action. The embargo included
restrictions on trade and travel and denied diplomatic recognition for
Northern Cyprus. To date, Turkey is the only jurisdiction that recognizes
Northern Cyprus as a state.
Sanctions to discourage threats to other
nations
Sanctions are used by international organizations and governments to
promote international peace and security by discouraging threats to other
nations. This is done by attempting to change behavior or foreign policy
through deterrence, prevention, or punishment. An example of such
measures would be US sanctions on individuals and entities involved in a
Russian-backed campaign of interference with the 2016 US presidential
election. In another example, the UN Security Council imposed sanctions on
the Democratic People's Republic of Korea (DPRK) to encourage the country
to engage in diplomatic negotiations. The sanctions included restrictions on
trade and financial transactions.
Sanctions can be used for traditional threats like military aggression, and to
address security challenges related to cyberattacks, election interference,
and cybercrime. Sanctions serve as a tool to hold responsible actors
accountable, and to discourage future threats to national and international
security in the digital age.
In 2016, Russia interfered in the US presidential election with the goals of
ruining Hillary Clinton’s presidential campaign, boosting Donald Trump’s
campaign, and increasing social tension in the US. Russia used disinformation
campaigns on social media and cyberattacks to hack into the websites of US
political organizations. In response, the US imposed financial sanctions on
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 14
Russian individuals and entities. These sanctions were meant to deter future
interference in US elections.
The DPRK has a well-known, state-funded cyber army that has been involved
in various international cyberattacks. Government entities are the most
frequent targets, followed by cryptocurrency, media, finance, defense, and
nongovernmental organizations. DPRK state-sponsored cyber activity
includes ransomware campaigns against US healthcare and public health
sector organizations, which are critical infrastructure entities. The UN and
other individual jurisdictions have imposed sanctions on North Korean entities
in an attempt to discourage cyberattacks and ransomware attacks.
Sanctions have also been used to target individuals and organizations that
provide financial or technical support to cybercriminals in Russia and the
DPRK. These sanctions are meant to disrupt the infrastructure of those who
enable ransomware attacks and discourage the use of their jurisdictions for
cybercrime.
Diminishing the power of regimes to commit
human rights violations
Sanctions are used both as punishment for human rights violations and to
discourage human rights abuses. The success of sanctions depends upon
factors such as international consensus, enforcement, and the willingness of
the targets to change their behavior. Sanctions can include freezing assets,
banning trade, and imposing financial restrictions. Targeted sanctions are
typically directed at specific individuals, entities, or organizations responsible
for human rights abuses.
Examples of sanctions used to diminish the power of a regime to commit
human rights violations include the sanctions on Myanmar in response to the
Rohingya genocide and sanctions in response to the Darfur crisis. Also, the
Global Magnitsky Act allows the US government to impose sanctions on
human rights violators anywhere in the world. Other jurisdictions have
enacted similar legislation to deter and punish human rights violations.
The Rohingya genocide is a series of ongoing persecutions and killings of the
Muslim Rohingya people by the Myanmar military. Several jurisdictions,
including the US and the EU, imposed asset freezes, arms embargoes, and
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 15
economic sanctions against Myanmar. The goal has been to pressure the
Myanmar military junta to cease human rights violations and transition to a
democratic government.
The UN Security Council and individual countries imposed diplomatic
sanctions on Sudan in response to the Darfur crisis. The power struggle
between Sudanese government soldiers and Darfur rebels included
allegations of widespread ethnic cleansing and genocide. The UN sanctions
on the Sudanese government included travel bans on government officials
and restrictions on their ability to participate in international forums. The
diplomatic isolation was intended to pressure the Sudanese government to
address the human rights violations in Darfur. After a great deal of pressure
from the UN, the African Union, and the neighboring countries, the Sudanese
government and the Darfur rebels agreed to sign the Darfur Peace
Agreement in 2006.
The Global Magnitsky Act of 2016 authorizes the US government to sanction
foreign government officials who engage in human rights offenses by
freezing their assets and banning them from entering the US. Several other
jurisdictions have implemented similar sanctions regimes. This targeted
approach holds violators accountable and discourages them from continuing
violations.
Counter-proliferation financing and
countering the financing of terrorism
Terrorism and the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMD) are
two specific security risks that financial and economic sanctions can be used
to combat. Counter-Proliferation Financing (CPF) and Countering the
Financing of Terrorism (CFT) are how financial measures are used to disrupt
these risks.
Counter-Proliferation Financing, or CPF, addresses the financing of WMD by
rogue actors and states. At present, however, there is no internationally
agreed-upon definition of “proliferation” or “proliferation financing".
United Nations Security Council (UNSCR) Resolution 1540 requires jurisdictions
to take "effective measures to establish domestic controls to prevent the
proliferation of nuclear, chemical, or biological weapons and their means of
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 16
delivery." Furthermore, Recommendation 7 of the Financial Action Task Force
(FATF) implores countries to implement sanctions to comply with UNSC
resolutions relating to the “prevention, suppression, and disruption of the
proliferation of weapons of mass destruction and its financing.”
In 2020, FATF expanded its expectations that require countries and the private
sector to identify and assess the risks of proliferation financing. Such risk
assessments will need to consider a range of factors, including:
• The tools available to assess potential risk exposure
• Mapping risks associated with dual-use goods and sensitive technologies
• Ability to link payments to an entity, individual, or good of concern
Countering the financing of terrorism is a core component of the FATF
standards.
Obligations cover:
• The implementation of targeted financial sanctions
• Investigation
• Information sharing
• Due diligence measures
Staying updated with current typologies is critical, as terrorists and their
supporters constantly modify ways to collect, move, and gain access to funds.
Over recent years, evolving risk areas have included crowdfunding, social
media, and cryptocurrencies.
Preventing the proliferation of weapons of
mass destruction
Sanctions are used to apply economic and diplomatic pressure, compelling
targets to comply with international nonproliferation agreements and
encourage peaceful resolutions to disarmament challenges. The UN, EU, and
US have imposed multiple sanctions on Iran for its nuclear program. The UN
imposed sanctions on the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea (DPRK) for
its nuclear program and weapons of mass destruction (WMD) program.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 17
In addition to sanctions, other international attempts have been made to help
control the development and spread of WMDs. The UN’s Nuclear Non-
Proliferation Treaty (NPT) is an international treaty aimed at preventing the
spread of nuclear weapons and weapons technology, promoting cooperation
in the peaceful uses of nuclear energy, and furthering the goal of nuclear
disarmament. The International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA) is the
international center for cooperation in the nuclear field within the UN. The
agency works with its Member States and multiple partners worldwide to
promote the safe, secure, and peaceful use of nuclear technologies.
Iran's nuclear program has been an international concern since the early
2000s with suspicions that Iran might be pursuing nuclear weapons in violation
of the NPT. The international community also wanted Iran to cooperate with
the IAEA. Sanctions against Iran led to a decline in the country’s revenue, a
shortage of goods, and higher inflation rates. These sanctions made it difficult
for Iranians to afford basic goods and services. Iran’s negotiations with several
world powers resulted in the 2015 Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action
(JCPOA). Under the JCPOA, Iran agreed to dismantle much of its nuclear
program in exchange for sanctions relief.
DPRK's nuclear and WMD programs have been a global security concern since
the early 2000s. DPRK withdrew from the NPT in 2003 and conducted multiple
nuclear tests. Sanctions were designed to pressure the regime to abandon its
nuclear weapons program, return to the NPT, and engage in denuclearization
negotiations. However, the regime has not completely abandoned its nuclear
weapons program. Negotiations and diplomatic efforts have been ongoing,
with periodic tensions and breakthroughs, but a comprehensive resolution
has not yet been achieved.
Preventing the proliferation of weapons of
mass destruction (Case example: DPRK)
The Democratic People’s Republic of Korea (DPRK) began constructing
nuclear facilities in the 1960s with Soviet assistance. It completed its first
nuclear reactor around 1986. In 1992, International Atomic Energy Agency
(IAEA) inspectors found the DPRK to be noncompliant with its application of
safeguards in connection with the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty (NPT). The
DPRK withdrew from the NPT in 2003 and tested its first nuclear weapon in
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 18
2006. In 2022, the Institute for Science and International Security estimated
that the DPRK had approximately 45 nuclear weapons.
Several attempts have been made to negotiate a reduction in the DPRK’s
prohibited activities. These attempts have failed and the DPRK has resumed
missile testing.
To curb the DPRK’s weapons programs, the United Nations Security Council
(UNSC) has passed several resolutions. Each time the DPRK demonstrated
another piece of its nuclear arsenal, the UNSC imposed additional sanctions.
Key UNSC resolutions include:
• In 2006, UNSC Resolution 1718 imposed a ban on the export of military and
weapons of mass destruction (WMD)-related items. It also established a
sanctions committee to oversee implementation.
• In 2016, UNSC Resolution 2270 targeted the DPRK’s coal, iron, and seafood
exports and placed restrictions on North Korean banks and individuals
involved in the country’s WMD program.
• In 2017, UNSC Resolution 2397 tightened restrictions on oil imports and
textile exports from the DPRK.
• In 2017, UNSC Resolution 2371 and UNSC Resolution 2375 imposed a total
ban on the DPRK exports of coal, iron, and seafood and restricted the
supply of oil and refined petroleum products to the DPRK.
The US has implemented its own set of sanctions against the DPRK. These
sanctions go beyond the UNSC resolutions, aiming to isolate the country
economically and diplomatically. While the effectiveness of the sanctions
remains a subject of debate, they continue to be a central element of the
international community's strategy to address the threat of WMD proliferation
emanating from North Korea.
Key takeaways
• UNSC resolutions have been the main source of international pressure on
the DPRK, targeting its:
o Economy
o Military potential
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 19
• Other jurisdictions can impose autonomous sanctions on top of UN
sanctions.
• The US has extensive autonomous North Korea sanctions.
Terrorism-related sanctions
Terrorism-related sanctions are measures imposed by governments or
international organizations to combat terrorism, disrupt terrorist networks,
and promote national and international security. These sanctions are designed
to isolate and expose individuals, groups, and entities involved in terrorist
activities while limiting their access to financial resources. Sanctions can also
restrict movement and raise awareness about individuals and entities involved
in terrorist activities.
International support for terrorism-related sanctions includes:
• United Nations Security Council Resolution 1267 (UNSCR 1267)
• US Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list
• Gulf Cooperation Council (GCC) terrorist lists
• Domestic terrorist watchlists
An example of a sanction to combat terrorism is the UNSCR 1267 which
targets individuals and entities associated with Al-Qaeda and the Taliban. The
resolution freezes assets, restricts travel, and prevents entities and individuals
from supplying arms and other types of support to these groups. The intent of
UNSCR 1267 is to disrupt operations and weaken their capacity to carry out
terrorist acts. One of the individuals subjected to sanctions under this
resolution was Osama bin Laden.
The US SDN list identifies and sanctions individuals, groups, and entities
involved in terrorism. Sanctions freeze assets and prohibit transactions with
SDN-listed individuals or entities. An example of an individual who was
designated as an SDN was ISIS leader Abu Bakr al-Baghdadi.
Qatar, Saudi Arabia, the United Arab Emirates, and other GCC nations have
maintained terrorist lists, often designating individuals and organizations
associated with the Muslim Brotherhood.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 20
Many jurisdictions have domestic terrorist watchlists. These watchlists are
used to identify and monitor individuals and entities involved in terrorism-
related activities, to prevent domestic acts of terrorism, and to maintain
national security. In the US, for example, the Federal Bureau of Investigation
maintains the Terrorist Screening Database and the No-Fly List, which includes
individuals considered a threat to national security.
Terrorism-related sanctions (Case
example: US sanctions on Iran)
In November 1979, US President Carter imposed sanctions on Iran after radical
Iranian students stormed the US Embassy in Tehran and took hostages.
Successive US administrations have since imposed comprehensive sanctions
against Iran which, among other things, restrict its access to international
financial markets and target multiple sectors of the Iranian economy.
Over the years, the US has accused Iran of providing financial and logistical
support to terrorist groups such as Hezbollah and Hamas. Iran was originally
designated as a state sponsor of terrorism by the US Department of State in
January 1984 and has since remained on that list. Other designated countries
include Syria, which was designated in 1979, North Korea, which was
designated in 2017, and Cuba, which was designated in 2021.
The types of sanctions the US has imposed on Iran as punishment for its
support for terrorist groups and acts of terror include:
• Energy sanctions: The US has imposed economic sanctions on Iran's
energy sector, which is a significant source of revenue for the country.
They have restricted Iran's ability to export oil, putting significant pressure
on its economy.
• Financial sanctions: The US has imposed financial sanctions that limit Iran's
access to the international banking system. They have made it difficult for
Iran to conduct financial transactions and receive payments for its exports,
curtailing its ability to sponsor terrorist activity.
• Arms embargo: The US has also enforced an arms embargo on Iran,
preventing the country from importing or exporting weapons. The
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 21
embargo aims to hinder Iran's ability to provide support to terrorist and
extremist groups.
• Asset freezes: The US has frozen the assets of individuals and entities
directly linked to Iran's alleged support for terrorism.
In 2015, the US Victims of State Sponsored Terrorism Fund was established by
act of Congress. Since its inception, it has paid more than US$6 billion worth of
compensation to eligible victims, including funds seized from Iran.
Key takeaways
• Terrorism-related sanctions can be imposed for a jurisdiction’s support for
terrorist organizations.
• Sanctions impact a jurisdiction’s economy by freezing assets, restricting
the energy sector, limiting financial access, and forbidding arms trade.
• Sanctions are meant to coerce a jurisdiction into changing its behavior.
This includes stopping support for terrorist groups.
Unintended consequences of sanctions
Multilateral sanctions involve multiple jurisdictions working together to impose
economic or political penalties on a target jurisdiction or government. This
type of sanction is often used as a tool of international diplomacy to address a
range of issues including human rights violations, nuclear proliferation, or
military aggression.
While these sanctions are intended to exert pressure on the targeted
government or entities, they can have unintended consequences on civilian
populations within the targeted jurisdiction. Unintended consequences of
multilateral sanctions could include economic hardships, limited healthcare
and education resources, and an erosion of civil liberties.
Sanctioned governments often use the external pressure of sanctions as an
excuse to restrict civil liberties and human rights. For example, they can
restrict individual freedoms and justify increased surveillance, censorship, and
repression in the name of national security. Multilateral sanctions can disrupt
trade, foreign investments, and financial transactions. These restrictions can
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 22
lead to a decline in currency value, inflation, and job loss. As a result, the
standard of living decreases for civilians.
Sanctions can restrict the flow of goods and services, impeding the delivery of
humanitarian aid. This includes food, medicine, and other essential items.
Restrictions on imports of medical equipment and pharmaceuticals, as well as
educational materials, can block access to these essential services for
civilians.
A fuel embargo can prevent a country from generating enough electricity.
This restricts the ability to operate pumps that supply drinking water to
civilians, hospitals, and schools. Sanctions that make seeds and fertilizers
unavailable or increase their cost could create food shortages. Sanctions can
also limit trade and delivery of parts and equipment necessary to maintain
critical infrastructure. Rebuilding infrastructure after sanctions are lifted can
be a lengthy and costly process. The time and cost of rebuilding can prolong
civilian suffering.
To mitigate unintended consequences, policymakers should consider the
design and implementation of sanctions, including efforts to minimize their
impact on civilian populations through targeted measures and humanitarian
exceptions.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 23
Who Imposes
Sanctions?
Introduction
Setup: An urgent export
You work in the compliance department of a leading South Korean
manufacturer. Your company makes toys and video game consoles. As a
compliance officer, you are responsible for reviewing and approving export
transactions based on financial crime risks. This requires close attention to
detail. You need to ensure that transactions adhere to internal policies and
applicable regulations.
You have just received information about a scheduled shipment of your
company’s finest gaming consoles. The shipment is for a new partner with
whom your company signed an export contract. According to the contract,
your company must send out the shipment by next week. Otherwise, it could
face financial penalties for breach of contract.
The business unit is excited to move forward with the deal. It offers a chance
to enter a fresh and lucrative market with a high-margin product. These high-
performing gaming consoles are packed with the most cutting-edge
features.
While reviewing the details of the transaction, you notice that the shipment is
destined for Iran, which is considered high-risk according to your company’s
internal compliance policy. Your company has previously exported toys to
Iranian entities. However, this is the first time it is exporting high-tech gaming
consoles to that country.
This product presents a higher risk than most because the advanced
technologies that it uses could potentially be considered dual-use goods.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 24
These are goods such as software, technology, documents, and diagrams
that can be used for both civilian and military applications.
As you continue your review, you discover that the gaming consoles contain
US-origin semiconductors, which are used in a variety of electronic devices.
You know that US laws apply to US-origin products regardless of their location.
Depending on the percentage of US-origin content in the goods in question,
you could be subject to the Export Administration Regulations (EAR) that are
enforced by the US Department of Commerce’s Bureau of Industry and
Security (BIS).
You consult with your company’s Research and Development department.
They confirm that the semiconductors are the only US connection and that
the gaming console is not categorized as a dual-use good.
You want to ensure that your company is able to fulfill the contract with the
new customer, but you need to consider the risks.
What would you do?
What should you do next?
• Apply for a license.
• Request more information on the US-origin parts.
• Approve the transaction.
• Get approval from the US parts manufacturer.
Feedback
The best response is to request more information on the US-origin parts. You
must determine if the consoles are subject to restrictions under EAR. Knowing
the exact US-content percentage of the items, as a proportion of their value,
can help determine the applicable export license requirements.
Applying for a license is not the best response. You must first determine if the
goods are subject to restrictions under EAR, given that they are destined for
Iran. If the goods are subject to restrictions, then putting the transaction on
hold and applying for a BIS license is the appropriate next step.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 25
Approving the transaction is not the best response. US laws may apply
extraterritorially, particularly when US-origin goods or technology are involved.
Getting approval from the US parts manufacturer is not the best response. It
cannot substitute for the need to obtain a BIS license if US export restrictions
apply.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Name the most active sanctions regimes.
• Describe the jurisdictional reach of sanctions.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 26
Most active sanctions regimes
Who imposes sanctions?
Sanctions can be imposed by a range of authorities, including the United
Nations (UN) Security Council, the European Union (EU), and individual nations
or states. Multilateral sanctions refer to those imposed by the UN, which are
applicable to all UN member states. The term can also be used to describe
regional sanctions imposed by bodies such as the EU, which are applicable
across several countries. Unilateral action is when an individual state, such as
the US, Canada, Australia, or the UK, imposes sanctions outside of any UN or
regional framework.
The legal basis for imposing sanctions will differ depending on whether they
are imposed at a multilateral, regional, or unilateral level. Depending on the
legal basis, sanctions may be imposed through resolutions, regulations, acts,
and executive orders.
The most globally recognized sanctions are those imposed by United Nations
Security Council Resolutions (UNSCRs). All UN member states are required to
implement UN sanctions within their national legislation. The primary purpose
of UNSCRs is to maintain or restore international peace and security.
The EU imposes sanctions as part of its Common Foreign and Security Policy
or to implement UNSCRs. EU sanctions can often be referred to as
autonomous sanctions, meaning those imposed by the EU only, and not those
which are mandated by the UN Security Council.
In addition to implementing UN and EU sanctions, as required, some countries
may choose to take unilateral action. Not all countries impose unilateral
sanctions, but those that do have different powers to implement and enforce
their sanctions.
The US imposes more unilateral sanctions than any other country. The US legal
authority is broad-ranging and can originate through either the executive
branch of government via Executive Orders issued by the president or the
legislative branch through the enactment of specific statutes.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 27
There is a wide range of other countries with the power to implement
unilateral sanctions. Some notable examples include the UK, China, Russia,
Canada, Australia, and Japan.
Those imposing unilateral sanctions may seek to act in unison with other like-
minded countries. For instance, in 2020 both Canada and the UK coordinated
their unilateral efforts to impose sanctions on Belarus under their respective
human rights regimes.
United Nations sanctions regime
The United Nations (UN) uses sanctions as a measure to promote international
peace and security. This power is based on Article 41 of Chapter VII of its
founding charter.
The UN Security Council has established key criteria for targeting individuals
and entities with sanctions. Among those criteria are:
• Threatening peace, security, or stability
• Violating human rights and international humanitarian law
• Obstructing humanitarian aid
• Recruiting or using children in armed conflicts
The UN Security Council consists of 15 member countries, five of which are
permanent members. These are Russia, the US, the UK, China, and France,
also known as the P5. Each member country has one vote. To enact sanctions
resolutions, at least nine members of the Security Council must vote in the
affirmative. There can be no objection from any of the P-5. This means each
permanent member has veto power.
After the Security Council adopts a resolution, it is legally binding under
Articles 25 and 48 of the UN Charter. Article 25 states that member states are
obligated to “accept and carry out the decisions of the Security Council in
accordance with the present Charter.” After passing a resolution, the UN
establishes a sanctions committee to monitor the implementation and
effectiveness of the sanctions regime. The UN also maintains the United
Nations Security Council Consolidated List, which includes all persons and
entities that are subject to UN sanctions.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 28
Although the UN passes sanctions, member states are expected to
implement and enforce them. Member States pass their own laws and
enforcement regulations, as well as create their own enforcement bodies,
such as bank regulators and government agencies. Article 48 of the Charter
constitutes an affirmation of the obligation of member states under Article 25
of the Charter to accept binding decisions by the Council.
Examples of UN sanctions include the 2017 UN Mali sanctions regime. The
regime was established against those actively damaging the process of
implementing the agreement on peace and reconciliation. Another example
are the sanctions against Islamic State in Iraq and the Levant (ISIL), al-Qaeda,
and associated individuals, groups, undertakings, and entities. This regime was
first established under Resolution 1267 in 1999.
European Union sanctions regime
EU sanctions, referred to as restrictive measures, are prepared by the
European External Action Service. They are then agreed upon by the Council
of the European Union. The goal of these measures is to “bring about a
change in policy or conduct of those targeted, with a view to promoting the
objectives of the EU’s Common Foreign and Security Policy (CFSP).”
Initially, sanctions are examined and discussed by a regional preparatory body.
Following this, proposed sanctions considerations proceed through the
Working Party of Foreign Relations Counsellors (RELEX), the Political and
Security Committee (PSC), and the Committee of Permanent
Representatives (COREPER II). After making it that far, the resolution must be
unanimously adopted by the CFSP foreign policy framework. The sanction
goes into effect after it is published in the Official Journal of the European
Union.
The EU’s key objectives when adopting sanctions are to:
• Safeguard the EU's values, fundamental interests, and security
• Preserve peace
• Prevent conflicts and strengthen international security
• Consolidate and support democracy, the rule of law, human rights, and the
principles of international law
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 29
The EU implements all sanctions enacted by the UN Security Council. It
transposes UN sanctions into EU law by means of Council Decisions and
Council Regulations. As such, the EU member states are responsible for
enforcing sanctions and adopting national legislation to impose them.
Examples of EU sanctions include those imposed on Venezuela in November
2017 and those imposed in 2014 for individuals and entities undermining or
threatening the territorial integrity, sovereignty, and independence of Ukraine.
United States sanctions framework
The United States has the most comprehensive sanctions framework
currently in operation. US sanctions can be introduced by both the executive
and legislative branches of government. Due in part to the importance of the
US dollar to the world economy, US sanctions can have a widely felt impact.
They are a compliance concern for organizations based all over the world.
The US president is given broad authority to impose sanctions under acts of
Congress, such as the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA)
and the Trading with the Enemy Act (TWEA). Under these acts, the president
imposes sanctions by executive order. For example, Executive Order 13622
authorized Iran sanctions based on the IEEPA authority. Statutes and
executive orders are then further implemented by enacting regulations.
The acts passed by Congress do not give unlimited authority to the president
to impose sanctions. As implied by its name, the IEEPA empowers the
president “during a state of emergency.” This stipulation must be reviewed
and, if appropriate, extended by the president each year. Congress may
terminate a state of emergency through a joint resolution. The president must
also follow reporting requirements imposed by Congress.
In addition to executive orders, the US Congress can introduce sanctions
regimes by passing a sanctions bill. Those sanctions are then enforced
through various agencies, federal regulators, and in some instances through
the enlistment of state regulators. Examples include the Countering America's
Adversaries Through Sanctions Act (CAATSA), which imposed sanctions on
Iran, North Korea, and Russia. Another example is the 2020 National Defense
Authorization Act, which included sanctions on Syria, Turkey, Russia, and North
Korea.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 30
Generally speaking, individuals, entities, and organizations are then added to
the sanctions list of the relevant regime pursuant to a specific executive order
or Congressional bill.
United States sanctions regulatory
agencies
Several government agencies within the United States are responsible for
implementing US sanctions. These include, but are not limited to, the Office of
Foreign Assets Control (OFAC), the Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS), and
the US Department of State’s Office of Economic Sanctions Policy and
Implementation.
OFAC is the agency within the US Department of the Treasury that is
responsible for implementing financial and economic sanctions. It may work in
consultation with other agencies, such as the Department of State. A core
component of OFAC sanctions is the Specially Designated Nationals and
Blocked Persons List, or the SDN list. This list includes the names and identifiers
of individuals, companies, vessels, and other entities whose assets are to be
blocked or frozen. Collectively, these individuals and companies are called
“specially designated nationals”, or SDNs. The SDN’s assets are blocked, and
US persons are generally prohibited from dealing with them. The SDN list is
frequently updated.
OFAC administers both targeted and comprehensive sanctions programs.
Examples of targeted sanctions include the Counter Narcotics Trafficking
Sanctions, Global Magnitsky Sanctions, and Transnational Criminal
Organization Sanctions. Examples of comprehensive sanctions include the
Iran Sanctions and North Korea Sanctions. The entire list of OFAC sanctions
regimes is available on OFAC’s website.
BIS is part of the US Department of Commerce. It maintains the Denied
Persons List, which is a list of persons for whom export privileges have been
denied, and the Entity List, which applies to entities. The BIS also administers
the Export Administration Regulations or EAR. The EAR applies to
commodities, technology, software, and other items subject to export
controls.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 31
Within the US Department of State, several offices hold sanctions roles. The
Office of Economic Sanctions Policy and Implementation develops and
implements foreign policy-related sanctions that are adopted to counter
threats to national security. The State Department also administers, by
delegation, its own lists that are applicable to sanctions, such as the Cuba
Restricted List and the Nonproliferation Sanctions list.
US primary versus secondary sanctions
US sanctions are often divided into two categories: US primary and secondary
sanctions. “US primary sanctions” apply to US persons or transactions in which
a US nexus, or connection, exists. Primary sanctions can include any type of
sanction, such as an asset freeze or trade sanction. They are imposed to
prohibit US persons from engaging in certain activities and carry potential
monetary penalties for violations. US secondary sanctions target non-US
persons for transactions outside of the US, with no US connection.
To understand US primary sanctions, it is important to define a “US person.” A
US person is:
• A US citizen or permanent resident, including "green card” holders,
wherever located
• Any entity organized under US laws and any jurisdiction within the US,
including foreign branches
• Any individual or entity in the US
In certain circumstances, a non-US entity owned or controlled by a US person
may be treated as a US person. Examples include the Iran and Cuba
programs. Importantly, any person, no matter their nationality, is also subject
to US jurisdiction, including US primary sanctions, when physically present in
the US or US territories.
US secondary sanctions extend the jurisdictional reach of US sanctions
beyond the US by giving certain US sanctions extraterritorial effect. “US
secondary sanctions” are designed to supplement US primary sanctions by
targeting non-US persons for transactions outside the US, where no US
person or connection is involved. In this case, the US threatens sanctions
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 32
against foreign persons for sanctionable conduct, including engaging in
certain business involving the sanctioned country.
The US has introduced the concept of US secondary sanctions into a limited
number of sanctions programs, including Iran, North Korea, and Russia.
US secondary sanctions and significant
transactions
The US Treasury Department’s Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) and
the State Department apply secondary sanctions to non-US companies and
individuals. These sanctions apply even when there is no contact whatsoever
with the United States. When deciding whether to impose secondary
sanctions, US authorities must determine whether a non-US person
“knowingly” facilitated a “significant transaction” in violation of the relevant
sanctions program. Several factors are considered in that determination.
The numerous statutory and executive orders that authorize secondary
sanctions do not clearly define what constitutes a “significant transaction.”
Instead, it is necessary to review the individual program guidance issued by
OFAC and the US State Department.
In general terms, OFAC and the State Department identify several factors that
they consider when determining whether a transaction is significant. These
factors include:
• The size, number, and frequency of the transaction
• The nature of the transaction
• The level of awareness of management and whether the transaction is
part of a pattern of conduct
• The connection between the transaction and a blocked person
• The impact of the transaction on statutory objectives
• Whether the transaction involves deceptive practices
• Other factors that the Secretary of the Treasury deems relevant on a case-
by-case basis
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 33
This list of factors is not exhaustive. US authorities often stress a case-by-case
approach to determining the significance of a transaction.
Another important factor is the provision of material assistance. This can
include providing financial, technology, and other support for a transaction. It
also can include providing goods or services in support of a designated entity.
The definition of “knowingly” is generally understood to mean that a foreign
person has actual knowledge of, or should have known, that the transaction
would be a violation of sanctions if it were undertaken by a US person.
US-origin goods and technology
US export and sanctions laws can be complicated for anyone. This is
especially true for the export or reexport of US goods or technology.
Companies and individuals in any country that export to jurisdictions that are
subject to relevant US restrictions must be aware of the extent to which their
exports comprise US-origin goods. This is because these laws apply to US-
origin products wherever located.
The US Department of Commerce’s Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS)
administers and enforces the Export Administration Regulations (EAR). These
regulations apply specifically to physical goods and commodities, such as
technology, software, and other items subject to export controls.
In terms of jurisdiction, EAR applies to:
• All items in the US, regardless of origin
• All US-origin items, wherever located
• US-origin parts, components, materials, and other commodities
incorporated abroad into foreign-made products; US-origin software
commingled with foreign software; and US-origin technology commingled
with foreign technology, in quantities exceeding "de minimis " levels
• Certain foreign-made direct products of US-origin technology or software
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 34
United Kingdom sanctions regime
The United Kingdom’s Foreign, Commonwealth & Development Office
(FCDO) is responsible for setting the UK’s sanctions policy and has obligations
to report to Parliament. The Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation
(OFSI) implements and administers financial sanctions. It also grants licenses
that allow activity prohibited by financial sanctions. The Financial Conduct
Authority, or FCA, requires firms to protect themselves from being misused
for financial crime, which includes compliance with UK financial sanctions. The
Department for International Trade, or DIT, implements trade measures,
sanctions, and embargoes, with Revenue and Customs acting as its
enforcement agency. The Department for Transport is responsible for
implementing and enforcing transport-related sanctions, and the Home
Office is responsible for administering, implementing, and enforcing travel
and visa bans.
Prior to the UK’s withdrawal from the European Union in 2020, the UK
implemented EU sanctions. As the UK prepared to leave the EU, it introduced
an autonomous human rights sanctions regime. The regime froze assets and
imposed travel bans against several individuals and organizations.
In 2018, the UK Parliament passed the Sanctions and Anti-Money-Laundering
Act (SAMLA), which enables the implementation of statutory instruments and
other regulations. SAMLA allows the UK to impose sanctions for the following
reasons:
• Preventing terrorism
• Protecting national security
• Promoting international peace and security
• Defending human rights
• Advancing foreign policy objectives
Because the UK is a global financial center, the separation of UK sanctions
from EU sanctions carries implications for global sanctions compliance where
there is a UK connection. UK and EU standards are not necessarily identical; in
fact, there are several areas in which UK requirements differ. Financial
institutions and other relevant organizations need to ensure that they monitor
relevant programs.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 35
The FCDO provides a list of all UK sanctions, and OFSI provides a parallel
consolidated list of persons and organizations under financial sanctions.
Influential organizations
Several regional organizations and standard-setting bodies influence the
global sanctions arena. These include organizations other than individual
governments, the European Union, and the United Nations. In some cases,
they impose sanctions and set standards that help shape national regulatory
and legal frameworks relevant to sanctions compliance standards.
Regional organizations, such as the League of Arab States, or the Arab
League, and the African Union, have demonstrated their willingness to use
sanctions outside of the UN framework. In general, these bodies apply
sanctions in ways that are often different from the UN, EU, and unilateral
frameworks. Sanctions imposed by regional bodies typically reflect regional
security concerns. For example, members of the Arab League engaged in a
boycott of Israel for many years.
In addition to regional organizations, the Financial Action Task Force, or FATF,
is the global money laundering and terrorist financing standard-setter. This
intergovernmental body establishes international standards that aim to
prevent illegal activities. These standards are directly relevant to the
imposition of UN sanctions, domestic designations, and sanctions compliance
standards.
As a policy-making body, the FATF works to generate the necessary political
will to bring about national legislative and regulatory reforms. This involves
identifying jurisdictions with weak measures to combat money laundering and
terrorist financing. Each year, FATF publishes two statements at the end of
each plenary meeting, in February, June, and October. They summarize
recent actions taken by jurisdictions and list the remaining strategic
deficiencies. The statements reflect the different levels of risk posed at any
given time by the jurisdictions under review.
The list system utilized by FATF to identify countries with some deficiencies is
informally referred to as the “grey list.”’ The list that identifies uncooperative
countries is often referred to as the “black list.” FATF member countries are
expected to apply countermeasures to the jurisdictions identified as non-
cooperative.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 36
Jurisdictional reach of sanctions
Who needs to comply with sanctions?
Sanctions are applicable to everyone. Different jurisdictions have their own
way of implementing sanctions, as well as determining which people and
entities need to comply. As a rule, citizens of a jurisdiction must comply with
their home jurisdiction’s sanctions, even if they are outside of their home
jurisdiction. For example, an individual on vacation overseas must comply with
their home jurisdiction's sanctions. An individual must also comply with the
sanctions laws of any jurisdiction where they are physically located. This is true
for the UN, EU, and the US, as well as most other sovereign jurisdictions.
UN sanctions are implemented by UN Member States through domestic
Member-State frameworks. Once UN Member States pass legislation to
comply with UN sanctions, individuals and entities within that Member State
must comply with the legislation.
EU persons must comply with both EU and UN sanctions. EU sanctions do not
have extraterritorial effect. However, EU sanctions apply:
• Within the territory of the EU, including its airspace and coastal waters
• Onboard any aircraft or vessel under the jurisdiction of an EU member
state
• To any person inside or outside the territory of the EU who is a national of
an EU member state
• To any legal person, entity, or body inside or outside the territory of the EU
that is incorporated or constituted under the law of an EU member state,
including branches of EU companies in third countries
• To any legal person, entity, or body in respect of any business done in
whole or in part within the EU
US persons must comply with sanctions issued by the Office of Foreign Assets
Control. This includes:
• US citizens and permanent residents wherever located
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 37
• Companies and other entities organized under US law
• All people and organizations, whatever their origin, physically in the US
• All branches of US companies and other entities throughout the world
Know your jurisdictions
Knowing your jurisdictions falls into three categories. First, you must know the
regulations and laws that apply in your local jurisdiction. Second, if your
organization is a multinational institution, you must be aware of the impact of
regulations and laws that apply in your headquarter jurisdiction. These will be
encompassed in your organization’s policies. Typically, organizations apply the
highest standard for the countries in which they operate. They grant
exceptions only if required by law in a specific jurisdiction. Finally, you must be
aware of regulations and laws in the jurisdictions in which your organization
operates, even if it has no physical presence there.
Regulators will review your due diligence to determine whether you are
complying with your jurisdiction’s laws and regulations and whether you are
collecting enough information to enable your organization to effectively
detect and identify suspicious activity. The requirements of identifying a
customer vary by jurisdiction so you must keep up to date with your local
regulations. For example, each jurisdiction decides which government
documents are acceptable for identity verification purposes. In one
jurisdiction a driver’s license might be sufficient—in others it may not.
Your organization’s policy will set its global standard, which might include due
diligence measures. For example, most jurisdictions require that beneficial
ownership be collected at a threshold of 25% or more ownership. So, you
need to know every customer who owns at least 25%. Your organization will
set the appropriate limit to be observed in each jurisdiction and must embed
the requirements set by the local regulator. For higher-risk customers, the
threshold might be reduced to 10%.
You must also be aware of the laws and regulations in the countries in which
your firm does business, even if your firm has no presence there. This is
particularly relevant for economic sanctions screening and to prevent
financial crime. For example, the Foreign Corrupt Practices Act in the United
States, and the Bribery Act 2010 in the United Kingdom, both have extra-
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 38
territorial reach. This is the legal ability of a government to exercise authority
beyond its normal boundaries.
Unilateral sanctions
Countries may choose to take unilateral action against an individual, group,
regime, jurisdiction, or country rather than, or in addition to, pursuing
multilateral sanctions. Each country has different powers to impose unilateral,
or autonomous, sanctions.
Unilateral sanctions can result in legal instruments such as:
• Executive orders
• Regulations
• Acts
These instruments are used to impose sanctions to address specific foreign
policy or national security objectives.
The range of, and legal authority for, unilateral sanctions varies widely by
country. The jurisdictional reach of unilateral sanctions will also vary. For
instance, the US in isolation has imposed broad-ranging unilateral sanctions
against Cuba. As the US action is unilateral, it is not legally recognized in other
jurisdictions.
Examples of unilateral sanctions include:
• Canada, in response to the threat of terrorism, implemented autonomous
sanctions under the Special Economic Measures Act (SEMA), which is
administered and enforced by the Canadian Minister of Foreign Affairs
• South Korea has imposed sanctions on a unilateral basis. Designations of
individuals, corporations, and organizations related to the financing of
terrorism are made under its Act on Prohibition Against the Financing of
Terrorism and Prohibition of Financing for Offences of Public Intimidation &
Proliferation of Weapons of Mass Destruction
• Japan has implemented an autonomous sanctions regime against North
Korea in response to the regime’s intercontinental ballistic missile tests and
wider nuclear weapons development program
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 39
• The United Kingdom targeted human rights violators in an autonomous
sanctions regime under the Global Human Rights Sanctions Regulations in
2020
• The United States has imposed unilateral sanctions, including asset freezes
and travel bans, on a wide range of individuals, entities, and countries which
they consider to pose a risk to US and global security
• Russia has imposed sanctions against Ukrainian citizens and companies, as
well as judges of the Constitutional Court of Ukraine
• China has also introduced its own sanctions regime; this includes measures
to target foreign companies which pose a national security threat
• Finally, Australia implements its own sanctions under the Autonomous
Sanctions Act of 2011
Extraterritoriality of sanctions programs
Extraterritorial jurisdiction, or extraterritoriality, is the ability of a state to make,
apply, and enforce laws, regulations, and other rules of conduct toward
persons, property, or activity beyond its territory. In the context of sanctions,
this means that a government asserts its authority to enforce sanctions
regimes on foreign entities and individuals, even if they are not within its
jurisdiction.
The US is the primary jurisdiction engaged in applying extraterritoriality to its
sanctions regimes. The EU believes the practice of extraterritoriality violates
international law and does not allow extraterritoriality to the sanctions
restrictions it imposes.
Extraterritorial sanctions can have significant global implications. They can
affect international trade, diplomacy, and relationships between nations. Two
key examples of US extraterritoriality are sanctions against Iran and its nuclear
program, and the embargo on Cuba that has been in place since 1962.
US sanctions against Iran and Cuba illustrate how extraterritorial sanctions can
impact international business and diplomatic relations by extending the reach
of one country's sanctions beyond its borders.
In an effort to diminish Iran's nuclear program, the US imposed extraterritorial
sanctions on foreign companies and entities conducting business with Iran.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 40
This meant that non-US companies engaging in certain types of transactions
with Iran could face penalties from the US government, even if those
transactions did not directly involve US entities. These sanctions aim to
discourage global business ties with Iran and put economic pressure on the
Iranian government.
The US sanctions against Cuba are one of the world's longest-running
boycotts initiated by one country against another. The initial sanctions against
Cuba, ordered in 1962, are still in place today. The Helms-Burton Act of 1996 is
a US law that extended the terms of the US embargo against Cuba. The Act
includes provisions that allow US citizens to take legal action against foreign
companies that traffic property into the US that was confiscated by the Cuban
government after the Cuban revolution. This extraterritorial aspect of the law
has been controversial as it can potentially lead to legal action against non-US
entities doing business in Cuba, even though their home countries may not
recognize the US embargo.
Extraterritoriality of sanctions programs
(Case example: Cynergy Bank)
UK-based Cynergy Bank (Cynergy) borrowed £30 million from Lamesa
Investments Limited (Lamesa). Russian oligarch Viktor Vekselberg was the
ultimate beneficial owner (UBO) of Lamesa’s parent company, Lamesa Group
Incorporated, located in Cyprus. After Lamesa made the loan to Cynergy,
OFAC designated Vekselberg as a Specially Designated National (SDN) for his
ties to the Russian government. The sanctions against Vekselberg extended
automatically to Lamesa.
Cynergy refused to pay the interest that was due every six months, stating
that making a transaction with Lamesa would violate US secondary sanctions
against Russia. As a result, Lamesa took legal action against Cynergy, arguing
that US sanctions did not apply to non-US entities. Therefore, Cynergy was not
responsible for the payments. After a series of court cases and appeals, the
legal system found that Cynergy was complying with US sanctions by not
paying the loan interest.
The US is the main jurisdiction that imposes sanctions with extraterritorial
reach. US secondary sanctions against Russia have extraterritorial reach. This
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 41
means that sanctions apply to a non-US person or entity that does business
with a Russian entity, or on behalf of a Russian entity.
When OFAC designated Vekselberg as an SDN, his assets were blocked,
including his majority ownership interest in Cyprus-based Lamesa. With the
understanding that non-US entities are subject to US sanctions if they
knowingly engage in "significant transactions" with a US sanctioned entity,
Cynergy stopped making payments to Lamesa.
In its legal claim against Cynergy, Lamesa argued that US sanctions did not
exempt the borrower from repaying the loan because Cynergy was based in
the UK. The court determined that Cynergy was complying with US secondary
sanctions by not making loan repayments. Even though Cynergy did not
qualify as a US person, it was still legally obligated to follow US sanctions
because the lender’s UBO was subject to US sanctions.
Key takeaways
US sanctions have extraterritorial reach.
• When an individual is designated as an SDN, the restrictions apply to assets
regardless of their geographical location.
• If an individual or entity engages in transactions with an SDN, they could be
violating US sanctions.
• Non-US entities may need to comply with US sanctions.
Sanctions blocking regulations
The divergent legal and regulatory requirements of various sanctions regimes
can produce complicated legal scenarios. For example, it may be impossible
to simultaneously comply with conflicting sanctions legislation between two
or more countries. The most notable of these scenarios involves blocking
regulation-type provisions, which are implemented by the EU, UK, Canada,
and more recently China. Both natural and legal persons need to know how to
comply with divergent and conflicting cross-border sanctions obligations.
The EU Blocking Regulation is one example of how complying with one set of
obligations may create conflicts with sanctions imposed by other countries.
The EU statute aims to protect EU businesses and individuals, charities, and
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 42
voluntary bodies that are conducting lawful activities outside the US from the
impact of certain extraterritorial US sanctions. The statute prohibits member
states from complying with these sanctions unless exceptionally authorized to
do so by the European Commission. These requirements apply to all EU
persons, EU incorporated subsidiaries and registered branches of US
companies, US natural persons located in the EU, and EU nationals who are
employees of US companies.
The EU and UK have issued guidance on implementing their blocking
regulation requirements. This covers enforcement for breaches, reporting,
damage recovery, and whether applying for a license from relevant US
authorities is considered “complying” with the prohibited US sanctions.
It is important to note that blocking regulations are not intended to “force”
companies to conduct business with certain sanctioned jurisdictions or
entities.
The impact of cross-border legal and regulatory conflicts and the implications
for global business is amplified by the continual expansion of entities subject
to US secondary sanctions. In such cases, local regulations or laws may
establish prohibitions in respect to terminating or declining relationships on
the grounds of US sanctions.
Given the increasing use of sanctions, careful consideration is required to
identify potential legal and regulatory conflicts. This should include measures
to mitigate against risk that could arise from conflict-of-law scenarios.
Sanctions blocking regulations (Case
example: JCPOA)
The Joint Comprehensive Plan of Action (JCPOA), signed in 2015, was a
nuclear agreement between Iran and the P5+1, together with the EU. The P5+1
refers to the five permanent members of the UN Security Council—France, the
UK, China, Russia, and the US plus Germany. As part of the agreement, Iran
regained access to significant assets that had been frozen previously. In
addition, Iran was able to resume oil sales on international markets.
In 2018, US President Trump announced the US withdrawal from the JCPOA
and reimposed sanctions which had been lifted via the JCPOA. The
extraterritorial reach of US sanctions meant non-US organizations, including
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 43
EU financial institutions that did business with Iran, could face secondary
sanctions.
The EU’s blocking statute offered protection to EU businesses that chose to
continue to do business with Iran. Its primary purpose was to protect their
economic interests by prohibiting actions aligning with foreign restrictive
measures that could be contrary to the EU's foreign policy objectives. The
Instrument in Support of Trade Exchanges (INSTEX) was created as part of
the EU blocking regulation. Established in 2019 by France, Germany, and the
UK, INSTEX’s mission was to act as a special purpose vehicle that would enable
continued sanctions lifting to reach Iran and allow for European exporters and
importers to pursue legitimate trade.
Although there was a strong and sustained demand by European exporters to
use the mechanism, it frequently met resistance from colleagues at finance
ministries and central banks. One reason for the resistance was attributed to
concerns over the impacts of US secondary sanctions. Nonetheless, in March
2020, INSTEX completed its first transaction to facilitate the export of medical
goods from Europe to Iran. Given that Europe had failed to sustain its imports
of Iranian oil and focused instead on humanitarian trade, Iranian officials
dismissed other INSTEX efforts to ensure continued trade. Without full
cooperation from Iran, INSTEX could not reliably net the monies owed by
European importers to Iranian exporters and vice versa. In 2023, the board of
INSTEX decided to liquidate the company.
Key takeaways
Blocking regulations:
• Are meant to protect economic interests and promote foreign policy
objectives
• Aim to facilitate humanitarian aid and allow for legitimate economic
activities
o The success of blocking regulations often depends on international
cooperation among participating jurisdictions.
o Exclusions can enable specific transactions with sanctioned entities
under strict conditions.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 44
Jurisdictional reach of European Union
sanctions
The jurisdictional reach of EU sanctions is broad. Sanctions can apply to
individuals and entities with ties to the EU both inside and outside of its
territory. EU member states are responsible for implementing and enforcing
autonomous EU sanctions within their respective jurisdictions. The same is
valid for sanctions issued by the UN Security Council.
For example, a German citizen residing in Hong Kong facilitates fundraising on
Hong Kong’s private debt market. The fundraising is for the benefit of a state-
owned Iranian company, which is designated by the EU. This activity would
violate the prohibition on making funds and resources available to a
designated entity by an EU person.
EU sanctions target individuals and entities involved in activities that violate
international law, human rights, or EU foreign policy goals. They have both
direct legal effect within EU member states and some extraterritorial reach,
meaning they apply to individuals and entities located outside the EU's
borders. EU sanctions can thus be directly applied against EU citizens and legal
entities such as companies, organizations, or governmental bodies involved in
activities that go against EU objectives.
This extraterritorial reach allows the EU to take enforcement measures
against EU individuals and EU-registered entities and their foreign branches
outside of the EU. Contrary to the US, the EU requires an EU nexus, or
connection, to the EU jurisdiction, whereas the US government may impose
secondary sanctions. Unlike primary sanctions, secondary sanctions are fully
extraterritorial and do not require a nexus with the US jurisdiction.
EU member states are required to adopt national legislation and regulations to
remain compliant with EU sanctions. Noncompliance with the sanctions could
result in penalties and legal consequences such as fines or criminal liability,
depending on the violation.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 45
Jurisdictional reach of US secondary
sanctions
The United States is the primary government that applies extraterritoriality to its
sanctions regimes. These “secondary sanctions” are directed at third-country, or
non-US, persons who engage in certain activities with the targets of existing US
sanctions. By enforcing secondary sanctions, the United States aims to discourage
non-US persons, wherever they are in the world, from conducting business with
designated entities and specific countries.
Numerous US statutory and executive orders provide the authority to impose
secondary sanctions. Although their use was limited before 2017, enactment of the
Countering America's Adversaries Through Sanctions Act (CAATSA) that year
made secondary sanctions a notable feature of evolving sanctions legislation.
“Secondary sanctions” is a broad term that covers a wide array of restrictions on
non-US companies and individuals. The precise restrictions and scope of
secondary sanctions can vary considerably between sanctions programs. Not all
programs have a secondary sanctions effect.
Typically, secondary sanctions prohibit non-US companies from engaging in
business activities that benefit a sanctioned country or entity. For example,
secondary sanctions could be applied in situations such as purchasing oil,
engaging in pipeline development, selling military equipment, and making funds
available in violation of US sanctions.
Unlike US primary sanctions, no jurisdictional US connection is required to trigger
secondary sanctions restrictions. This connection could be to the US financial
system, US economy, or a US individual. Due to the absence of a US jurisdictional
connection, secondary sanctions do not impose fines or similar penalties on a
non-US person for “violations.” Instead, persons or entities engaging in activity that
is “sanctionable” are potentially subject to restrictions that can range from loss of
access to the US economy to being placed on the Specially Designated Nationals
and Blocked Persons (SDN) List.
As a result, through secondary sanctions, the US makes, applies, and enforces laws
