and regulations in respect to persons, property, and activity beyond its territory—
what is known as extraterritorial jurisdiction. The legal implications of secondary
sanctions are significant because they impose US restrictions on non-US parties,
even when they have no contact with or presence in the United States.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 46
How Do Sanctions
Change Behavior?
Introduction
Setup: Mr. Cano's mortgage
You work in the busy mortgage lending department of a regional bank in
Mexico. As a mortgage loan officer, you are often the first point of contact for
potential borrowers. You have a monthly sales quota, which sometimes makes
your job stressful. But you enjoy helping people obtain a mortgage so they can
purchase their dream home. Typically, when you meet with a customer, you
gather initial information about the customer’s employment, financial history,
and properties owned. Then you provide the customer with details about
different mortgage products and help them fill out the mortgage application.
Today, you have a meeting with a new mortgage customer named Gabriel
Cano. Mr. Cano has a checking account with your bank and is a resident of
Mexico City. His financial history seems flawless. He is meeting with you today
to apply for a mortgage to finance a house locally. You think he will easily
qualify for a mortgage.
While Mr. Cano fills out a self-assessment in your office, he informs you that he
owns a house in Cuba. He plans to sell the house below market value to his
brother who lives in Argentina. He wants to use the funds from the sale of the
house in Cuba to make the down payment on the house in Mexico City. Mr.
Cano asks you if it is possible for his brother in Argentina to transfer
approximately US$100,000 from the sale of the house in Cuba to his account
at your bank.
This seems like an odd request. You recall from sanctions training that there
might be some international restrictions regarding capital flows to and from
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 47
Cuba. You tell Mr. Cano that you will have to research his question and get
back to him.
After your meeting, you explain the situation to your colleague, Daniela, hoping
to get some advice. Daniela reminds you that the US maintains a
comprehensive economic embargo on Cuba due to political and ideological
conflicts involving human rights concerns, the status of political prisoners, and
the lack of free elections. Because your bank has ties to the US, and often
transacts in US dollars, ignoring these restrictions could result in legal
complications or even penalties. However, Daniela thinks these restrictions
might not apply in this case because the funds will come from Argentina and
not directly from Cuba.
What would you do?
What action should you take?
• Report the activity to OFAC.
• Proceed for humanitarian reasons.
• Escalate to your compliance team.
• Use an escrow arrangement to comply with sanctions.
Feedback
The best response is to escalate this case to your compliance team. The
transaction is prohibited under US law because it is related to an investment in
real estate located in a country that is comprehensively sanctioned by the US.
The compliance team will be able to check whether any exceptions or
licenses exist that would permit this activity.
Reporting this suspicious activity to OFAC is not the best response. The
reporting process only exists for blocked property and rejected transactions.
It is not meant for hypothetical violations.
Proceeding with the transaction for humanitarian reasons, such as facilitating
family support, is not the best response. This would require a very careful legal
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 48
review to ensure it falls within the scope of allowed humanitarian transactions
under OFAC regulations.
Using an escrow arrangement to comply with sanctions is not the best
response. If no exception or license for such activity is available, this could be
an additional violation of the comprehensive US sanctions against Cuba. The
escrow arrangement could be considered facilitation if it provides a service or
supports a trade or financial transaction with Cuba.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Identify to whom sanctions apply.
• Describe the types of sanctions and give examples.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 49
Who is subject to sanctions?
Individuals and entities required to comply
with sanctions
Everyone is subject to sanctions based on jurisdiction. This includes individuals
and entities. However, not everyone needs to have a sanctions compliance
program. Most individuals will not have a sanctions compliance program and
will not interact with sanctions law in their personal lives.
Organizations will need to have a sanctions compliance program:
• If they are directly regulated for sanctions compliance
• If they are exposed to sanctions risk based on the nature, size, and
complexity of their business activity
In the case of the latter, each organization will need to assess its risk exposure
to determine what sanctions controls, if any, should be implemented.
Citizens of a country, and permanent residents, must comply with sanctions
regardless of whether they are outside of their home country. This is true for
US, EU, and most other autonomous sanctions. If a person is on vacation
overseas, their country’s sanctions laws still apply. Conversely, any individual,
regardless of citizenship, must comply with the sanctions law of any country
where they are physically located.
For legal entities, the rules are similar to those for individuals. Any legal entity
physically located in a jurisdiction is subject to that jurisdiction. Foreign
branches, agencies, and subsidiaries in the US must comply with US sanctions.
Any foreign branches, agencies, and subsidiaries physically located within the
territory of the EU, including its airspace, must comply with EU sanctions.
Organizations should assess their sanctions risk exposure when developing a
sanctions compliance program. While most organizations will not need to
have a formal sanctions compliance program, they will still need to comply
with sanctions. Regulated organizations, such as financial institutions, are
required to have sanctions compliance programs. This is primarily because of
their direct involvement in the movement of funds. Nonregulated
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 50
organizations must also comply with sanctions. Sanctions issues might arise
within their supply chain or end user. Additionally, some industries including
finance, cosmetics, and energy, have higher exposure to sanctions.
Organizations should consider their activities along with their suppliers and
buyers when determining their need for a sanctions compliance program.
Eligible targets of sanctions
While any person might become sanctioned, these restrictions are typically
limited to nations, political organizations and leaders, economic agents, and
other individuals engaged in illegal networks or activity. An individual would
probably not personally conduct due diligence on a vendor prior to buying a
cup of coffee to determine whether the vendor is sanctioned. However, the
vendor could be held liable, for example, if the beans were sourced from a
sanctioned jurisdiction such as Iran. There are two limitations on who may be
sanctioned. First, a country may not sanction its own citizens without violating
its own laws. Second, sanctions need to consider human rights and
humanitarian obligations.
Sanctions are an alternative to the use of violence and often have political
objectives. With the growth and interconnectivity of the financial system,
sanctions have expanded to achieve nonpolitical means, such as deterring the
proliferation of terrorism. As our understanding of the consequences of
sanctions has increased, sanctions have become increasingly more targeted
to reduce the impact on nontargeted individuals. An example is the use of
sectoral sanctions. These types of sanctions are limited sanctions that target
specific economic sectors such as energy, finance, or defense.
If a person is based overseas, their country’s sanctions laws still apply. This is
true for the EU, the US, and most other jurisdictions. Any individual, regardless
of citizenship, must comply with the sanctions laws of any country where they
are physically located. The rules for legal entities are similar to those for
individuals. Any legal entity physically located in a jurisdiction is subject to the
sanction laws of that jurisdiction.
While any one person could be sanctioned, countries may be limited from
sanctioning their own citizens and residents. For example, most developed
countries cannot seize their citizens’ property without a formal hearing or trial.
Protections often depend on a person’s connections to the sanctioning
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 51
country. A foreign person with strong connections to a sanctioning country
may have more protection.
Fundamental or international human rights and humanitarian obligations limit
sanctions. These limitations often apply when countries seek to apply
multilateral sanctions. These obligations do not undermine the legality of a
sanctions regime but may provide protection within the overall program.
Facilitation
Facilitation is the act of making something easy or easier. In the context of
sanctions, facilitation is the act of making non-sanctioned activity easier for a
third party that would otherwise be sanctionable had the facilitator engaged in
it directly.
Within the context of a sanctionable transaction, facilitation includes providing
personnel, software, technology, or goods and services of any kind. It also
includes purchasing, selling, transporting, swapping, brokering, financing,
approving, or guaranteeing goods or services. Facilitation even includes
referring business opportunities to foreign entities. According to the Office of
Foreign Assets Control (OFAC), US persons are prohibited from facilitation.
Other jurisdictions, such as the EU, have anti-circumvention regulations that
are similar.
Most US sanctions programs have provisions against facilitation, which could
vary slightly across regimes. While each prohibition is particular to the sanction
program, US companies should assume that facilitation prohibitions in one
program can be applied throughout other programs unless previously having
engaged with legal counsel. Large, complex US organizations with multiple
foreign subsidiaries and extensive overseas operations are especially at risk of
violating facilitation prohibitions. These US organizations cannot design
programs, including policies and procedures, that would allow their foreign
subsidiaries to engage in the sanctioned transaction.
Foreign subsidiaries of US companies that engage in US-sanctioned activities
must act independently of the US parent company. For example, if a foreign
subsidiary required a complex transaction with an OFAC-sanctioned
counterparty to be reviewed for compliance with domestic legislation, the US
parent company would be prohibited from providing legal assistance.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 52
Examples of facilitation include:
• A US citizen, who is the director of a European company, taking part in a
business decision to enter a deal with an OFAC-sanctioned entity.
• US employees working for a US-based branch of a foreign corporation and
advising the overseas parent company on conducting transactions that
would be prohibited if they were to engage in them directly.
Facilitation (Case example: Alfa Laval)
Alfa Laval (AL US) is a US-based company that sells oil storage tank cleaning
equipment. AL US, through its subsidiary, Alfa Laval Tank, Inc. (AL Tank)
violated OFAC sanctions by referring business and selling equipment to an
affiliate in the Middle East.
Alborz Pakhsh Parnia Company (Alborz), an Iranian distributor of oil products,
contacted AL Tank’s portfolio manager about purchasing its oil tank cleaning
machines and shipping them to Iran. After a series of emails regarding
product information and pricing, the portfolio manager forwarded the Alborz
request to its subsidiary, Alfa Laval Middle East (AL Middle East). To evade
sanctions against Iran and sell products to Alborz, AL Middle East and Alborz
developed a plan that falsely named a Dubai company as the end-user on
export documents.
OFAC alleged the violations were part of a conspiracy to circumvent US
sanctions. After AL Middle East received the email from AL Tank referring an
Iranian customer who wanted to order products from AL Tank, the Dubai-
based operation devised a plan with the Iranian customer. The plan involved
listing a Dubai distributor with whom AL Middle East did business as the end-
user of the shipment on its export forms. AL Middle East knew shipping
products to Iran was a violation of the Iranian Transactions and Sanctions
Regulations (ITSR).
AL Middle East did not tell AL Tank that it had falsified the end-user on export
forms. When AL Tank shipped items to the Dubai-based firm, AL Middle East
arranged for the items to go to the customer in Iran. The US Department of
Commerce Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) performed a post-shipment
verification of AL Tank’s shipment to Dubai. The verification found the items
had been reshipped from Dubai to Iran.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 53
AL Middle East ultimately enabled the shipment of US goods to Iran without
informing AL Tank. For this conspiracy and the sanctions violations, AL Middle
East faced a penalty of US$615,844 from OFAC. The settlement amount of
US$415,695 was far greater than the shipment value of less than US$18,600.
Key takeaways
• When engaging with a sanctioned country, there is potential to engage in
facilitation, either knowingly or inadvertently.
• Organizations should have clear processes for handling and escalating
customer contacts with a sanctions nexus, or connection.
• Escalating customer contacts should involve legal and compliance.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 54
Sanctions types
Types of sanctions and embargoes
Sanctions can take many forms. They include financial, economic, and trade
sanctions, embargoes, and other types of restrictive measures imposed by
regulatory bodies. Sanctions can limit activity with sanctioned parties and
target specific sectors, jurisdictions, or countries. They can be targeted at
individuals, entities, or groups, or apply to an entire country, as in
comprehensive sanctions. Understanding the different types of sanctions
enables you to support a strong governance, risk management, and
compliance framework.
Sanctions include:
• Financial and economic sanctions
• Trade sanctions
• Asset freezes
• Travel bans
Financial, economic, and trade sanctions are imposed to restrict economic
activity. They can restrict specific sectors, ban investments, and prohibit the
supply of certain services.
Trade sanctions restrict or prohibit trade, including import and export bans on
certain goods. These sanctions can also include sectoral sanctions, which
target a specific sector of the economy or a specific economic activity. Arms
embargoes are imposed to prevent the supply, sale, or transfer of items used
for military purposes to specific individuals, groups, or countries.
Asset freezes are imposed to prevent the assets belonging to a sanctioned
person or entity from being used.
Travel bans are imposed to restrict the movement of sanctioned persons.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 55
Sanctions can also be comprehensive or targeted.
• Comprehensive sanctions are imposed, broadly speaking, on an entire
jurisdiction or country
• Targeted sanctions, also known as selective or smart sanctions, focus their
impact on specific leaders
Financial and economic sanctions (Case
example: Apartheid)
In 1962, the UN General Assembly passed a nonbinding resolution
condemning apartheid and called for economic sanctions against South
Africa. Apartheid was a system of segregation or discrimination based on
race. The South African government established an apartheid system in 1948.
The apartheid government required passes for nonwhite males to travel,
dislocated nonwhites from their homes, and prohibited nonwhite individuals
from voting due to their race. The African National Congress (ANC) was the
primary organization used by nonwhites to fight against the injustices of
apartheid. The ANC held protests against the South African government and
organized boycotts. In 1962, many of the ANC members were imprisoned. In
the late 1980s, through coordinated, international sanctions encouraged by
the ANC, the South African government began reforming its unjust system
and dismantling its apartheid policies.
In 1964, the South African government imprisoned anti-apartheid activist
Nelson Mandela for his involvement in the armed resistance to its apartheid
policies. He remained in prison for 27 years. It was not until 1989 when the US,
UK, and other developed nations passed legislation banning investment in
South Africa that its ruling government began to respond. The sanctions
broke fiscal and trading links with South Africa, making the apartheid
government unable to uphold itself and its policies. Sanctions against South
Africa included bans on air travel and agricultural imports. The promotion of
South African tourism was also prohibited. Bank loans to South African
companies were banned along with imports of coal, iron, steel, and uranium.
When Mandela was released in 1990, he said sanctions could not yet be
relaxed because nonwhites still did not have political rights in South Africa.
Mandela stated: “I might be out of jail, but I am not free.” While most sanctions
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 56
against South Africa were lifted in 1991, the last sanctions were not lifted until
1993. In 1994, South Africa held its first democratic elections where all people
could vote regardless of the color of their skin. Mandela was the first South
African president elected in a fully representative democratic election.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions might lead to involuntary change.
• Sanctions are typically considered an alternative to war and violence; they
can also be used to foster internal change.
• Sanctions might be issued when there are political injustices within a
jurisdiction.
Asset freeze and making funds available
(Case example: Russian Oligarchs)
Roman Abramovich, a Russian former government official and oligarch, was
the majority owner of the Chelsea Football Club (CFC) in the UK’s Premier
League. In March 2022, following the Russian invasion of Ukraine, the UK
government sanctioned Abramovich for his alleged close ties to Russian
President Vladimir Putin. His assets were frozen; he was prohibited from
conducting transactions with UK individuals and businesses and was subject to
a travel ban.
As CFC’s future became uncertain, the UK government issued a special
license for CFC to continue to play football matches and negotiate contracts
with players. The UK lifted restrictions in May 2022 when the club was bought
out by a US-led consortium. However, Abramovich’s assets remained frozen.
As Russia invaded Ukraine, the EU, UK, US, and other jurisdictions imposed
sanctions against numerous Russian oligarchs, including Abramovich. Their
goal was to pressure the oligarchs into convincing President Putin to stop the
aggression against Ukraine and to prevent the flow of funds to Putin’s regime.
In March 2023, the Russian Elites, Proxies, and Oligarchs Task Force
announced it had frozen US$58 billion worth of sanctioned Russian assets. The
oligarchs' frozen assets included mega yachts, private jets, and luxury
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 57
residences. The comprehensive asset freezes led to legal challenges in the EU
and the UK.
According to press reports, Abramovich offered the government of Ukraine
his mediation to help negotiate a ceasefire with the Putin administration,
which never materialized. In exchange, Ukraine’s president Zelensky
reportedly pleaded with the US government to keep Abramovich’s name off
the OFAC sanctions list. In doing so, Roman Abramovich stopped short of
criticizing the Putin administration. By contrast, the fellow Russian billionaire
Oleg Tinkov went as far as to renounce his Russian citizenship. Following his
open criticism of Russia’s war in Ukraine, the UK’s Treasury removed Tinkov
from its sanctions list.
Key takeaways
• Asset freezing is intended to restrict the actions of sanctioned individuals.
• Without access to resources, individuals are unable to fund dangerous
illegal acts such as a war of aggression.
• Individuals or entities can be removed from sanctions lists with approval
from the appropriate governing body, upon meeting certain conditions.
Trade sanctions including arms embargoes
(Case example: Democratic Republic of
Congo)
In July 2003, the UN passed Resolution 1493 placing an arms embargo on the
Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC). The embargo stated that no one, either
inside or outside of the Congo, should provide direct or indirect assistance to
movements and armed groups in the DRC. The embargo, which included
both military and financial assistance, had a limited duration and was subject
to review, renewal, and modification by the UN.
In 2022, the UN removed its arms embargo on the DRC, leaving in place a
notification requirement. The relevant UN Sanctions Committee must be
notified in advance of any shipment of arms and related materiel to the DRC
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 58
with information such as the end-user, delivery date, and the itinerary of the
shipments.
The UN originally determined that violence in the DRC was a threat to
international peace. Rebel military groups were violating human rights and
committing crimes against civilians. Widespread crimes included massacres
and sexual violence against women and girls. To protect innocent people, the
UN created the UN Organization Stabilization Mission (MONUSCO) in the DRC.
The UN passed Resolution 1493, which prohibited weapons supply and military
assistance to armed groups. The goal of the arms embargo was to bring an
end to the violence.
Resolution 1552 extended the arms embargo for two more years, until July 31,
2005. In April 2005, Resolution 1596 extended the embargo’s coverage but
allowed some arms to be imported into the DRC. Imports were allowed if the
UN Sanctions Committee was notified in advance.
In March 2008, to allow UN member states to provide military supplies and
assistance to the DRC, the UN removed all restrictions on arms shipments to
the DRC government. However, the notification requirements remained in
place.
In June 2022, Resolution 2641 reduced the notification requirement even
further, leaving notifications in place for heavy artillery such as rocket
launchers. In December 2022, Resolution 2667 ended the notification
requirement altogether. At the same time, under Resolution 2666, the UN
renewed MONUSCO’s peacekeeping mandate for the DRC.
Key takeaways
UN arms embargoes:
• Have limited duration and are subject to review, renewal, and modification
• Can change based on an embargoed country’s stability
• Can be relaxed, supplemented with notification requirements, or
strengthened
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 59
Trade sanctions: Items subject to export
control (Case example: Electronics for
Russian weapons)
In September 2023, a New York resident and two UK nationals were convicted
in a US court in a sanctions and export control circumvention scheme. Millions
of US dollars’ worth of semiconductors, integrated circuits, and other dual-use
electronic components had been unlawfully exported to sanctioned end-
users in Russia through two New York-based front companies. The individuals
used a chain of three other entities based in the UK and Turkey to facilitate the
scheme. The dual-use electronic components in question are commonly
used in the production of Russian weapons systems.
Ivan Davydov, of Brooklyn, New York; Dmitry Andreev, of London, UK; and
Temir Murtazov, of Manchester, UK were involved in the export control evasion
enterprise. They received orders from Russian end-users in the defense and
technology sectors who were seeking specific items or parts from the US. The
team used aliases to purchase the electronic components from US
manufacturers and distributors. They used two corporate entities registered
in Brooklyn to acquire the items. Once the components arrived in New York,
the team acted with the assistance of foreign intermediaries to ship the items
to corporations in other countries where they were then rerouted to Russia.
There were numerous bank accounts used to conduct financial transactions,
furthering the scheme.
Many of the requested components were the same as those found in seized
Russian weapons platforms and intelligence equipment in Ukraine, including
multi-purpose guided missiles, helicopters, and battle tanks. The individuals
knew the electronic components had potential military applications. They
were also aware of US export control laws and took action to conceal
activities to deceive US manufacturers, distributors, and law enforcement.
Electronic message exchanges showed the individuals discussing how
shipping to Russia had become dangerous after a shipment of electronic
components was detained at a US airport.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 60
Key takeaways
• Front and shell companies are often used in schemes to circumvent
export control restrictions.
• Controlled goods are shipped to third countries from where they can be
reexported to sanctioned jurisdictions.
• Numerous bank accounts in several jurisdictions could conceal the money
trail and complicate investigations.
Comprehensive sanctions (Case example:
Sanctions on Iraq)
A comprehensive sanctions program aims to restrict most trade and prohibit
most other commercial activity with an entire jurisdiction. On August 2, 1990,
Iraqi President Saddam Hussein ordered the invasion of Kuwait to take over
Kuwaiti oil reserves and expand Iraqi power into the Persian Gulf region. Four
days later, the United Nations Security Council (UNSC) imposed
comprehensive sanctions against Iraq. The financial and trade sanctions were
strictly enforced, with exceptions made for humanitarian aid such as food and
medicine. By March 1991, Iraq was forced out of Kuwait by an American-led
military coalition. However, the sanctions remained in place with no clear plan
for lifting them.
The comprehensive sanctions prohibited all trade with Iraq. The original
purpose of the sanctions was to compel Iraq to withdraw from Kuwait, to pay
reparations, and to disclose and eliminate any weapons of mass destruction
(WMD).
The UN sanctions against Iraq severely damaged the Iraqi economy. Hundreds
of thousands of middle-class citizens fled abroad in search of a better life. The
sanctions prohibited Iraq’s oil exports and immobilized its foreign assets. As a
result, Iraq’s war debt reached US$70 billion, an equivalent of about seven
years' worth of its pre-war oil revenues. After more than six years of economic
devastation, inflation rose by thousands of percent. In early 1996, the Iraqi
government stopped printing money to try to contain inflation.
Iraq’s sewage treatment and water purification systems and facilities were
largely destroyed during the Gulf War. Under the sanctions, Iraq was not able
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 61
to import chlorine for water purification or machine parts to repair damaged
purification plants and related facilities.
Iraq’s hospitals fell into disrepair as a result of the sanctions. The buildings had
broken windows, leaky roofs, and broken doors. There were no ventilation
systems, electricity, or working elevators. Expensive imported equipment,
including basic items, was not being replaced.
Key takeaways
• Comprehensive sanctions aim to restrict most trade and prohibit most
other commercial activity with an entire jurisdiction.
• Comprehensive sanctions can cause inflation.
• Restricting imports under a comprehensive sanctions program can result
in limited available resources for infrastructure maintenance.
Targeted sanctions (Case example:
Magnitsky Act)
Sergei Magnitsky was a Russian attorney and auditor representing the owner
of Hermitage Capital Management, an investment firm that specialized in
Russian markets. Magnitsky discovered a US$230 million tax fraud scheme that
involved a group of officials in the Russian Interior Ministry and three
Hermitage-owned companies. The Russian officials executed the scheme
using stolen identities and forged documents.
Shortly after Magnitsky reported the scheme to the Russian authorities, he
was arrested and charged with tax evasion by the same Russian officials he
had accused of fraud. He spent over a year in prison without a trial and was
denied access to his family and his attorneys. He ultimately died in prison. His
death led to the adoption by US Congress of the 2012 Magnitsky Act and the
2016 Global Magnitsky Human Rights Accountability Act.
The Global Magnitsky Act promotes respect for human rights at all levels of
government. Targeted human rights sanctions can be applied to anyone
involved in human rights violations or corruption, from senior officials to low-
level officers, including accomplices without governmental links. These
sanctions are meant to deter degrading behaviors and corruption by making
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 62
the entity or individual responsible suffer concrete repercussions of the
abuses perpetrated by them.
The Act allows for unilateral, fully-blocking sanctions to be imposed on human
rights offenders and corrupt actors. In particular, it enables the US
government to sanction public officials involved in human rights abuses
anywhere in the world. Offenders’ assets can be frozen and they can be
denied entry to the US.
There are also incentives for governments outside the US to improve
accountability. Foreign cooperation in the spirit of the Act demonstrates a
shared commitment to combat human rights abusers. While the Global
Magnitsky Act originated in the US, other jurisdictions have since adopted
similar laws, including the UK and the EU. Some countries and territories have
expanded their existing sanctions regimes to include human rights abuses
irrespective of jurisdiction.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions can be used to promote respect for human rights at all levels of
government by holding violators accountable.
• Individuals or entities who are targeted by the sanctions can have their
assets frozen.
• Jurisdictions have adopted or expanded sanctions regimes to include
human rights abuses in any jurisdiction.
Sanctions exceptions, exemptions, and
licenses (Case example: Indirect exposure)
Sensible Tech is a US-based technology company that is licensed to distribute
its software and technology solutions via third-party distributors. The license
authorizes sales to financial institutions listed under the sanctions regulations
for Russia and Ukraine.
Sensible Tech’s primary distributor sells products to a Belarussian financial
institution on the OFAC Specially Designated National (SDN) list without
Sensible Tech’s approval. The financial institution is listed under Belarus
sanctions regulations, so the transactions fall outside the license scope. This
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 63
potentially exposes Sensible Tech to US sanctions violations due to indirect
exposure to a sanctioned entity.
Sensible Tech’s products were targeted toward small and midsize financial
institutions. Their primary distributor, Rebel RegTech, is an established brand in
Russia. Rebel RegTech agrees to distribute Sensible Tech’s products to
smaller financial institutions. As part of its internal controls, Sensible Tech has a
sanctions compliance program, a designated compliance officer, and
ongoing monitoring of its distributors. It provides procedures and training to
Rebel RegTech and tailors its market strategy policies to financial institutions in
Russia. Sensible Tech explains the terms of the license to Rebel RegTech and
states that primary US sanctions apply to US persons and entities located
inside the US and to transactions with a US connection.
Despite these controls, Rebel RegTech does not follow the terms of the
license and sells SensibleTech’s products to any interested party, including at
least one Belarussian SDN. By doing so, Rebel RegTech creates potential
liability for Sensible Tech for violating OFAC sanctions. If found responsible,
Sensible Tech could be liable to civil penalties including fines, administrative
action such as license revocation, or legal action such as the seizing of assets.
Key takeaways
• Organizations should establish and maintain robust internal controls with
regard to distributors.
• Organizations should ensure third parties understand and agree to comply
with license terms.
• Organizations can be held responsible if a third party engages in sanctions
violations.
• Sanctions violations can result in civil penalties, administrative action
including license revocation, or legal action.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 64
Sectoral sanctions (Case example:
Sanctions against Russia)
Sectoral sanctions were originally enacted by the US in July 2014, several
months after Russia invaded and illegally occupied Crimea. These sanctions
targeted Russia’s largest companies in the finance, energy, and defense
sectors. The sanctions severely limited foreign investment among other
things. The EU followed up with similar sanctions in September 2014.
In 2022, when Russia launched its all-out invasion of Ukraine, pre-existing
sectoral sanctions were expanded and new sanctions were adopted by more
than 30 jurisdictions, including the EU, UK, US, Japan, Canada, South Korea,
Taiwan, and Australia. They were implemented to limit Russia’s effective ability
to wage war without those nations becoming directly involved in the conflict.
The sanctions imposed since 2022 have expanded the scope of existing
sanctions while including new sectors. The new sectoral sanctions:
• Established a price cap on seaborne Russian oil and petroleum products
• Prohibited the export of aircraft engines and replacement parts to Russia
• Imposed restrictions on the Russian mining sector, including an import ban
against certain metals
• Added professional services including accounting, company
administration, trust management, and architecture to the list of restricted
sectors
• Imposed bans on the broadcasting of Russian government-controlled
media outlets
• Prohibited the facilitation of deals involving certain Russian financial
instruments
• Removed more than a dozen Russian banks from the SWIFT financial
messaging network
When the Russian banks were removed from the SWIFT messaging network,
they were prevented from making or receiving international payments. This
included the inability to obtain foreign currency or transfer assets abroad. The
banks were required to deal directly with other banks, which added delays
and extra costs, and ultimately deprived the Russian state of revenue. Some of
Page 65
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
the EU member states had been initially reluctant to impose this sanction as
they were concerned about businesses being paid for outstanding services
and possible disruption to the global payment system. Due to threats of
exclusion from SWIFT in the past, Russia developed an alternate messaging
system, SPFS, which is not widely used.
Key takeaways
• Sectoral sanctions focus on particular economic and industrial sectors.
• Sectoral sanctions can be used to limit a jurisdiction’s ability to wage war by
undermining its military potential.
• Sectoral sanctions can limit imports and exports, as well as industrial and
technological development.
Sanctions that restrict participation in
global events
Governing bodies of organizations such as sports teams or professional
organizations can suspend or ban entire jurisdictions or entities from
participating in events. These types of sanctions are different from
government sanctions as they do not have the same force of law. They are
based on an organization’s ability to regulate its activities. These sanctions can
be more damaging to a jurisdiction’s reputation than to its economy. The
reputational risk to the group or organization is often perceived as a powerful
deterrent against unwanted actions or undesirable behaviors.
Situations that restrict participation in an event are different from sanctions
that prohibit attending an organization’s event or conference. For example,
government-issued travel bans against individuals can prohibit their
participation even if they are formally invited and are expected to be in
attendance.
An organization’s governing body might apply sanctions to prevent
participation in a global event, such as the Olympic Games. This is typically
done to show solidarity with a cause, or in response to governmental
sanctions. Organization-applied sanctions can include bans on participating,
hosting, or having the sanctioned jurisdiction’s flag displayed at an event.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 66
The sanctions against Russia for its full-scale invasion of Ukraine in 2022
resulted in multiple organizations restricting and even banning Russian
nationals from participating in international events. For example, both the
Federation Internationale de Football Association (FIFA) and the Union of
European Football Associations (UEFA) banned Russia’s national team and
club teams from participating in their competitions. While the Russian national
team was banned from the 2022 FIFA World Cup in Qatar, both the Iranian and
Syrian national teams were allowed to participate, despite their home
countries being under more stringent western sanctions than Russia.
In another example, Russian contestants were banned from participating in
the Eurovision international song contest in 2022. Also, Russia’s membership in
the European Broadcasting Union, the show organizer, was suspended,
preventing participation in all future Eurovision contests.
Scientific institutions have banned or canceled their collaboration with Russian
counterparts. The European Organization for Nuclear Research (CERN)
condemned the Russian invasion of Ukraine and stated it would not engage in
any new collaboration with Russia.
Sanctions that restrict participation in
global events (Case example: World
Economic Forum)
The World Economic Forum (WEF) in Davos, Switzerland is a prestigious
gathering of political, business, and other world leaders. The forum’s purpose
is to exchange ideas, promote peaceful cooperation, and create lasting
business connections. Because of its prestige, nations send delegations,
including presidents and prime ministers, to attend. However, attendance at
the WEF in Davos is not guaranteed. For example, in 2016, the forum’s
organizing committee revoked its invitation to the Democratic People’s
Republic of Korea after the country conducted its fourth nuclear test. This was
in violation of a UN ban.
In the period between 2007 and 2021, Russian Presidents Vladimir Putin and
Dmitry Medvedev addressed the Davos Forum five times. Following Russia’s
invasion of Ukraine in 2022, the WEF banned Russian delegations from
attending the WEF Davos. The WEF further stated that it was not engaging
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 67
with individuals sanctioned by any jurisdiction. It also froze all relations with
Russian entities.
WEF President Borge Brende stated that not inviting Russia sent a strong
signal to Moscow. Before Russia would be invited back, it would need to show
it was taking actions to comply with international law. Despite comprehensive
US sanctions against their country, non-sanctioned Iranian delegates were
invited to attend. President Brende further stated that the WEF had a moral
obligation to demonstrate support for Ukraine. For example, Ukrainian
President Volodymyr Zelensky was allowed to make a keynote address at the
WEF Davos 2022 by video link from Kyiv.
In response to the WEF ban, Russia heavily promoted its own annual St.
Petersburg International Economic Forum (SPIEF) as a “Russian Davos.” In a
stark contrast with the WEF Davos, the SPIEF 2022 took place on a much
smaller scale and was boycotted by Western business leaders.
Key takeaways
• Preventing sanctioned entities from participating in global events:
o Demonstrates opposition against the actions of a sanctioned entity
o Denies them the opportunity to gain prestige and business connections
from the event
o Shows support for sanctions
• Sanctioned entities may be permitted to attend if they are engaged in
promoting peace.
Sanctions that restrict media
The spread of information has always been an important aspect of foreign
policy. During the Cold War, for example, Radio Free Europe was established
by the US government to broadcast information by radio to countries behind
the Iron Curtain.
Disinformation is the intentional spread of false information with the intent to
undermine trust in public institutions. Information removes uncertainty.
Disinformation, however, creates more uncertainty. With the advent of the
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 68
internet and social media, the ability to spread disinformation has rapidly
increased. Many jurisdictions seek to decrease the spread of disinformation by
sanctioning those involved in its creation, but stopping the spread of
disinformation, including through sanctions, has proven difficult.
Liberal democracies, in particular, have been challenged by disinformation.
Governments seek to balance the civil liberties of free speech and public
media with national security while ensuring trust in public institutions.
Foreign agents and foreign state-owned media companies can spread
disinformation using various outlets, including social media. Most recently,
these efforts have been aimed at undermining public trust in institutions and
processes, such as the electoral process. While governments often provide
exceptions or licenses to sanctions programs for the dissemination of media,
press, information, and even internet access, sanctions have recently evolved
to include targeted actions against disinformation.
In response to disinformation, many liberal democracies and institutions,
including the EU, US, and UK, have ordered measures targeting various foreign
agents and media companies. A key objective of such measures, among
others, has been to limit their ability to spread disinformation on technology
platforms, such as Facebook, YouTube, and X (formerly Twitter). However,
preventing the spread of disinformation is becoming increasingly difficult. This
is because media companies can facilitate the spread of content through
multiple third parties and unofficial, unverified online accounts.
Sanctions that restrict media (Case
example: Russian media)
In March 2022, the EU sanctioned five Kremlin-backed media outlets including
the television news channel “Russia Today” and the radio station and news
agency “Sputnik.” The sanctions suspended their broadcasting licenses as
punishment for their involvement in Russia’s efforts to spread disinformation
about its invasion of Ukraine. The ban meant that EU-based media outlets,
social media platforms, and broadcasters were no longer permitted to
broadcast, enable, facilitate, or otherwise contribute to broadcasting content
produced by these sanctioned organizations.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 69
This action is unprecedented because media regulation is typically left to the
discretion of EU member states. Despite efforts to contain the spread of
disinformation, some third parties still use virtual private networks (VPNs) to
distribute falsehoods and other banned content.
The Russian government used media outlets to manipulate information and
promote outright disinformation about the Russian invasion of Ukraine. The
disinformation was intended to destabilize the countries surrounding Russia,
the EU and its member states, and to gain support for the Russian
government. The EU sought to prevent the spread of disinformation by
sanctioning Kremlin-backed media companies and suspending their
broadcasting licenses.
In addition to media outlets, individual broadcasters were also sanctioned for
spreading misinformation about the war in Ukraine. For example, the UK
government sanctioned a well-known TV anchor, Sergey Brilev, who worked
for the Russian state-owned media company Rossiya. Brilev was described as
supporting Russian President Vladimir Putin by using his television platform to
spread Russian war propaganda.
Most sanctioned media outlets have voluntarily removed content from their
websites; however, some third parties have found ways to share banned
content through unofficial media accounts. Non-sanctioned users have been
posting links to the content on their personal social media accounts. VPNs are
also used to provide anonymous access to information on the internet.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions against media outlets are used to prevent the spread of
disinformation.
• Sanctioned media outlets, social media platforms, and broadcasters are
not permitted to broadcast, enable, facilitate, or otherwise contribute to
sanctioned content.
• Third parties could post disinformation from sanctions targets on individual
sites or through virtual private networks (VPNs).

Sanctions: Permissible
Activities and Delisting
Introduction
Setup: A Dilemma for Drilluxe
You are a senior sanctions compliance manager for Drilluxe, a drill
manufacturing company based in Canada. Drilluxe has manufacturing facilities
in the United States and Germany.
You recently marked a potential new customer as a true match for a
sanctioned entity in Iran. The CEO of your company asked your boss, the
General Counsel, if there is anything that she could do to pursue the sale. It is a
good opportunity to generate revenue before the end of the year, which is
two months away.
Your boss suggests that you research licenses. You know that the US issues
general licenses for sanctions regimes to allow certain activity that would
otherwise be prohibited. These licenses could be issued for humanitarian
reasons. You review the existing general licenses and determine that none
apply to your situation.
You begin researching specific licenses. You learn that a specific license
requires you to submit a written application to the Office of Foreign Assets
Control, or OFAC. OFAC would determine if the transaction is in the best
interest of the US and if it aligns with the goals of the sanctions program.
Your research finds that OFAC has a general policy of denial in place for the
type of transaction for which you are seeking a license. You call OFAC for
additional information. The representative tells you the processing time will
likely be several months and possibly more than a year.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 71
What would you do?
Which step should you take next?
• File an application and approve the sale.
• File an application and suspend the sale.
• File an application and mark it as “urgent.”
• Recommend against a license.
Feedback
Recommending against applying for a license is the best option. You have
information that shows a specific license would not be ready in time to meet
the end-of-year deadline. And the available general licenses are not
applicable to your business. You know you are legally obligated to obtain the
license to do business with a sanctioned entity.
Filing a license application and approving the sale while you wait is not the best
option. Filing the application does not mean that the proposed transaction is
approved. An organization must wait for OFAC’s written authorization. Until
then, the proposed transaction would be illegal.
Applying for a license and suspending the sale in the meantime is a possible
option. However, your research shows there is no guarantee for the length of
time the application review process will be completed. The process would
most likely extend beyond the end of the year deadline imposed by your
company. Therefore, this is not the best option.
While OFAC will proceed quickly in urgent situations, there is nothing here to
indicate urgency. Humanitarian considerations are the primary reason OFAC
considers an application as urgent. For this reason, marking your application as
urgent is not the best option.
