Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Define licenses and explain their purpose.
• Discuss common licensing errors, assumptions, and challenges.
• Explain the purpose and process of delisting.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 73
Licenses
The purpose of a licensing program
Certain activities that involve frozen assets can only be done with a license. A
license is a written authorization issued by a sanctions regulator that permits
an activity that otherwise might be prohibited or restricted under a particular
sanction.
The purpose of a licensing program is to balance the risk of assets being used
by a sanctions target for restricted activities while meeting the human rights
or basic needs of a sanctions target. In addition, a licensing program helps to
avoid unintended economic consequences for unrelated industries and
parties.
The laws or regulations passed to implement financial sanctions generally
include language that allows otherwise prohibited transactions to take place
under specific circumstances.
A licensing program protects national security, foreign policy, and economic
interests without imposing undue regulatory burdens on legitimate
international trade.
There are two types of licenses: general and specific. A general license, or
exemption, authorizes the performance of certain categories of transactions.
A specific license, or exception, is issued on a case-by-case basis and is
subject to certain limitations.
General licenses authorize a particular type of transaction for a class of
persons without the need to apply for a license. They are usually posted on the
sanctions regulator’s website and subscribers are notified of its presence.
OFAC has issued one or more general licenses under nearly every one of its
economic sanctions programs.
Additionally, where a general license does not exist, a written request for a
specific license must be filed. Specific licenses authorize the undertaking of
certain specified activities. A person applying for a specific license must
provide adequate evidence to support the application and demonstrate that
all application criteria have been met. In general, it is necessary to apply for a
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 74
license from the regulatory body in the jurisdiction in which the target’s frozen
funds are held.
General licenses or exemptions
A general license is an authorization, usually from the US or UK regulators,
allowing an individual or entity to engage in an activity that would otherwise be
prohibited due to sanctions. The EU uses a similar instrument called
derogations, which are normally embedded within sanctions regulations
rather than issued separately.
General licenses, or exemptions, include specific permissions and conditions
and typically have expiration dates. General licenses can be revoked or
extended. Each regulating agency has specific rules for licenses. For example,
the UK Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI) has a reporting
requirement for any payments received from a sanctioned individual or entity
under a general license. General licenses can be used by any sanctioned
individual or entity, as long as they comply with the specific conditions and
permissions of the license.
General licenses authorize basic-needs activities such as humanitarian aid,
legal services, financial services, or insurance services. A general license will
automatically apply if the activity falls within the scope of the parameters of
the license. If the activity were to fall outside the scope of the license, the
individual would need to apply for a specific license.
For example, the US Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) issued several
general licenses to ensure sanctions do not hinder legitimate humanitarian
assistance to non-designated persons. A general license issued under the
Syrian Sanctions Regulations authorizes transactions in economic sectors
including telecommunications, finance, health services, and education. These
and other transactions would have been prohibited without a general license.
In the UK, the OFSI requires reporting all general license activity within a
specific number of days, and that transaction records are retained for at least
six years. OFSI general licenses typically have an expiration date and can be
revoked at any time. An example of an OFSI general license is one regarding
the Chelsea Football Club. The club suffered when its Russian oligarch owner,
Roman Abramovich, was sanctioned due to his alleged ties to Russian
President Vladimir Putin. The football club was placed under a general license
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 75
granting it a limited ability to sell tickets, accept event bookings, and sign
contracts with players. The license permitted the football club to make
payments to employees.
Specific licenses or exceptions
A specific license, also called an exception, is issued by the administering
agency in response to a request from an individual or entity. In the EU, it takes
the form of a member state authorization. A person can apply for a specific
license when a general license or derogation does not apply to their situation.
Each license application is reviewed and approved or denied on a case-by-
case basis. The application must include a detailed description of the
proposed transaction. In the US and UK, it must be submitted in English or
include an English translation.
Persons engaging in transactions listed in a specific license must ensure the
license conditions are strictly observed.
A specific license is a written document issued by the US Office of Foreign
Assets Control (OFAC) or the UK Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation
(OFSI) in response to a request filed by a person or entity. A specific license
authorizes a set of defined activities that are prohibited by a sanctions
program, and not allowed under a general license. Applications for specific
licenses must be signed and filed with OFAC or OFSI. Any individual or entity
with an interest in a transaction or proposed transaction can apply for a
specific license.
Examples of specific licenses include:
• Release of blocked funds: A request for the release of funds blocked or
frozen due to a possible link to OFAC-administered sanctions.
• Agricultural and medical activities in Iran: A request to complete
transactions that fall outside the scope of an existing general license.
A specific license application approval is not guaranteed. The applicant must
wait for OFAC or the Secretary of the Treasury to issue the specific license
before completing the transaction. Each application is considered carefully,
and interagency consultation might occur. OFSI reviews applications and
administers decisions within four weeks of submission in most cases.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 76
Licensing authorities
To minimize the unintended effects of sanctions, a license may be requested
to allow access to frozen funds to pay for some of the sanctions target’s basic
needs. Licenses allow an individual to engage in specific or otherwise
prohibited transactions. The major sanctions regime authorities each have a
specialized unit that reviews and approves license applications. This could
include licenses to provide humanitarian aid to sanctioned jurisdictions.
In the UN, individual Member States, international organizations, and
nongovernmental organizations would seek an exemption, or general license,
from the relevant UN sanctions committee. The entity that receives the
license must observe the Member State’s related regulations and licensing
requirements.
In the EU, each member state has a government unit, such as the national
Treasury or Ministry for Foreign Affairs, that considers and grants licenses from
the prohibitions in the Sanctions Regulations of the Council of the European
Union. Licenses are only approved in certain situations specified in the EU
regulations.
In the US, the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) administers and
enforces economic sanctions programs. OFAC’s Licensing Division reviews
and decides on formal written applications for specific licenses from
individuals and entities.
Also in the US, the Department of Commerce's Bureau of Industry and
Security (BIS) administers and reviews license applications for exports,
reexports, transfers, and deemed exports such as technology transfers to
foreign nationals in the US that are subject to the US Export Administration
Regulations.
In the UK, the Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI) considers
and processes license applications in its dedicated licensing team. OFSI
considers the aims of the sanctions regime and relevant licensing grounds set
out in the regulations.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 77
Scope of licensing
Licensing grants permission for specific activities or transactions that would
otherwise be prohibited under sanctions. The scope of licensing outlines what
activities are allowed under the license. The scope of licensing can vary
depending on the specific sanctions regime and the goals of the issuing
government or authority. It is common for the issuing government or authority
to broaden the scope of a license to allow entities to conduct diplomatic or
humanitarian efforts with sanctioned jurisdictions. Anyone engaging in
transactions covered by a general or specific license must ensure that all the
license conditions are strictly observed.
Imagine a bank is holding frozen assets for a customer who wants to transfer
funds from that bank to a recipient outside the jurisdiction. The first step is to
verify the exact nature of the sanctions that affect the customer and have the
customer apply for a license from the regulator in the bank’s jurisdiction, as
this is where the funds are being held. Assume the application is successful,
and the license is granted. This license only authorizes the bank to move funds
up to a specified aggregate amount out of the jurisdiction in which it operates.
The license does not authorize the receipt of the transferred funds by the
bank or third party outside the “home” jurisdiction.
The recipient may also have to apply for a license to receive funds belonging
to the sanctions target, assuming the target is also a sanctions target in the
destination jurisdiction. It is common practice for a remitter bank to ask to see
evidence from the beneficiary bank or third party that has been issued a
license allowing them to receive funds from the sanctions target. Note that
this is true even within the EU. One cannot assume, for example, that an EU
sanction covering all member states only requires a single license for frozen
funds to be paid from one member state to another. Transferring funds
always requires a careful review to ensure full compliance with all applicable
regulations. Transfers between EU member states are no exception.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 78
Scope of licensing (Case example: Syria
Property Ltd.)
Syria Property Ltd. (Syria Property) is a real estate management company
incorporated in the UK. Syria Property is a subsidiary of Syria Finance Ltd.
(Syria Finance), an entity incorporated in Syria. Syria Property holds several
business accounts with significant balances with UK Bank PLC (UK Bank) from
which transactions are made daily.
Syria Property has a portfolio of properties rented out to businesses or
individuals, and it receives regular rental income from these properties. The
company must pay operating costs on the properties, such as maintenance
expenses, salary payments to its employees, utility bills, and taxes. When Syria
Finance became a designated person, UK Bank was obligated to freeze Syria
Property’s funds. Under these conditions, Syria Property is required to obtain a
license to regain access to its accounts at UK Bank.
UK Bank can credit interest to Syria Property’s accounts, but the company
cannot make any withdrawals without a license. Syria Property must apply for
the license and then provide the license information to the bank. There is no
obligation for UK Bank to apply for the license on the company’s behalf.
Once provided with the license, UK Bank would be able to determine which
activities are permitted on behalf and for the benefit of Syria Property. These
could include payments under employment contracts, travel expenses for
employees and directors, payments to UK authorities, national insurance,
routine property maintenance, and insurance of buildings.
UK Bank should implement enhanced monitoring of Syria Property’s accounts
to ensure any withdrawals made do not violate the license scope. This could
include manual screening to check each payment that goes out of the
company’s accounts. The bank might further be required to provide its
sanctions regulator with detailed periodic reports and bank statements for all
accounts. Similarly, the license might prohibit any payments outside of the UK.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 79
Key takeaways
• Customers apply for licenses and then provide them to their bank.
• Banks rely on customer-obtained licenses to process authorized
payments.
• Banks need to take additional steps to ensure customer transactions are
within the license scope.
Effective application of humanitarian
licenses
Sanctions are generally not intended to interrupt the export of medicines,
foodstuffs, agriculture, and other humanitarian activity to sanctioned
countries. Consequently, sanctions regimes generally provide specific
exemptions under their respective financial sanctions and export control
frameworks that permit certain humanitarian-related transactions and
activities.
A license application is required for some humanitarian exemptions. In other
cases, the relevant sanctions authority may already have issued permission to
undertake this activity. For example, a US general license may apply, or a
relevant EU or UK exemption may be in place.
Applications for licenses to support humanitarian activity should clearly state
the appropriate licensing grounds and provide solid evidence. Many
authorities provide a standard humanitarian license form. The application
should include a summary of the project, including what the persons or entity
will do, with whom they will interact, and any key financial information. It should
identify any trusted individuals and government funding involved, and other
factors that would support the application, such as relevant due diligence
already undertaken.
It may be necessary to apply to different authorities and jurisdictions beyond
those that manage financial sanctions in situations such as:
• Multiple applications
• Export of dual-use and controlled goods
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 80
• Use of US-origin goods, such as certain medical equipment
Remember, there may be some activities that are prohibited and cannot be
licensed. These vary from regime to regime and depend on the relevant
regulation. For humanitarian programs, the most common scenario that
prevents the issue of a license is activity that would benefit a UN-designated
terrorist.
Effective application of humanitarian
licenses (Case example: Taliban in
Afghanistan)
Literacy Aid is a nongovernmental organization (NGO) that helps promote
education and literacy in developing countries. Their primary recipient country
is Afghanistan. Although the NGO is based in the UK, they have US-based
donors and operate through US banks.
After the US withdrew from Afghanistan in August 2021, the Taliban took
control of the country. Several members of the Taliban were subject to UN
sanctions including ministers in the government. Literacy Aid wanted to
conduct humanitarian activities in Afghanistan, but they were unsure whether
they could continue due to sanctions regulations.
Four months later, the UN Security Council (UNSC) approved a humanitarian
exemption and OFAC issued a general license that allowed certain
transactions in Afghanistan. This authorized NGOs to provide aid to the
country.
While consulting with other charities and regulators, Literacy Aid learned that
various members of the government of Afghanistan were subject to
sanctions. To ensure they were not violating regulations, they suspended their
program while waiting for further guidelines.
The UN realized the need for NGOs to operate in Afghanistan and worked to
introduce a humanitarian exemption to the regulations. This exemption
provided relief from asset-freezing measures imposed by sanctions. OFAC
also issued General License (GL) 19 that allowed humanitarian aid and
assistance to reach Afghanistan. GL 19 allowed activities to support education
in Afghanistan, including combating illiteracy, increasing access to education,
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 81
international exchanges, and assisting education reform projects. The license
also permitted other humanitarian activities.
Literacy Aid asked their US bank to use GL 19 to transfer money from their US
donors to their correspondent bank account in Afghanistan. Part of the
donations would be used to pay taxes to the government of Afghanistan,
which included blocked persons. GL 19 did not authorize financial transfers to
any blocked persons other than for the payment of taxes, fees, import duties,
or the purchase or receipts of permits, licenses, and public utility services.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions might have unintended consequences that require humanitarian
aid.
• NGOs can use licenses or exemptions to ease the delivery of humanitarian
aid.
• General licenses and exemptions can allow US financial institutions to
operate accounts and process funds transfers for individuals and entities
engaging in humanitarian activities.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 82
Licenses: Errors, erroneous
assumptions, and challenges
Licenses: Common errors and assumptions
When dealing with licenses, you should be aware of some common errors and
assumptions. Errors can lead to noncompliance with sanctions regulations,
with potential legal consequences. The licensing process can be lengthy and
complicated. In addition, sanctions regimes change rapidly. You could easily
get confused by differences across jurisdictions.
Typical errors include:
• Misunderstanding the concept of general licenses
• Relying on past approvals
• Incorrect interpretation
• Failure to stay up-to-date
By examining some of the common errors, erroneous assumptions, and
challenges related to licensing, you will be better equipped to avoid them in
the future.
Licenses can be specific to certain activities or entities. Violations can occur if
you assume a license authorizes all activity types. A license does not permit
unrestricted transactions with a sanctioned entity. Some individuals might
attempt to use licenses to bypass or evade sanctions altogether. This can
involve misrepresenting the nature of activities or attempting to use licenses
for purposes beyond their intended scope, potentially leading to severe
penalties.
Be sure to correctly interpret the scope of the license. Misunderstanding the
allowed scope of activities, the entities covered, or the specific items or
services that are authorized can lead to unintended violations. Certain dual-
use goods or technologies with both civilian and military applications do not
require a license.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 83
Licenses are typically granted on a case-by-case basis, and past approvals
might not apply to new situations. Also, the licensing process often involves
careful review, and assumptions about timing can lead to mismanaged
expectations.
While political dynamics can influence sanctions policies, changes in
regulations might not occur immediately or at all. However, not keeping
abreast of sanctions developments could result in using outdated or incorrect
information when applying for or utilizing a license.
Incomplete documentation can also hinder the ability to demonstrate
compliance during audits or investigations. Ensure that all documentation is
kept up-to-date.
Licenses: Challenges
Licensing is a challenging, detail-driven process. As with any complex process,
there is potential to make mistakes. Awareness of some of the challenges
associated with licensing will help you avoid these mistakes.
The process of requesting and granting licenses varies significantly
depending on the sanctions regime, the entities involved, and the nature of
the licensed activities. For example, an organization that operates in multiple
jurisdictions will face different challenges and considerations as compared to
an organization with only a local presence. Regardless of size, all organizations
face similar stringent criteria during the licensing process.
There are several operational challenges associated with licensing. For
example, licenses have strict criteria for submission. Entities must ensure they
are:
• Meeting specific eligibility expectations
• Providing appropriate documentation that supports the requested activity
• Meeting the broader expectations of a sanctions regime
This process can be complex and time-consuming.
Another challenge is the uncertainty associated with timing. The amount of
time regulatory authorities take to review an application varies by jurisdiction.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 84
The review time can lead to unpredictable timelines or delays for license
approval or denial.
A license’s terms and conditions are subject to interpretation. For example, if
an entity struggles to understand the precise scope of authorized activities
outlined in a license, it might need to seek additional clarification. This could
entail additional time and expense. It is important to fully understand the
scope of the license because not doing so could lead to unintentional
violations.
Sanctions regulations can be complex and multifaceted, involving a range of
measures and considerations that can be daunting. Each jurisdiction’s license
type requires its own specific information, which will vary by jurisdiction. The
process for applying for a license will vary by license type. For example, the
process for applying for a license to manage frozen funds will be different
from the process for applying for a license for restricted goods.
Smaller businesses or organizations with limited resources might struggle to
allocate the necessary time, personnel, and expertise to effectively manage
the licensing process and ensure compliance.
Licenses: Challenges (Case example:
Expanse Imports)
Expanse Imports is a US-based fashion company known for its Latino style.
Recently, Cuban textile factories expressed an interest in becoming suppliers
to the company. The US has had a trade embargo on Cuba for many years.
However, recent diplomatic efforts have relaxed those restrictions.
The director of Expanse Imports was eager to negotiate and begin trading
internationally. He had some knowledge of Cuba through family, so he
decided to visit Cuba and enter preliminary trade discussions. He did not
obtain relevant legal advice or approval from the US Office of Foreign Assets
Control (OFAC).
Without proper authorization, Expanse Imports’ efforts in Cuba were
unsuccessful. The Cuban textile factories turned the director away, citing legal
barriers. Back in the US, Expanse Imports faced repercussions for their
oversight regarding the director’s travel to Cuba and his discussions with
prospective Cuban partners.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 85
The director of Expanse Imports failed to determine what specific import
restrictions applied and what types of licenses were required. Licenses for
wholesale and retail distribution for domestic consumption in Cuba are
approved on a case-by-case basis. In addition, classifications of prohibited or
otherwise controlled goods could sometimes be vague, and expert advice
might be necessary. The director should have checked the OFAC website to
get a better understanding of restrictions that were not clear. In addition, the
director overlooked the licensing documentation requirements. The license
application process would have required a comprehensive documentation
package outlining the nature of the proposed trade, the intended recipients,
and the end use of the imported goods. Because Expanse Imports was a small
business, the director did not anticipate the extensive documentation
requirements, resulting in significant licensing delays and rejections.
Eventually, Expanse Imports hired a reputable law firm specializing in
international trade controls and economic sanctions. The firm advised the
company on its US compliance obligations, including the types and scope of
licenses needed to conduct lawful business with Cuban textile manufacturers.
Key takeaways
• It is important to understand which transactions are permissible or
prohibited.
• Carefully examine licensing requirements and communicate with
regulators, as appropriate.
• Successfully navigating licensing challenges requires an understanding of
geopolitical developments.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 86
Delisting
Purpose of delisting
The term “delisting” refers to the removal of an entity, individual, or item from
a sanctions list. The purpose of delisting is to ease or lift sanctions on specific
targets that have taken corrective actions or met certain conditions.
A designated person on a UN Sanctions Committee list can file a delisting
request with the UN Focal Point for Delisting or through their state of
residence or citizenship. Those on the ISIL (Da’esh) and Al Qaida Sanctions
Lists must submit petitions for delisting through the Office of the
Ombudsperson. While the Focal Point can help with facilitating
communication between the relevant parties, the decision to delist a person
lies with the relevant UN Sanctions Committee.
The government or international body that imposes the sanction typically
manages the delisting process. Delisting can occur for several reasons:
• Compliance or behavior change: Delisting might occur when the targeted
entity or individual demonstrates a change in behavior or takes steps to
address the concerns that led to their sanctioning. This could include
ending support for terrorist activities, ceasing human rights abuses, or
complying with international agreements.
• Innocence or error: Delisting might occur if it is determined that an entity
or individual was included on the sanctions list by mistake, either due to
incorrect information or a misunderstanding.
• Positive political developments: In some cases, delisting could be part of a
broader diplomatic effort to encourage positive political developments,
such as negotiations, peace agreements, or conflict resolution.
• Economic or humanitarian reasons: Delisting might be considered if the
sanctions have caused unintended economic hardships or humanitarian
consequences, and there is a desire to alleviate those effects.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 87
• Goodwill or confidence building: Delisting can be used as a gesture of
goodwill to encourage cooperation and build trust between nations or
entities.
How is a person delisted?
Delisting can be a complex and carefully considered process. Delisting occurs
for one of several reasons: if the targeted individual dies; legal dissolution of an
entity; or at the request of the sanctioned target. Other factors that might
contribute to a delisting decision include changes in political or diplomatic
circumstances, new evidence, or evolving international agreements. With the
exception of the delisting reason of death, the approval-rejection process
occurs on a case-by-case basis and is made by the relevant sanctions
authority. The criteria and procedures for delisting are typically outlined in the
sanctions resolutions or regulations.
Delisting decisions are usually made by the body responsible for overseeing
the specific sanctions regime. For example, at the UN level each committee
considers the presented information. If there is a consensus among the
committee members that the individual or entity no longer meets the criteria
for sanctions, the delisting request is approved.
Death or dissolution of an entity leads to an automatic delisting as the original
reasons for imposing sanctions may no longer apply. For this reason, the
sanctions are no longer necessary.
A request by the targeted individual or entity includes information and
evidence demonstrating a change in circumstances or behavior, justifying the
removal of sanctions. This could involve showing that the reasons for the
sanctions no longer apply, or that the individual or entity has taken measures
to address the concerns that led to the sanctions.
Independent audits conducted by reputable third-party organizations are
intended to verify the prohibited activities have ended. These audits also
provide an objective assessment of compliance as well as opinions or
testimonials from professionals, scholars, or industry experts that validate the
efforts made to end the prohibited activities.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 88
Delisting requests: Key jurisdictions
Delisting request processes for sanctions can vary significantly among
different jurisdictions, depending on the organization, jurisdiction, or entity
imposing the sanctions. Key jurisdictions typically refer to jurisdictions or
organizations that play significant roles in imposing and enforcing sanctions.
These key jurisdictions include the UN, the EU, the UK, and the US.
A dispute between key jurisdictions could lead to conflicting sanctions
regimes. Sanctions conflicts could result in diplomatic confusion, potential
fines, and reputational damage. For example, if the EU were to delist an
individual, but OFAC maintained the individual on its sanctions list, a legal and
regulatory discrepancy would be created between the EU and the US. This
situation could have diplomatic, economic, legal, and operational implications.
To request delisting as a target of sanctions imposed by the UN, the target
must make a petition to a designated UN Focal Point for Delisting. Petitions
can also be made to a target party’s jurisdiction of residence or jurisdiction of
citizenship. The process is similar to when the target seeks to have assets
unfrozen or a travel ban lifted.
Targets listed by the EU can apply directly to the EU General Court, but only
for autonomous sanctions, and only when the listing criteria is no longer met.
Additionally, the EU reviews its sanctions list periodically and can decide to
delist targets on its own initiative.
In the UK, sanctions targets may request a ministerial review. Section 23 of the
Sanctions and Anti-Money Laundering Act 2018 provides guidance on how to
request the review for designated persons in the UK. The request for review
must be submitted to the appropriate Sanctions Unit.
Delisting requests from US sanctions lists involve submitting petitions to OFAC
and demonstrating the criteria for removal have been met. In contrast,
removal from the US Department of Commerce’s Bureau of Industry and
Security (BIS) list of denied parties, which includes targets subject to US trade
restrictions, happens only when the prohibition itself expires. Individuals on this
list, however, can apply to be delisted from other BIS lists.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 89
Delisting requests: Other jurisdictions
Country-level sanctions exist around the world, so each jurisdiction will have a
specified process for handling delisting requests and appeals. Other
jurisdictions include China, Japan, Singapore, Australia, Canada, and
Switzerland. The delisting processes for these jurisdictions are similar to the
processes in key jurisdictions, which are the UN, the EU, the UK, and the US.
The specific delisting procedures, criteria, and decision-making processes
vary by jurisdiction. Delisting requests are reviewed using criteria established
by the competent agency and supporting evidence submitted by the
individual or organization wanting to be delisted. The final delisting decision is
up to the government agency responsible for sanctions.
China’s delisting process involves diplomatic channels and negotiations
between the concerned parties. The Ministry of Foreign Affairs (MFA) and the
Ministry of Commerce (MOFCOM) are the primary authorities for sanctions
administration and enforcement in China.
Delisting from Japanese sanctions lists involves the sanctioned party
submitting a formal request to the relevant government agency, such as the
MFA. Singapore usually imposes sanctions aligned with the United Nations
Security Council (UNSC) resolutions. Delisting from Singaporean sanctions lists
involves submitting a request to the relevant government agency, such as the
MFA.
Delisting from Australian sanctions lists involves submitting a formal request to
the Department of Foreign Affairs and Trade (DFAT). Delisting from Canadian
sanctions lists is dealt with by the Office of the Superintendent of Financial
Institutions (OSFI) or Global Affairs Canada.
Switzerland’s Federal Department of Economic Affairs, Education and
Research (EAER) handles delisting requests. The Federal Council can lift
sanctions by abolishing or amending the ordinance that imposed the
sanctions. The responsible agencies and courts generally reject delisting
requests if the individual or organization is mentioned on sanctions lists issued
by the UN or the EU.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 90
How delisting impacts your KYC and
ongoing monitoring
Delisting an individual or entity from a sanctions list has implications for KYC
processes and ongoing monitoring. Delisting does not remove the obligation
to comply with AFC regulations. Organizations should continue to perform
ongoing reviews and updates on delisted customers. Consider how
information about delisted parties might be relevant to other investigations.
Ongoing monitoring might be necessary to ensure that illicit activity does not
occur post-delisting. Financial institutions and businesses should stay
informed about changes in sanctions lists, regulations, and best practices to
ensure their compliance efforts remain effective and up-to-date.
Delisting should trigger a review of KYC information. Organizations that are
subject to sanctions regulations might need to assess whether the delisted
individual or entity’s risk profile has changed significantly. This involves
updating customer records, reevaluating potential risk factors, and ensuring
that the delisting is included in the customer's profile.
Delisting typically indicates that the individual or entity is no longer subject to
specific sanctions measures. However, the risk assessment process does not
end with delisting. Organizations must consider other risk factors associated
with the customer. These risk factors include their financial behavior, business
activities, and jurisdictional risk.
Even after delisting, the delisted individual or entity’s activities and
transactions should still be monitored to ensure they do not engage in any
suspicious or potentially illegal activities. Other risks could still exist even
though the specific sanctions-related risk no longer applies.
Organizations could choose to apply enhanced due diligence measures for a
certain time period after delisting. This could involve more frequent reviews of
the customer's transactions, increased scrutiny of higher-risk activities, or
additional documentation requirements.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 91
How delisting impacts your KYC and
ongoing monitoring (Case example:
GlobalTrade Corp.)
Michelle is a compliance officer at a bank, where GlobalTrade Corp.
(GlobalTrade) is a customer. GlobalTrade is a multinational trading company
specializing in the import and export of industrial machinery and equipment.
Its business involves negotiating contracts, arranging logistics, and ensuring
compliance with various regulations and customs requirements.
GlobalTrade’s customer profile shows that they were sanctioned over
allegations of arms smuggling by a third country. Despite the fact that
Michelle’s bank has no legal obligations to comply with the sanctions
regulations of that country, the bank significantly reduced its exposure to
GlobalTrade. After a thorough investigation, GlobalTrade was subsequently
cleared of the allegations and delisted. As a result of the delisting, Michelle
needs to ensure that the customer’s KYC information is up-to-date.
As a compliance officer, Michelle is aware that delisting has significant
implications for KYC procedures and ongoing monitoring. Regulations require
organizations that work with a delisted entity to update their records. This
includes reassessing risk profiles, documenting changes, communicating
internally, and adjusting monitoring systems.
Before the bank eventually re-engages in business with the customer,
Michelle collects updated documentation and information that verifies the
customer’s identity and business activities. She confirms the customer’s
sanctions status through official sources.
Next, Michelle reassesses the customer’s current risk rating. Before the
delisting, the customer was considered high-risk due to its sanctions
designation. The delisting should decrease the customer’s risk rating.
The compliance team should document the entire delisting process, including
the investigation, findings, and communication with relevant authorities. They
should gather this information and review it to identify any gaps in the KYC
process.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 92
Michelle needs to ensure that all relevant internal stakeholders are informed of
the customer’s delisting and the subsequent steps the bank is taking.
Stakeholders include risk management, legal, and relationship management.
Finally, the compliance team should determine if they need to make any
changes to the transaction monitoring system. The system should be fine-
tuned to ensure that any alerts triggered are consistent with the customer’s
risk rating and capture any unusual activity.
Key takeaways
• A customer’s risk rating will likely change after a customer is delisted.
• Documentation is a key component of the KYC process.
• Transparent communication is necessary when maintaining a coordinated
approach to KYC.
• After a customer is delisted, the monitoring system should be adjusted to
align with the revised risk profile.
