Consequences of
Noncompliance with
Sanctions
Introduction
Setup: Who is liable?
You work in the sanctions and export control department for a US-based,
multinational car manufacturer. Your role includes providing guidance to other
departments, including sales, procurement, and logistics, to ensure compliance
with export control laws and to prevent sanctions violations. You also manage
the application process for export licenses and permits when required.
One day, you receive an escalation about a completed transaction. Your
company's regional office in the United Arab Emirates (UAE) sold a batch of
cars to a distributor in Azerbaijan. The distributor arranged for the delivery of
the cars to its dealerships in Azerbaijan and used third-party shippers to
transport them from the UAE.
While there was no immediate indication of an Iran transshipment connection,
the customs declaration showed that the cars entered Azerbaijan through the
town of Astara, which borders Iran. This implies that the shipment traveled from
the UAE to Iran by sea and from Iran to Azerbaijan by land.
Knowing that the US maintains a comprehensive economic embargo on Iran,
your company’s senior management is now concerned about potential liability.
They come to you seeking your guidance. They believe that, as the
manufacturer, your company should not be held responsible under strict liability
rules because the distributor managed the shipment.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 94
What would you do?
How do you respond?
• Confirm distributor liability.
• Clarify that an unintentional violation is no concern.
• Have the distributor return the cars.
• Take remedial action.
Feedback
The best response is to take remedial action. This should involve
strengthening the compliance program and self-reporting the violation to the
Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC). OFAC is generally more lenient
toward organizations that self-report violations, enhance their compliance
programs, and cooperate with investigations.
Confirming distributor liability is not the best response. Organizations cannot
shift their liability for financial crimes to third parties or vendors. Nor can they
imply that third parties hold ultimate responsibility for compliance.
Clarifying that an unintentional violation is no concern is not the best response.
Strict liability means that a parent company can face severe economic
consequences, even when it unknowingly or unintentionally violates sanctions.
Having the distributor to return the cars is not the best response. Returning
the goods involved in a violation does not erase the occurrence of the
violation. The penalties and consequences still remain.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Discuss the consequences of noncompliance with sanctions.
• Describe the types of risk associated with sanctions.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 95
Consequences to the individual
and the organization
Individuals are subject to sanctions
Individuals are subject to sanctions and their associated fines and penalties.
When a country sanctions an individual, it means the country has imposed
restrictions or penalties, usually as a form of punishment. Sanctions could also
be imposed as a means of exerting pressure on the individual or the
government with which they are associated. The level of impact on an
individual will vary depending upon the nature and severity of the sanctions,
the individual's role, and the specific circumstances. Sanctions could affect an
individual directly and indirectly. For example, sanctions directly affect an
individual’s finances. Their assets could be frozen, and their access to banking
services and transactions could be blocked. In addition, an individual’s travel
might be restricted. Indirectly, sanctions are likely to affect the person’s
reputation, family, friends, and business partners.
Sanctions can affect an individual financially, legally, socially, and
psychologically.
A sanctioned individual will have difficulties managing personal finances when
their access to funds has been restricted. Sanctions can limit access to bank
loans and credit cards. Sanctions can deprive an individual of making a living or
being hired for a job. The inability to conduct domestic and international
business can lead to income loss and business closures.
A sanctioned individual might be subject to criminal or civil penalties, including
fines, asset seizures, and even imprisonment. These fines and penalties vary
by jurisdiction. In the US, this may include a fine of up to US$1 million and 20
years in prison. In Australia, prison time can be up to 10 years and include fines
of AU$450,000 or three times the value of the transaction.
Being sanctioned could lead to social isolation due to strained relationships
with friends, family, and colleagues. Professional ties are often severed. Travel
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 96
restrictions affect mobility by limiting travel to certain jurisdictions or
preventing participation in international events.
Sanctions can lead to shortages of essential goods and services, affecting the
quality of life for the individual and their family. Restricted access to medical
supplies and other necessities can have serious health consequences,
including significant psychological stress. Even after sanctions are lifted, the
individual could face challenges in rebuilding their financial stability, reputation,
and personal relationships.
Individuals are subject to sanctions (Case
example: Mikhail Fridman)
Mikhail Fridman, a prominent Russian businessman, accumulated his wealth
through banking, oil, and retail. In February 2022, he faced EU sanctions due to
his purported ties to Russian President Vladimir Putin. The sanctions were part
of the EU’s continuing efforts to place restrictions on Russia’s economy and its
wealthiest powerbrokers, in response to Russia’s invasion of Ukraine.
According to the Security Service of Ukraine, Fridman had invested about
US$22 million in Russian defense enterprises since the start of the war. The
billionaire’s legal team subsequently challenged the EU’s allegation that he
was connected to the aggression against Ukraine.
Fridman's legal team contested the EU's claims that labeled him an ally of the
Russian president, dismissing them as “unreliable” and “lacking substance." To
prove his lack of involvement, Fridman offered to make a big donation to the
government of Ukraine while he lobbied to be removed from the EU sanctions
list. Fridman even solicited letters from several members of Russia’s
opposition to plead for sanctions relief. He also told the press that the
sanctions had “destroyed” his life.
In March 2022, Fridman was sanctioned by the UK. He subsequently took the
UK Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI) to court after it
refused to allow him to spend £30,000 a month for the maintenance of his
£65 million London home. Fridman also wanted to be allowed to spend
additional money on maintaining his luxury lifestyle. In October 2023, the High
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 97
Court of Justice upheld OFSI’s rejection of Fridman’s claims, agreeing with
OFSI that it had already licensed him to:
• Make past-due payments and one-off payments totaling around £1.4
million and €29,000.
• Make future payments of £760,000 a year for living expenses.
• Spend £26,000 a month on security staff and the maintenance of a
closed-circuit television security system.
Key takeaways
• Being sanctioned has consequences for individuals such as asset freezes,
travel bans, and reputational damage.
• After sanctions are in place, they are difficult and sometimes impossible to
remove.
• After funds have been frozen, they can only be released for use for items
and services deemed essential by the sanctioning body.
Organizations are subject to sanctions
Sanctions are generally strict liability for organizations. Strict liability means the
organization is liable even if it did not intend to violate the sanctions or
knowingly violate the sanctions. Organizations are also liable even if they have
robust sanctions compliance programs in place. For example, if an
organization is conducting business with a government, individual, or entity
under sanctions, it could face restrictions on financial transactions, trade, and
other business activities. Consequences of violating sanctions include supply
chain issues, reputational damage, financial blocking, legal and compliance
risk, and operational restrictions. Although most organizations try to comply
with sanctions by implementing sanctions compliance programs, violations
still occur.
Sanctions on an organization can restrict the organization’s access to
important markets, customers, and sources of raw materials. These
restrictions could further hinder the organization's growth and profitability.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 98
Financial restrictions include freezing assets, blocking financial transactions,
and imposing restrictions on banking services. These restrictions can disrupt
the organization’s financial operations, making it difficult to conduct routine
transactions, pay suppliers, and manage cash flow.
Supply chain disruptions could lead to delays in receiving goods and services,
production interruptions, and increased costs. Operational matters including
business decisions, contracts, and partnerships might need to be reassessed,
leading to additional disruptions and delays.
Sanctions can limit expansion and growth opportunities, resulting in a reduced
ability to expand into new markets or form new partnerships. They can also
decrease the organization’s ability to implement strategic initiatives and
potential revenue streams.
There are also long-term effects of sanctions. Technological and innovation
setbacks can result from limited or hindered access to innovative
technologies, expertise, and research. Talented individuals might be hesitant
to join a company facing legal and reputational risks.
Organizations are subject to sanctions
(Case example: Oleg Deripaska)
As of early 2018, Russia-based Rusal was the second-largest aluminum
company in the world. It was majority-owned by Oleg Deripaska, a Russian
businessman known for his close ties to the Russian government. In 2018,
OFAC sanctioned Deripaska for his alleged ties to Russian President Vladimir
Putin and his involvement in various projects in the Russian energy sector.
In 2019, OFAC lifted the sanctions on Rusal after having confirmed that
Deripaska would reduce his direct and indirect shareholdings below 50% while
remaining personally sanctioned. In exchange for its delisting, Rusal agreed to
extensive audit, certification, and reporting requirements. Rusal’s business had
suffered financially in the period that it was under US sanctions.
The US sanctions against Rusal had immediate financial consequences,
resulting in a significant revenue drop due to a loss of business. The sanctions
also restricted the company's access to international markets, particularly the
US market. Rusal could no longer attract investor capital or engage with global
financial institutions. The company faced further operational challenges in
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 99
procuring raw materials and carrying out international trade transactions.
These challenges affected its supply chains worldwide. Overall, the US
sanctions significantly damaged Rusal's business reputation.
In addition, Rusal’s US designation as a Specially Designated National (SDN)
affected the global aluminum industry. For example, it led to major disruptions
in the transportation, construction, and packaging segments of the sector.
Crucially, they resulted in short-term aluminum shortages followed by a
substantial price increase.
After engaging for over a year with a team of negotiators representing Rusal’s
immediate parent company, London-listed EN+ Group, OFAC lifted its
sanctions on Rusal in 2019. With the sanctions lifted, Rusal was able to access
unblocked funds and use them for equipment upgrades. The compromise
with OFAC eliminated Deripaska's control over Rusal. His personal lawsuit
against OFAC to seek sanctions relief was dismissed by a US court.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions on large organizations can disrupt entire industries.
• Close connections between an organization’s ownership and government
entities can lead to the imposition of sanctions against the organization.
• Diplomatic negotiations between governments and organizations can
result in sanctions relief.
Transfer of liability
Transfer of liability refers to shifting legal responsibility for potential losses,
obligations, or damages from one party to another. According to financial
crime legislation, organizations may not shift liability to third parties or vendors.
Strict liability holds each party accountable for its actions, even if it committed
a violation unknowingly. For example, if an organization is unaware that a
customer deals with a criminal party, it could be held liable.
Successor liability is a legal principle that states that a successor organization
assumes the liabilities of its predecessor. If an organization acquires a
company, it is liable for any past transgressions its predecessor might have
committed. It is also liable for the actions of its subsidiaries.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 100
An organization could still be liable for financial crime violations even if it has a
robust compliance program. When the organization violates financial crime
legislation, the regulator considers the compliance processes it had in place
and whether it acted knowingly.
Contractual clauses for AFC are standard in contracts. However, the
organization should not just rely on a clause. To ensure that its customers are
not committing financial crimes, it should:
• Check that its customer is complying with the clause.
• Undertake other compliance processes, such as regular customer
screening and extensive KYC.
The organization must also consider what happens to its products beyond the
point of sale. It should conduct due diligence on its customers and its
customer’s customers. Because of successor liability, organizations must
consider whether any company they are acquiring has had any financial crime
violations in the past. Parent companies should ensure that their subsidiaries
are correctly implementing their compliance policies. There might be
different rules and regulations in different jurisdictions, but a multinational
company could still be liable for the actions of a subsidiary in another
jurisdiction. For example, US sanctions are extraterritorial, which means that
they apply beyond the territory of the US.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 101
Types of risk
Types of risk: Operational, legal,
concentration, and reputational
Controls that prevent financial crime should consider four key risks:
Operational, legal, concentration, and reputational.
Operational risk is direct or indirect loss of operations due to inadequate or
failed internal processes, people, or systems, or as a result of external events.
Legal risk is the possibility that criminal penalties, lawsuits, or contracts that
cannot be enforced might harm an organization.
Concentration risk stems from over-exposure to a single customer or group
of related customers.
Reputational risk comes when an institution known to have weak controls is
then targeted by criminals or avoided by stakeholders who lose confidence in
the institution.
Operational risk is complex and includes an organization’s ability to maintain
AFC controls in an evolving regulatory environment across multiple
jurisdictions. Typically, a global organization makes the policies of its home
regulator its base standard. The organization will then adjust to each host
country’s laws. Evolving regulations might become misaligned with current
business models and controls. Compliance programs must continually be
updated.
Legal risk stems from potential violation of regulations, laws, and ethical
practices. Governments might issue administrative penalties or fines. Third
parties, such as customers who feel damaged, might file lawsuits. Adequate
AFC controls add protection from crime and inappropriate relationships.
Concentration risk can be reduced by AFC controls and strategic
diversification. Customer due diligence, enabled by technology, helps manage
exposure. Concentration could occur in borrowing, funding, purchasing,
provision of key services, or any other business relationship. Risk could
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 102
increase through actions by a customer, or external actions involving a
customer.
Reputational risk is difficult to quantify. Trust takes a long time to earn but can
be lost quickly. A single news story—even fake news—can drive away
customers and investors. Many organizations deserve their reputations, good
or bad, based on their chosen business practices and ethics.
Operational risk
Operational risk is the direct or indirect loss of operations due to inadequate
or failed internal processes, people, or systems, or as a result of external
events. Operational risk focuses on how tasks are accomplished within an
organization and not necessarily what an organization produces.
Operational risk is inherent in all financial products, activities, processes, and
systems. The effective management of operational risk is a fundamental
element of an organization’s risk management program. To manage
operational risk, an organization should have policies and procedures that
address the process for review and approval of new products, activities,
processes, and systems.
Operational risk refers to the risk an organization faces while performing daily
business activities, procedures, and using systems. Human error, people, and
decisions made by people tend to cause most of an organization’s operational
risks.
Operational risk can also be caused by other factors, including:
• Natural disasters
• Ransomware, cyberterrorism
• Inadequate technology systems
Natural disasters could result in business disruption and technology failure,
making an organization vulnerable to operational risk. Ransomware is a form
of cyberterrorism. It has the potential to disrupt core business activities,
resulting in operational outages. It can also result in an inability to access
critical business and customer data. Applying effective cybersecurity risk
management and mitigation principles can reduce the risk of a ransomware
attack’s success and minimize its negative impacts.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 103
Inadequate screening software implementation, ineffective processes either
manual or automated, unknown gaps in sanctions screening systems, and
untimely updates to an organization’s screening software increase the risk of
missing important information while processing transactions. Organizations
should regularly monitor operational risk profiles and material exposures to
losses. Appropriate reporting mechanisms should be implemented at the
board, senior management, and business line levels that support proactive
management of operational risk.
How regulators enforce sanctions
Sanctions authorities are responsible for implementing, investigating, and
enforcing sanctions. Enforcement guidelines provide a basis for the sanctions
authorities to determine whether a violation occurred and what, if any, civil
monetary penalty should be imposed. The UN and EU rely on member states
to adopt sanctions programs and then to enforce sanctions violations. In the
US, the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) follows its own enforcement
guidelines to determine an appropriate enforcement action based on the
facts and circumstances of the case. In the UK, the Office of Financial
Sanctions Implementation (OFSI) relies on its enforcement guidance to issue
monetary penalties for sanctions violations.
The UN Security Council (UNSC) imposes sanctions for all UN Member States.
Member States are obligated to comply with UNSC sanctions.
EU member states are responsible for the implementation of all sanctions
within their respective jurisdictions. EU sanctions are reviewed at regular
intervals by the Council of the EU. The Council decides whether sanctions
should be renewed, amended, or lifted.
OFAC is the most notable sanctions enforcement authority. An OFAC
investigation can result in a finding of no violation, a request for further
information, a cautionary letter, a finding of a violation, a finding of a violation
with civil monetary penalty, or a criminal referral. Individuals can face up to
US$1 million in fines or up to 20 years imprisonment for willful violations.
To calculate the civil penalty, OFAC first determines the base amount by
considering whether the violation qualifies as egregious. The egregious or
non-egregious determination is based on factors including willful or reckless
violation of law, awareness of the violation, harm to the sanctions program
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 104
objectives, and individual characteristics of the individual or entity involved. It
also considers whether the individual voluntarily self-disclosed the violation.
Then, OFAC considers aggravating and mitigating factors, including whether
the violator took remedial action or cooperated with OFAC’s investigation to
calculate a final penalty.
In the UK, OFSI investigates and imposes civil monetary penalties for breaches
of financial sanctions. Sanctions regimes are categorized based on jurisdiction
or theme, such as chemical weapons or terrorism.
Strict liability
The strict liability legal standard means that an individual or organization can
be held liable for sanctions violations even without having knowledge or
reason to know it was engaging in such a violation. In the US, the Office of
Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) considers the facts and circumstances of an
apparent violation when determining the appropriate enforcement action.
OFAC may consider mitigating factors in assessing the final penalty. Examples
include the implementation of an OFAC compliance program, self-disclosure
of an apparent violation, reckless behavior, or remedial measures taken in
response to the violation, and others.
Organizations are subject to strict liability even if they have a risk-based
sanctions compliance program in place. To minimize the risk of a sanctions
violation, the compliance program should address whether:
• The activity will involve a blocked individual or sanctioned jurisdiction.
• A nonsanctioned entity has or will have ties to any blocked individual or
sanctioned jurisdiction.
• A US individual will be involved in or supervise the activity or transaction
with a blocked individual or sanctioned jurisdiction.
It is important to note that OFAC generally looks favorably on organizations
that self-report violations, take remedial action, or cooperate with OFAC’s
investigations.
Organizations that operate internationally often do business with jurisdictions
that do not fully comply with other jurisdictions’ sanctions regimes. To mitigate
the risk of sanctions breaches, authorities expect sanctions clauses to be
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 105
included in commercial agreements. Contractual sanctions clauses are where
both parties agree not to violate sanctions. At a minimum, the contract
clauses should include:
• Status under sanctions representation
• Use of Proceeds covenant
• Representation and covenants covering continued compliance with
sanctions
• Maintenance of policies and procedures
Since June 2022, the UK Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI)
has had the power to issue monetary penalties for sanctions violations on a
strict liability basis. Similar to OFAC, it considers the facts and circumstances
when determining the appropriate enforcement action.
Strict liability (Case example: Contract
clauses)
Carmen is a compliance specialist for a global bank conducting a review of a
letter of credit. The buyer is a low-risk active customer of the bank. The seller,
S-Shape SLP, is a noncustomer represented by a bank in another jurisdiction.
Carmen is unable to verify the seller’s choice of shipper because it has no
internet presence. The seller’s bank refers Carmen to its service-level
agreement that states that it conducts sanctions screening and due diligence
on transacting and related parties as needed. The seller assures the bank that
further review of the shipper is not necessary. Carmen decides to seek
additional guidance.
Carmen shares the information with her manager. He is also confused by the
contract language. The contract language implies that the seller’s bank only
screens some of the parties, but that is not clearly stated. The US OFAC
operates with strict liability for noncompliance. The bank’s sanctions policy
states that they will bear liability for a sanctions violation, regardless of the
terms of the contract.
Carmen submits a request for information (RFI) to the seller’s bank asking for
supporting documentation on the shipper. The seller’s bank responds that
they have screened and conducted due diligence on the shipper and there
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 106
were no hits in the screening system. The seller’s bank does not provide any
additional information.
The information the seller’s bank provided is not enough to ensure that there
are no sanctioned or otherwise restricted parties involved. Even if the seller’s
bank performed adequate screening, the buyer’s bank would be liable if there
was a sanctioned party involved.
The key consideration in this case is who would retain liability for a sanctions
violation. Even though there is a contract clause regarding sanctions risk,
there are still risks including:
• The seller’s bank is reluctant to provide information.
• The contract clause itself is unclear.
There would be significant legal and regulatory implications if the shipper is a
sanctioned entity. Carmen decides there is too much potential liability to
proceed with the transaction.
Key takeaways
• Various responsibilities can be managed by different parties to a contract.
• In strict liability cases, a covered institution still retains liability.
• Legal liability and compliance obligations cannot be outsourced, even with
contractual provisions in place.
How regulators enforce sanctions (Case
example: Construction Specialties)
Construction Specialties Inc. (CS) is a company headquartered in the US that
sells specialized building materials. CS owns a wholly-controlled subsidiary,
Construction Specialties Middle East L.L.C. (CSME) which is in the United Arab
Emirates (UAE). In 2023, CS agreed to settle its subsidiary’s potential civil
liability for three apparent violations of OFAC sanctions on Iran.
The violations occurred when CSME’s senior leadership oversaw the purchase
of building materials from the US and then knowingly re-exported them from
the UAE to Iran. Allegedly, CS was not aware of CSME’s activity; however, due
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 107
to the strict liability standard, CS was subject to regulatory enforcement and
penalties.
Strict liability means a parent company can face severe economic
consequences even when it did not intentionally violate sanctions.
Multinational companies encounter challenges when they pursue business
opportunities in high-risk jurisdictions. This is especially true when employees
act on their own initiative, disregard policies and controls, and evade
applicable sanctions. In this case example, the subsidiary circumvented
policies, procedures, and sanctions programs while conducting business,
presumably without the parent company’s knowledge.
The maximum monetary penalty in this case is US$2.2 million. OFAC
determined there were three violations, and that CS had voluntarily self-
disclosed them.
OFAC settled with CS for US$660,594. OFAC considered the following
mitigating factors in their decision-making process:
• CS’s headquarters in the US appeared to have been unaware of CSME’s
activity.
• CS’s sanctions compliance program appeared to have been reasonably
designed to comply with the restrictions in place at the time of the
violation.
• CS’s remedial response included immediately terminating the responsible
employees, promptly initiating an internal investigation, hiring new key
compliance personnel, and implementing updated company-wide
corporate compliance policies.
• CS cooperated with OFAC’s investigation, promptly disclosed CSME’s
apparent violation, responded to information requests in a timely manner,
and agreed to end the statute of limitations.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions violations can lead to significant civil monetary penalties.
• Voluntary self-disclosure reduces the penalties for sanctions violations.
• Mitigating factors that can impact penalties for sanctions violations include:
o The type of sanctions program in place at the time of the violation
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 108
o The remedial response to the violation
o Cooperation with the investigation
Legal risk
Legal risk is the possibility that criminal penalties, lawsuits, or contracts that
cannot be enforced might harm an organization. This includes financial loss
and reputational damage from not complying with laws, regulations, or
contractual obligations. While adequate AFC controls add protection from
crime and inappropriate relationships, governments could still issue
administrative penalties or fines.
Legal risk is often related to other types of risk. For example, an adverse
judgment against a bank such as a lawsuit, civil order, or guilty plea, could
affect a consumer’s perception of that bank. Based on the perceived
reputational damage or reputational risk, customers and clients might avoid
the bank.
In the context of AFC compliance, legal risk is often linked to operational and
reputational risk. For example, a regulatory action such as a cease-and-desist
order is escalated into an adverse legal action such as a deferred prosecution
agreement. Legal suits could be filed against the board of directors for lack of
oversight. Shareholders or other stakeholders might take legal action against
the board or other senior management due to the organization’s failure to
properly oversee a compliance program.
Related risks associated with legal risk include fines, penalties, and the cost of
remediating a legal action. This could require additional funds to be used in
case of an adverse judgment. These funds would not be available for other
purposes such as strategic change. Legal risk could affect an organization by
impacting its ability to deliver on contractual obligations, thereby resulting in
secondary legal risks. Another unrelated risk is an inability to perform certain
business due to legal freezes or holds.
As with other types of risk, once the media learns about a business disruption
due to pending or finalized legal action, current or prospective customers
might think the organization is no longer safe and could take their business
elsewhere.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 109
Legal risk (Case example: Arab Bank Plc.)
Sanctions breaches often carry substantial legal risk, which can arise separately
from or as part of an enforcement action. Private citizens can sometimes take
legal action directly against an organization, even when regulators or
prosecutors have already held it civilly or criminally liable.
In 2014, a US jury found the Arab Bank Plc. liable for having knowingly provided
support to Hamas, a US-designated Foreign Terrorist Organization. The suit was
the first of its kind filed under the 1990 Anti-Terrorism Act (ATA). The law
allowed US citizens to sue a foreign bank for its involvement in terrorist activity.
There were nearly 600 victims listed as plaintiffs in the suit. A verdict was
reached finding the bank liable for having knowingly provided terrorist support.
The Arab Bank Plc. lawsuit was unique because it was brought by the family
members of US citizens who had been harmed, some fatally, in a series of
terrorist attacks.
The terrorist attacks were carried out by supporters of Hamas, which had been
designated as a terrorist group almost a decade before the attacks. There was
no evidence presented to suggest that the bank willfully intended to support
terrorism. However, evidence showed that the bank had facilitated payments in
support of the terrorists’ families.
In 2018, the lawsuit was dismissed on a legal technicality. If it had succeeded,
the victims would have been entitled to minimum compensation worth US$100
million. A new case was filed in Israel in late 2019, whereby 1,132 plaintiffs sought
damages against the Arab Bank Plc. for its facilitation of the same transactions.
The compensation being sought was over US$5.5 billion. Although the case was
ultimately also dismissed, the bank ceased its US operations and lost many of its
relationships elsewhere as well.
Key takeaways
• Legal risk is often connected to other types of risk, especially regulatory
risk.
• A compliance failure could result in private lawsuits.
• Enforcement actions can attribute responsibility to an entire organization
and individual employees.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 110
Concentration risk
Concentration risk stems from over-exposure to a single customer or group
of related customers. It tends to arise when an organization has centered its
assets, investments, or strategy too heavily in a particular area. The concern is
that, if the venture were to suddenly collapse, the organization would lose all
of the funds it invested.
Concentration risk can also arise when an organization is heavily invested in an
area that has not been tested through the regulatory process. This means
there are no advisories or legal opinions on how to safely proceed with a
proposed venture. As that venture becomes subject to more scrutiny, it can
become either too high risk, or prohibited altogether, pending further action.
Concentration risk is part of an organization’s financial strategy. Finance is a
highly competitive business that relies on organic growth strategies or a
willingness to engage in higher-risk ventures. Concentration risk could occur
when there are uncontrollable or unpredictable outside forces. It can spread
and affect entire sectors or markets, making it difficult to overcome. If
overlooked, serious concentration risk could result in unplanned service
outages, disruption of service to customers, brand and reputational damage,
reduced negotiating strength, poorly planned transitions to new service
providers, and higher costs.
High-risk ventures often involve risks that build over time, such as Russian-
adjacent business entities. When the geopolitical landscape changes, those
risks become evident. Other examples include ventures in unregulated
cryptocurrency, cannabis-related activity, or engaging in high-risk
jurisdictions.
An organization might become involved in a higher-risk venture when that
venture has not yet been prohibited, or the venture has not yet been the
subject of an enforcement action. Rather than taking a measured approach to
a high-risk venture, client, or strategy, an organization might immediately and
fully commit. This leads to overconcentrating their assets or their client’s
assets into a high-risk venture. Later, AFC compliance guidance or restrictions
could stop the new venture, freezing the underlying invested assets. This
could cause significant loss for the organization.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 111
Concentration risk (Case example: Fortum)
Following the imposition of unprecedented sanctions on Russia in 2022, many
companies were left scrambling to recover their assets. The energy sector
was particularly affected. The Nordic energy company, Fortum, had invested
more than €6 billion in Russia through its subsidiary, PAO Fortum. In April 2023,
the Russian authorities seized control of PAO Fortum, triggering a full removal
of its Russian assets from its financial records.
These actions caused Fortum to record a €1.7 billion decrease in the value of
assets for the second quarter of 2023. This decrease represented nearly 20%
of the company’s €9.8 billion equity that had been previously recorded at the
end of March 2023.
Fortum had consolidated its assets in Russia. Sectoral sanctions had been
placed on the Russian energy sector in 2014 when Russia invaded Crimea.
Fortum was aware that it was operating in a high-risk sector and a high-risk
jurisdiction. This increased its concentration risk.
The 2022 Russian sanctions meant that many companies were unable to
divest, or sell, their Russian assets at anything close to market value. For
example, the French car company, Renault, sold its Russian subsidiary for a
nominal amount of one euro while writing down the value of its assets by €2.2
billion. Meanwhile, Germany’s Volkswagen reported a €2 billion write-down.
The energy sector was hardest hit, with the oil majors, such as British
Petroleum, Shell, and TotalEnergies reporting losses of approximately €40
billion. Higher oil and gas prices outweighed the bigger companies’ losses, but
Fortum suffered as a smaller company.
Since 2008, Fortum’s Russian subsidiary has generated more than €4 billion in
EBITDA or earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation, and amortization.
According to Fortum’s CEO, the net cash loss from its Russian operations
totaled approximately €2 billion in 2023. Since the Russian authorities seized
the subsidiary’s assets, Fortum was not able to gain any profit from its
divestment.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 112
Key takeaways
• To decrease concentration risk, avoid operating in high-risk jurisdictions
and sectors.
• Consider the potential impact a change to sanctions regulations could
have on your business and adjust the concentration risk accordingly.
• Mitigate concentration risk by diversifying across different markets. This
spreads the risk and reduces reliance on a single market.
Reputational risk
Reputational risk is the risk of adverse publicity, which can erode trust and
respect for an organization. Adverse publicity, such as involvement in a
financial crime scandal, could lead to a negative public view of an
organization’s business practices and associations. This type of negative
attention could result in a loss of public confidence in the organization.
Reputational risk is based on the public’s perception of wrongdoing.
Reputational risk is not quantifiable, but it could affect other areas of an
organization. For example, it could cause existing customers, partners, or
investors to leave the organization. It could also cause potential customers,
business partners, or investors to avoid the organization.
Reputational risk can happen to any organization, regardless of size or
industry. Reputational risk can come from employee actions, failure to meet
stakeholder expectations, lack of quality products or services, or failure to
adapt to changes.
Employee actions can affect an organization’s reputation. This includes
management and third parties hired to work within the organization. For
example, an organization president who is accused of embezzling funds is a
reputational risk. Even if the allegations are false, the negative impact could be
enough to ruin the organization’s reputation.
Systems, processes, and products that do not meet shareholder expectations
could also damage an organization’s reputation. For example, an automobile
manufacturer that must issue recalls due to faulty products risks the loss of
sales, loyal customers, or even shareholders. Reputational risk could also
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 113
come from a breach of customers' data. The organization could be viewed as
careless or irresponsible for not properly protecting sensitive customer data.
Reputational risk could occur when an organization does not stay up-to-date
with regulatory and industry standards. Modern technology, especially social
media and other broadcast tools, can spread information to large audiences
in a short amount of time. This could accelerate the distribution of reputation-
damaging information.
Reputational risk (Case example: Clarita
National Bank)
Clarita National Bank (CNB) is a mid-sized financial institution headquartered in
Atlanta, Georgia. For the past few years, CNB has been actively onboarding
various small banks throughout the Middle East as part of its correspondent
banking offering. In contrast, several of CNB’s direct competitors in the US
market have withdrawn from the region, citing increased money laundering
and sanctions risks. In exchange for offering access to the US financial system
via its accounts with large US financial institutions, CNB has been charging
client banks higher than average fees. Following a series of media leaks, CNB
was investigated and prosecuted by US federal and state regulators for
flagrant sanctions and money laundering failures.
CNB ultimately settled for US$100 million in total fines and penalties, fired the
chief executive officer, the chief operating officer, the chief risk officer, and
pledged to conduct a root-and-branch overhaul of its AFC programs.
However, just as CNB filed another periodic report with the regulators
regarding progress made toward the achievement of its new compliance
milestones, another media leak broke out. It revealed sanctions breaches at
CNB that had not been accounted for, involving an OFAC-designated global
drug trafficker and entities controlled by the Iranian government.
Following news of a second regulatory investigation in three years, CNB began
to experience severe reputational damage. Several major depositors,
representing large corporations in the Atlanta metropolitan area, withdrew
their funds. Two US correspondent banks in New York, one in London, and one
in Frankfurt, Germany terminated their relationships with CNB. Several days
later, a bigger regional rival withdrew its offer to acquire CNB’s commercial
real estate lending division.
Sanctions Frameworks and Governance
Version 2.0
Page 114
The reputational consequences suffered by CNB extended well before its
direct business activities. An American League baseball club pulled out of a
negotiation to renew CNB’s premium sponsorship package and preemptively
removed all references to CNB from its website and online marketing
materials.
Key takeaways
• Patterns of regulatory failures can result in reputational risk.
• Reputational risk can materialize in various forms:
o Loss of depositors
o End of correspondent banking relationships
o Reduction in share price
o Unilateral termination of pending deals
o Loss of sponsorship packages

Congratulations! You have completed this learning experience.
You should be able to:
• Explain the purpose of sanctions.
• Identify who imposes sanctions.
• Describe the types of sanctions and their effects on behavior.
• Describe the permissible activities and delisting process under sanctions.
• Discuss the consequences of noncompliance with sanctions.

Assessing Sanctions
Risk
Introduction
Setup: Mrs. Thompson
You have just started your new job as a junior compliance officer at a
prestigious private bank in London. In your first week, you receive an
onboarding file for a new customer named Elena Thompson. The file was sent
by Miriam, one of the most successful relationship managers at your bank. She
has a good reputation for managing ultra-high net worth clients. You see a
note in the file that says, "Please expedite—very important client." Miriam is
enthusiastic about onboarding this new client because Mrs. Thompson is a
well-known socialite. This relationship could attract other affluent clients to
your bank. However, as you begin to explore the customer's background and
financial details, you notice several red flags about Mrs. Thompson. First, she is
the wife of a former customer, Mr. Robert Thompson. He was offboarded
from the bank due to sanctions related to his business activities and
connections to Russia. Additionally, Mrs. Thompson does not have a
demonstrable source of income, and it appears that the funds to open the
account are coming from her husband. Furthermore, she is a citizen of a
sanctioned country. These findings raise significant concerns and warrant
further investigation to ensure compliance with regulatory requirements.
You document these red flags and put “Critical red flags, to be discussed
during the next onboarding committee” on the onboarding memo. Later in
the day, Miriam approaches you and requests immediate approval to proceed
with the onboarding process. You inform her that your manager is currently
reviewing the file. Miriam is not happy with your answer and tells you she will
speak with your manager directly. Shortly afterward, your manager contacts
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 6
you and requests that you remove the remarks from the memo. He tells you
to write “Good for approval” before sending the file to the onboarding
committee. You know onboarding Mrs. Thompson would pose a huge risk to
your bank. You also know the relationship manager is very influential at the
bank. As you go to meet your manager to discuss the onboarding memo, you
are not sure what will happen next. But you know that your job is to provide
him with a recommendation about the customer.
What would you do?
What should you do?
• Expedite the file.
• Contact the internal hotline.
• Provide a mitigation for each red flag.
• Speak to the press.
Feedback
The best answer is to contact the internal hotline. This hotline provides a
confidential and independent way for reporting potential compliance
violations. By bypassing your manager, you can ensure that your concerns are
addressed without any potential interference or bias.
You should not expedite the file as requested. Mrs. Thompson is not on any
sanctions list, but there are other red flags that indicate potential risks. She
might be facilitating sanctions evasion for her husband and his businesses.
Further assessment is needed. You should not ignore the other red flags,
despite what onboarding this customer means for the business.
You should not provide a mitigation for any of the red flags. Mitigation for the
red flags without proper investigation or justification could be perceived as a
cover-up. A cover-up implies intentionally concealing information or actions
that should be disclosed.
You should not speak to the press. Internal compliance matters often involve
sensitive information about the clients and the organization. Most
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 7
organizations have specific policies and procedures in place regarding
communication with the press. Speaking to the press without proper
authorization could result in disciplinary action, including termination of
employment.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Describe how to assess sanctions risk.
• Explain the sanctions risk of specific products and lines of business.
• Discuss the framework for an effective sanctions compliance program.

Page 8
Sanctions risk assessment
Categories of risk: Customer, jurisdiction,
product, channel
An overall customer risk rating is usually derived as a composite of the
following four categories of risk in money laundering prevention: customer,
jurisdiction, product, and channel.
The risk associated with the customer can be higher if the customer is
nonresident, if the customer’s legal structure is complex, if the customer is in
an industry often exploited for financial crime, or if the customer’s business is
cash-intensive.
Some jurisdictions are higher risk because their own laws, regulations, and
enforcement controls to prevent financial crime are weak, or nonexistent.
Each product or service carries a different level of risk. For example, private
banking is considered higher risk, as transactions may be confidential and
attempt to mask true ownership.
Channel risk increases with non-face-to-face business relationships or
transactions, or when payments are sent to or received from unknown or
unassociated third parties.
Customers can be higher risk if they use nominee shareholders, shares in
bearer form, or a company ownership structure that is very complex. In many
countries, bearer shares are banned because of their potential for abuse as
tax evasion and money laundering. Also, customers are higher risk if they
conduct their business relationship with your organization at a geographic
distance and this is not the expected account activity. For example, a foreign
citizen, with no connection to a country where an account is being opened,
requires explanation. Your organization might have a list of industries or
customer types considered higher risk which automatically get additional
scrutiny.
Higher risk jurisdictions, as defined by the Financial Action Task Force, are
countries that have been identified as having strategic deficiencies in their
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 9
national anti-money laundering and counter-financing of terrorism regimes.
They pose significant threats to the global financial system.
Certain products are higher risk. An example would be reloading a prepaid
device via a kiosk at a gas station. Others are lower risk. An example is
providing a small amount of foreign currency to an existing customer for a
holiday abroad.
Some delivery channels are higher risk. Correspondent banking relationships
create significant money laundering and terrorist financing risks because the
domestic bank carrying out the transaction has to rely on the foreign bank to
identify the customer, determine the real owners, and monitor such
transactions for risks.
Risk appetite
Risk appetite is the level of financial crime risk acceptable to an organization,
within the parameters of its business and strategic goals. Your organization’s
risk appetite statement must be approved by your board of directors and
periodically reviewed to consider any changes in the business and relevant
risk factors.
Your organization’s risk appetite might change over time. For example, your
organization seeks a merger with another entity that has been traditionally
involved in higher risk activities. This situation implies the newly formed
organization’s risk appetite has changed and will now include higher risk
activities.
To implement a risk appetite framework, you must determine the types of risk
your organization is willing to accept to meet its strategic objectives, set its
risk tolerance level, and create a risk appetite statement.
The risk appetite statement should be easy for all stakeholders to understand.
The information in the statement should be positive. It should include key
background information and assumptions, strategic and financial plans, and
quantitative measures that translate into risk limits.
Over time, your organization might periodically reassess its risk exposure, and
adjust its risk appetite statement. For example, a potential new customer
might raise your organization’s risk exposure. You can choose to reject the
customer, limit access only to certain products or services, or place enhanced
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 10
risk controls on the customer’s account. Your organization might use the
same risk appetite statement to review customers who no longer meet the
organization’s risk appetite.
The board of directors sets the risk appetite statement and tolerance levels.
Business executives are accountable for managing risk limits within their
business units.
Risk limits allocate your organization’s overall risk appetite to appropriate
business units with specific risk categories and concentrations for each.
Lastly, your organization needs to conduct regular independent assessments
to make sure internal risk controls, risk management, and risk governance are
effective and function properly.
Risk-based approach to business
Every organization has its own appetite for risk. This determines the type of
customer it will accept, the types of products it will offer, and the jurisdictions
and channels in which it will do business.
Once this is established, the organization will allocate a risk rating to these
categories. For example, an individual customer with a regular job and a salary
opening a savings account would be low risk, assuming the source of funds
can be corroborated and there is no relevant, negative news. A politically
exposed person, or PEP, is higher risk. Products, jurisdictions, and channels
also present a range of risks.
Using a risk-based approach allows your organization to effectively use its
resources by classifying customers based on the level of perceived risk that
they could pose from a financial crime perspective.
Those decisions drive the level and frequency of customer research and
updating the customer profile.
Risk assessment as a whole has become more important in recent years
because the fight against financial crime has taken a more holistic approach,
with regulators emphasizing a risk-based approach to every way you interact
with your customers.
Accurately judging the risk level that a customer might be involved in financial
crime is an important prerequisite for the “risk-based” approach. Financial
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 11
institutions need to conduct due diligence on business operations, industries,
customer characteristics, and regions, in order to obtain adequate, complete,
and truthful customer information as the basis of analysis.
A risk-based approach focuses effort where it is most needed and will have
most impact. It requires the full commitment and support of senior
management, and the active cooperation of all employees.
Adopting a risk-based approach implies the adoption of a risk-management
process for dealing with financial crime. This process encompasses
recognizing the existence of the risks, undertaking an assessment of the risks,
and developing control strategies to mitigate and monitor the identified risks.
Financial crime risk is just one element of risk financial institutions face. Others
include credit risk and market risk. Combining the management of these risks
enables risk managers to assess the financial crime risk and devote resources
to mitigating the highest risks.
Determine inherent risk
Inherent risk is the level of AFC risk in place before any mitigation controls are
applied. There are four categories of inherent risk: customer, jurisdiction,
product, and channel. These categories of risk can be applied to other types
of financial crime, such as human trafficking.
Inherent risk is the starting point of most risk assessments because it
considers the likelihood and impact of noncompliance prior to applying
mitigation controls.
The process for determining inherent risk could vary, depending upon the
organization and its risk assessment framework. Generally, the following steps
could be taken to determine an organization’s inherent risk.
First, identify and gather relevant information. Collect data on customers,
jurisdictions, products, and channels, including customer profiles, country risk
assessments, product specifications, and channel characteristics.
Next, analyze and assess the risk factors associated with each category. For
customers, you should consider their industry, transaction volume, and
geographic location. Determining jurisdiction risk might involve assessing
political stability, the regulatory environment, and sanctions status. To
determine product risk, consider the complexity, potential misuse, or
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 12
attractiveness of the product for illicit activities. To assess channel risk, you
might evaluate vulnerabilities in the delivery or communication channels.
Then, assign risk ratings or scores. Develop a risk rating or scoring system to
assign levels of inherent risk to each category. This can be based on a scale or
numerical values. The rating should reflect the potential AFC risk associated
with each category.
For example, a customer from a high-risk jurisdiction asks to open an account
to engage in high-value wire transfers through an offshore channel. In this
situation, the inherent risk would be assessed as high for both the jurisdiction
and the product categories. The channel might also contribute to the overall
inherent risk, depending on factors such as the regulatory oversight of
offshore channels.
Inherent risk can be measured using the inherent risk matrix. The level of risk
increases in tandem with the probability and severity of an offense. If there is a
high probability of an offense, and its impact is high, then the inherent risk is
high as well.
Controlling risk
Controlling risk is critical to the overall success of an institution. Financial crime
risk assessments help to develop control strategies to mitigate and monitor
the identified risks. Some examples of these control strategies are policies
and procedures, training, four eyes checks, and segregation of duties. These
controls fall into three categories: preventive, detective, and corrective.
Internal control frameworks are designed to control the risks related to
compliance, operations, and reporting. The framework should align with the
organization's risk appetite.
The five components of the internal control framework are the overall control
environment, risk assessment, control activities, information and
communication, and monitoring.
The overall control environment is the set of standards, processes, and
structures that provide the basis for carrying out internal control across the
organization.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 13
A risk assessment measures the likelihood or probability that the business will
unwittingly engage in financial crime. The risk assessment identifies inherent
risks, the effectiveness of existing controls, and captures the residual risk.
Control activities in financial crime prevention include preventive, detective,
and corrective controls. Preventive controls include robust due diligence,
recordkeeping, and record retention. Detective controls include reporting
suspicious activity to the appropriate authorities. Corrective controls include
the eventual exiting of a customer relationship where necessary.
Information and communication include proper training for employees, third-
party providers, and the board of directors. Regular updates should be given
when financial crime typologies evolve, so that front line staff know what to
look for.
The final element of risk control is monitoring. This is usually undertaken by a
separate department, such as internal audit, or independent assurance within
the business or the compliance lines, who will test that all policies, procedures,
and controls have been properly observed and applied.
Residual risk equation
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 14
Financial institutions measure risk by conducting a risk assessment.
You can use the equation: inherent risk – controlled risk = residual risk.
Inherent risk is the risk before controls are applied. Inherent risk generally
considers the risk factors of customer, jurisdiction, product, and channel.
All of that comes together to create inherent risk.
The next part of the equation is controlled risk. Your organization has many
controls, including CDD, sanctions screening, governance, training, adequate
staffing, testing, and transaction monitoring.
Residual risk is the risk that remains after you implement effective controls.
To understand inherent risk, it is important to know the specific risks
associated with each risk factor.
Customer risk can be higher if the customer is a nonresident, if the customer’s
legal structure is complex, or if the customer’s business is cash-intensive.
Jurisdictions with higher risk include those with poor AML regulations or areas
with high levels of corruption.
Each product or service carries a different level of risk. For example, private
banking is considered higher risk, as transactions might be confidential in an
attempt to mask true ownership.
Channel risk increases with non-face-to-face business relationships or
transactions, or when payments are sent to or received from unknown or
unassociated third parties.
Your organization has established a set of controls to help mitigate inherent
risk. A lower residual risk rating indicates that the financial institution is
operating with appropriate controls. A higher residual risk rating suggests that
the financial institution should consider investigating its controls to maintain its
risk tolerance levels. In addition, your organization should have carefully
documented policies and procedures, and carefully documented research to
know your customer.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 15
Risk-rating by applying your institution's
policies and tools
Your organization will set out its appetite for certain types of customer and
financial crime risk ratings in its risk appetite statement. Most organizations
apply a scoring methodology to ensure a consistent allocation of risk ratings.
Typically, the rating is derived using a grid to arrive at the inherent risk a
customer, jurisdiction, product, and channel, pose to the organization. For
example, the template might have customer types down one axis and the
product types along the top axis. The risk rating is generated by combining
multiple factors. For example, a politically exposed person (PEP) using a wire
transfer service might be rated high risk by this system.
Your organization’s policies will state the type of customers and industries it
will accept. For example, it might not accept any customer involved in the
production of military weapons. It will also divide its customers into categories,
for example PEPs, high-net-worth individuals, high-risk industries, and entities
owned or controlled by sanctioned individuals, among others.
In addition, it may set out jurisdictions for which it has no appetite, which will
almost certainly include sanctioned nations.
These statements of risk appetite consider all elements of financial crime
including fraud, bribery, corruption, anti-money laundering, and sanctions
legislation.
Your first step in risk-rating your customer is to make sure you have correctly
classified the customer type and industry, where applicable. Many
organizations use recognized Standard Industrial Classification Codes, or SIC,
published by the authorities in their jurisdiction. Many organizations rate
jurisdictions for risk based on publications such as those from the Financial
Action Task Force and the list of comprehensively sanctioned jurisdictions
from the Office of Foreign Assets Control. Your organization might also have a
subcategory for high-risk countries.
Once you have determined the correct customer type, you must now rate the
risk against the product type they will take. For example, if a customer from a
comprehensively sanctioned jurisdiction requests wire transfer services, the
risk rating would be extremely high due to the comprehensive sanctions
imposed on the jurisdiction and the product type.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 16
It is important to note that risk scoring methodologies or automated
algorithms only work effectively if they are used correctly. Make sure you input
the correct information when seeking the score.
Risk-rating by applying third-party tools
Organizations need to conduct a risk assessment to understand the types of
risk and their potential impacts. The risk assessment process involves collating
qualitative and quantitative data about risks, such as the number of wire
transfers sent internationally or to high-risk jurisdictions. This data helps
determine inherent risk, control strength, and the remaining residual risk. The
company can then determine what steps it needs to take to mitigate risk and
align to its risk appetite.
Since the risk assessment relies on data, it can be difficult and subject to error.
Many organizations develop or purchase risk assessment tools. Sometimes
organizations combine multiple internal and third-party tools. These tools can
be used to quickly upload data, verify accuracy, look for gaps, and calculate
the final scoring.
Risk calculation involves assigning a scoring methodology, such as relative
weighting, to the risks and controls of an organization. A company using an
internal or third-party risk assessment tool must weigh inherent risk and
control strength correctly to ensure that residual risk is correctly calculated.
For example, if a particular control is weighted too heavily, it could inflate the
overall control strength, which would incorrectly decrease the residual risk.
An internal or third-party risk assessment tool might be leveraged for data
collation prior to those calculations, to weight and validate the calculations’
formulas, look for data gaps, and complete the overall calculation. It all
depends on the tool and its respective users. Whether automated or manual,
the tool must be tailored to the institution so that it not only includes the
correct calculations but can be refined to include risks unique to that
organization. Some companies take a hybrid approach and manage higher-
risk products or client assessments themselves while relying on third-party
tools to automate their lower-risk products. This allows the company to save
time on those risk areas but still have an audit trail for verification.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 17
Principles for an effective sanctions risk
assessment
We know that risk assessments are the foundation of a sanctions compliance
program (SCP). But what constitutes a high-quality risk assessment? The
answer to this question varies according to the size of an organization and its
activities, customers, products, services, and geographic locations. Essentially,
a high-quality sanctions risk assessment accurately and completely analyzes
the extent to which an organization might be exposed to sanctions risk. It
takes a holistic view of the entire organization to identify exposure to high-risk
jurisdictions, activities, and relationships.
Organizations should conduct a risk assessment in a manner, and with a
frequency, that adequately accounts for their potential risk exposure.
Depending on the size and functions of the organization, robust risk
assessments consider sanctions risks in the following categories:
• Clients and customers
• Products and services
• Supply chain
• Intermediaries
• Counterparties
• Transactions including currency
• Geographical exposure
In assessing risk, organizations should leverage existing information to inform
the process. It is essential to ensure that risk assessments are based on sound
information and draw upon up-to-date due diligence.
There are several good practice examples applicable to conducting sanctions
risk assessments.
Good practices include:
• Conduct a risk assessment that is suited to the business’ size and
complexity
• Account for customers’ directors and beneficial owners
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 18
• Record the methodology and procedures used
• Validate the accuracy of the data
• Include a mixture of both qualitative and quantitative analysis
• Use the same methodology for each risk assessment
• Include all suppliers, business partners, and other third parties
• Ensure the assessment is conducted on a solid, accurate understanding of
requirements
• Use up-to-date and complete due diligence records
Common failings include:
• Omit suppliers, business partners, and other third parties
• Conduct the assessment based on a poor understanding of requirements
• Use of outdated or incomplete due diligence records
Risk assessments should be updated, as appropriate, to account for any
changes in the risk environment, such as the introduction of new sanctions.
The conduct and root causes of any apparent violations or systemic
deficiencies identified by the organization should also inform the frequency of
risk assessments. Finally, strong SCPs should include a methodology to
identify, analyze, and address any risks identified in the assessment.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 19
Sanctions risk from specific
products and business lines
Retail banking sanctions risk
Retail banking refers to the provision of financial services and products
directly to individual consumers rather than businesses. Financial institutions
that offer retail banking are particularly vulnerable to sanctions risk because of
their wide range of banking products, diverse customer base, and complex
operations. This risk arises from engaging in various banking activities, such as
cross-border wire transfers, and virtual currency transactions, which can be
exploited for illicit purposes. To manage sanctions risks, financial institutions
that offer retail banking must thoroughly assess the risk of individual
customers, different transaction types, and counterparties.
In assessing customer risk, it is important for financial institutions to obtain
general proof of identity, such as name, date of birth, or nationality, to ensure
compliance with regulations. This data allows the institution to conduct
screenings against sanctions lists and identify any potential risks.
When evaluating the nature of a customer's business, financial institutions
should inquire about the purpose behind specific transactions or activities.
Any inconsistencies with expected account behavior should be considered a
red flag. For example, if a customer expresses interest in purchasing property
in a foreign country, the financial institution could inquire about the reason
behind the investment, the intended use of the bank account, and the source
of funds being transferred. Understanding the origin of the customer's wealth
and their occupation can provide valuable insights.
The jurisdiction is also important to understand sanctions risk. Factors such as
tax residence, employment details, family connections, location of other
accounts, property, assets, and travel patterns should be considered to gain a
comprehensive understanding of this risk category. For example, if a
customer intends to open an account in a country where they have no
residence and have previously resided in a region associated with sanctions,
this would be a red flag that would warrant further investigation.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 20
Commercial and investment banking
sanctions risk
The commercial and investment banking lines of business generally pose
elevated risk for sanctions violations. Customers in this sector often utilize
complex ownership structures and intermediaries, such as special purpose
vehicles for lending purposes, making it challenging to identify owners and
controllers. You must obtain sufficient information about these structures to
ensure that the customer does not have a significant owner who is a sanctions
target, and to comply with beneficial ownership rules set by the EU, UK, and
US. Jurisdiction is another key risk factor. You must consider the potential
impact of sanctions imposed on specific regions or countries. Vigilant CDD
helps ensure compliance and mitigate sanctions-related risks.
Customers in commercial and investment banking frequently rely on
intermediaries, which adds a layer of complexity. When assessing the nature
of a customer's business, you should investigate how the customer performs
CDD on its own customers, counterparties, intermediaries, suppliers, and end
users. Additionally, determining the industry in which the customer operates
and examining their actual and expected counterparties is important. Certain
industries, such as arms dealers, natural resource companies, and those
dealing with goods with potential dual uses, are more prone to sanctions
violations.
Examining jurisdiction and geography is important in assessing sanctions risk.
Considering the customer's complex structure, you should analyze the
location of operations, counterparties, and the geographic reach of
transactions. To clarify the extent of the customer's geographic footprint, you
should:
• Understand the cross-border products and services to which they have
access.
• Segregate funds with sanctions exposure.
• Evaluate the flow of funds with sanctions exposure through the
organization.
In the investment products business line, pay attention to debt and equity
financing. For example, US sanctions on Russia or Venezuela prohibit certain
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 21
entities from raising finances directly or indirectly through the issuance of new
debt or equity, or both. You must assess whether proposed transactions are
structured to circumvent these sanctions restrictions and consider the
aggregate ownership rules when applicable.
Wealth management and private banking
sanctions risk
Wealth management and private banking offer personalized financial services
to wealthy individuals. To assess the level of sanctions risk, you should
thoroughly analyze several factors to identify customers with a higher chance
of potential sanction violations.
Factors to consider include:
• Connections between customers and sanctioned individuals or activities
• Involvement in high-risk countries or industries targeted by sanctions
• Complexity of organizational structures used
• Adverse media related to sanctions and customer activities
These factors ensure that customers with a higher likelihood of being involved
in potential sanction violations are accurately identified and subjected to
enhanced due diligence (EDD) measures. By carefully evaluating these
factors, financial institutions can effectively manage their exposure to
sanctions breaches.
In private banking and wealth management, it is common to engage
professional intermediaries such as accountants and lawyers to handle asset
transfers and wealth management. Unfortunately, these arrangements can
also enable financial sanctions evasion by sanctioned individuals. They might
exploit nontransparent corporate ownership and asset management vehicles
to move funds and assets discreetly. One example is investments in the global
art market, which is notorious for its intrinsic opacity and strictly enforced
client confidentiality.
Conducting thorough screening of potential wealth management and private
banking customers is crucial to identify any potentially risky connections. This
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 22
includes scrutinizing all individuals associated with the account, such as
beneficial owners and authorized signatories.
Red flags that could indicate potential suspicious activity include:
• The use of corporate entities, such as shell companies, to conceal
ownership or the source of funds, especially when involving countries
under sanctions
• The use of shell companies for international wire transfers
• Engagement with third parties to shield the identities of sanctioned
individuals or politically exposed persons, such as sanctioned oligarchs
• Accounts that experience rapid increases in value without a legitimate
reason
• Newly established accounts attempting to send or receive funds from
institutions on sanctions lists or those excluded from the Society for
Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication (SWIFT)
• Unusual foreign exchange transactions that indirectly involve financial
institutions that are subject to sanctions
Correspondent banking sanctions risk
Correspondent banking is the provision of banking or business services by
one bank, the correspondent bank, to another bank, the respondent bank. By
establishing multiple correspondent relationships globally, respondent banks
can undertake financial transactions for themselves and their customers in
jurisdictions where they have no physical presence. Large international banks
typically act as correspondents for thousands of other banks around the
world.
Correspondent banking is vulnerable to sanctions violations, in large part
because the correspondent does not, or cannot, do typical due diligence to
know the respondent’s customers. Stakeholders associated with
correspondent banking activities need to consider specific risk elements
within these relationships.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 23
When evaluating sanctions exposure in a correspondent bank relationship,
financial institutions should apply the same rules to the correspondent bank
that apply to their own institution.
The Wolfsberg Group has developed a specialized questionnaire to collect
correspondent banking Know Your Customer (KYC) information that can be
utilized for sanctions compliance purposes.
The key areas to examine regarding sanctions concerns in a correspondent
banking relationship include:
• The parent company and its branches and equivalents
• The executive committee, or equivalent, and the supervisory board
• Any other significant controlling interests and their ultimate beneficial
owners (UBO)
• Customer base and account activity for potential high-risk clients
• The geographic regions in which their customer base is located
In a correspondent banking relationship, the correspondent institution should
monitor the respondent institution’s transactions. It should establish a
framework for assessing the adequacy of the respondent sanctions
compliance program. This process involves several steps, including using
questionnaires and establishing a due diligence program with appropriate
risk-based policies.
Downstream activities and the risk of nested accounts pose an elevated
sanctions risk. Some respondents are, themselves, correspondents to third
banks—a practice called “nesting.” Nested accounts further shield
correspondent banks from knowing the parties involved. For example, Iranian
bank A holds an account with Turkish bank B. In turn, Turkish bank B has a
correspondent relationship with US bank C. Many sanctions compliance
violations involve the use of nested accounts.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 24
Trade-based sanctions risk
Trade-related activities pose significant risks in terms of both sanctions
violations and sanctions evasion. Activities that take place within free-trade
zones and transit points in areas that border on sanctioned jurisdictions, as
well as those that involve front and shell companies, are particularly high-risk.
A free-trade zone is a designated area within a country where goods can be
imported, stored, processed, or re-exported with minimal regulatory
oversight. By leveraging key trade documents and conducting enhanced due
diligence, financial institutions can identify suspicious activities and report
them on a timely basis.
When facilitating cross-border trade, financial institutions generally have
access to the principal trade-related documents, such as bills of lading and
commercial invoices. These documents allow financial institutions to identify
and report most suspicious activities that might be indicative of attempts to
breach or circumvent sanctions programs.
However, conducting effective due diligence in trade finance transactions can
be challenging. The involvement of multiple stakeholders and the constant
flow of information throughout the course of a transaction make it difficult to
maintain accurate and up-to-date records. Additional complicating factors
are the complex nature of export control regulations and the heavy rate of
