manual document processing.
When reviewing trade-related activities, financial institutions should assess
multiple factors, including the end use, end user, and logistics involved in a
transaction. It is equally important to examine the origin and final destination
of the goods being traded.
International trading hubs, such as Hong Kong and the United Arab Emirates,
have long been identified by government agencies as common
transshipment points for sanctioned goods. For example, the Jebel Ali Free
Zone in Dubai has high exposure to Iran-related trade. Following the Russian
invasion of Ukraine in February 2022, several former Soviet Union countries
have been identified by regulators as Russia’s preferred transshipment points
for procuring restricted western technologies. The Eurasian Economic Union,
of which non-sanctioned Kazakhstan, Kyrgyzstan, and Armenia are members,
facilitates these trades.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 25
Insurance sanctions risk
Organizations providing insurance and reinsurance services must comply with
sanctions regulations to the same extent as any other financial institution. For
example, if a US insurer or reinsurer finds out that a policyholder is 50% or
more owned by an OFAC-sanctioned person or that a blocked person stands
to benefit from a third-party policy, they must freeze any assets and
payments, including premiums and claims. They must also report any blocked
property, such as blocked insurance policies, to OFAC within 10 business days.
Syndication or pooling arrangements as well as treaty-based reinsurance
agreements, which involve multiple insurers or reinsurers sharing risks and
resources, might unintentionally involve restricted countries or entities.
Identifying such risks can be challenging.
Entities within the insurance sector that fall under US jurisdiction, such as
agents, brokers, carriers, and claims adjusters, must strictly adhere to OFAC
sanctions against anyone on the Specially Designated Nationals and Blocked
Persons (SDN) List. US sanctions are enforced on a strict liability basis. Even
unintentional violations could result in civil and criminal penalties for both
individuals and companies.
To ensure compliance, insurance firms should establish robust CDD policies,
procedures, and processes. This includes conducting thorough checks and
employing appropriate screening tools to identify and screen beneficiaries
against sanctions lists. By implementing effective CDD measures, insurers can
mitigate the risk of facilitating transactions involving sanctioned parties, thus
safeguarding their operations and reputations.
The decentralized nature of the insurance business exposes it to potential
sanctions breaches. Insurers can proactively minimize their exposure to such
liabilities by means of policy exclusion clauses. These clauses render coverage
void for parties which are found to be in violation of sanctions.
In 2020, Generali Global Assistance Inc., a US-based travel insurer, reached a
settlement with OFAC, agreeing to pay US$5.9 million for repeated violations
of sanctions against Cuba between 2010 and 2015. The insurer had
reimbursed its Canadian affiliate for medical and other expenses incurred by
Canadian policyholders while traveling to Cuba, which violated US sanctions.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 26
Sanctions compliance program
Framework for a robust sanctions
compliance program
A robust, effective sanctions compliance program (SCP) enables an
organization to comply with regulatory expectations concerning sanctions
compliance. It also helps to manage the organization’s sanctions risk. Several
sanctions authorities have issued guidance on what constitutes the essential
components of an SCP. The most comprehensive such guidance is the 2019
Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) “Framework for OFAC Compliance
Commitments” document, or “the Guidelines.”
Sanctions authorities generally encourage the use of a risk-based approach to
sanctions compliance. This involves developing, implementing, and regularly
updating SCPs. SCPs follow a similar methodology to that adopted by anti-
money laundering compliance programs.
According to OFAC, the five essential components of an SCP are:
• Management commitment
• Risk assessment
• Internal controls
• Testing and auditing
• Training
Although the Guidelines relate to organizations subject to US jurisdiction,
regulators in many other jurisdictions also expect relevant entities to maintain
effective SCPs. OFAC’s five essential components serve as a useful indicator
of best practice in any jurisdiction.
Regulators will generally consider favorably an organization with an effective
SCP at the time of an apparent violation.
For instance, OFAC may, in appropriate cases, consider the existence of an
effective SCP at the time of an apparent violation as a factor in whether a case
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 27
is deemed “egregious.” Egregious determinations negatively impact the
calculation of the civil penalty for the violator.
Sanctions compliance is not an expectation only for the financial sector, but
also several other sectors, including, but not limited to:
• Corporations
• Importers
• Exporters
• Humanitarian organizations
• Maritime
• Energy
• Technology
• Art dealers
Framework for a robust sanctions
compliance program (Case example: Fraser
Corporation)
Fraser Corporation, a mid-sized technology company, recently experienced
an incident that highlighted the urgent need for an in-depth enterprise-wide
sanctions risk assessment and the establishment of a robust sanctions
compliance program (SCP). Following the incident, Fraser Corporation faced
public backlash from customers, shareholders, and regulatory authorities who
questioned the company’s commitment to ethical business practices and
compliance with international sanctions regulations. The company’s senior
management hoped that a comprehensive risk assessment could rectify
compliance gaps and prevent similar incidents in the future. After the
assessment, the company realized it was exposed to varying customer,
jurisdiction, product, and channel risks.
Fraser Corporation unknowingly engaged with a supplier involved in illicit
activities and subject to sanctions by multiple jurisdictions. This discovery led
to severe consequences, including reputational damage, regulatory scrutiny,
and potential legal repercussions. Fraser Corporation's senior management
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 28
initiated a comprehensive risk assessment to identify sanctions vulnerabilities
across the organization. After engaging compliance experts, conducting
interviews, and analyzing internal processes, the company came up with the
following conclusions:
• For customer risk: The company identified high-risk customers as
representing about 10% of its customer base, implemented enhanced KYC
measures, and conducted enhanced ongoing monitoring.
• For jurisdiction risk: The company analyzed the regulatory landscape in the
jurisdictions where it operates, identified high-risk countries, and tightened
its country policies.
• For product risk: The company reviewed its product portfolio to identify
high-risk products and developed new guidelines for its sales and business
development teams.
• For channel risk: The company evaluated its distribution channels and
tightened oversight for those with high exposure to the risk of sanctions
breaches.
Based on the findings, Fraser Corporation allocated extra funds to develop a
robust, properly resourced, enterprise-wide SCP. This included overhauling
policies for customer onboarding, enhanced due diligence, ongoing
monitoring of client relationships, and continuous internal training.
Key takeaways
• Companies without an SCP might face regulatory scrutiny, legal liability,
and customer backlash.
• Senior management is responsible for a robust SCP.
• A risk assessment can identify sanctions vulnerabilities.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 29
Three lines of defense
The first line of defense is the line of business. Also known as the front line,
these customer-facing employees are responsible for implementing AFC
policies and procedures and performing customer due diligence. The first line
of defense may also create operations, risk, and control teams that support
the business in complying with AFC requirements.
The second line of defense is the risk and control, or AFC compliance,
functions. This includes the money laundering reporting officer, who manages
and monitors AFC activities. Members of the second line of defense should
avoid potential conflicts of interest, which include not being incentivized to
receive a direct share of business profits.
The third line of defense is internal audit. They perform an independent review
of the controls applied by the first two lines of defense.
The line of business creates and implements procedures to comply with AFC
policy requirements and communicates them to all personnel. This line of
business must also promote and execute a culture of compliance.
The AFC compliance function is the second line of defense. This line is
responsible for developing policies to ensure compliance with AFC
regulations and legislation and appropriate AFC training for all employees. The
money laundering reporting officer should be the contact for all AFC issues
for internal and external authorities. The AFC compliance function must have
sufficient independence from the business lines to prevent conflicts of
interest. This line is responsible for monitoring, detecting, and reporting
potentially suspicious activity to relevant authorities and for conducting
higher-risk customer reviews.
The audit function is the third line of defense. This line should report to the
audit committee of the board of directors. The audit function should
independently evaluate the risk management and controls of the organization
through periodic assessments. These include the:
• Adequacy of controls to mitigate identified risks
• Effectiveness of the:
o Staff’s execution of controls
o Training
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 30
o Compliance oversight and quality controls
The audit function must have knowledgeable employees with sufficient AFC
audit expertise.
Role of sanctions compliance officer
A sanctions compliance officer’s (SCO) main responsibility is to ensure that an
organization complies with sanctions regulations and requirements imposed
by relevant authorities such as the United Nations, European Union, and
relevant national authorities. An SCO must stay updated on changes in
sanctions regulations, ensure the organization is compliant, and communicate
with regulatory agencies. The officer must ensure a comprehensive and
effective sanctions compliance program (SCP) is in place to manage
sanctions risk. The officer should have a reputation for integrity, possess the
necessary knowledge of AFC practices, in particular sanctions risks, and have
sufficient time and authority to carry out their functions independently and
effectively.
Your organization’s commitment to sanctions compliance should be
demonstrated through the appointment of an SCO. This individual may be the
same person serving in other senior compliance positions, as some
organizations designate a single person to oversee all areas of anti-financial
crime. However, the SCO must have technical expertise in global regulators’
sanctions guidelines and requirements, and they must apply this knowledge to
their organization’s financial and commercial activities.
The role of the SCO should include the following:
• Establishing and maintaining a framework for conducting organization-
wide and individual risk assessments related to financial crimes, in
alignment with regulatory guidelines
• Implementing adequate and up-to-date AFC policies and procedures and
ensuring they are updated as needed to meet legal or regulatory changes
• Monitoring the organization’s compliance with AFC obligations, including
evaluating the effectiveness of implemented controls, policies, and
procedures
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 31
• Communicating with regulators and ensuring that suspicious transactions
are reported to the national Financial Intelligence Unit according to their
guidelines
• Ensuring that appropriate training on financial crime risks, methods, trends,
and typologies is provided regularly to staff members
By fulfilling these duties and obligations, the sanctions compliance officer
contributes to a robust compliance framework while safeguarding the
institution's operations and reputation.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 32
Sanctions Due Diligence
Introduction
Setup: Correspondent banking
You are the chief compliance officer of Etoile Finance Groupe, a global
financial services group headquartered in Luxembourg. Your organization is
currently negotiating a settlement with the US Office of Foreign Assets
Control (OFAC) following the discovery of alleged multiple violations of its
Counter Terrorism Sanctions program. Given your role, you are actively
involved in the negotiation process. You are in regular communication with
OFAC, the group’s board of directors, chief legal officer, and external legal
counsel.
Etoile Finance Groupe has a subsidiary by the name of Banque Etoile SA.
OFAC alleges that between February 2018 and September 2020, Banque
Etoile SA processed US$15.6 million worth of transactions through financial
institutions located in the United States for, or on behalf of, eight corporate
customers based in Lebanon.
At the time the transactions were made, the Lebanese entities were 50% or
more owned, individually or in aggregate, by persons listed by OFAC. They
were on its Specially Designated Nationals and Blocked Persons (SDN) List as
Specially Designated Global Terrorists. These entities were subject to the
same blocking sanctions as the listed individuals. Until several months ago, the
entities had accounts with the Banque Etoile SA Beirut branch, through which
Etoile Finance Groupe does business in Lebanon.
OFAC found that the Beirut branch was unable to screen beneficial ownership
information for its corporate customers to identify the presence of SDNs. In
some instances, OFAC found that the branch did not even know their
beneficial owners were SDNs. The case came about because several
transactions originating from Banque Etoile SA had been declined and flagged
by US-based correspondent banks.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 33
The Beirut branch used a defective KYC system that was incapable of
including beneficial ownership information in an electronic format. That
information was held only in paper form. Therefore, beneficial ownership
information was not included in the automated screening process. Moreover,
in a few cases, the branch did not obtain that information at all, because the
KYC procedures were difficult for frontline staff to follow.
You have been tasked by the board of directors to create a plan of action to
demonstrate to OFAC your organization’s commitment to sanctions
compliance.
What would you do?
Which measure should you include?
• Replacing the screening technology vendor
• Re-training your compliance personnel
• Digitizing KYC records
• Reinforcing contractual sanctions clauses
Feedback
Digitizing KYC records is the best response. It must be a key step in the plan of
action. The alleged sanctions violations occurred in part because beneficial
ownership records, which were not held in an electronic format, could not be
included in the automatic sanctions screening.
Replacing the screening technology vendor is not an appropriate solution. The
violations alleged by OFAC occurred because of systemic issues with KYC files
held at the Lebanese branch. The violations were not due to the quality of the
screening process or of the screening software supplied by the current
vendor.
Re-training compliance personnel is not appropriate, as doing so would not
address any of the underlying problems identified. Instead, additional training
for relationship managers employed by the Beirut branch would be
necessary. According to OFAC’s findings, in several instances beneficial
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 34
ownership information had not been recorded at all because the KYC
procedures were difficult for frontline staff to follow.
Reinforcing contractual sanctions clauses would not ease OFAC’s concerns.
The root cause of the alleged violations had to do with the low quality or
availability of KYC information, which in turn impacted the effectiveness of
sanctions screening.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Explain how to conduct KYC in sanctions compliance.
• Describe how to conduct enhanced research for sanctions compliance.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 35
The importance of KYC in
sanctions compliance
Controls across the customer life cycle
Organizations manage financial crime risk by implementing several controls
throughout the customer’s life cycle. This includes implementing controls at
onboarding, on an ongoing basis throughout the customer’s relationship with
the organization, and during offboarding. The key controls that organizations
implement include:
• Know your customer (KYC)
• Transaction monitoring
• Investigations
KYC is one of the first controls executed. At pre-onboarding, it includes the
creation of a customer profile and concludes when a decision is made to
onboard the customer. KYC is also an ongoing control. Organizations are
expected to keep the customer’s records updated throughout the customer
relationship. Transaction monitoring and investigations are both ongoing
controls.
KYC at onboarding includes common types of due diligence. These include
identification and verification, simple, standard, and enhanced due diligence,
and screening for sanctions, political exposure, and adverse media. Your
organization will designate processes and thresholds.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 36
Almost every organization creates a customer profile with a risk score. This is
used to decide whether a customer should be onboarded. Ongoing KYC
controls, also known as a KYC refresh, involve reviewing customer records
periodically and during trigger events. Ongoing KYC controls use a risk-based
approach to screen customers and capture any changes in customers’
records. Perpetual KYC is the practice of maintaining accurate and up-to-date
customer data. This is done through real-time updates based on changes in
customer behavior and circumstances.
Transaction monitoring is an ongoing effort that looks at the customer’s past
activity over an extended period. It detects complex patterns that could
indicate money laundering, tax evasion, or other financial crimes. Transaction
monitoring focuses on unusual transactions, the circumstances leading up to
them, and the counterparties involved. When an alert is generated, an analyst
reviews the alert and either clears it or escalates it.
Investigations start when a customer’s profile or behavior raises questions
during ongoing KYC or transaction monitoring. If the investigation concludes
that a suspicion has occurred, a suspicious activity report (SAR) must be filed.
KYC is intended to manage risk
The purpose of having controls to Know Your Customer, also called KYC, is to
manage the financial crime risk to your organization. In most jurisdictions,
collecting customer information is also a legal and regulatory requirement.
The process enables your organization to collect information on customer
identity and fit and the key customer risk factors so that you can assess the
possibility of financial crime that could arise from that relationship or
transaction.
Most organizations use three risk classification levels for customers—low,
medium, and high—or a combination of them.
Once it has KYC information, your organization can then decide, based on the
risk assessment, whether it wants to accept the customer and outline
mitigating factors on the identified risks or decline the business.
Organizations that have strong AFC programs need effective KYC controls to
manage risk.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 37
Your organization will have determined the type of risk it is willing to accept
when it does business. This is referred to as the organization's risk appetite.
Once that is established, the types of risk those customer and business types
will pose to your organization can be identified and classified. These risks are
then analyzed to determine the appropriate controls that should be put in
place to mitigate and reduce them. These controls must be appropriate,
specific, and risk-based. Combined, this is known as a risk-based approach.
Throughout the life of the relationship between your organization and that
customer, your organization will rely on the due diligence you complete at this
initial stage. Because so much emphasis is placed at the beginning of a
relationship, this is the time when criminals might tailor their actions to make
them appear legitimate. Criminals might choose to wait until later to start their
suspicious activities.
By undertaking reasonable and comprehensive due diligence at the initial
customer acceptance stage, you develop a stronger customer profile for your
organization to use later, for example when you monitor transactions to
determine if a particular activity is unusual or suspicious.
A full, complete, and up-to-date customer profile makes screening and
monitoring controls more effective and efficient. In most jurisdictions, there
are reporting obligations in place to promptly report any suspicious activities
to the authorities.
KYC in a sanctions compliance program
KYC plays an important role in a sanctions compliance program. Sanctions
KYC specifically focuses on evaluating a customer's exposure to sanctions,
establishing a sanctions risk rating for each customer. This process involves
gathering comprehensive information about customers in order to make
informed decisions regarding client onboarding. This information helps
financial institutions identify red flags and understand the potential sanctions-
related risks associated with engaging in business relationships with
customers. By conducting thorough sanctions KYC, organizations can identify
any connections to high-risk jurisdictions, sanctioned entities, or high-risk
industries. KYC in sanctions mitigates the risk of sanctions violations and
supports compliance efforts by ensuring that organizations have a clear
understanding of their customers' sanctions risk profiles.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 38
To assess a customer's sanctions risk, organizations need to establish a
sanctions risk rating for each customer. This rating is determined by analyzing
various factors, including the customer's geographic footprint, industry,
ownership structure, source of funds, and transaction purposes. Red flags
arise if the client has connections to high-risk jurisdictions, operates in high-
risk industries, or is involved with sanctioned entities. Understanding the origin
of funds and ensuring transaction alignment with sanctions are crucial for
maintaining compliance.
The KYC process used by organizations typically involves completing a
sanctions questionnaire. It might also include conducting independent
sanctions research at the start of the customer relationship. Additionally, the
KYC process should encompass the customers and the organizations they
are associated with, such as branches, agents, subsidiaries, and the
percentage of revenues derived from activities in sanctioned jurisdictions.
This comprehensive assessment informs the scope of the relationship, guides
future due diligence efforts, and determines the sanctions risk rating.
By leveraging information provided by the customer or by the relationship
manager and by conducting a thorough analysis of the customer's profile and
transactional behavior, organizations can assign an appropriate sanctions risk
rating. This risk rating serves as an important tool in assessing the level of
sanctions-related risk associated with a customer. It enables financial
institutions to make informed decisions as to whether to continue the
relationship, or to apply necessary risk mitigation measures, such as enhanced
due diligence.
Risk-based approach to KYC (initial and
ongoing)
A risk-based approach to KYC helps you identify the risks each customer
brings to your organization in respect to possible money laundering and
terrorist financing risk. The risk-based approach is defined by your
organization’s risk appetite, or the level of risk your organization is willing to
accept based on its strategic objectives.
The risk-based approach categorizes customers into levels of risk: such as
high, medium, low, and perhaps more levels based on the policies of your
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 39
organization. The level of risk determines the amount and type of due
diligence you must do.
KYC is an ongoing effort. Your institution will have risk-based time frames for
conducting periodic reviews. For example, customers might be reviewed
annually if they are high risk, at two years for medium risk, and every three
years if they are low risk. The frequency will vary based on your organization's
policies.
Regulators want you to apply a risk-based approach. They want you to
understand your customer because you have done a sufficient amount of
research, and you have documented all that research.
The risk-based approach sets out the process for you to collect information
about the identity of the customer and the key customer risk factors, and to
assess the risk that could arise from that relationship or transaction.
An example is the due diligence required for opening a simple checking
account. Without a risk-based approach, a senior citizen from New York
wanting to deposit his pension checks would be subject to the same due
diligence process as a politically exposed person who is part of a foreign
government wanting to deposit a large amount of funds.
KYC does not end after onboarding a customer. Depending on the risk
classification of the customer, there should be ongoing monitoring of
customer transactions and a regular, periodic review of the customer's profile.
Note that customers might not remain in the same risk category throughout
their relationship with your organization. For example, an ordinary individual
might run for local government office and, if elected, become a PEP. That
would change their risk classification from low to medium or high, based upon
the role they perform.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 40
Transaction monitoring versus screening
Transaction monitoring and screening are processes used by organizations to
detect and combat financial crime and ensure compliance with regulations.
Transaction monitoring is the continuous observation of customer
transactions to identify suspicious or illicit activities. Transaction monitoring
begins after the customer has been onboarded. Organizations can detect
complex patterns that might indicate money laundering, tax evasion, or other
financial crimes.
Screening is the review of customer information to identify high-risk
customers or entities. Screening begins before the customer is onboarded
but is also performed on an ongoing basis. There are several types of
screening. These include:
• Name screening
• Payment screening
• Adverse media screening
• Politically exposed person (PEP) screening
Transaction monitoring reviews customer activity and the risk it might have to
the organization. If unusual or suspicious activity is detected, a suspicious
activity report (SAR) should be submitted. For example, large transactions,
frequent transfers, or transactions involving high-risk jurisdictions might
trigger alerts for further investigation.
Screening involves the review of customer names, payment details, adverse
media, and connections to PEPs.
• Name screening identifies individuals who might be considered high risk
for being involved in financial crime. It screens customer’s names against
watchlists including sanctions lists. It is initially performed prior to
onboarding, but it is also conducted on an ongoing basis.
• Payment screening is the review of payments made and received by
customers. If an unusual payment is detected, it is stopped and held until
investigations are complete.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 41
• Adverse media screening is the review of negative news about a
customer, beneficial owner, or associated party.
• PEP screening identifies politically exposed persons, their family members,
and close associates.
There is an important difference between transaction monitoring and
screening. Transaction monitoring detects suspicious activity after the
transactions have already happened. Screening identifies high-risk individuals
and entities before transactions are completed and on a regular basis.
Transaction monitoring versus screening
(Case example: InsureCo Limited)
InsureCo Limited is an insurance holding company based in the US. It has
settled potential liability for violating the Office of Foreign Assets Control’s
(OFAC) Cuban Assets Control Regulations (CACR). The CACR prohibits
transactions to Cuba from anyone subject to US jurisdiction, which includes
foreign entities owned or controlled by a US person. InsureCo Limited's
subsidiary, InsureCo Europe, processed transactions in an apparent breach of
the CACR. This included premium payments and claim payouts under Cuba-
related travel insurance coverage. InsureCo failed to implement adequate
internal controls, such as transaction monitoring and name screening and
therefore failed to comply with US sanctions.
InsureCo Europe, a subsidiary of InsureCo Limited, issued travel insurance
policies covering Cuba-related travel to individual travelers and group
policyholders. The policies provided coverage for risks such as lost or
damaged luggage or property, death, medical emergencies, repatriation, and
trip cancellation. InsureCo Europe processed over 20,000 transactions
amounting to almost US$370,000. These transactions were in violation of the
CACR. They included premium payments totaling almost US$290,000
received for travel insurance coverage and claim payments of over
US$80,000 made under these coverages. The company had not
implemented robust monitoring or screening controls for years, despite the
inherent risks.
InsureCo Europe had screened the names of the policyholders and travelers
but had not considered the wider implications of US sanctions. The policies did
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 42
not violate EU sanctions so InsureCo Europe approved them. However, as a
foreign subsidiary of a US person, by doing so it automatically triggered liability
for the parent company.
US sanctions might apply to foreign-based entities, so it is important to
monitor transactions and screen names and payments to mitigate potential
violations.
Key takeaways
• Transaction monitoring and regular name screening are important internal
controls.
• US sanctions might be applicable to foreign-based entities.
• Insurance providers need robust policies and procedures to ensure that
accepting premiums and paying out claims do not violate sanctions
programs.
Four research steps
The four-step research model is a method to improve the quality of your KYC
research. The steps are:
• Assess
• Explore
• Organize, and
• Present
First, you should assess what you already know, and the knowledge gaps you
need to fill. Start by determining what you need at the end, so you can use
your resources wisely.
Second, you should explore to find the answers you need about the situation
and those involved.
Third, organize your information in a meaningful way. Decide what is relevant
and important.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 43
In the final step, present the results based upon what you know about the
customer.
You can see how these four steps loop around. First you assess, then you
explore, and as you organize your information, you can better assess gaps.
Sometimes that pushes you back to the explore step. An example of this is
enhanced due diligence. At first, during standard due diligence, you thought
you knew enough about the customer. But when organizing your information,
you realized that this customer deserved a deeper look, so you did enhanced
due diligence by exploring some more to get a better picture of the
customer.
The final step, where you present the results based upon what you know
about the customer, might be the most important. Several audiences will rely
on the hard work you have done. The present step is where you document,
persuade, and defend your research choices in ways that meet the needs of
all your audiences—now and over the life of the relationship with this
customer.
Make sure you don’t do redundant work. Regulators want you to be thorough,
but your organization’s business staff wants to ensure that the compliance
process is easy for your customers. Keep your organization’s limited budget
and staff in mind. Be a compliant and good business partner who creates a
positive customer experience.
Used together, these four steps will help you to be more effective and
efficient.
Laws about contacting the customer
If it is necessary to contact the customer during research, a member of the
relationship team should make the contact. Avoid “tipping off” the customer
by accidentally or deliberately disclosing to any person information that might
prejudice a financial crime investigation. In many jurisdictions, tipping off is a
criminal offense for which a person can be fined or even imprisoned.
Disclosing information that is already publicly known is not considered tipping
off. For example, when an organization is confirming whether a customer is on
a publicly accessible sanctions list, the customer already is or could be aware
of that list.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 44
Anyone who contacts a customer must be trained and must fully understand
the sensitivity and responsibility of not tipping off. For this and other good
reasons, contact is usually best made by someone with an established
relationship, such as frontline staff. AFC staff can prepare and coach the
frontline staff through these contacts.
Regulations and laws vary by jurisdiction. They include the protection of data
used and stored. In every situation, you must thoroughly document your work,
and treat those records as secure, confidential information.
Never inform customers that they are part of an AFC investigation. Suspicious
activity reports (SAR) are especially confidential, so your organization will have
a related policy and process. Generally, your organization must not mention
anything concerning a SAR, including that you are considering filing, or that
you have filed.
Sanctions lists and laws are public records. Sanctions legislation generally
does not prohibit organizations from telling sanctions targets that their assets
have been frozen, or about other sanctions-related questions or actions.
However, because transactions involving sanctions targets could also involve
an AML element, organizations should have controls in place to maintain
confidentiality and avoid tipping off about that other portion of an
investigation.
Data security and privacy
Financial institutions have a high duty of care, and often legal obligation, to
ensure the security and privacy of data they hold about their customers. Data
you hold about a customer should be shared only with others who need to
know and have the requisite permission and authority to view it. It must be
stored securely. In many jurisdictions, it is prohibited by law for data collected
for one purpose to be used for another purpose, such as marketing. Once it
has served its purpose, data must be securely destroyed. Many countries have
rules and regulations about how long data should be retained. Your
organization will have a policy on how long data should be stored and when
data should be destroyed.
Many countries have specific national privacy or data security laws, including
laws and regulations that apply to financial institutions. One of the strictest is
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 45
the EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) for how organizations
protect EU citizens' personal data and privacy.
These laws place challenging requirements on companies that collect data.
They may require the consent of the subject individual to process their data. In
some instances, data may not be transferred across national borders, which
present specific requirements for global financial services organizations.
These laws also have strict requirements for notifying the authorities and the
individuals affected if a data breach occurs.
The data that is collected is comprehensive and often purposeful. Care must
be taken to ensure physical data is properly protected. It should not be left on
an employee’s desk overnight. When stored electronically, data must be
entered into your organization’s approved databases in accordance with
policy. Some organizations prohibit the use of desktop folders. Most
organizations also prohibit the use of USB sticks for storing data as these are
easily lost. Data should not be retained indefinitely and must be destroyed in
accordance with your organization’s destruction policy.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 46
Customer identification and
research in sanctions compliance
Levels of due diligence
Due diligence is the process of establishing and verifying the identity of
customers, assessing the risks associated with the customers and their
transactions, and ensuring compliance with applicable regulations. Due
diligence aims to protect organizations from potential risks, financial crimes,
and regulatory non-compliance. To apply due diligence, you must conduct
thorough research, gather relevant information, and verify the identity and
background of individuals or entities involved in a business relationship or
transaction.
Different levels of due diligence exist due to varying levels of risk. These
include customer due diligence (CDD), enhanced due diligence (EDD), and
simplified due diligence (SDD).
The levels of due diligence are:
• Customer due diligence (CDD): This is sometimes referred to as standard
due diligence. CDD involves a comprehensive review of customer
information including identity verification, financial background, beneficial
ownership, and negative news screening. CDD is typically applied to most
customers to ensure they meet general compliance and risk management
standards. In some jurisdictions, the identification and verification of the
customer and the beneficial owners is known as the customer
identification process (CIP).
• Enhanced due diligence (EDD): This is conducted for high-risk customers
or transactions that require a more in-depth review. This level involves
additional scrutiny, such as validating source of wealth and source of funds,
identifying the purpose of the relationship, and assessing the risks of
potential politically exposed person (PEP) connections.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 47
• Simplified due diligence (SDD): This is a streamlined version of due
diligence used in some jurisdictions for customers who are considered low
risk. The acceptance of SDD varies based on the jurisdiction and regulatory
requirements. Some jurisdictions may have stricter regulations that
demand a more robust due diligence approach for all customers,
regardless of risk.
Customer identification process for a
natural person
The basic information required to identify a customer who is a natural person is:
• Name
• Address
• Date of birth
• Tax Identification Number or National Identification Number, which can help
you determine the customer’s nationality
The requirements of identifying a customer vary by jurisdiction, so consult your
local regulations.
Your organization’s customer identification process (sometimes called CIP)
should reflect the different customer types and the different levels of risk they
could pose to your organization. After you collect the basic required
information, you should be able to make an initial assessment of the customer’s
risk profile. For higher risk customers, your organization might require additional
information such as a prior bank reference or source of wealth. Higher-risk
relationships will also require greater scrutiny of documents. Keep in mind that
some identification documents are more vulnerable to fraud than others.
When applicable, your organization should apply equally effective customer
identification procedures for those who open accounts online as for face-to-
face customers. Your organization should verify this information using reliable,
independently sourced documents and information. You need to be as
confident and accurate as is reasonably possible given the available resources.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 48
Customer identification process for a
natural person (Case example: Alisher
Uspanov)
Alisher Uspanov is a prospective retail banking customer at Capital Bank.
During the customer identification process, his name is flagged as a potentially
valid name match on the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) Specially
Designated Nationals (SDN) list. The screening team does additional research
to determine if it is a true match to the SDN list. The identifying information is
similar, so the case is escalated for further research. The investigator
determines which sanctions are relevant to the case and whether any
sanctions breaches have occurred. The bank does not discover any breach
but records the case and reports its findings to OFAC.
The screening team reviews the alert to establish whether this is a true match
with a sanctioned individual. They discover that the OFAC SDN is named
Alisher Usmanov while Capital Bank’s prospective customer is Alisher
Uspanov. The names are not an exact match, but they are very close, so the
team compares other identifying details. They find that Alisher Uspanov and
the SDN were both born in Uzbekistan, but in different years: in 1955 and 1953,
respectively. Their residential addresses are different but there are still
enough similarities to escalate the case to the investigations team. The team
ultimately concludes there is no true match. They do not find any evidence of
sanctions breaches, so they close the case and record their findings.
Given that Capital Bank considers Uzbekistan a high-risk jurisdiction for
financial crime purposes, it decides to turn down Alisher Uspanov’s request for
an account with its retail banking division. Although the individual was
determined to be distinct from the SDN entry to which he had similarities, the
bank nonetheless reports its findings to OFAC.
Key takeaways
• The retail banking line of business is vulnerable to sanctions risk because of
sanctioned individuals.
• Banks should have procedures to screen potential customers against
sanctions lists.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 49
• Banks should determine whether matches are exact and take further
action when needed.
• Banks should keep records of their decisions and report when necessary.
Customer identification process for a legal
person
Legal person customers include limited liability companies, corporations,
partnerships, and financial institutions.
The basic information required to identify a legal person is:
• Name of the company
• Address
o Place of incorporation
o Place of business
• Identification number issued by government
The organization’s procedures will determine what to do when certain
documentation is not available. This could ultimately include not onboarding
the client if alternatives are not available.
It's important to note that the underlying principles of customer identification
for natural persons have equal application to customer identification for legal
persons. In addition to the minimum requirements, you might need to collect
additional documentation for higher risk customers. Examples include an
original or certified copy of the Certificate of Incorporation and Memorandum,
or Articles of Association. Make sure you know and follow the requirements for
your jurisdiction and organization.
You will also need to know the nature and purpose of the business, and
whether it is legitimate.
Additionally, your organization should take reasonable steps to verify the
identity and reputation of any agent that opens an account on behalf of a
corporate customer, unless that agent is an officer of the corporate
customer.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 50
As with natural person identification, your organization should verify this
information using reliable, independently sourced documents and
information. You need to be as confident and accurate as is reasonably
possible given the resources you have available.
Customer identification process for a legal
person (Case example: Borrower X)
Borrower X, a well-known company in Southeast Asia, is seeking a loan from a
bank to acquire Group Y. During the customer identification process, the bank
requests the necessary documents to confirm Borrower X’s identity. During
the due diligence process, the bank discovers that Borrower X might be
controlled by a sanctioned entity.
The bank finds open-source intelligence that Borrower X is a special purpose
vehicle for another entity that is listed on the Office of Foreign Assets Control
(OFAC) Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list and on the UK’s sanctions list.
The bank is unsure if its evidence is correct, so it decides to conduct
enhanced due diligence (EDD). The bank ultimately rejects Borrower X as a
customer.
Newspaper reports in the non-mainstream media suggest that Borrower X is
a special purpose vehicle acting on behalf of an Indonesian oil firm, which is an
OFAC SDN.
However, despite consulting various screening providers, the compliance
team cannot find enough information about Borrower X to determine
whether the reports are accurate.
The team requests a certified copy of the certificate of incorporation. Then,
they verify the authenticity of corporate documents and assess the legitimacy
of the business operation. The team notices that the place of incorporation
and business are similar to one of the Indonesian oil firm's production
subsidiaries.
The compliance team decides to conduct EDD. This includes scrutinizing the
corporate affiliations, contracts, and business relationships of Borrower X. The
objective is to identify any indicators of involvement in illicit activities, sanctions
violations, or reputational risk.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 51
Next, the bank evaluates the sanctions risk associated with lending to
Borrower X. This assessment considers the identified potential sanctions risk
that it might be controlled by an SDN.
After it receives the final report, the bank makes an informed decision to
reject Borrower X as a customer because of its high sanctions exposure.
Key takeaways
To reduce sanctions risk:
• Conduct thorough due diligence on legal persons before onboarding
them.
• Identify and evaluate any potential links to sanctioned entities.
• Follow a risk-based approach when onboarding potential customers.
EDD for a natural person
The amount of enhanced due diligence, or EDD, that you conduct for a natural
person will vary by the risk category. You should use what you already know
about a customer to research further. Since EDD can be labor-intensive, it
should be conducted on your high-risk customers. In a risk-based approach,
not all customers will need EDD. Regulators only require that you do a
reasonable job, so it’s important to use your best judgment. Your organization
will have a policy on EDD and procedures that include additional information
about the customer, such as a better understanding of source of wealth or
source of funds.
EDD is intended to be very broad. This is where you look for additional, in-
depth information, not just confirming what you already know. Don’t just look
directly at the customer, but indirectly, from various angles, to best
understand the inherent risks.
Examples of what to look for include but are not limited to:
• Related parties, indirect links to politically exposed persons, or other high-
risk individuals or entities
• Litigation, political, or regulatory risks
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 52
• Overall reputation and integrity
• Source of wealth and required supporting documents
Some of the things you will find might push you in a new direction to research.
Consider the following scenarios:
• There might be some litigation attached to an individual linked to the
customer which you might not have been aware of.
• A customer, while not a PEP himself, might be related by marriage to a PEP,
such as the ruling family of a country.
• The customer might not have any negative news, but might be linked to a
business associate who has a controversial reputation.
• Consider name variations. Maybe the name was transcribed from Russian
into English, so it has various spellings. Might your customer be registered in
various countries under those different spellings?
• Source of wealth is important. How did the customer accumulate the
wealth that he currently possesses? Once you determine where the
wealth comes from, you can request the necessary supporting
documents.
EDD for a legal person
Enhanced due diligence, or EDD, for a legal person requires you to extend
your knowledge of the nature of your customer, the customer’s business, and
the type of transactions that will pass across the account. It is a combination of
further fact-finding and verifying the information already disclosed by the
customer. Your organization will have policies and procedures for the
activities you must undertake. For example, they might include a search of
corporate registries for the history of the customer’s directors. They might
also include a search of local court databases, and local or international press
archives.
EDD investigations adopt the risk-based approach, use all reasonable sources
to satisfy local regulatory requirements, and provide relevant knowledge for
an informed business decision.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 53
EDD to determine the beneficial owners and controllers of your customer can
be challenging. Secrecy laws in some jurisdictions do not require disclosure of
the shareholders at the stage of registering a corporation. You must be as
certain as you can be that you have identified the ultimate beneficial owners
and controllers correctly and documented your searches properly. Make
inquiries if you discover the customer is a shell company. What is its purpose?
Will the owners and controllers change? Not all shell companies are used for
financial crime; they could have a legitimate purpose such as a trust-related
activity.
Find out about your customer’s customers. What products or services does
the customer sell? Is it domestic or international? Is payment immediate, in
small amounts, and in cash? Will the customer be supplying a big contract that
will be paid by bank transfer every three months?
What products will the customer take from your organization? Remember
some products are at higher risk than others. Do the products your customer
says it will use match its business purpose?
EDD for a legal person (Case example:
MarcoPolo Ltd.)
A bank in Germany is examining the onboarding application of MarcoPolo Ltd.
for the use of trade finance services. The company is involved in the trade of
semiconductors in Europe and is listed on the Frankfurt Stock Exchange. Sixty
percent of the company's shares are available for trading by the general
public. The remaining shares are held by a well-known fund, L2. The bank's
AML risk methodology considers the prospective client as high-risk for two
reasons. First, they are involved in the trade of controlled goods. Second, their
major shareholder, L2, is 30% owned by a sanctioned individual. Therefore,
enhanced due diligence (EDD) is applicable during onboarding and for
transaction monitoring.
The sanctioned individual indirectly holds only 12% in the company. Yet, the
German bank requests a shareholding structure to ensure that no other
identifiable sanctioned interest exists for either L2 or the free float. No red
flags are identified.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 54
CDD is performed on four declared clients of MarcoPolo Ltd. All of them are
legal entities registered in Europe. There is a lot of information available on the
internet that supports their legitimacy as businesses.
The client is onboarded on the assumption that all trade finance transactions
will be thoroughly screened against sanctions and controlled goods lists, the
end-users are all located in Europe, and all necessary licenses have or will
have been obtained.
Four months into its onboarding, the customer presents trade documents
involving a new buyer. It is a company based in Malta. The bank applies EDD for
this new counterparty due to the lack of transaction history. It discovers that
the new buyer has no offices and only one employee, while the trade
documents indicate a forwarding company as the end user. Further due
diligence reveals that this new buyer is owned by a Russian national who
relocated to Malta. He previously worked for a defense company in Russia.
The bank refuses to proceed with the transaction and submits a suspicious
activity report (SAR) to the financial intelligence unit (FIU). This triggers a new
review for MarcoPolo Ltd.
Key takeaways
• EDD is necessary for high-risk trade finance clients during onboarding,
transaction monitoring, and ongoing reviews.
• Obtain details on ownership structure, major suppliers, and buyers to
establish the geographical aspects of the supply chain.
• Trade documents should be checked for originator and end user;
necessary licenses should be obtained.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 55
Who reads the KYC customer profile, and
why?
The end result of your KYC research is to produce a customer profile that will
clearly identify the customer, the nature and intended purpose of the
account, the source of funds, and other customer information useful in your
customer risk rating model. In the Assess step, you need to know who your
intended audience is, and what they need to know. There are multiple
audiences that you need to satisfy.
Know your target audience, and what they need to know.
Regulators will review KYC files to determine whether you are complying with
your jurisdiction’s laws and regulations and whether you are collecting enough
information to enable your organization to effectively detect and identify
suspicious activity.
Other government officials may look at your work.
Law enforcement might evaluate your work. When your organization files a
suspicious activity report (SAR or STR), that often uses vital contextual
information collected during KYC due diligence. Law enforcement has access
to all of these reports from the various organizations and a single SAR may
often provide the missing piece to an investigation.
Your organization’s transaction monitoring staff will rely on your work. So
perhaps the most important purpose of KYC is to produce documentation
that others within your organization can use throughout the lifecycle of the
customer relationship.
Finally, your customer files may be independently reviewed for quality by
internal staff in your organization’s lines of business, dedicated compliance
function, or audit function. They will confirm that you did enough research and
that you followed appropriate policies and procedures.

Beneficial Ownership
Introduction
Setup: The art enthusiast
You work in the main German branch of a global bank. You are a KYC analyst,
supporting a relationship manager. Your department specializes in wealth
management, a sector known for its confidentiality, big-ticket transactions,
and the intricate financial needs of its customers.
One of your new customers is a man by the name of Bernard Dupont. He is a
Parisian enthusiast of fine art. He spends much of his time adding to his large
art collection. Bernard asks the relationship manager to help him finance the
purchase of a US$2 million impressionist painting under the bank’s fine art
lending program. The painting has recently been offered for sale by a
reputable art gallery in London. The gallery acts as an intermediary, selling the
artwork on behalf of its client, Trinity Limited.
You are eager to help your relationship manager with this important customer.
However, during routine due diligence, you identify a concerning connection.
A recent widely publicized data leak from a Cayman Islands-based trust and
company services provider (TCSP) indicates that Trinity Limited’s ownership
has been structured through two offshore companies. One of these
companies appears to be wholly owned, and the other appears to be
informally controlled, by EU-sanctioned Russian oligarchs with close ties to
Russia’s government. Curiously enough, the two oligarchs happen to be
brothers.
According to the TCSP records leaked to the media, the two offshore
companies have had stakes of 25% and 27%, respectively, in Trinity Limited.
This adds up to 52% aggregate ownership. However, the documents showing
this ownership structure are 21 months old. You know that the EU’s aggregate
ownership rule applies. If Trinity Limited is currently 50% or more owned by
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 57
entities that are 50% or more owned or controlled each by two Russian
individuals sanctioned by the EU, it is also subject to the same sanctions.
You recall from your recent sanctions training that Russian oligarchs
frequently use offshore structures to fund purchases of luxury assets, such as
valuable artwork. They also use these structures to evade western sanctions
by obscuring their ownership and control.
What would you do?
What is your next step?
• Ask your legal department.
• Ask Mr. Dupont for more information.
• Apply for a license from the German sanctions regulator.
• Offboard Mr. Dupont.
Feedback
The best response is to request further information from Bernard Dupont to
complete enhanced due diligence on Trinity Limited, using up-to-date data.
You would do this through the relationship manager. It is possible that the
ownership structure has changed in the past 21 months. Your goal is to
determine if persons sanctioned by the EU currently own 50% or more of
Trinity Limited, individually or in the aggregate, or have joint control over it. If
so, Trinity Limited would be subject to an asset freeze.
Asking your legal department for advice might not be helpful at this time. You
need to have a current snapshot of Trinity Limited’s ownership structure
before doing so.
Applying for a license from the German sanctions regulator would be
premature at this stage. You first need to establish Trinity Limited’s ownership
to determine whether it is subject to EU sanctions.
Initiating the offboarding of Bernard Dupont is not recommended at this
stage. The customer hasn’t yet entered into any transaction with Trinity
Limited and may be genuinely unaware of potential sanctions exposure.

Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Define beneficial ownership and explain why it is important.
• Describe the difference between ownership and control.
• Calculate beneficial ownership based on OFAC, EU, and UK rules.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
