Page 59
Identifying beneficial ownership
The importance of identifying beneficial
ownership
Criminals employ different strategies and schemes to evade detection and
optimize their profits from illicit operations. Bad actors often try to avoid
detection so they can enjoy their illicit gains. To combat financial crimes, it
becomes essential to track the movement of illicit funds and to accurately
identify the beneficial owners of assets and funds. In addition, identifying
beneficial ownership functions as a protective measure for financial
institutions. It can shield them from substantial risks and the potential for
severe consequences. By establishing robust mechanisms for identifying
beneficial ownership, stakeholders can fortify their defenses against financial
crimes.
The Financial Action Task Force (FATF) has recognized the importance of
beneficial ownership identification and has incorporated it into its
recommendations. FATF Recommendation 24 and 25, along with its Guidance
on Transparency and Beneficial Ownership, provide a framework for handling
transparency in relation to beneficial ownership.
It is risky to rely solely on surface-level information about transactional parties'
identities and asset ownership. Failing to prioritize this task can lead to legal,
operational, and reputational risks for the financial institution. Therefore, it is
crucial for the financial institution to recognize the importance of beneficial
ownership and establish a robust system to fulfill this obligation, considering
the fast-paced and globally complex nature of their daily transactions.
While beneficial ownership identification is commonly associated with AML
operations, its significance extends to sanction compliance as well. Breaching
global sanctions is considered an illegal activity. Therefore, financial institutions
should conduct enhanced due diligence when any suspicion arises during the
initial due diligence process. In addition, determining beneficial ownership
allows for a comprehensive risk assessment of customers and their
transactions. It enables the identification of higher-risk individuals or entities
associated with financial crimes.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 60
The following are some examples of triggering events that deserve careful
consideration:
• Changes in beneficial ownership, shareholding, or directorship
• The nationality of relevant individuals
• Activity in high-risk jurisdictions
• Involvement in export-controlled activity
FATF's transparency obligations emphasize the significance of beneficial
ownership identification. These obligations require countries to ensure that
accurate and up-to-date beneficial ownership information is accessible to
competent authorities, financial institutions, and other relevant parties. By
fulfilling these obligations, countries enhance transparency, deter financial
crimes, and strengthen the effectiveness of AML and sanctions measures
globally.
Beneficial ownership
The term beneficial owner, when used to refer to an account in an AFC
context, is generally understood to mean the person or persons having
ultimate control over the funds in that account. This can be through ownership
or other means.
The term, control, is important. An account can be in one name while control
over that account might be with someone else. If a person is opening an
account and you discover through your account opening process that she
represents another person, you are obligated to ask further about the identity
of the other person.
Often beneficial ownership is determined by the percentage of control. The
most straightforward example is the percentage ownership within a
company. For AFC purposes, most jurisdictions require that beneficial
ownership be collected at a threshold of 25% or more. That means you need
to know every customer who owns at least 25% of a company. Your
organization will set the appropriate threshold. Sometimes, for high-risk
customers, the beneficial ownership threshold is as low as 10%.
You must also identify your customer’s ultimate beneficial owners. The
difference is that a regular beneficial owner could be either a natural person
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 61
or a legal person. Ultimate beneficial owners are always natural persons. That
is why regulators require you to know the ultimate beneficial owners. You
must identify the ultimate beneficial owners before establishing a formal
business relationship and certainly before transacting on the account.
The ultimate beneficial ownership regulations seek out the natural persons
because legal persons can be easily and quickly formed, restructured, and
dissolved, and assets can be moved in and out quickly. Behind it all, though, is a
natural person who wants those assets.
Your organization must establish and maintain written procedures that are
reasonably designed to identify and verify the ultimate beneficial owners of
legal entity customers.
Ultimate beneficial ownership
There are two distinct types of beneficial owners: those with ownership and
those with control. The determination of ownership thresholds varies
depending on the jurisdiction. In the context of AFC, some jurisdictions have
specific ownership percentage thresholds to define beneficial ownership,
such as 25% or more ownership or control. A "controller" refers to an individual
or entity that exercises significant control over the operations, decision-
making, or financial affairs of a legal entity. This is often called the control
prong.
Sometimes there are layers of legal entities in a structure. However, all legal
persons ultimately track back to a natural person or persons. These natural
persons are the ultimate beneficial owners (UBO).
Some jurisdictions expect ultimate beneficial owners holding 10% or more to
be identified.
It is important you know your organization’s policy on the threshold for
identifying ultimate beneficial ownership, which could be as low as 10%.
UBOs are the people who benefit from the activities of a legal person or a
group of legal persons. The vast majority of UBOs are legitimate, but bad
actors will try to hide behind complex structures to disguise their criminal
activities and perpetrate financial crime.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 62
In the US, if an ultimate beneficial owner cannot be found under the
ownership or control prong, then one or more qualifying senior managing
officials should be recorded as UBOs.
The European Union adopts a similar approach. If all possible means of
discovering the UBO have been exhausted, then the natural person or
persons holding the position of senior managing officials are considered to be
the UBOs.
In general, where it proves impossible to identify the UBOs, a red flag is raised.
You must ask yourself why a UBO might want to hide in this way, and you must
be confident that the controllers are properly identified and are not colluding
in financial crime.
Beneficial ownership: Legal arrangements
Risks are generally higher if a criminal can hide behind corporate vehicles,
such as private investment vehicles or shell companies, where it is difficult to
identify the beneficial owners of these entities. These legal arrangements may
serve to obscure the links between the proceeds of a crime and the
perpetrator.
For example, a sole trader business, also called a sole proprietor, might not
provide much data because in some jurisdictions those entities don’t have to
register with the government.
Foundations, nongovernmental organizations, and charities are sometimes in
a formal government register. Learn what details each country captures. If
there is no registry requirement, you’ll have to carefully research who is
involved.
Care must be taken when accepting customers with complex legal
arrangements, particularly if they operate in jurisdictions with secrecy laws.
An example where a complex legal arrangement might be used to disguise
the beneficial owner is a trust. Trusts have settlors. The settlor is the person
who sets up the trust and puts assets in it. Trusts also have beneficiaries. The
beneficiary is the person for whom the trust is set up. This is usually because
they are unable to manage the trust assets for themselves—either because
they are too young, or they are not good at managing their own money. The
assets held in trust are held for the beneficiary's benefit.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 63
The result is different persons might own, benefit from, and control the trust,
depending on the applicable trust law, and the provisions of the document
establishing the trust. In some countries, trust law allows for the settlor and
beneficiary, and sometimes even the trustee to be the same person. This is a
method for the beneficial owner of the assets to conceal their identity.
To be sure you know your customer, it is important you identify the natural
person or persons at the end of the chain.
Beneficial ownership: Legal arrangements
(Case example: Oligarch)
A Russian oligarch, with a history of corruption and money laundering, utilizes a
complex scheme involving multiple companies and a trust to hide his
beneficial ownership of high-value assets. The scheme involves transferring
ownership interests to family members to evade future sanctions. The
scheme is put together with the help of a senior executive of a professional
service provider (PSP), who previously assisted the oligarch with managing his
wealth under his own name. A PSP is an individual or organization that offers
specialized expertise, skills, and knowledge in a particular field to assist
customers in achieving their goals or solving specific problems, such as estate
planning or asset protection.
To help the oligarch hide his beneficial ownership, the senior executive of the
PSP establishes another company called CSP2. CSP2 will hold legal ownership
of the shares of a holding company called HC Ltd. on behalf of a trust known
as the Atlantic Trust. Initially, the oligarch is the sole beneficiary of the Atlantic
Trust. However, he soon has the beneficiary changed from himself to his
children. The change is intentional because the oligarch expects to be added
to sanctions lists in several jurisdictions, such as the US, EU, and UK.
In total, HC Ltd. owns 11 offshore companies that hold considerable amounts
of highly-valued publicly traded stocks. These stocks are used as collateral for
significant loans obtained from various western banks. These loans are then
used for engaging in multiple transactions among the offshore companies
that have no clear business purpose or justification. For example, one of the
companies obtains from another a substantial interest-free loan of US$500
million, while a third company's annual report states that it has no obligation to
repay the debts it has contracted. The only purpose of these complex
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 64
transactions is to move funds from jurisdiction to jurisdiction to shield
oligarch-controlled assets from potential seizure.
Key takeaways
• Trusts are frequently used as vehicles for hiding beneficial ownership.
• KYC is important for untangling complex ownership structures.
Shell company risk
A shell company or corporation is a company that, at the time of
incorporation, has no significant assets or operations. A similarly named "shelf"
company is a corporation that has had no activity. It has been created and put
"on the shelf" so that it can be sold later to someone who prefers a previously
registered corporation over a new one. Both shell and shelf companies are
generally kept dormant and used later to appear legitimate while masking the
beneficial owner. A front company is an entity that conducts some legitimate
business while also shielding another company from liability or scrutiny.
Financial criminals might use a front company to conceal illicit activity. For
example, they might operate a car wash to launder the profits of drug
trafficking. While there are legitimate uses for shell, shelf, and front
companies, within the context of researching and accepting customers, they
are considered high risk.
Shell companies can be established with the primary objective of claiming the
proceeds of crime as legitimate revenue or commingling criminal proceeds
with legitimate revenue. According to the Financial Action Task Force (FATF),
the use of shell companies to facilitate financial crime is a well-documented
typology.
Shell companies can be set up in onshore and offshore locations.
Their ownership structures can take several forms:
• Shares can be issued to a natural or legal person in registered or bearer
form.
• Some shell companies can be created for a single purpose or to hold a
single asset.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 65
• Some shell companies can be established as multipurpose entities.
Shell companies are often legally incorporated and registered by the criminal
organization but have no legitimate business purpose. Often purchased from
lawyers, accountants, or non-reputable corporate service providers, they are
convenient vehicles for bribery and corruption, money laundering, and
sanctions evasion.
Sometimes, the stock of these shell corporations is issued in bearer shares,
which means that whoever carries them is the purported owner. Tax haven
countries and their strict secrecy laws can further conceal the true ownership
of shell corporations. In addition, the information may be held by professionals
who claim secrecy.
When FATF reviewed the rules and practices that impair the effectiveness of
financial crime prevention and detection systems, it found in particular that
shell corporations and nominees are widely used mechanisms to launder the
proceeds from crime. As a result, shell companies are considered to
represent a higher risk of financial crime.
Shell company risk (Case example: Hidden
Ltd.)
Hidden Ltd. was established in Panama one month after the commencement
of the war in Ukraine and the imposition of new sanctions on Russia. It opens
an account with a US bank, and has a capital of just US$100. The company has
a nominee shareholder and a nominee director. Both of them are employees
of a Panamanian law firm that provides fiduciary services. The declared
beneficial owner as disclosed to the US bank is Andrey Brolov. He is a 25-year-
old Russian individual who recently graduated from university.
Just a week after onboarding, Hidden Ltd. receives an incoming payment of
US$10 million. The payment represents a loan from Concealment Ltd., a
company registered in the British Virgin Islands. The bank temporarily
suspends Hidden Ltd.’s account to conduct due diligence.
When the US$10 million loan is received, the bank asks the customer to
provide information regarding the relationship between the two companies
and the purpose of the loan. In response, the customer states that the
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 66
beneficial owners of both companies are business associates, and the loan
was granted to Hidden Ltd. for the purpose of purchasing a yacht from a third
company, Veil Ltd.
The bank initiates an investigation into the ownership of the sender,
Concealment Ltd. The bank discovers that the real owner is concealed behind
a complex corporate structure.
The bank conducts further investigation to determine the owner of Veil Ltd.,
which again reveals a convoluted ownership structure. Upon searching for the
specific yacht on the internet, the bank discovers that before the war in
Ukraine it was frequently used by Russian oligarch Mikhail Rublev’s family.
Based on these findings, the bank suspects that all the companies involved
are controlled by Rublev. It appears that these companies were created with
the intention of hiding the oligarch’s link to the yacht. It is highly likely that
Brolov, the declared owner of Hidden Ltd., is merely a front man. The bank
decides to keep the account frozen, files a suspicious activity report, and
contacts law enforcement.
Key takeaways
• Shell companies are sometimes set up to hide assets of criminals or
designated parties.
• Nominee shareholders and directors may conceal the true owners.
• Shell companies can be funded by loans from companies with obscure
ownership structures.
Beneficial ownership: How to research
According to Financial Action Task Force Recommendation 10, an institution
should take reasonable steps to verify the identity of beneficial owners.
However, the exact account-opening procedures and customer acceptance
policies will depend on the type of customer, the risk, and the local
regulations.
Many jurisdictions have moved to a centralized registry. Make sure you know
and follow the requirements for your jurisdiction.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 67
There is debate in a number of jurisdictions whether an organization can rely
on representations made by the potential customer—the US explicitly allows
such reliance—for both the beneficial owners and the controllers. Your
organization may rely on the potential customer’s certified representation as
long as reliance is reasonable and the organization believes the customer is
truthful.
So now you have a list of beneficial owners, but in some jurisdictions you must
go further to verify the identities of those customers. The exact requirements
for verification will be based on your organization’s uniquely tailored risk-
based approach.
Internet search: Government corporate
registry online databases
Online databases maintained by government corporate registries are very
useful sources of information in that they compile legally required information
on legal entities. If your customer is a legal person engaged in financial
services, the government’s database will show their status. Unlike general,
open-source searches that yield whatever the search engine acquires, these
databases often include requirements on the type of information that is
solicited from users and made public. For example, they could be a financial
intermediary, or they could be a fully authorized bank. Understanding their
status will help you determine the activity they might undertake.
Government corporate registry databases also show the status of “approved”
or “authorized” individuals. They are useful verification sources for checking an
individual customer’s status.
Government corporate registry databases also include their current guidance.
This can be helpful for checking what you need to do in a certain situation, or
to check rules in another country.
Government corporate registry databases are especially useful for
determining the exact status of a financial services firm. These databases can
be used to verify whether the information a potential customer or
counterparty has provided is credible. For example, a limited liability company
might be registered through its state division of corporations, but the
information of record is the registering agent for the company, not the
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 68
owners or controllers themselves. They can usually be searched using very
simple terms. They can indicate whether a beneficial owner was previously
cited based on conduct, such as being banned from trading securities. Also,
the databases provide information that can link a legal entity customer back
to an actual legal person. The permissions granted to a firm, such as mortgage
brokering, deposit taking, or trading in the securities markets will help you
verify the activity you should expect.
Depending on the database, information on when the company was founded,
changes to shareholders, and whether the business is active, inactive, or
dissolved can be found. This becomes particularly relevant if a potential
customer or counterparty is relying on a legal entity as a front or shell
company. If the information they provide does not appear in or does not
match the information found in a database, it could be suggestive of potential
risk. Similarly, there might be information in the database that can be used to
confirm the legitimacy of the corporate entity.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 69
Calculating beneficial ownership
Ownership versus control
The ownership of a legal person is often described as having two prongs. The
first prong is the official or formal owner. This is the natural or legal person who
owns the entity and who benefits from its activities.
The second prong is the control prong. The formal owner of a company is not
always its controller. The controller has significant responsibility to control,
manage, or direct a legal entity customer.
In many cases the two prongs might be different people. A sole proprietorship
or sole trader would, of course, be both prongs.
You need to know the controllers of legal persons and legal arrangements so
you can measure risk.
For the first prong, various countries treat the beneficial ownership threshold
differently. Photocopies are acceptable for documentary methods in most
jurisdictions. This means that the ultimate beneficial owners do not need to
present themselves to the organization or send in their original documents.
The verification should follow the same process for verifying the identity of
your natural person customers. In the EU and UK, most beneficial owners are
likely to be people who hold 25% or more of the ownership or voting rights of
the entity. Even when they have a passive ownership role, if financial benefits
are being transferred to a sanctioned individual, the entire business can be
exposed to sanctions risk.
For the second prong, the control prong, an organization must collect the
identity of persons who exercise control over the company. This may include
the chief executive officer, chief operating officer, managing director, or
others. This might also be one of the ultimate beneficial owners identified
previously, who acts on a day-to-day basis for the others. The EU definition of
ultimate beneficial ownership is broadly the natural party owner of a legal
entity, or a person who benefits from that entity’s transactions. The controllers
could be sanctioned or could be known close associates of a sanctioned
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 70
person. In that case, there is a significant risk that they could use the legal
entity customer’s accounts to engage in sanctions evasion.
Legal persons often try to conceal their owners and controllers. They use
nominees or powers of attorney—especially in offshore companies,
foundations, and trusts. Keep in mind that the ultimate beneficial owner might
be hidden behind several nested companies.
Beneficial ownership: How to calculate
In the example, Company A is your customer. You need to do some
calculations in order to identify its beneficial owners and assess sanctions
exposure.
Your jurisdiction’s regulations, or your organization’s policies, will tell you the
level at which you must identify beneficial owners. In this example, your
organization uses the standard 25% threshold.
After getting to the uppermost level of natural Persons B, C, and D that
indirectly own Company A, you discover that both Persons B and C are
subject to sanctions. Ultimately, you find that Person C can be classified as a
UBO and that Company A is subject to sanctions.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 71
To identify UBO under most AML regulations, you should multiply ownership
stakes in cases of indirect ownership. For example, Person B owns an indirect
stake of 11.14% in Company A, via their indirect participation in the capital of
Companies 1 and 2 and their direct stake in Company 3. Using the same
method, we can calculate that Persons C and D indirectly own 26% and 20.7%
of Company A, respectively.
Given the 25% threshold, only Person C can be classified as a UBO. Neither
Person B nor Person D can be categorized as such.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 72
For the purposes of sanctions compliance, a different methodology must be
used. Instead of multiplication, each level of ownership should be looked at
separately. All of Companies 1, 2, and 3 are subject to sanctions because each
has minimum qualifying ownership by a sanctioned person. Therefore,
Company A is also subject to sanctions, even though Person B is not that
company’s UBO for AML purposes.
For Person C, even though Companies 4, 5, and 6 are all sanctioned, sanctions
against Person C do not translate into additional sanctions against Company A
because Company 4’s stake in it is just 26%.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 73
OFAC 50% rule
US sanctions regimes have specific requirements regarding the application of
sanctions restrictions to legal entities with owners that are sanctions targets.
The US Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) applies the 50% Rule to legal
entity ownership, whether direct or indirect. That is, if a sanctions target owns
50% or more of another legal entity, the legal entity is also subject to the
sanctions restrictions, even if it is not itself named as a sanctions target.
OFAC uses the 50% Rule to determine whether companies should be
considered sanctioned because their owner appears on the Specially
Designated Nationals and Blocked Persons, or SDN List.
The OFAC 50% rule
The OFAC 50% Rule also requires that sanctions restrictions be applied if
exactly 50% or more of an entity’s shares, or their equivalent, are owned by a
sanctioned target. For example, if sanctioned person A owns 55% of company
B’s shares, company B is also subject to sanctions, even though company B
does not appear on the SDN list.
The 50% Rule does not simply apply to the ownership of a single company. It
also stipulates that beneficial ownership is based on the total, or aggregate,
number of shares that sanctioned targets own across a corporate structure.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 74
This rule prevents sanctions targets from creating complex corporate
structures in which they spread their ownership out so that they don’t hold
50% or more of any one corporate entity. If two or more sanctioned persons’
aggregate ownership totals at least 50% of an entity, that entity is considered
to be sanctioned.
For example, Lion Corporation is owned by three entities. These are called:
Hyena, Giraffe, and Hippo. Between them, these entities own 100% of Lion
Corporation, with Giraffe owning 16%, Hyena owning 39%, and Hippo being the
major shareholder with 45%.
If Hyena and Giraffe are both designated by OFAC as SDNs, then Lion
Corporation itself is considered to be automatically sanctioned under the
OFAC 50% Rule. This is because Hyena’s 39%, combined with Giraffe’s 16%,
makes a combined total of 55% sanctioned ownership, going above the 50%
threshold. This is the case even if the major shareholder, Hippo, is not
sanctioned.
The 50% Rule presents numerous compliance challenges, as entities that are
50% or more owned by sanctioned actors can be owned through dynamic,
indirect ownership structures spread across multiple jurisdictions.
For this reason, it is never enough to simply screen the name of a legal entity
that is your customer. Sanctions due diligence requires verifying whether that
customer is part of a larger structure in which a sanctioned target may, in
aggregate, directly or indirectly hold 50% or more ownership.
EU and UK 50% rule
Under EU and UK sanctions regulations, the applicability of sanctions to non-
designated entities is determined by the 50% rule, which requires beneficial
ownership or control of 50% or more for the EU, or more than 50% for the UK.
Ownership can be measured by the percentage of proprietary rights, while
control can be more complex, involving factors such as board control, voting
rights, or an ability to exercise influence. Financial institutions should conduct
thorough due diligence to identify instances where a sanctioned individual or
entity controls another entity and gather appropriate documentation that
explains the nature of that control. This ensures compliance with regulations
and helps prevent sanctionable dealings with non-sanctioned entities.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 75
According to the EU's 50% rule, if an entity is 50% or more owned or controlled
by a sanctioned individual or entity, it is also subject to sanctions. The legal
threshold in the UK is more than 50%. Control encompasses various factors,
including holding 50% or more in the EU, or more than 50% in the UK, of voting
rights, having the right to appoint a majority of the board of directors, exerting
dominant influence, utilizing the entity's assets, and managing the business on
a unified basis.
In the UK, the Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation provides common
examples of control in its regularly updated general guidance for financial
sanctions. In the EU, frequent control scenarios are described in the guidelines
on implementation and evaluation of restrictive measures (sanctions) in the
framework of the EU common foreign and security policy. These guidelines
are issued by the general secretariat of the council.
For example, Mr. X, who is listed on the EU Sanctions Consolidated list, owns
25% of Company E. The rest belongs to Mr. Y, who is not sanctioned. However,
through proper due diligence, you find out that Mr. X exercises control over
Company A and Company B as sole director and CEO, respectively, and
exerts dominant influence over Trust C as protector. Consequently, Company
E should also be subject to EU sanctions.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 76
EU aggregate ownership
From 2022 to mid-2024, the EU’s aggregate ownership rule was specifically
applicable under the EU sanctions regime against Russia in relation to its
military aggression against Ukraine. It has since been extended to all EU
sanctions programs. This rule states that if an entity is 50% or more owned or
controlled in the aggregate by two or more listed persons and they are
simultaneously subject to EU blocking sanctions, that entity is also subject to
the same restrictions.
To correctly aggregate ownership, financial institutions should implement
enhanced due diligence (EDD) measures. This includes identifying all direct
and indirect shareholdings, or equivalent ownership interests, to determine if
an entity is 50% or more owned or controlled by several listed persons.
The EU aggregate ownership rule originally applied within the context of the
EU Sanctions Regulations on Russia, namely Council Regulation 269/2014 of
March 17, 2014 (as amended). Below are some examples to demonstrate this
rule.
Company A is 35% owned by Person X, the former speaker of the Russian
State Duma, and 25% owned through a complex legal structure by Person Y,
the mayor of Sevastopol. Both individuals are subject to an asset freeze under
Regulation 269/2014. Since Company A is 50% or more owned in the
aggregate by two listed persons, it is also subject to an asset freeze.
If Company A is 35% owned by Person X and 25% owned by Captain Moussa
Dadis Camara, who is subject to an asset freeze under Council Regulation
1284/2009 (Guinea Sanctions), Company A is also considered sanctioned
because the aggregate ownership rule currently applies under the entirety of
the EU’s sanctions regimes without distinction.
It is important to note that the rule does not apply under Council Regulation
833/2014, which incorporates sectoral sanctions, export controls, and similar
non-blocking restrictions against Russia. For example, if Company A is 35%
owned by Bank Rossiya, which is subject to blocking sanctions under
Regulation 269/2014, and 25% owned by Gazprombank, which is subject to
sectoral sanctions under Regulation 833/2014, the company is not subject to
any EU sanctions to the extent that no aggregation applies.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 77
Aggregate ownership rules US and EU (Case
example: A Turkish company)
A Turkish company maintains active bank accounts at a US bank and a
German bank. The company is 35% owned by a Russian national, 20% by an
Iranian national, and 45% by a Turkish national.
The Russian and Iranian nationals are designated in both the EU and US as
sponsors of a Russian-based drone factory whose production is used in the
Russian war against Ukraine. In the US, they are both put on OFAC’s Specially
Designated Nationals (SDN) List. In the EU, they are both listed on the EU's
Consolidated Sanctions List by virtue of Council Regulation 269/2014 with
regard to the Russian national and Council Regulation 2023/1529 with regard
to the Iranian national.
According to OFAC rules, the Turkish company is considered to have an
aggregate holding of 55% of SDNs—35% of the Russian SDN and 20% of the
Iranian SDN. As a result, the US bank proceeds to freeze the accounts of the
Turkish company since it meets the criteria of being 50% or more owned in
the aggregate by two SDNs. In this case, the aggregate holding is 55%.
Under EU rules, the two individuals are included on the EU Consolidated
Sanctions List, although through different legal instruments. The Russian
national is included pursuant to Council Regulation 269/2014, while the Iranian
national is included per Council Regulation 2023/1529. Prior to mid-2024, the
German bank would not have proceeded with the freezing of the Turkish
company's accounts since it is not 50% or more owned or controlled by
persons subject to an asset freeze under the same Russia-related legal
instrument. From mid-2024 onwards, the German bank must freeze the
accounts.
Moreover, the German bank decides to de-risk from this customer by closing
their accounts and terminating the business relationship. The bank's updated
terms and conditions contain a sanctions clause allowing for such termination.
In doing so, the German bank is careful to send the remaining balance to a
bank with no US nexus, as this would trigger a violation of US sanctions.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 78
Key takeaways
• Under OFAC rules, the aggregate rule applies even if the asset freeze
designations are under different sanctions programs.
• Under EU rules, the aggregate rule also applies if the asset freeze
designations are under different legal instruments, in line with updated EU
guidance.
OFAC, EU, and UK 50% rules (Case example:
Mr. Anderson)
When determining beneficial ownership, it is important to note that the US, EU,
and UK 50% rules are different. Under OFAC rules, only ownership is
considered when deciding whether US sanctions apply, whereas the EU and
UK rules consider both ownership and control.
Both OFAC and the EU set the threshold for beneficial ownership at 50% or
more, while the UK threshold requires ownership or control of more than 50%.
The UK is no longer a member of the EU and has its own autonomous
sanctions policies. However, the fundamental concept of ownership and
control remains consistent in both jurisdictions’ sanctions laws and
regulations.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 79
Mr. Anderson
The case of Mr. Anderson highlights the distinctions between OFAC, EU, and
UK 50% rules. Mr. Anderson is subject to asset freezes in the US, the EU, and
the UK.
From a US perspective:
• Company B is 50% or more owned by Mr. Anderson; Company B is subject
to OFAC sanctions.
• As the settlor of the Trust, Mr. Anderson is not considered as its owner.
Therefore, Company C is not subject to sanctions.
• Company D is ultimately owned by Mr. Anderson’s junior business partner,
not Mr. Anderson.
Company B’s 49% ownership stake in Company A is below the 50% threshold.
As a result, the bank account held by Company A is not a blocked asset.
From an EU or UK perspective:
• Company B is more than 50% owned by Mr. Anderson, which satisfies both
jurisdictions' requirements; Company B is subject to EU/UK sanctions.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 80
• Mr. Anderson can be considered as exercising de facto control over the
Trust as its settlor and a family member of its protector and beneficiary.
Company C is subject to an asset freeze.
• Mr. Anderson exercises control over Company D as sole director of
Company F. Company D is sanctioned.
Company A is fully controlled by Mr. Anderson, so that Company’s bank
account should be frozen.
Key takeaways
• Under the OFAC 50% rule, only ownership is considered.
• Under EU and UK 50% rules, both ownership and control are considered.
• Control can be established through directorships or other roles within a
legal entity or arrangement.
• Structures which might have been set up to circumvent sanctions should
be investigated.
Ultimate beneficial ownership: How to
reconcile AML and sanctions
When determining ultimate beneficial ownership for AML and sanctions
purposes, financial institutions need to align their due diligence procedures
because the requirements and objectives differ.
To effectively address both money laundering and sanctions risk, the
threshold for identifying owners or controllers of a legal entity, especially in
complex legal structures, should be set at a level that considers both aspects.
This is important because sanctions risk involves the possibility of listed
persons holding in aggregate 50% or more of a legal entity.
In high-risk situations, it might be necessary to set the threshold as low as 10%
or even 5%. This ensures comprehensive risk mitigation and effective
compliance with relevant regulations.
Financial institutions implement due diligence measures for AML purposes to
determine the beneficial owners of legal entities. The recommended
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 81
threshold is typically 25% as advised by the Financial Action Task Force (FATF).
However, financial institutions may choose a lower threshold if their local
regulations or internal rules require it.
This differs from the 50% rules mandated by sanctions regulations in the US,
EU, and UK to identify ownership by listed persons. Simply using the 50%
threshold is insufficient due to aggregate ownership requirements, at least in
the US and the EU. The aggregation rule aims to prevent listed persons from
evading sanctions by distributing shares across multiple owners, each with
less than a 50% stake. Thorough sanctions due diligence requires the
identification of the true owners of these complex structures.
To address both AML and sanctions requirements, financial institutions might
apply different thresholds based on the risk level of their customers. For low-
risk and moderate-risk clients, such as domestic entities with lean structures,
the 25% threshold might be sufficient. However, for higher-risk clients with
complex structures, especially if they are registered in low-disclosure foreign
jurisdictions, a threshold as low as 10% might be used.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
