Sanctions Screening
Policy and Process
Introduction
Setup: The vessel "Serena”
You’ve just started your job as a junior sanctions compliance officer at a global
financial institution. You excelled in your onboarding training, and now you
must make decisions about real-time transaction screenings. This new
challenge will test your ability to navigate the complex and evolving world of
AFC compliance.
Today, you receive an automated alert for a payment sent by a company in
Italy to your customer, a company in the UK. The companies themselves do
not raise any red flags, but the purpose of the payment catches your
attention. The payment is for maintaining an unnamed luxury yacht, which you
believe could have a link to a sanctioned individual. Understanding the
potential repercussions of indirectly aiding a designated person, you promptly
decide to confer with your manager.
As you discuss the matter, your manager reveals further concerning details.
Transaction monitoring data reveals that the UK company was engaged in a
series of transactions relating to a mega-yacht called “Serena” over a period
of about two years. Its owner is a Russian businessman who was sanctioned
three months after the last transaction by both the US Office of Foreign
Assets Control (OFAC) and the UK Office of Financial Sanctions
Implementation (OFSI). There is a good chance that the latest incoming
payment is also related to the vessel, Serena.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 83
What would you do?
What should you do next?
• Focus on potential liable individuals.
• Report a sanctions breach.
• Request information from the remitter’s bank.
• Have someone contact the sender in Italy.
Feedback
The best response is to request information from the remitter’s bank. By
submitting a request for information (RFI), you can find out additional
information about the unnamed vessel, such as the registration number. This
information will help you determine whether the transactions were for
maintenance of the vessel, Serena, or any other vessel with a sanctions
connection.
Focusing exclusively on potentially liable individuals is not the best response.
This could potentially distort the audit trail. It is crucial to document all
individuals involved in the transaction to maintain comprehensive records.
Reporting a sanctions breach is not the best response. Immediate reporting
to authorities is typically premature. However, if additional information
confirms the involvement of a sanctioned person, it may become necessary
to report the transaction as rejected.
Having someone contact the sender in Italy is not the best strategy. The Italian
company is not a customer of your bank, and you should not reach out to
them directly.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 84
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Discuss the importance of having a screening policy.
• Name the important sanctions lists and how they are used.
• Explain the screening process for payments and trade activity.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 85
Screening policy
Sanctions screening
Screening is the process of checking information obtained about a person,
entity, goods, or services against official sanctions lists. It could also involve
payment details, adverse media, and connections to PEPs. Screening is a key
control of an effective sanctions compliance program.
Organizations may go beyond official lists and screen for information that
could pose a heightened risk. Examples include:
• Items subject to export control
• Entities owned by designated parties
• Individuals, entities, and vessels posing a particularly high risk
By screening against sanctions lists, organizations can identify whether a
customer or entity with whom they have a relationship has been sanctioned
or is linked to a sanctioned party or jurisdiction.
Sanctions screening occurs at the beginning of a customer relationship and
on an ongoing basis. Payment screening should be undertaken by your
organization at the time of payment. Screening can be automated via a
subscription service or done manually by regularly checking lists for any
updates.
When screening generates an alert, the data is reviewed and assessed. One
alert generally includes the internal record information and the hits against the
various discrete entries on the sanctions lists with the percentage match, or
threshold. Once screening has been undertaken against a customer and all
relevant parties, any alerts are reviewed to determine if they are true or false
positives. When a true match is identified, the alert is escalated through a
dedicated flow in the screening tool, or via other communication channels in
the case of manual screening. The results of the screening will become part of
the due diligence for that customer and will assist in determining whether
there is any sanctions risk.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 86
The sanctions compliance officer receives the alert, reviews it, conducts
further investigation if necessary, and reports as appropriate. When screening
payments, any results of the screening will assist in determining whether the
business activity or payment can be made.
Types of sanctions screening
Screening against sanctions lists is essential to determine whether any
information related to a person, entity, goods, or services is associated with
direct or indirect sanctions exposure. Generally, sanctions screening is
conducted either manually or through automated processes. Automated
screening solutions require ongoing maintenance and supervision, while
manual screening necessitates thorough documentation. The goal is to
ensure that all relevant parties involved in a deal, transaction, product, or
business relationship are thoroughly reviewed against the applicable
sanctions list.
Screening takes place through various stages, including customer
onboarding, real-time transaction screening, routine list updates, and regular
list maintenance. To establish robust screening governance, certain factors
are important:
• System calibration
• Frequency of screening
• Screening programs
• List management
Automated screening is commonly used by large organizations to streamline
the real-time screening process. It is often implemented through vendor-
provided or in-house systems that automatically compare data against
specific lists at predetermined intervals. Each transaction could generate
multiple potential matches, or hits, based on factors such as originator,
beneficiary, address, or payment field language. These hits are consolidated
into alerts for review or escalation. Reviewers then examine the matching
terms to determine if they are false positive matches or if further information
is required.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 87
Manual screening involves inputting relevant names into the system and
directly comparing them against the source lists. Lists from organizations such
as the US Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC), and jurisdictions such as
the EU and UK offer search capabilities to facilitate this process.
The calibration settings of screening systems affect the number of generated
hits. Improper tuning could result in an excessive or insufficient number of hits,
thereby increasing sanctions exposure. For example, searching with 100%
matching sensitivity might overlook deliberate or negligent transpositions, or
misspellings.
Sanctions lists are updated regularly, so it is essential to screen frequently.
Most organizations conduct initial sanctions screening during customer
onboarding. Subsequently, they often perform weekly or monthly batch
screenings to ensure that customers or parties in their account records have
not been added to any sanctions lists.
Organizations must ensure that all relevant sanctions lists are included in the
screening processes, and that their system’s lists are updated to match the
most current versions.
Sanctions restrictions apply to all business
activities
Sanctions pose a broader and more extensive challenge as compared to AML
restrictions due to the nature of the regulations. Sanctions regulations
encompass all aspects of business, including customers, counterparties, and
jurisdictions. While certain sanctions programs, such as the US Office of
Foreign Assets Control, target specific individuals or entities, other programs
have wider applicability. Consequently, these regulations, which primarily
impose business restrictions, can result in indirect risk exposure.
Sanctions risk can arise when a business's beneficial owner or controller is
subject to sanctions, even if the business itself operates in a non-sanctioned
country. Also, any business involved in transportation or shipping, including
shipping containers, can create sanctions risk.
Mitigating sanctions risk is a complex task. Unlike suspected money laundering
transactions that have some contextual clues, sanctions risk applies
throughout various stages of business activities.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 88
Typically, business activities follow a set of standard milestones, including
negotiation, due diligence, contracts, invoicing, payment, and shipping.
Although there might be variations within these milestones, sanctions risk can
emerge at any point along the way.
During negotiations, the business developer might be unaware that a
sanctioned party is an owner or controller of a company. Insufficient due
diligence at this stage could set the deal in motion, unknowingly involving
sanctioned individuals or entities. Contracts between parties might contain
provisions for payments involving other parties, such as standby letters of
credit, where concealed sanctions-sensitive players could be listed as payees.
Additionally, goods might come from a sanctioned country, even if the
manufacturer of the goods is not on the sanctions list. Finally, the
transportation of goods involves various components such as vessels, crews,
shipping containers, and potential transshipment through sanctioned
countries. Involving these jurisdictions, even unintentionally, could expose the
business to sanctions penalties.
Organizations must be vigilant at each stage of their operations to identify and
mitigate sanctions risk effectively.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 89
Regulators provide lists of
sanctions targets
Sanctions lists
Sanction lists are issued by international and local authorities. They contain
information on individuals and entities that are subject to sanctions
prohibitions. Organizations use these lists to screen their customers, third-
party associates, and payments. The purpose of screening is to ensure that
organizations do not engage with sanctioned customers or process
transactions that would violate sanctions. It is crucial to keep these lists
updated to prevent any potential sanctions violations.
Sanction lists are publicly accessible on the issuing authority’s websites in
different formats. Organizations may also subscribe to third-party
aggregators that maintain their own updated databases. This approach could
enhance the effectiveness and efficiency of the screening process.
Sanction lists contain the names of individuals, legal entities, ships, securities,
and ports that are subject to sanctions prohibitions, such as asset freezes or
sectoral sanctions.
These lists are essential tools for organizations to screen their clients,
associates, and wire transfers against sanctioned designations. It is crucial to
keep the sanction lists updated to identify and prevent sanctions violations in a
timely manner. This includes identifying prospective or existing clients,
vendors, and third parties who are subject to sanctions prohibitions. In
addition, organizations should identify wire transfers that should not be
processed if they involve sanctioned counterparties, ports, or vessels.
Sanction lists are issued by international authorities, such as the UN and the EU
and local authorities, such as the US Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC)
and HM Treasury in the UK. One example is the Specially Designated Nationals
List (SDN), which is widely used and published by OFAC. This list includes
specially designated global terrorists, narcotics traffickers, and other
designated persons.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 90
Sanction lists are typically released on the authorities' websites in PDF or XML
format. Organizations often subscribe to third-party data aggregators that
collect and maintain up-to-date databases containing all the lists from various
authorities.
Sanctions lists: Mandatory and
supplementary
Mandatory and supplementary sanctions lists are tools used by governments
and international organizations to ensure compliance with sanctions
regulations.
Mandatory sanctions lists are often established by international bodies. These
lists are generally supranational—such as those including targets designated
by the United Nations Security Council Resolutions (UNSCR). All Member
States of the international body are obligated to enforce them. Mandatory lists
might also include local sanctions regimes. Financial institutions must
incorporate mandatory lists into their screening processes, depending on the
jurisdiction in which they are located and where they operate.
In addition, financial institutions may choose to utilize supplementary lists as an
extra measure to reduce sanctions risk exposure. Supplementary lists can
include negative news lists or lists of individuals who are known to have
evaded or attempted to evade sanctions.
The main differences between mandatory and supplementary sanctions lists
lie in their nature, enforcement, and scope.
Here are some examples of mandatory sanctions lists:
• UN lists
• EU lists, if the financial institution operates in Europe
• US lists, due to transactions in US dollars and the extraterritorial coverage
of US sanctions
• Host country’s lists
• Lists of the parent company’s country, if the organization is a branch or
subsidiary of an organization outside of the host country
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 91
• Lists of other countries, especially if the financial institution uses their
currency
Supplementary sanctions lists are created and imposed by individual
countries or organizations independently of mandatory lists. These lists allow
countries or organizations to address specific concerns, policy objectives, or
national security. Enforcement of supplementary sanctions lists can vary
among different countries or organizations.
The process for authorities such as the Office of Foreign Assets Control
(OFAC) to add an individual or entity to the Specially Designated Nationals
(SDN) list could be long. To minimize sanctions risk exposure, financial
institutions should consider screening against names found in advisories and
other reliable sources. For example, third-party data aggregators compile lists
of entities allegedly owned, controlled, or associated with SDNs.
Sanctions lists: Trade-related
The Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) is responsible for administering and
enforcing trade controls in the United States.
The BIS issues the Export Administration Regulations (EAR). These regulations
include a list of names of foreign individuals or entities, known as the Entity List.
The BIS Denied Persons List (DPL) includes individuals or entities whose export
privileges have been denied.
To comply with US trade control prohibitions, financial institutions can utilize
the Consolidated Screening List (CSL), maintained by the BIS. This
consolidated list combines multiple export screening lists, allowing institutions
to conduct comprehensive screenings.
In the US, the BIS is the responsible authority for administering and enforcing
trade controls. The BIS issues the EAR to identify foreign individuals and
entities subject to trade prohibitions related to commodities, technology,
software, and other goods under export controls.
Furthermore, the BIS maintains the Entity List, which includes foreign persons
subject to licensing requirements and other provisions for exporting,
reexporting, or transferring goods. These foreign persons encompass
businesses, research institutions, government and private organizations,
individuals, and other legal entities.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 92
Additionally, the BIS maintains the DPL, which is comprised of individuals and
companies denied export privileges. These parties are prohibited from
participating in the export of any controlled US goods or transferring
ownership of US-made products to foreign individuals or countries. It's
important to note that the DPL lists parties denied export privileges, while the
entity list imposes licensing requirements.
To screen against trade control prohibitions, financial institutions can utilize
the CSL, which consolidates multiple export screening lists. The CSL
encompasses parties for whom the US government imposes restrictions on
certain exports, reexports, or transfers of items.
In other countries, customs agencies maintain intelligence databases on
goods imported and exported, the value of goods, and details about the
importer, exporter, owner, and receiver.
Reading a sanctions list: Individuals
Sanction listings for individuals are used to confirm whether there is a true
match with a customer. Reading a sanctions list for individuals can be a
complex task, as it involves understanding the information provided and
interpreting the relevant details. Sanctions lists for individuals contain different
types of identifiers, or types of information about a sanctions target. Examples
of identifiers include name, date of birth, jurisdiction, national identification
number, entities with which a target is linked, information about penalties
imposed against a target, registered legal address, and website URL. The
challenges in navigating a sanctions list for individuals arise from the need to
handle diverse formats, interpret complex information, overcome language
and naming barriers, track updates, integrate data, and ensure compliance
with legal obligations.
The most common identifier is the name or names of a sanctions target. The
name of the individual should be presented in its original language, such as
Cyrillic or Arabic, and its transliteration into Latin. Aliases or “also known as”
(AKA) are included as additional names, since individuals targeted by
sanctions often use different name variations to evade detection. For
example, an individual might reverse the order of their first and last names, or
remove letters to change the spelling.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 93
Another important identifier is jurisdiction. It is related to information
concerning a target’s nationality, place of birth, country of residence, and
other jurisdiction connections. Sanctions targets can have connections to
more than one jurisdiction.
Additional identifiers include:
• National identification numbers, such as passports
• Entities with which the target is linked, such as political parties, positions
held within militia or insurgent groups, executive positions or directorships,
commercial activities, associations, or activities with Specially Designated
Nationals
• Information about other sanctions or penalties imposed on a target. For a
US financial institution or corporation, this would include sanctions imposed
by authorities such as the EU, UK, or UN.
You should be aware of the reliability of the source of information. For
example, information on a passport is considered reliable because it
originates from government authorities who have verified an individual’s
identity.
Reading a sanctions list: Legal entities
Reading a sanctions list for legal entities can be challenging due to the diverse
range of data that need interpretation. These lists contain various types of
identifiers or information about the sanctioned entities. Examples of identifiers
include the registered name and number, the addresses, associated
jurisdictions, names of associated entities and individuals, contact information,
and details of any imposed penalties. The challenges in navigating a sanctions
list for legal entities arise from the need to interpret complex information, stay
updated with changes, integrate data effectively, and ensure compliance with
legal obligations. Successfully overcoming these challenges is essential for
organizations to effectively screen and mitigate risks associated with
engaging with sanctioned entities.
Sanctions lists for legal entities are utilized to verify if there is a true match with
a customer. Legal entities, including corporations, partnerships, trusts,
foundations, and other similar arrangements, can also be listed as targets.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 94
When reading a sanctions list for entities, the most common identifying
factors are the entity’s registration name, registration number, and country of
registration. Additional names by which the company is known might also be
included. Names might appear in their original language, such as Arabic or
Cyrillic, in addition to their transliteration into Latin.
Other important identifiers include:
• Jurisdictions
o Examples include country of registration, countries where the entity
operates, and relevant addresses.
• Illegal activities of the legal entity
• License number associated with the entity
• Connections to individuals and entities
o Examples include owners, directors, controllers, executives, group
entities, and individuals associated with the entity.
• Contact details
o Examples include website addresses, email addresses, phone numbers,
and fax details for the entity.
• Any additional sanctions or penalties imposed on the target entity
When analyzing a sanctions list, it is important to consider the reliability of the
information source. For example, information regarding registration name and
number is generally deemed reliable as it originates from the relevant
authority.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 95
Reading a sanctions list: Trade-related
Trade-related sanctions lists contain similar identifiers to individual or legal
entity lists, and they include information related to restricted goods or
services, and the duration of the restrictions. The type of list, such as the
Bureau of Industry and Security's (BIS) Entity List or Denied Persons List,
indicates the specific type of restriction. These lists also indicate whether the
entity or individual requires licensing or is an outright prohibition. Properly
assessing the geographical connections of clients is important for accurately
evaluating any potential impact or risk of violating trade restrictions.
Trade-related sanctions lists aim to identify individuals and entities that are
subject to trade restrictions. Some of the identifiers used are similar to those
for individual and legal entity sanctions lists, such as registration name and
number, registered address, and associated jurisdictions. Additional identifiers
related to trade restrictions include the:
• Types of restricted goods and services
• Time period over which the restrictions apply
• Expiration date
Depending on the list, the type of restriction is also identified.
For example, the BIS Entity List requires individuals or entities to meet
additional licensing requirements for specific goods and services, while the
BIS Denied Party List prohibits these individuals or entities from exporting the
specific goods altogether.
Trade-related sanctions lists often highlight the specific items or technologies
subject to trade restrictions. This information helps identify potential matches
and assess the nature of the trade-related risks associated with the listed
individuals or entities.
Analysts should be careful when identifying the geographical links associated
with a customer. These links encompass citizenship, residency, registration
country, countries of operation, as well as the locations of subsidiaries,
suppliers, and end-buyers. You must understand the geographical
connections to fully comprehend the risk of violating trade restrictions. This
knowledge is important in effectively managing sanctions risk, both when
dealing with specific clients and when processing particular transactions.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 96
Analysts can manage risks and ensure compliance by looking at the relevant
restrictions and the client's location.
Fuzzy logic and partial matches
Fuzzy logic is a matching technique that is used to increase the effectiveness
of screening processes by overcoming problems such as flawed records and
databases. This technique is accomplished through algorithms that use
degrees of similarity to determine the probability that two names are the
same. Fuzzy logic can find matches in misspelled names, incomplete names,
and names with different spellings but similar sounds or phonetics. In addition,
fuzzy logic accepts different formats for date of birth and other
inconsistencies.
A partial match means the entity being screened is similar to an entry on a list,
based on fuzzy logic and potentially other identifying factors, such as date of
birth. Partial matches require further human intervention to determine if the
match is a true match.
The use of fuzzy logic is critical in accommodating partial matches and
variations in naming conventions. This approach acknowledges that data
sources often provide inconsistent and diverse information, making it
challenging to rely solely on exact name matches. Partial matches identify
similarities between names or entities, even if they are not exact matches.
Fuzzy logic considers variations in naming conventions across different
cultures and languages, such as the order of first, middle, and last names. It
also considers transliterations, which involve representing names or words
from one language to another using different alphabets or phonetic systems,
such as converting Arabic characters into Cyrillic script. Romanization refers
to the conversion of text from a non-Latin script to a Latin one.
The use of fuzzy logic is particularly important in international investigations
where names can be written and spelled differently based on cultural or
transliteration practices. The algorithms used under fuzzy logic include:
• Phonetic, identifying similar sounding names.
o "Katherine Navel" and "Catherine Naval"
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 97
• Edit Distance or Damerau-Levenshtein metric, calculating the number of
character changes required to transform one name into another.
o "McDowd" and "MacDawd"
Based on the fuzzy logic configuration, an organization will generate partial
matches. These partial matches should then be investigated to determine
whether they are true matches or false positives.
False positives
A “false positive” is when you receive indication that something is present
when actually it is not. These false indications or false alarms happen often in
customer research and when using people and systems to screen names or
transactions. Something about a name or transaction might cause a filter to
raise a red flag. The filter might be a human who thought he noticed
something but was mistaken. The filter might be a human who misread some
information. The filter might be a system that wasn’t finely tuned enough to
recognize the difference between two similar names. It is impossible to be
perfect, so false positives will always be “noise” in the process you will need to
identify and clear efficiently. But before you clear something as a false
positive, you must know why it was triggered so you can be confident it was,
indeed, false.
There are some common reasons false positives are triggered. Knowing
those will help you clear them efficiently.
Sometimes a scenario is triggered falsely. If you have a scenario that watches
for payments received from a high-risk country, but an alert relates to a
payment received from a low-risk country, then there is probably something
wrong in the scenario design or coding. The system is misreading the
countries, and the false positive can probably be cleared.
Sometimes a reasonable explanation is available to help you clear a false
positive. For example, a professional who receives a monthly salary might
have received an annual, one-time bonus check from his employer. If you
know that your organization’s transaction monitoring systems are not
sophisticated enough to understand the one-time bonus check, you can
quickly clear this false positive after making the appropriate documentation.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 98
Certain customers might trigger frequent alerts, especially those who have
complex transactions, or those who make transactions with high-risk
countries. The alerts for these customers can be repetitive and predictable. A
quick review of prior alerts in your case management system might show
numerous documented clearings of similar alerts. You might be able to
document that information as part of your basis for quickly clearing the
current alert as a false positive.
Of course, if the alert or the information you find is valid and cannot be quickly
explained, then your investigation begins.
It is critically important that false positive alerts are recorded and reported
back to your analytics or Information Technology (IT) team, whoever is
responsible for developing and maintaining scenarios. They can identify any
coding errors and correct the scenarios or customer databases to reduce
further false positives.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 99
Screening process
Sanctions screening process
Sanctions screening is an important control measure for AFC compliance,
particularly to ensure sanctions compliance. A sanctions screening process
involves the identification of crucial and relevant data. It also includes checking
the obtained information against sanctions lists through screening tools such
as automated screening tools. Appropriate search phrases and parameters
should be adopted for screening. Based on the results returned, organizations
should conduct the necessary follow-up actions. Sanctions screening should
be conducted at specific points during a transaction.
Organizations can mitigate the risk of engaging in transactions with
sanctioned individuals or entities by implementing effective sanctions
screening processes.
Sanctions screening should be conducted at three specific points during a
transaction: during onboarding, at the time of payment, and through ongoing
batch processes. When conducting sanctions screening, the first step is for
the organization to identify where the risks come from in the transactions:
• The items to be exported and where they are exported from and to
• The parties involved
• Individuals, entities, and vessels posing high risks
Next, the organization should consider which screening method to adopt,
namely manual or automatic, and payment screening or batch screening.
Manual screening involves employees checking against sanctions lists, while
automatic screening involves a software tool. Payment screening happens
when a payment is involved. Batch screening is when a large number of
names or transactions are screened at the same time. The former would be
executed when the organization is instructed to make a payment, while the
latter occurs when payment has already been processed.
There are four main outcomes from the screening process:
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 100
• Target match
• Escalate
• False positive
• False negative
When the outcome is a target match, the organization should stop the
transaction and review all transactions involving the individual. For the
“escalate” outcome, there is sufficient information or other criteria to require
additional research. If the false positive outcomes are frequent, the
organization should review its search parameters and the data from which it
relies on for sanction screening. A false negative outcome is usually a result of
human error during input.
Payment systems and communication
protocols
Payment systems and communication protocols ensure the efficiency and
effectiveness of the overall screening system. Payment systems refer to the
mechanisms and infrastructure used to facilitate the transfer of funds
between parties. Communication protocols are the rules and conventions
governing the exchange of information between different entities involved in
a transaction.
Payment screening is the screening or filtering of relevant payment
instructions prior to their execution to ensure compliance with AFC and other
regulations. This type of screening activity focuses on transaction messages.
Financial institutions might need to use advanced technology solutions to
achieve an effective real-time, or ex-ante, payment screening process. Each
organization has its own process and standards of how sanctions payment
screening is undertaken.
Payment systems operate on various communication protocols. The speed at
which financial transactions are conducted makes payment screening
challenging. Therefore, an effective real-time, or ex-ante, payment screening
process is essential. It ensures that screening occurs before transactions are
completed and prevents potential violations.
Examples of communication protocols include:
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 101
• The Society for the Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunications
Code (SWIFT)
• The Clearing House Interbank Payments System (CHIPS)
• Single Euro Payments Area (SEPA) credit transfer
• The Federal Reserve’s Fedwire
• Regional systems, such as the Large Value Transfer System in Canada
The most well-known provider of financial messaging services is SWIFT. SWIFT
messages have predefined structured formats with set fields. These fields are
important in providing transaction information. However, variations in data at
toll gates, where different financial institutions input transaction descriptions,
can introduce human error or discrepancies.
Data transfers in payment systems rely on communication protocols to
facilitate the exchange of information. Efficient and secure data transfer is
essential for the smooth functioning of payment systems, enabling the
accurate and timely processing of payment transactions.
SWIFT message screening
The Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication (SWIFT) is
a global messaging network used by financial institutions to securely
communicate with one another. SWIFT message screening helps them
gather important information about the sender, recipient, and transaction
amount. It is a process to review financial transactions to identify potential
financial crime risks.
Transaction screening plays a key role in detecting and preventing illegal
financial activities. Unlike transaction monitoring which happens ex-post, or
“after the fact,” SWIFT message screening occurs ex-ante, or “before the
fact,” in real time at the time a payment instruction is issued and before the
transaction is completed. The process can be complicated by factors such as
human error, varying requirements among system participants, and different
information retention protocols.
The International Organization for Standardization (ISO) is an international
body that develops and publishes standards in various industries. The
introduction of the ISO 20022 standard for the exchange of financial
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 102
messages will bring significant changes to the screening process. The new
standard has a direct impact on the screening process by enhancing both its
effectiveness and efficiency in several ways.
The ISO 20022 standard offers a more comprehensive and structured
message format as compared to the previous standard (ISO 15022). As a
result, financial messages will use enriched data, allowing for more accurate
screening. The increased level of detail in data enables better identification
and detection of financial crime risks.
ISO 20022 promotes better interoperability and data exchange among
different financial systems and institutions. This standardized approach
facilitates seamless communication and information-sharing. It enables
financial institutions to access and process standardized data fields,
streamlining the screening process and improving its accuracy.
ISO 20022 provides a global standard aligned with evolving regulatory
requirements and industry best practice. Financial institutions will benefit from
a more unified and consistent approach to screening, ensuring more
consistent compliance with applicable regulations.
Trade activity screening
Trade activity screening is an important part of an organization’s efforts to
comply with sanctions regulations. The screening process for trade activity
involves multiple entities, such as customs agencies, free-trade zone
authorities, and regulatory bodies. In most jurisdictions, specialized databases
are used to capture all relevant information on both imported and exported
goods, including the value of the goods and details on the importer, exporter,
owner, receiver, and forwarder. Customs declaration forms are commonly
used to gather such information and can also help detect potential cases of
sanctions evasion or trade-based money laundering that require further
investigation.
Trade activity screening should be conducted at different stages of a trade
finance transaction, following progress in the submission of various
documents. It should not be limited to the individuals or legal entities involved
in the transaction. Using a maritime trade deal as an example, trade activity
screening should include:
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 103
• The vessel used for transportation, including its name, registration number,
owners, consigner, and consignee
• The recent voyage history of that vessel
• The shipping company
• Shipping routes
• Agents or third parties involved, if any
• The ports of call, including at origin, transit, and destination
Key documents that contain information requiring verification, either
independently or by cross-referencing with other shipping and transaction
records, usually include:
• Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunication (SWIFT)
messages
• Letters of credit
• Bills of exchange and bills of lading
• Commercial invoices
• Insurance certificates
• Ship manifests
• Certificates of origin
Trade-related screening can be challenging due to the global nature of trade
activity. Documentation might vary in format and language, making it difficult
to standardize the screening process. Additionally, certain items might be
subject to sanctions only when they exceed specific quantities, quotas, or
have specific physical characteristics. This information, which often comes
unstructured, might not be readily available for automated systems to screen,
resulting in the need for labor-intensive manual processing.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 104
Trade activity screening (Case example:
Unusual transactions)
Company A is a trading company. It typically uses letters of credit as means of
payment when purchasing goods from overseas. Letters of credit are used in
international trade transactions to ensure secure payment between the buyer
and the seller. Typically, Company A’s purchase orders have a value of
approximately US$1 million. However, in a recent batch of purchases,
Company A made several purchases of similar items from multiple sellers.
Some of the new sellers were located in or near high-risk jurisdictions.
Interestingly, the value of each transaction in this batch was only about 30% of
the usual amount, but the total value of all the transactions combined
exceeded US$3 million. Some of the transactions in this batch were also paid
in cash in advance. Company A’s bank’s screening program should have
flagged these transactions due to their high-risk nature.
Company A's transaction history has been consistent with its expected
behavior. It has been conducting stable transactions using letters of credit,
which is an acceptable practice for importers.
However, there are certain red flags in its recent batch of orders. First, the
sudden shift toward smaller transactions and down payments is suspicious.
Importers prefer not to make payments before the goods are shipped, as this
poses a risk of losing money. Second, making multiple purchases of similar or
identical items from overseas suppliers would result in additional shipping and
insurance costs. It goes against sound business logic. Finally, the fact that
some of the sellers are based in or near high-risk jurisdictions further adds to
the suspicion surrounding these transactions.
The financial institution's screening process should initially flag some of these
transactions, particularly those requiring a letter of credit and involving similar
items from high-risk jurisdictions or neighboring states. This should have
triggered enhanced due diligence.
It is important to constantly update relevant databases and stay informed of
sanctions-related developments. What might appear to be a legitimate
transaction one day could be subject to sanctions the next day. Therefore,
maintaining up-to-date information in your screening system is essential for
effective compliance.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 105
Key takeaways
• Trade activity screening begins as soon as a trade transaction begins.
• Knowing your customer’s usual trade pattern is important for trade activity
screening.
• If parties in a trade transaction are located in or near high-risk jurisdictions,
enhanced due diligence should be conducted.
Dual-use goods risk
Dual-use goods are those that can be used for both civilian and military
purposes. Examples include lasers, sensors, navigation equipment, chemicals,
microorganisms, toxins, and even building materials. Goods required for civil
nuclear and biological research and development are dual use because they
are often necessary to produce weapons of mass destruction (WMD).
Dual-use goods are usually subject to restrictions under global control
regimes. These regimes are agreements between jurisdictions that export
WMD components to prevent WMD proliferation. They include the Wassenaar
Arrangement, the Nuclear Suppliers Group, and the Missile Technology
Control Regime. These regimes promote transparency and responsibility.
They stop terrorists from acquiring WMD components. They also prevent
jurisdictions from accumulating them in a way that would threaten global
stability. Participating states commit to setting national policies and export
controls.
Illicit military or WMD programs seek dual-use goods because they otherwise
lack the required raw materials, sophisticated technology, or advanced
methods to develop these programs.
To evade dual-use controls, bad actors could conceal their role as the
ultimate end user. One method uses multiple layers of intermediaries to
obtain an export license by providing details of fictitious end users or
believable alternative uses. The bad actor might ship the goods to a third
jurisdiction and re-export them from there. This approach makes it harder for
investigators to follow the trail. The actor can avoid additional scrutiny by
mislabeling the cargo with a vague description, such as “spare parts.”
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 106
Uncontrolled dual-use goods are easier to trade because they do not require
export licenses. However, they could still attract scrutiny because of their
proposed end use.
Organizations involved in trade and trade finance should have effective
controls to enable staff to identify dual-use goods in transactions. Staff should
know when exporters need government licenses and should document
copies of such licenses. Organizations must not focus solely on military or
“lethal end-use” goods or approve transactions when goods descriptions are
vague or confusing. Internal and third-party data sources enable this
screening.

Version 2.02

Sanctions Screening

Introduction
Setup: AST thresholds
You are a sanctions screening officer for a small financial institution in France.
You are responsible for reviewing potential matches returned from your fully
outsourced automated screening tool (AST). You have been using the same
system for a few years. The AST has been reliable and the support from the
vendor has been satisfactory. When you receive an alert, you examine the
reason for the alert and any supporting documentation. Then you assess the
risk or concern and gather additional information if necessary.
A few days ago, you noticed that the quantity of alerts returned each day had
approximately tripled. You were surprised and a bit confused. You saw a larger
number of potential name matches that were not closely related. In addition,
you noticed that in many cases the customer’s country of residence and the
country flagged in the alert did not match. Reviewing these alerts required a
lot of resources and left you with less time to focus on other tasks. At first, you
did not know what to do and thought it would return to normal the next day.
A couple of days have passed and the number of alerts is still unexpectedly
high. You contact the vendor to find out if they have made any changes. The
vendor says that a major software update occurred last week. As a result, the
name-matching threshold was automatically reset from 90% to 70%. The
other key change was switching the country matching to “global” instead of
only searching for the customer's country of citizenship or residence. Now
you understand why you have been receiving a lot of false positive hits in
which the names are not closely related and the countries do not match.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 108
What would you do?
What would you do next?
• Continue to work as usual.
• Review the thresholds with your boss and vendor.
• Clear the false positives quickly.
• Escalate to senior management.
Feedback
The best response is to review the thresholds with your boss and the vendor.
An important part of sanctions screening is to note any unexpected results
from your AST. It is your responsibility to coordinate with your immediate
supervisor and the vendor to ensure that the thresholds are set appropriately.
You should never continue working as usual if you notice a sudden,
unexplained change in the number of alerts. The sudden, and large, change
could be an indication that the AST is not working as intended. You need to
uncover the reason.
You should not clear the false positives as quickly as you can. You might
overlook some true matches. Since you know there is an unusually high
number of alerts, you should talk to your boss about the issue.
You should not escalate this issue to the senior management team at this
time. You should communicate any issues about the results of your AST so a
solution can be found. At this point, alerting senior management would be
premature.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Define automated screening tools and explain why they are important.
• Describe how analytics are used to enhance screening.
