Automated screening tools
Automated screening tool
Large financial institutions often utilize automated screening tools (AST) in
their screening processes. ASTs are software systems that can replace
manual methods and streamline screening. Their primary function is to check
lists, generating hits that might be combined into alerts, typically based on a
customer's record. To establish an effective screening program, the
Wolfsberg Group recommends implementing a screening application.
Organizations should consider factors such as institution size, geographic
reach, business scope, and technology infrastructure when selecting an AST.
In addition, AST implementation should be tailored to each organization. They
must analyze their specific risks and functional needs.
Information technology is important for establishing an effective screening
program. The accuracy and reliability of screening results depend heavily on
the quality of the data that is input into the automated screening tool. The
New York State Department of Financial Services (NYDFS) outlines data quality
and alert generation processes in its Part 504 requirements, emphasizing the
need for a structured approach in the information technology component.
The effectiveness of automated tools relies upon proper configuration and
regular updates to adapt to changing regulations. In addition, ongoing
employee training and vendor support are essential for maintaining the
efficiency of these tools.
ASTs should be viewed as aids rather than substitutes for understanding
customer relationships and activity levels. Therefore, a comprehensive
approach should combine both manual and automated screening methods.
The selection of appropriate lists for screening is critical, with consideration
given to sanctions enforced by the host country and countries with regular
interactions. Careful control and periodic review are necessary to prevent
misuse.
Effective collaboration between compliance teams and information
technology is vital to ensure data accuracy and integrity throughout the
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 110
organization. Operational controls should limit user access based on role
requirements, following the principle of least privilege, or only having the
access necessary for the individual to perform his or her role. Regular reviews
of access rights and governance are necessary to prevent unauthorized
changes or breaches.
Automated screening tool algorithms
Automated screening tools (AST) utilize algorithms to assess the likelihood of
matches to sanctions targets and assign weights to determine potential hits.
The calibration settings of ASTs can influence the number of generated hits.
Multiple alerts for the same target might arise due to its presence on multiple
lists or matching various elements in payment messages. AST algorithms
incorporate features like noisy or neutral word filtering, common word
weighting, and forenames versus surnames weighting. These algorithms
enhance screening accuracy by considering the significance of words within
a name. For example, "National Bank of" receives a lower weight, while
forenames versus surnames weighting aids in identifying targets with
different first name spellings.
Fuzzy logic enables the system to handle ambiguous or partial matches when
comparing individuals' or entities' names against sanction lists. It introduces a
degree of flexibility in determining the similarity of a potential match,
considering factors such as spelling variations or different name formats.
Scenarios refer to predefined patterns or rules used in ASTs to identify
potential matches or suspicious activities. They capture specific patterns or
combinations of data that indicate a higher risk of sanctions violations, such as
transaction amounts and countries involved.
Noisy words which consist of common conjunctions and articles, such as "of,"
"and," and “the,” are assigned lower weights or disregarded during screening.
This ensures that potential matches are not overlooked due to minor
variations in these words.
The common words algorithm assigns importance based on word frequency,
which helps distinguish between entities with similar names.
The forenames versus surnames algorithm gives less importance to first
names compared to last names. This reduces the risk of missing targets due
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 111
to variations in first name spelling, for example, "Jean Luc Pickard," "Gean Luc
Pickard," and "J.L. Pickard." However, it is essential to consider cultural naming
conventions and variations for optimal effectiveness, especially when dealing
with names from different regions.
By employing these algorithms, ASTs improve screening accuracy and
increase the likelihood of detecting potential matches with sanctions targets
while minimizing false positives.
Fuzzy logic and partial matches (Case
example: Mr. Ivanov)
A financial institution, XYZ Bank, employs an automated screening tool (AST)
to facilitate sanctions screening. However, variations in spelling might cause
potential matches to be missed. To address this challenge, the bank’s AST
utilizes an algorithm known as fuzzy logic to deal with imprecise information in
sanctions screening. This allows for flexibility when matching names or entities
against sanctions lists.
For example, the AST generates a partial match result for a prospective
customer. The compliance team then conducts a manual review of the partial
match. The team confirms that the customer is, indeed, a sanctioned
individual on an international sanctions list. This demonstrates the
effectiveness of the AST and the value of incorporating fuzzy logic algorithms
into the sanctions screening process.
Fuzzy logic uses the Damerau-Levenshtein metric of distance to assess how
close a match is to a true match. It also uses phonetic spellings and common
variations on names, as well as variations on different alphabets such as Arabic
or Cyrillic. It gives match results as a percentage: 100% is an exact match,
while at 95% there will be a slight variation in spelling. As the percentage falls,
the matches become less accurate, and false positives might be generated.
During routine screening, XYZ Bank’s AST identifies a transaction involving
"Alexsandr Ivannov." However, there are variations of this name in the
customer database. The AST generates a partial match between "Alexsandr
Ivannov" and "Alexander Ivanov." This partial match indicates a degree of
similarity that requires further investigation but falls short of an exact match.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 112
In accordance with regulatory guidelines, XYZ Bank's compliance team
reviews the partial match result. They recognize that the fuzzy logic
implementation contributes to this outcome by considering degrees of
similarity beyond precise matches. The team understands the significance of
partial matches in identifying potential sanctions violations.
During the review, the compliance team confirms that "Alexsandr Ivannov" is,
indeed, the same individual listed on an international sanctions list. By
leveraging the capabilities of fuzzy logic and acknowledging the importance
of partial matches, XYZ Bank successfully identifies the sanctioned individual
among its customers.
Key takeaways
• Fuzzy logic allows for robust screening by considering various degrees of
name similarity.
• ASTs generate partial matches which need further human review.
• Fuzzy logic effectively enhances compliance with sanctions regulations.
False positives (Case example: Automated
sanctions screening)
Global Bank, a multinational financial institution, has implemented an
advanced automated screening tool (AST) with fuzzy logic algorithms to
comply with sanctions regulations. Fuzzy logic is a matching technique used
to increase the effectiveness of screening processes. During routine
transaction screening, the AST generates an alert for a payment involving
"Fazal Rahim" who is a potential match to a sanctioned individual on the United
Nations Security Council (UNSC) Consolidated List. However, the "Fazal Rahim"
in the payment has a different birth year and other different identifying
information. The case was determined to be a false positive, so the screening
team allows the payment to proceed. False positives in ASTs are a common
challenge and require investigation to avoid unnecessary disruptions and
protect legitimate business relationships.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 113
Global Bank’s AST raises an alert for a payment involving "Fazal Rahim.” The
alert suggests a potential match with a sanctioned individual on the UNSC list.
The compliance team conducts a comprehensive analysis.
They begin by checking the identifying details on the UNSC list. This reveals
that the sanctioned individual was born in 1974 in Kabul, Afghanistan. He holds
an Afghanistan passport which has the number listed. It also lists several
known addresses for the individual, all in Afghanistan. The screening team
compares these details with the information that the bank has on the
payment. They discover that the Fazal Rahim making the payment was born in
1981 in the UK. He holds a British passport and has a different address in
England.
As a result, the team confirms that "Fazal Rahim" is a legitimate business
partner and not affiliated with any sanctioned entities or activities listed on the
UNSC list. This is an example of a false positive scenario, where the AST's alert
incorrectly flagged a legitimate transaction.
Through thorough analysis, Global Bank’s compliance team successfully
identified the false positive, safeguarding their business partnership while
maintaining compliance with sanctions regulations.
Key takeaways
• ASTs might generate false positives, reducing screening efficiency.
• False positives can delay legitimate transactions.
• Careful investigation and analysis are required to identify false positives.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 114
Automated screening tool: Managing alerts
It can be extremely challenging for a financial institution to manage the
volume of hits and alerts generated by an automated screening tool (AST).
Each organization has its own internal processes that outline how alerts should
be assessed and managed, and how decisions should be made and
documented. Managing alerts effectively with an AST requires a combination
of technological expertise, compliance knowledge, and proper governance.
Strategies financial institutions employ to manage alerts include risk-based
prioritization, standardized investigative workflows, automation, blacklist and
whitelist management, and training.
One approach to managing alerts is to calibrate the matching threshold to
generate alerts that closely align with the information on target lists. For
example, setting a threshold of 90% or higher similarity for the targets can
reduce the number of alerts to review. However, regulators discourage using
this strategy without considering the effectiveness of identifying potential
risks.
Another method is the use of "whitelists" that include individuals or entities that
triggered AST alerts but were found not to be matches to any list. Additionally,
you should maintain an internal blacklist. It contains names of people
associated with illegal activities, sanctions violations, regulatory
noncompliance, or reputational risk.
To manage alert volume, financial institutions should create specific scenarios
and rules for screening. The AST's efficiency in generating alerts improves
when institutions accurately define the typologies and scenarios to be
screened. Also, financial institutions should be selective in the lists they screen
against. Screening against every available list can result in numerous false
positives, especially when a target appears on multiple lists.
Additionally, financial institutions should use risk-based prioritization to
manage the alerts. It involves assigning a risk score to each alert based on the
severity of the potential risk. This allows the screening team to prioritize their
review of alerts with higher risk scores, ensuring that the most critical alerts
receive immediate attention.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 115
Automated screening tool: Calibrating
thresholds and updating algorithms
Automated screening tools (AST) employ interdiction software to match
parties in a financial institution's databases with names on lists, or to flag
unusual transactions. Threshold calibration determines which alerts to
generate. A higher threshold increases the chances of false negatives, while a
lower threshold results in excessive false positives. Understanding the
institution's risk areas is essential for setting thresholds accurately. Higher
thresholds are suitable for data under an organization’s control, while lower
thresholds accommodate external variances. Regular updates and algorithm
reconfiguration are essential. It is important to document the calibration
process and emphasize that screening tools complement human analysis
without replacing it.
To establish accurate thresholds for screening, financial institutions need to
implement effective risk management strategies and carefully manage their
data sources. The specific origin of the data and the institution's control over it
are important in determining the appropriate threshold levels.
When financial institutions have a high degree of control over their data, such
as internal databases and customer records, they can apply higher threshold
settings. This approach is suitable because the institution can ensure data
quality and consistency, which minimizes the chances of false positives.
When dealing with data from third-party sources, you should use lower
threshold settings. This adjustment acknowledges the inherent variability and
potential inaccuracies in external data, effectively accommodating external
variances.
It is important to regularly review algorithms to keep up with evolving financial
crime trends. For example, internal investigations could uncover new patterns
and trends in financial crime activity, which might require adjustments to the
screening algorithms. The responsibility for the calibration process, including
tuning the thresholds of an alert system, typically lies with the second line of
defense or specialized teams within an organization.
It is important to document any changes made to the screening model,
adjustments to threshold levels, and testing results. This documentation
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 116
serves as a reference, aids in auditing and compliance efforts, and ensures
transparency and accountability in the screening process.
Additionally, keep in mind that screening tools are facilitators, not substitutes,
for human analysis. The final determination of an alert ultimately relies on the
expertise of compliance professionals.
Automated screening tool: Scenarios
Automated screening tools (AST) utilize scenarios, which are pre-established
rules or models that reflect known typologies to detect unusual behavior or
potential violations in the screening process. These scenarios are created
based on industry sources, risk assessments, and historical indicators. By
matching transactions or activities against these predefined scenarios, the
AST system can flag unusual transactions that require additional review. For
example, a financial institution might develop a scenario that focuses on
previous cases where payment messages contained prohibited keywords,
thus improving the accuracy of the screening process. These scenarios
enable institutions to proactively identify and address potential risks, thereby
ensuring compliance with regulations, and mitigating the possibility of
violations.
Identifying scenarios for screening involves several key approaches.
The first approach is to review industry sources. Information from reputable
industry sources such as the Financial Action Task Force (FATF) and FATF-
style regional bodies provides valuable insight into emerging typologies and
trends.
Second, an organization's internal risk assessment results contribute to the
identification of relevant scenarios. This assessment reflects the specific risks
the organization faces, allowing for a tailored approach to screening.
Next, historical detection indicators, including positive hits and true matches,
are analyzed to identify patterns and indicators that can inform the creation of
effective scenarios.
These scenarios serve as additional screening criteria beyond the standard
ASTs. To be effective, organizations must develop scenarios that align with
their unique business experience and risks. There should also be a testing
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 117
period to ensure AST accuracy in flagging potential risks within the
organization's operational environment.
In addition, scenarios are important in managing the volume of alerts
generated by ASTs. By focusing on specific risks, tailored scenarios enhance
the efficiency of the screening process.
Regular testing using simulated examples further refines scenarios.
Organizations modify names from known screening lists using techniques
such as transliteration or reversed letters to test the AST's alert generation
threshold. This iterative process ensures that scenarios effectively identify
potential matches while minimizing false positives, optimizing operational
efficiency, and maintaining the integrity of the screening process.
Automated screening tool: Understanding
mapping requirements
Effective sanctions compliance relies on the critical task of mapping systems
and databases to automated screening tools (AST). Specifically, accurate
screening in ASTs depends upon proper mapping from different fields within
payment messages. Misalignment could lead to an increased number of false
positives or false negatives.
Organizations should review their system architecture when it is initially
developed and after any subsequent changes to it. The scope and
sophistication of these reviews should align with the size and complexity of
the organization. Organizations should also consider reviewing their system
architecture at least annually, regardless of whether changes have occurred,
to ensure operational effectiveness and identify areas for improvement and
enhancement. These measures contribute to robust sanctions compliance
and effective risk mitigation.
Proper mapping helps avoid significant errors. Some payment message fields,
such as Bank Identification Code (BIC), contain specific information exclusive
to those fields. Screening them against an entire sanctions list can result in a
high number of false positives. By accurately mapping the BIC field to
sanctioned BIC codes, the AST can effectively reduce false positives.
Conversely, incorrect mapping could lead to under-screening, increasing the
risk of false negatives. For example, if the “purpose of payment field” is only
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 118
screened against sanctioned BIC codes, other critical information is likely to
be overlooked.
Conducting annual reviews, regardless of changes to the system architecture,
helps maintain operational effectiveness and identify areas for improvement.
Regular reviews help organizations stay up-to-date with any changes in
systems, databases, or data sources, ensuring that the mapping remains
accurate and effective.
Collaboration between sanctions compliance and information technology (IT)
teams is vital for developing a comprehensive understanding of the usage,
storage, and extraction of data throughout the organization. This collaboration
ensures that the compliance teams can effectively align their efforts with IT
processes.
By emphasizing proper mapping, conducting regular reviews, and fostering
collaboration, organizations can enhance their sanctions compliance efforts
and mitigate risks effectively.
Automated screening tool: Data flows and
validation
Information technology (IT) and data are fundamental to a properly
functioning automated screening tool (AST). So, it is important that AFC
compliance teams work closely with IT teams. Compliance needs to
understand how data works within the organization, the data that is available,
the data quality, and where the data is stored.
In larger organizations, understanding data can be difficult due to diverse data
types such as sales, operations, and risk data. These data require review and
comparison to ensure that important information is not being overlooked.
AFC compliance teams rely on IT to accurately identify, validate, and analyze
relevant data points. IT can also mitigate the risk of inadvertent omissions that
could lead to inaccuracies within AST programs and compliance breaches.
Accurate data is important for an AST to function optimally. AFC compliance
teams work closely with IT to understand diverse data types and use them
appropriately to meet legal and regulatory requirements. It is important to
understand how data is being extracted, transformed, and loaded between
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 119
systems. This process is known as extraction, transformation, and loading
(ETL).
During the data extraction (E) phase, the organization collects large datasets
on customer transactions, including sales, risk, and operations data. In the data
transformation (T) phase, variations in data formats are standardized to
ensure consistency. Finally, in the data loading (L) phase, the standardized
data is added to the AST for screening against sanctions lists.
Modifying or losing important data could result in serious compliance
breaches. To avoid this, organizations are advised to establish strong
governance frameworks for ETL processes, including regular audits and
reviews. These proactive measures act as safeguards, identifying and
resolving potential issues before they become problematic.
The collaboration between IT and AFC compliance, along with a deep
understanding of ETL processes, and the implementation of governance
frameworks, ensures the integrity and compliance of AST programs. These
collective measures protect against risks while demonstrating the
organization's commitment to maintaining a trustworthy and effective
compliance framework.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 120
Analytics
Artificial intelligence and machine learning
As criminals innovate, so, too, do financial institutions. While transaction
monitoring systems have traditionally been rule-based, the use of machine
learning, a subfield of artificial intelligence (AI), has introduced new systems
that can learn without programming. Artificial intelligence is a branch of
computer science that studies how computers can be designed to mimic
human reasoning. Machine learning is the capability of a machine to imitate
intelligent human behavior. By combining AI and machine learning, transaction
monitoring systems are able to include the consumption of several additional
data points to fine-tune the creation of alerts.
What do AI and machine learning add to your capabilities as an investigator?
They can give you better information to work with, as machine learning looks
for patterns and provides accurate results, while artificial intelligence can
solve complex problems to generate optimal accurate results. From an
investigative perspective, the process used to establish a reasonable
explanation for the alert remains the same, but the advantage of AI and
machine learning systems for the investigator is the low probability of false
positives compared to rule-based systems.
Additionally, for the organization, there are also significant benefits, including
the deployment of skilled resources towards additional value-adding activities.
As an investigator who receives an alert, it helps to understand why the alert
might have been generated. While this is easy to do in a rule-based system, it
is harder with systems using machine learning and AI. Machines can be trained
to look for patterns and behaviors where a rule-based system cannot. An AI-
based transaction monitoring system can read vast amounts of data from
multiple sources, review the data for red flags, and report back to the machine
learning component, which then uses this data to produce more accurate
alerts.
Both machine learning and AI can reduce false positives in transaction
monitoring systems by mimicking the behavior of the analyst, reducing the
need for human intervention.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 121
Analytics technology in sanctions screening
Analytics technology can enhance sanctions screening processes by
leveraging advanced data analysis techniques to identify and mitigate
potential risks associated with sanctions violations. By analyzing vast amounts
of data, analytics technology can provide valuable insights and improve the
effectiveness and efficiency of sanctions screening. Assuming accurate
underlying data, a well-calibrated analytical search engine can efficiently scan
through business lines, accounts, relationships, and transactions to identify
sanctions risk factors. This includes uncovering previously unknown
connections and providing valuable intelligence about beneficial owners,
associates, and affiliated parties involved in a transaction. Conversely, manual
searches of customer information would require moving into and out of
customer profiles or reviewing information in a linear pattern. The more data is
involved, the more prone to error a manual approach is.
Mitigating sanctions risk relies heavily on data. Typically, financial institutions
manually review customer information, counterparties, and associated
owners or associates to identify persons or entities with potential sanctions-
related concerns. This manual process presents challenges as the volume of
data in need of processing increases, thus increasing the risk of missing or
misinterpreting critical information.
By utilizing optimized analytics, financial institutions can efficiently analyze the
same set of customer information across personal and business relationships,
and across different countries, where permissible. For example, if a customer
provides information such as their address, email, phone number, and
payment accounts, it could be cross-checked to identify any potential
connections to other parties within a bank. This approach proves particularly
valuable if any of these data points could be definitively linked to previously
identified sanctions matches or blacklisted customers.
Conducting a comprehensive analysis of this data in bulk enables the
discovery of previously unknown business partners, identification of beneficial
owners who share common phone or email addresses, and verification of the
accuracy of the information provided. An example would be distinguishing
between genuine and fictitious business addresses. This mass review of data
facilitates the identification of meaningful connections and enhances the
overall effectiveness of sanctions risk mitigation efforts.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 122
Robotic process automation
Robotic process automation (RPA) is a technology used to automate manual
processes with the help of information technology (IT). It speeds up work and
allows the compliance function to focus on important and higher-risk issues.
This combination of efficiency and focus transforms how you handle
compliance and gives you a strategic advantage in dealing with complex
regulations. RPA makes processes smoother and makes you more resilient to
changing regulatory requirements.
Selecting the right automation tool is essential as it should align with
compliance needs and company-specific risks. While it is common to rely on
outside experts or vendors for automation, it is important to remember that
the company cannot outsource its own compliance with applicable rules and
regulations.
RPA enables organizations to streamline their compliance processes by
freeing up human resources from repetitive tasks. It enables the team to
focus on critical thinking and risk-based analysis. For example, once data is
collected, RPA bots could perform pre-defined analytical tasks, such as
applying rules-based algorithms to identify suspicious patterns or behaviors.
These bots could efficiently analyze vast amounts of data and flag
transactions that require further investigation.
Integrating automation requires a tailored and strategic approach, starting
with a comprehensive risk assessment. This ensures transparency and
accountability by aligning automation with the organization's risk profile. The
assessment can be presented for audit purposes to demonstrate the maturity
and reliability of any RPA program.
For new technologies, a thorough evaluation is necessary from AFC and
privacy viewpoints. These analyses should be documented for compliance
and audit records. Notably, the EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR)
prohibits automated decisions that determine the fate of individuals. Relying
on them for customer acceptance or rejection from a sanctions standpoint
risks legal breaches under the GDPR. This underscores the need for a cautious
approach to RPA, emphasizing risk management, legal liability and adherence
to privacy laws in any automation rollout.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 123
Data visualization
Data visualization enables AFC compliance teams to interpret and
communicate complex data effectively. By using different visual
representations, such as charts, graphs, and interactive dashboards, AFC
compliance professionals can identify patterns, anomalies, and suspicious
activities more efficiently. Data visualization enhances the decision-making
processes, leading to more effective risk mitigation and a strengthened
defense against financial crime.
Before you use data visualization, define the objectives. Understand the
message or insights you want to convey to your audience. Save
documentation utilized to develop data visualization material in a separate
folder. You might need it later for regulatory, legal, and audit purposes.
To effectively communicate data through visualization, you must understand
your audience. Tailor the visualizations to their expertise level, adjusting the
complexity and level of detail accordingly. Select visualization types, such as
charts, graphs, and heatmaps, that best suit the data. By aligning the
visualizations with their level of understanding, you can enhance
comprehension and engagement.
Maintaining accuracy and consistency is important when presenting data
visualizations. Validate the accuracy of the data to establish credibility and
reliability. Ensure that data sources and methodologies are consistent to
enable accurate interpretation and meaningful comparisons. Also, provide
context to enrich the visualizations. Explain the significance of patterns,
trends, or anomalies within the data. Provide contextual information to help
your audience understand insights that you have derived.
To optimize the user experience, consider incorporating interactive features.
These features could enable users to explore the data, drill down into details,
and discover deeper insights. Filters, tooltips, and interactive charts empower
users to interact with the visualizations and extract specific, relevant
information. When using data visualization, it is a good idea to seek feedback
from colleagues, stakeholders, or subject matter experts. Collaboration and
feedback can help refine the visualizations, ensuring they align with the
objectives and requirements of AFC compliance. Soliciting input from others
can provide valuable perspectives and enhance the effectiveness of the
visualizations in conveying information accurately and efficiently.
Building a Sanctions Compliance Program
Version 2.02
Page 124
Network analysis in AFC investigations
When investigating potential financial crime cases, it is important to consider
whether the suspicious activity is part of a wider network and whether there
could be additional criminals involved. One way to do this is through network
analysis.
Network analysis is an investigation tool that searches for common traits
among account holders and activities that could link them as part of a
network. The goal is to uncover hidden connections and expose illegal
schemes that might involve multiple parties, layering of transactions, and
complex structures. Some organizations build internal network analysis tools,
whereas others use third-party investigative software.
Network analysis involves mapping out complex networks of relationships and
transactions to gain a comprehensive understanding of the flow of funds and
the entities involved.
The network tool analyzes factors from the account under investigation, such
as postal codes, physical mailing addresses, names, email addresses, device
identification numbers, IP addresses, and telephone numbers. It then
searches the customer base for any similar information. Some network
analysis tools also allow searches outside the customer base for networked
parties, such as through social media.
Network analysis can identify customers registered to the same address as
the customer being investigated. Several customers registered to a single
address could indicate an organized crime group. Network analysis can also
help identify multiple customers who pay into one common suspicious
account, which could indicate a group of money mules.
By creating a virtual “picture” of suspicious behavior, network analysis can
quickly identify any co-conspirators and accounts with similar suspicious
behavior, streamlining the reporting process. Some jurisdictions allow
coordination among financial institutions in order to file consolidated
suspicious activity reports.

Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques

Page 5
Sanctions Evasion
Through Ownership and
Trade
Introduction
Setup: New dishwashers
You work in the trade finance department for a multinational bank based in
London. Your job involves reviewing pending trade deals for potential financial
crime red flags, watchlist matches, and sanctions list matches.
As of this morning, most of the screening alerts under your review have been
discarded as false positives. You decide to review one more trade deal before
your lunch break.
You are surprised to see that this deal originates in Kazakhstan. Your bank has
processed only a few deals involving Kazakhstan in the past few years. This is
because your bank identifies Kazakhstan as high-risk for Russia sanctions
evasion. You assume that this detail alone must have triggered the alert.
At first glance, this trade deal looks ordinary. Your initial review shows that the
seller of the goods is an entity incorporated in the UK. It is a longtime customer
of the bank. The buyer is a private limited company based in Kazakhstan. The
goods are listed as “consumer electronics.” The contract indicates that the
buyer will be getting brand-new dishwashers for £620 per unit.
You are not familiar with the dishwasher brand mentioned in the underlying
transaction. A quick internet search helps identify it as a premium household
appliances brand, with prices starting at £820. You are intrigued by this
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 6
discovery. Also, you are aware that in the UK dishwashers are classified as
luxury items if their unitary price exceeds £630. This triggers your suspicion
that the dishwashers might have been underpriced in the trade documents.
As a result, you start looking more closely into the companies involved. Your
UK-based customer has been classified as low-risk since last year’s KYC
review and presents no apparent red flags. However, the Kazakh buyer
presents several red flags. According to your research, the company was
established two years ago. It does not have a website and only generates five
search results on Google. In addition, its sole director and owner is linked to
several Russian companies.
What would you do?
Which action would you take next?
• Clear the review.
• Contact the UK company.
• Escalate to compliance.
• Contact the Kazakh company.
Feedback
The best action would be to escalate to the compliance department. You
discovered red flags that are strongly indicative of potential Russia sanctions
evasion. First, the underpricing of luxury consumer electronics. Second, the
seller’s location is in Kazakhstan, which neighbors Russia. Third, the buyer’s
lack of presence online and links to Russia. The compliance department is
well-equipped to research this situation further and make a well-informed
determination.
Clearing the review would be irresponsible. The alert does not match the
criteria for clearing. Letting the deal go through could result in your bank
facilitating the illegal exportation of luxury goods from the UK to Russia
through Kazakhstan.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 7
Contacting the UK company is not recommended. Customer outreach is an
option but given your initial findings, it should be considered only after
compliance has conducted their review. Despite the customer’s low-risk
classification, there is still some possibility that they could be involved in the
sanctions evasion scheme as the Kazakh buyer’s UK-based accomplice.
Contacting the Kazakh company would not be helpful. The Kazakh company is
not your customer, which means they have no obligation to share any
information with you. Trying to contact them might alert them to the fact that
they are being investigated for a potential sanctions evasion scheme.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Discuss the importance of knowing your customer in identifying evasion.
• Describe how customers try to conceal beneficial ownership.
• Explain how customers try to evade sanctions through trade-related
methods.
• Identify common maritime evasion typologies.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 8
Relationships are the primary
defense
Identifying evasion by knowing your
customer
The relationship an organization establishes with each customer is important
in supporting sanctions compliance and identifying evasion attempts. Evasion
refers to the deliberate act of concealing, misrepresenting, or manipulating
financial information or transactions to avoid detection.
Given the complexity of transactions, it is also necessary for organizations to
know the customers’ counterparties and the parties who own or control
them, to understand who might be attempting to evade sanctions. A
counterparty is the other side of a transaction. It can be the seller where the
customer is the buyer or vice versa.
Sanctioned individuals often know about due diligence, filters, and name
screening. They also understand the regulatory requirements they need to
meet to avoid detection.
Organizations should assess the effectiveness of their KYC controls and
processes to protect against potential violations. Organizations should also
stay up-to-date with regulatory changes and conduct thorough periodic
reviews of customer profiles in their databases against relevant sanctions lists.
The customer relationship should be the primary defense against sanctions
evasion. A customer relationship includes all contact with a potential
customer.
The relationship includes the dialogue that takes place during onboarding and
conversations that occur as the customer uses the organization’s products.
Individuals in management, marketing, operations, and compliance roles can
take part in this communication.
It is important to fully understand the nature of the customer, the business the
customer is engaged in, the structure of the company, its owners, and where
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 9
and with whom it does business. This includes counterparties. With this
knowledge, organizations can potentially detect activity that does not have a
valid business purpose and proactively identify unusual customer behavior.
Throughout the entire customer relationship, it is necessary to update the
customer profile.
While detection of evasion primarily depends upon the customer relationship,
organizations should update their sanctions screening processes accordingly.
This helps organizations ensure that they are not unknowingly complicit in
allowing sanctioned entities to conduct activities.
Identifying evasion by knowing your
customer (Case example: EuroCapital
Alliance Bank)
OFAC imposed penalties on EuroCapital Alliance Bank (ECAB) for violating US
sanctions. ECAB is a subsidiary of a large European bank specializing in wealth
management, as well as corporate and investment banking. Under OFAC
regulations, ECAB was required to cease processing transactions for
sanctioned individuals and entities.
Despite available KYC information, ECAB operated banking and securities
accounts in sanctioned jurisdictions. ECAB’s biggest client was a Russian
energy company, Siberia Oil, which is majority-owned by a sanctioned Russian
oligarch. Additionally, ECAB conducted business on behalf of its clients
through the US financial system.
As a subsidiary, ECAB failed to implement the compliance policies and
sanctions compliance practices of its parent company. As a result, ECAB
processed transactions for clients in Iran, Syria, Sudan, Cuba, and Russia. The
transactions involved counterparties, such as US correspondent banks and US
registered broker-dealers in securities.
ECAB had reason to know that these transactions involved sanctioned
jurisdictions because it had collected KYC data. The KYC files included
corresponding address information indicating their clients’ residences. In
addition, ECAB’s automated screening system should have flagged the
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 10
clients. Instead, the compliance department discovered the accounts during
a periodic review.
After this discovery, ECAB implemented internal controls to block certain
payments on sanctioned client accounts. However, ECAB’s controls failed to
prevent all types of transactions. Some clients continued to conduct
commercial transactions and securities-related transactions. ECAB
processed nearly 300 transactions, totaling approximately US$3 million. This
was an apparent violation of OFAC sanctions regulations.
Additionally, ECAB failed to investigate the ownership details of Siberia Oil. On
the day of his sanctions designation, the oligarch divested 45% of his 75%
ownership to his wife. Although the oligarch no longer had majority ownership,
he could potentially control the company through his wife and evade
sanctions.
ECAB voluntarily disclosed the violations to OFAC. It implemented extensive
measures to improve the compliance program and sanctions screening
controls. It also adopted procedures for screening clients to prevent further
risk.
Key takeaways
KYC data and controls should be:
• Used effectively to minimize the risk of sanctions violations
• Integrated into sanctions screening tools
• Reviewed to help identify the ultimate beneficiary of a transaction
• Tested and audited consistently to ensure compliance
• Monitored in subsidiaries by parent companies to prevent compliance
failures
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 11
Identifying evasion by knowing your
employee
Evasion, or the deliberate act of manipulating information to avoid detection,
can occur in two ways. External evasion occurs when a customer or third-
party violates regulations. Internal evasion occurs when an organization’s
employees commit a violation. An example of internal evasion could be an
employee failing to apply internal controls or intentionally overriding them.
Another example could be when an employee uses client accounts to
conceal the origin of funds.
To reduce the risk of noncompliance, as well as unethical or illicit behavior,
organizations should ensure they have a comprehensive understanding of
their employees. Establishing strong know your employee (KYE) processes
help to identify potential risks within an organization and protects sensitive
information and assets.
Organizations should conduct initial and ongoing KYE screening to identify
employees who might be noncompliant or susceptible to illicit activity. If there
were a lack of internal controls, or if controls were not properly implemented,
violations would be likely to occur. For example, employees could use various
methods to intentionally evade AFC controls and regulations, such as
removing information from a payment message. Another method is
whitelisting individuals or entities. If an employee were to potentially whitelist a
business or individual, they would be allowing high-risk payments and other
blocked transactions to pass through unflagged.
To prevent KYE risks, organizations should have dual controls in place. Dual
controls is a principle where at least two employees are required to complete
an internal control task. Sometimes, this is known as the “four eyes” principle.
This principle is often applied when verifying the employer, date of birth, and
name of the prospective customer. One staff member would conduct the
initial review, and a second person would review the work to verify its
accuracy.
In addition to ongoing KYE screening and controls, organizations should have
an internal escalation process for reporting any suspicious employee
behavior.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 12
Concealing beneficial ownership
Shell company versus front company or
proxies
People could use shell companies, front companies, and proxies for legitimate
purposes. However, financial criminals could use them illegitimately.
A shell company or corporation is a company that, at the time of
incorporation, has no significant assets or operations. Financial criminals might
use them to hide assets or funds.
A front company is an entity that is meant to shield another company from
liability or scrutiny. Financial criminals might use them to hide the identity of
end-users or the final destination of goods.
Proxies refer to having someone else function as if they were the actual
owner or controller of a fund, property, or business. Financial criminals might
use them as third parties to distance themselves from illegal activities.
Shell companies tend to exist primarily on paper and do not engage in
independent business operations, while front companies have a physical
presence and have characteristics of a legitimate business. Examples of front
companies include car washes and restaurants. Financial criminals could use
proxies to disguise beneficial ownership. Proxy schemes might involve
complex ownership structures and include business associates, family
members, and friends. In some instances, corporate records will only have the
names of nominee directors. These are individuals appointed to act on behalf
of the actual directors.
The illicit use of shell companies, front companies, and proxies undermines
the financial system. Financial criminals could use them to commit fraudulent
activity, hide beneficial ownership, evade sanctions, and avoid other AFC
regulations. To prevent misuse, organizations should know who their
customers are, and identify any connections or cross-connections between
other individuals and other organizations. These actions can help
organizations detect shell companies, front companies, and proxies as early
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 13
as possible. Third-party due diligence systems can be key to finding this type
of connected information.
Using different names to conceal beneficial
ownership
Financial criminals often use different names to conceal beneficial ownership.
They use different names to:
• Disguise the ownership of assets or funds.
• Mask their involvement in illegal business activities.
• Complicate tracing efforts and evade authorities.
To detect name-changing tactics, organizations should conduct ongoing
name-screening measures. Name screening helps identify potential high-risk
individuals, entities, and jurisdictions. Organizations should also use beneficial
ownership registries as an additional verification measure against attempts to
disguise beneficial ownership.
Changing the spelling, varying the order of names, or using a completely
different name is a common tactic used to conceal beneficial ownership. For
example, someone named Abu Mohamed al-Adnani might change parts of
his name to Abou, Abu-Mohammad, al Adnani, and so on. He could also
choose an entirely different alias such as Adrian Marks. If a high-risk
counterparty wanted to evade detection, they might change the spelling of
their name slightly. For example, an individual named Alex Johnson might
change his name to Alec Jonsson. He might provide a false address that does
not exactly match the listed information the organization has on file.
If an individual is subject to restrictions, they might act through a nominee, a
relative, or a representative, to make detection more difficult. Another
technique is to use generic names. For example, Foreign Trade Bank is a
state-owned bank and acts as the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea’s
(DPRK) primary foreign exchange bank. Although the DPRK is subject to
sanctions, an organization might overlook a name like Foreign Trade Bank in
transactions without further investigation.
Name screening involves checking individuals, entities, and companies against
watchlists, sanctions lists, and other databases. Organizations should use
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 14
name screening to detect multiple identities or aliases involved in a
transaction. Beneficial ownership registries collect and maintain identifying
information about the true beneficial owners of corporations and other
business structures. Combining the use of name screening and beneficial
ownership registries helps organizations prevent the misuse of different
names to conceal beneficial ownership.
Restructuring of ownership interests
Financial criminals often use tactics, such as restructuring ownership interests,
to make it appear as if they no longer own or control legal entities. The aim is
to prevent authorities and organizations from restricting their illicit activities.
The restructuring of ownership interests can occur in two ways—sham
divestments or “sale” of ownership interests and the reorganization of existing
ownership interests.
Sham divestments refer to transactions in which a financial criminal sells
assets or equity to close associates or other affiliated persons. The
reorganization of existing ownership interests occurs when a financial criminal
divests their ownership stake in a company to avoid reaching majority
ownership or significant ownership thresholds.
In most jurisdictions, ownership changes can occur quickly by simply filing the
information with the proper authority or registry for a small fee. This makes it
challenging for an organization to maintain diligent oversight of its customer
population, understand whom it can and cannot do business with, and ensure
that it stays compliant with relevant laws and regulations.
Sham divestments might create the illusion of legitimate divestment and can
include the use of friends, colleagues, subordinates, business partners, and
family members. The concept involves ensuring that the financial criminal no
longer holds the appearance of ownership of the assets or shares in a
company. However, they continue to influence or control the assets or the
company’s operations. For example, Luna is a known money launderer and
sells her 50% stake in a company to her daughter. Despite this divestment,
Luna plans to continue to exercise full control over the company. The sale is
an attempt to prevent the assets and activities of the company from falling
under the same restrictions that might apply to Luna.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 15
The reorganization of existing ownership interests is to avoid a total or
aggregate ownership of 50% or more, in relation to the aggregate ownership
rule. Financial criminals use this tactic to make it appear as if their ownership
falls below the threshold and allow entities to avoid sanctions restrictions.
Restructuring of ownership interests (Case
example: SOGAZ)
In 2014, the US issued sanctions on Bank Rossiya in Russia. Shortly before the
sanctions, the bank decreased its indirect stake in a Russian insurance
company called SOGAZ. The bank divested a percentage of its ownership
interests to a subsidiary. After it restructured its ownership interests, no single
sanctioned entity controlled 50% or more of SOGAZ. Although the bank and
other sanctioned entities continued to have stakes in SOGAZ, this divestment
ensured that SOGAZ would avoid penalties. It also prevented SOGAZ from
being subject to the same sanctions that applied to the bank.
The US listed Bank Rossiya as a Specially Designated National (SDN) on the
OFAC Crimea sanctions list. This move effectively excluded the bank from the
global financial system.
The bank had a 51% stake in SOGAZ through a wholly owned subsidiary called
ABRos. Before the sanctions came into effect, the bank decreased its indirect
stake in SOGAZ from 51% to 48.5%. It divested 2.5% of its stake to a subsidiary
of SOGAZ called SOGAZ Realty. Additionally, SOGAZ was 12.5% owned by
Gennady N. Timchenko through an entity he controlled called Kordeks. Mr.
Timchenko was also an SDN. The bank’s transfer of ownership allowed SOGAZ
to avoid sanctions restrictions and penalties since they no longer controlled
more than 50%.
Shortly after the bank’s transfer of ownership, OFAC introduced an aggregate
ownership rule. This rule aimed to ensure that a company would fall under
sanctions if the combined stakes of sanctioned entities added up to 50% or
more. Following the introduction of this rule, the bank’s subsidiary, ABRos,
further reduced its ownership to 32.3%. As a result, both the bank and Mr.
Timchenko, through the entity Kordeks, owned, in total, less than 50% of
SOGAZ. As sanctioned entities owned only 44.8% of SOGAZ, the company was
able to avoid penalties.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 16
Key takeaways
If ownership has changed:
• Apply additional due diligence to the entity’s ownership history even if the
ownership percentage is below the 50% threshold.
• Ensure a sanctioned entity is not in effective control through a complex
ownership scenario.
• Confirm that an entity did not alter its ownership history before contacting
the organization.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 17
Trade-related evasion methods
Concealing an end-user’s identity
Financial criminals will typically attempt to distance themselves from the
source or destination of funds.
To hide their identities, financial criminals try to use several evasion
techniques. One such example is concealment. When used for illicit purposes,
concealment is the act of hiding, disguising, or modifying certain information
in a transaction to avoid detection and prosecution.
In trade transactions, financial criminals might try to disguise the origin of
goods or funds by falsifying transaction details. They might also modify the
transactions to hide the receiver of goods. Some red flags consistent with this
type of evasion technique include third-party payments or overly complicated
trade deals.
In legitimate contexts, financial institutions and other organizations might
conceal an end-user's identity to protect sensitive information and maintain
privacy. However, in trade-based money laundering, concealment is used for
evasion purposes. It can take many forms.
Financial criminals might rely on subtle changes to their identity or the nature
of a transaction to generate and move funds, even in the face of global
sanctions and AFC compliance programs.
A common method is to falsify the nature of the parties involved in the deal.
For example, financial criminals might falsify the name of the seller or buyer to
avoid detection. They might also use a buyer in another jurisdiction to
purchase the merchandise and then re-export the goods. Another method is
to modify or remove the names of financial institutions involved in backing
instruments, such as advance payment guarantees or letters of credit.
Arms, ammunition, and other sensitive merchandise require an end-user
certificate that declares the goods will not be sold or shipped to someone
else. Financial criminals might falsify the information in an end-user certificate
to bypass regulatory authorities and verification controls.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 18
Red flags of concealment of an end user’s identity could include transactions
involving:
• Businesses with complex business structures.
• Unusual or anonymous payment methods.
• New accounts with minimal information.
• Multiple accounts or the use of third parties.
• Parties who frequently change identifying information.
Dual-use goods repackaged to conceal the
intended use
It could be simple for government agencies and organizations to detect
military-use items such as missiles or tank treads that have little to no
likelihood of civilian use. However, due to the nature of dual-use goods,
detection could be more challenging.
To hide some dual-use goods, such as sensors and navigation equipment,
financial criminals could put them in containers that include unregulated
contents to avoid suspicion. Other dual-use goods are easier to disguise and
repackage. For example, a microchip could be repackaged inside a civilian-
use appliance, like a coffee maker. Other illicit actors could then use the
microchip in weapons systems.
Dual-use goods represent the highest risk when they are uncommon or are
smaller components of a larger item. Those goods would be easier to
repackage.
To bypass national or international trade controls of dual-use goods or their
components, financial criminals will often repackage the goods to make them
appear low-risk. For example, scanning devices for use in missile guidance
systems could be repackaged in containers with unregulated contents, such
as medical hearing devices or musical equipment.
Financial criminals might repackage the goods and use a combination of
other evasion methods to ensure a successful trade deal. Often, repackaging
will include the falsification of trade documents on which the seller might
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 19
provide false information about the origin, components, or intended use of the
goods.
Red flags of repackaging dual-use goods in trade-based money laundering
can include packages that:
• Show evidence of a breach
• Show inconsistencies with the stated method of shipment or destination
• Have unusual pricing regarding the goods listed
• Have high insurance rates inconsistent with the goods listed
• Weigh more or less than the intended shipment
• Include goods that are not on the packing list
• Include goods that are on the packing list but are not declared on customs
forms
• Have altered labels, such as a vague point of origin or description of the
goods
Transshipment
Transshipment is the delivery of goods or containers to an intermediate
destination and then on to a further or final destination. It can also mean a
change of carrier or vessel.
Transshipment can be a necessary step to reach a final destination. For
example, an ocean vessel might not be able to navigate river waters, making
transshipment necessary by a smaller vessel, truck, or train. However,
transshipment could also be used by financial criminals as an evasion method.
They might hide a restricted shipment within another, unrestricted shipment.
They might also arrange for goods to be shipped or transferred to or from a
jurisdiction with weak regulations.
In legitimate contexts, transshipment is necessary when large quantities of
goods are going to multiple buyers in different jurisdictions. In this example, an
ocean vessel might need to distribute the goods, and intermediary stops
along the transportation route could be common.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 20
In illicit contexts, financial criminals use transshipment to bypass trade
regulations and restrictions. For the purpose of evasion, a common method is
to switch cargo between vessels at transshipment points to complicate
tracking efforts. Or, financial criminals might choose complicated transit
routes involving multiple transfers and modes of transportation.
Red flags of transshipment could include shipments with:
• Vague or unclear delivery timelines and no justification
• A freight forwarding firm listed as the final destination instead of a buyer’s
address
• Shipping routes that are uncommon or unusual for the listed goods
• Frequent changes in modes of transportation and no clear reason
• Inconsistencies in packaging or shipping documentation
• Stops in or near a high-risk jurisdiction
• Lack of identifying information on the involved parties
Transshipment (Case example: Sending
goods to Iran)
Industry Sensors Corporation (Corp.) (ISC) is a US-based manufacturer of
pressure transducers. These devices, which are designed to measure
pressure, are used in various industries. ISC has a global client base and
conducts frequent trade transactions.
ISC was approached by a prospective customer, Turkish-based Aqua Cleanup
Enterprises (ACE). ACE does not have much of a trade history and claims to
be using pressure transducers to produce clean water.
ACE tells ISC that they would prefer to have the goods shipped to a freight-
forwarding company in the Middle East. Despite the lack of information about
the customer and the unusual shipping instructions, ISC agrees to onboard
the customer. Subsequently, ACE ships the goods to Iran.
ACE was hired as a trusted third party to act as a fake customer or “straw
buyer” to purchase the goods from ISC. The real buyer, Karim, cannot buy the
pressure transducers directly, nor can he have them shipped to his address in
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 21
Iran, which is subject to US comprehensive sanctions. In addition, pressure
transducers are considered dual-use goods. They have a civil application for
use in the production of clean water. They can also be used to enrich uranium
as part of a weapons of mass destruction program. As such, they are subject
to export controls.
To conceal its trade ties to Iran, ACE arranged for the shipment to go directly
to a freight forwarding company in Bahrain. The cargo manifest contained
ACE’s name, a vague description of the intended use of the merchandise, and
no specific address for the final destination.
After the shipment arrived at the freight forwarding company’s address, the
packages containing the goods were immediately sent by air to Dubai. There,
the packages were added to a truck full of commercial electronics before
being loaded onto a vessel in a nearby port. The new shipment’s cargo
manifest says the goods are “consumer electronics” and are headed for
Bandar Charak, Iran.
Key takeaways
• Sanctions evaders could use transshipment and “straw buyers."
• It is common to use multiple modes of transportation for the transfer of
restricted goods.
• Exporters with high exposure to sanctions evasion should have robust KYC
controls.
Falsification of trade documents
International trade is reliant on paper-based documentation systems. Trade
documentation can include a contract, commercial invoice, shipping
manifest, insurance documents, and a bill of lading. A bill of lading is a piece of
paper stating what someone is shipping, where it comes from, and the final
destination.
This process of conducting trade is highly susceptible to misuse. For example,
financial criminals could falsify the description of illicit goods in trade
documents in a shipment to avoid detection. They could also alter the value of
illicit goods on commercial invoices.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 22
To avoid triggering controls, financial criminals often make minor alterations or
falsifications on trade documents. The most common methods are to
manipulate the price of goods and the nature of the goods.
Techniques for price manipulation include over and under-invoicing. In the
case of over-invoicing, financial criminals most likely want to make up for the
value of illicit goods. Therefore, they falsify an invoice by subtly increasing the
price. Financial criminals will provide a false justification for the price variation.
For example, the justification could be that the goods are high-quality or rare.
Documentation representing a shipment or trade transaction could be taken
at face value. Financial criminals manipulate details about the nature of illicit
goods in trade documents to minimize inspections. They might state that a
shipment contains “stones” on the shipping manifest when it actually contains
valuable gemstones, such as diamonds or rubies.
Financial criminals could also falsify details about the parties involved in
documentation, such as a contract or a bill of lading. They might falsify
identifying information, company names, or addresses if the trade involves
any high-risk entities, such as sanctioned individuals.
Given the numerous documents and details required in trade transactions,
there are plenty of opportunities for financial criminals to falsify information.
Authorities should always check trade documents to ensure authenticity and
integrity. Banks and other financial institutions should also ensure that all trade
transactions align with industry standards and follow compliance protocols.
Use of front and shell companies in
sanctions evasion (Case example: Mutassim
Gaddafi)
In 2022, a Maltese court ordered the Bank of Valletta (BOV) to return
approximately €90 million to Libya because of a front and shell company
scheme. Although front companies are not illegal, investigators found that
Maltese-registered companies harbored stolen funds from the Libyan state
treasury. Additionally, Mutassim Gaddafi, son of the late Libyan dictator,
Muammar Gaddafi, owned the companies.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 23
Despite being subject to sanctions, Mutassim maintained BOV accounts
through these companies. The Libyan state alleged BOV neglected to
perform proper due diligence that could have prevented Mutassim from
opening any accounts.
Despite EU sanctions that prohibited Mutassim from engaging in business
activities, he maintained several BOV accounts using companies he owned in
Malta.
To bypass controls, Mutassim used an alias as his name at BOV and in the
Maltese registrar of companies. Additionally, an auditor and former Malta
Labour Party treasurer, Joe Sammut, opened shell companies on Mutassim’s
behalf. By early 2011, Mutassim’s accounts accumulated at least €60 million.
One company, Mezen International, was closed in 2010 and the remaining
balance was transferred to a second company, Capital Resources Limited.
The main source of funding for the Mezen International account was another
company, Moncada International Limited. Sammut registered this company to
his address. The funds in the companies’ accounts were used to fund lavish
purchases, such as hotel accommodations, trips, and shopping sprees. There
was no credible evidence to suggest that Mezen International or Capital
Resources Limited operated as legitimate businesses. In the case of Capital
Resources Limited, the Libyan Attorney General stated that it had funds far
more than what it could have earned legitimately. As such, the Libyan state
considered the funds as illicit gains and not private funds. It requested BOV to
return all shares in the companies and the funds in the accounts.
Key takeaways
To identify evasion attempts, you should:
• Conduct due diligence on new customers.
• Understand the source of funds.
• Determine if shell companies have legitimate business purposes.
• Verify ownership structures to ensure owners are not using aliases or
bypassing regulations and restrictions.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 24
Transshipment, falsification of trade
documents, and use of front companies
(Case example: MaxStar)
MaxStar is a general trading business. They are registered in the United Arab
Emirates (UAE) and have held an account in a UAE bank for 15 months.
Recently, MaxStar purchased luxury goods including designer footwear,
clothing, and accessories from a US-based company. Shortly after receiving
the products in the UAE, MaxStar arranged a sale-purchase deal with a
company registered in Kazakhstan. MaxStar had not engaged in any business
activity since its inception. Due to Kazakhstan’s proximity to Russia, which has
been under sweeping Western sanctions, MaxStar’s recent transaction
triggered an alert in the bank’s transaction monitoring system.
The UAE bank requested additional trade documents from MaxStar. Several
details attracted attention.
• The outbound customs declaration showed that the price per unit of the
luxury goods was 30% lower than the listing price, although the goods
themselves were brand-new.
• The invoice for delivery listed the price of transportation as being three
times higher than the normal amount for the known delivery route to
Kazakhstan.
• The carrier was a Russian company.
• The bill of lading stated that the delivery address was in Kazakhstan, but the
name of the receiving party corresponded to an entity registered in Russia.
Due to the above red flags, the bank had reason to doubt the authenticity of
the information provided by MaxStar and to suspect that its customer was
circumventing the prohibition on the export of US-origin luxury goods to
Russia. Further research confirmed that MaxStar was a front company and
was used as an intermediary on behalf of a Russian individual, who was also
the owner of a Russian luxury clothing resale shop. The company in
Kazakhstan did not have a physical presence and had a parent company
based in Russia.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 25
Key takeaways
• Restricted goods are often mislabeled and undervalued to bypass
restrictions.
• Front companies located in a third country can act as intermediaries for
the purchase or sale of restricted goods.
• Verify end-user and conduct enhanced due diligence.
• Examine trade documents for any inconsistencies or unnecessary
complexity.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 26
Maritime
Switching cargo on the open sea
Ocean cargo vessels are required to maintain detailed records of their routes.
To switch cargo on the open sea or deliver cargo to a sanctioned destination
without leaving a record of their presence, financial criminals use methods
known as “loitering” and “spoofing.”
Vessels are required to have Automatic Identification System (AIS)
transponders to signal their location. These instruments can convey the
vessel’s course, speed, and cargo. In addition, they reveal the jurisdiction
where the vessel is registered. Some vessels will turn off their AIS
transponders to be able to anchor or drift in a high-risk area. This is known as
loitering.
Spoofing is another evasion method. This occurs when a vessel continues to
transmit AIS data while hiding its identity or manipulating its location.
Financial institutions and government agencies track vessels through satellite
monitoring. By loitering, two vessels switching cargo would not transmit
details of their precise location. This would not alert the authorities.
Vessels switching cargo can also engage in spoofing through the
manipulation of AIS data. Spoofing can be done by forcing a stronger signal to
override the AIS-transmitted signal or through other hacking means. For
example, a vessel can spoof its AIS and alter the details of its jurisdiction, route,
and final destination. Some financial criminals might spoof AIS signals to
project coordinates that appear to be coming from one vessel but are
coming from a decoy vessel.
Not all loitering or spoofing indicates illicit activity. For example, a vessel might
switch off its AIS transponder to hide its identity or location from pirates in
high-risk areas, or when the vessel might be in an area with poor signal
coverage.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 27
There are many red flags that could indicate the illicit switching of cargo on
the open sea. Red flags can include vessels:
• With scattered AIS tracking lines
• With close AIS proximity to a high-risk jurisdiction
• With more than one AIS number
• That are broadcasting the same AIS number as another vessel
• That frequently turn off their AIS, especially for extended periods of time
• That turn off their AIS in an area where the quality of coverage is good, or
where nearby vessels are transmitting AIS signals without disruption
Switching cargo on the open sea (Case
example: Spoofing)
Jacoby Carrier is a vessel registered in Mauritius, a jurisdiction off the coast of
Africa. It is scheduled to deliver shipments to various international ports. When
it was on its direct route from Hong Kong to California, the Automatic
Identification System (AIS) transponder stopped transmitting. The AIS
reactivated later, near the Sea of Japan (East Sea).
A naval vessel using data from surveillance satellites found these behaviors
suspicious. The naval vessel cooperated with the naval vessels of other
jurisdictions to exchange information. They found evidence that Jacoby
Carrier switched cargo with another vessel on the open sea.
While on its scheduled route, Jacoby Carrier’s AIS stopped transmitting signals
as it approached the southern tip of Japan. Upon reactivation of the AIS 36
hours later, Jacoby Carrier was anchored further down the scheduled route
along Japan’s coast. At the same time, a Japanese naval vessel was tracking
maritime traffic from North Korea. Following standard procedure, the
Japanese vessel attempted to communicate with Jacoby Carrier and inquire
what they were doing in that area. Jacoby Carrier’s captain blamed poor
signal coverage for the disruptions, but there was no supporting evidence.
The Japanese vessel suspected Jacoby Carrier was loitering and intentionally
turned off its AIS.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 28
The Japanese vessel reported this information to South Korean and US naval
vessels. Together they enhanced maritime surveillance. They realized that,
when the AIS turned back on, the AIS number and jurisdiction registration for
Jacoby Carrier had changed. Instead of broadcasting its registration in
Mauritius, it altered its AIS details so that it appeared to come from Singapore.
The naval vessels suspected Jacoby Carrier of manipulating AIS details to
conduct illicit activity. This is known as spoofing.
Satellite images showed that Jacoby Carrier added between 10 to 15 shipping
containers onboard from unmarked vessels that were not previously
accounted for. The naval vessels concluded that Jacoby Carrier engaged in
the switching of cargo with North Korea-linked vessels and violated sanctions.
Key takeaways
To enforce sanctions:
• Identify AIS best practices to detect AIS disruptions and manipulations.
• Report suspicious activity in accordance with international maritime
regulations.
• Governments use surveillance satellites to prevent sanctions violations.
• Maritime vessels cooperate and exchange information with other relevant
vessels, agencies, and authorities.
Falsification of trade documents (Case
example: Russian oil price cap)
In December 2022, the Group of Seven (G-7), the EU, and Australia jointly
imposed a price cap on the sale of Russian crude oil. In April 2023, OFAC
issued an advisory to warn US service providers, including commodities
brokers and oil traders, of possible circumvention of the policy. According to
the advisory, non-US persons involved in the export of Russian crude oil could
be providing incomplete or false documentation to mislead their US
counterparts.
To mitigate the risk of evasion, OFAC provided additional guidance identifying
key risk indicators or red flags. One such red flag is when trade
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 29
documentation contains vague price information, without breaking down the
total price paid into various components.
The price cap policy aimed to curtail Russia’s crude oil revenues while
maintaining a steady supply to global markets. Under the policy, G-7, EU, and
Australian maritime service providers were allowed to transport or otherwise
deal in Russian crude oil as long as it was sold at or below the price cap.
Regulators found that Russian-origin crude oil was often sold above the price
cap with the help of Oil Price Cap Coalition service providers due to falsified
trade documentation. For example, a common trade-based money
laundering technique involving price manipulation is used to inflate
intermediate costs, such as shipping, freight, customs, and insurance. These
intermediate costs are deliberately excluded from the price cap and falsified
on invoices, contracts, and receipts. In other instances, Russian-origin crude
oil is sold as originating outside of Russia, whether on the basis of fraudulent
certificates of origin or as a consequence of a blending with non-Russian oil.
To be able to take advantage of a safe harbor, organizations should apply risk-
based due diligence to identify and report suspicions of potential evasion. The
term safe harbor is a legal provision in a statute or regulation that provides
protection from legal liability. Risk-based due diligence includes scrutinizing
customer-provided self-attestations regarding compliance with the price cap
policy should there be any doubts over their accuracy and authenticity.
Key takeaways
Parties involved in trade transactions should:
• Follow regulatory guidelines.
• Conduct a close review of the document collection process.
• Maintain and retain key trade documentation.
• Review sanctions advisories for up-to-date typologies and red flags.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 30
Sanctions Evasion
Through Payments
Introduction
Setup: A new algorithm
You are a sanctions screening analyst at a major US financial institution. You
are based in its global compliance hub in Jacksonville, Florida. Your job involves
manually reviewing sanctions and watchlist screening alerts. Today, you
receive an overnight batch of alerts for review. One alert is for an international
wire transfer denominated in US dollars. The wire transfer is from a bank in
Austria to a bank in the Middle East. Both foreign banks have correspondent
accounts with your financial institution’s New York branch.
The alert was generated by a recently revised screening algorithm. It matches
new payment instructions against similar payment instructions that were
rejected within the preceding 30 calendar days. The degree of similarity, as
calculated by the algorithm, should be 90% or higher.
So, this payment message is at least 90% the same as a payment message
that was recently rejected. Thanks to the revised algorithm, you are
automatically shown the discrepancies between the two payment messages.
