Because the algorithm was recently revised, you check your screening
department handbook for directions. It explains that, when you find such a
discrepancy, you should manually review the two payment messages line-by-
line.
You scrutinize the two payment messages. You immediately spot what you
think is an indication of wire stripping, which is the intentional removal of
specific information from a SWIFT message to avoid flagging by an automatic
screening tool. The original payment message was rejected on the previous
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 31
business day, a Friday. The remittance information field contained free text
indicating that the payment was for the benefit of the National Iran Oil
Company. It also included a partial address in Tehran, Iran.
The second payment message does not refer to the Iranian company, nor
does it contain an Iranian address. It reads “National Oil Company.” You
suspect that one of your colleagues might have intentionally changed the
message between Friday and Monday. The changes were likely to avoid a
match to an OFAC-sanctioned entity or to Iran, which is a comprehensively
sanctioned jurisdiction under US law.
What would you do?
What should you do now?
• Email the internal hotline.
• Contact OFAC.
• Escalate to Compliance Investigations.
• Clear the alert.
Feedback
Your next best step is to escalate this alert to Compliance Investigations. While
the wire request can only be, and will be, rejected, it appears that an insider
intentionally changed the message to avoid detection. Compliance
Investigations staff are well-equipped to initiate an internal investigation into
this specific transaction. In addition, they could potentially take further action
concerning similar payment requests that were sent to your financial
institution earlier.
You have no reason to email the internal hotline at this time. You would use the
hotline if you became aware that the transaction had been processed. Or, if
you flagged the transaction to your management but no action was taken
within a reasonable timeframe.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 32
Contacting OFAC is not appropriate at this stage. Once the pending
transaction is rejected by your financial institution, it has 10 business days to
report it to OFAC.
Clearing the alert is not the best option. That would violate the applicable
sanctions regulations and your financial institution’s policies. In addition, it
would put your financial institution at risk.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Describe the payment methods used for evasion.
• Explain how SWIFT messages are separated.
• Discuss the risks associated with certain payments and transactions.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 33
Stripping
Stripping to evade sanctions
Stripping is the intentional removal or modification of information from a
record. The most common method involves removing key information from a
transaction, such as the sender’s name or the business’s name. Stripping
might occur with or without the knowledge of the other participants in the
transaction. The purpose of stripping is to avoid detection when an
organization screens a message against a sanctions list.
Organizations encounter compliance risks due to stripping. The removal of
key information from a record prevents screening tools from generating
alerts. This allows transactions involving high-risk customers, such as
sanctioned parties, to bypass controls.
The most common form of stripping is “wire stripping.” When a wire payment
travels through multiple parties before reaching the intended destination,
there are multiple opportunities for financial criminals to abbreviate, omit, or
alter information. For example, a complicit organization could alter the name
of sanctioned state-owned Iranian Airways “Mahan Air” to read “Mehan,” or “M.
Airways,” in a wire payment.
There could be many reasons for stripping information from wire payments
and transactions. Businesses might deliberately remove information to
generate profits or keep business relationships. For example, a business could
initiate a contract with an entity that the business knows is subject to
sanctions. In this case, the business might remove information from payments
or communication to avoid alerting others who are processing or are
otherwise involved in the transaction.
Motivations for stripping could also include patriotism, solidarity, or political
beliefs. For example, bank employees in jurisdictions outside the one that
issued sanctions might act with a sense of patriotism if they or their
government disagree with the sanctions. In such cases, managers might allow
stripping efforts to evade the sanctions.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 34
Another motivation could be self-interest. Stripping could be a result of
collusion between two or more parties like an organization’s staff, importer,
and supplier motivated by the profits of a business deal. For example, a
sanctioned party might offer a bribe to persuade a bank employee to avoid
generating an alert.
Stripping to evade sanctions (Case
example: Correspondent bank in Kenya)
A Sudanese bank is subject to OFAC sanctions and wants to send a payment
in US dollars to a beneficiary bank in another jurisdiction. The Sudanese bank
sends the payment information through a SWIFT message, or electronic
payment message, to a small correspondent bank in Kenya, some of whose
employees have been bribed. The Kenyan bank can process US dollar
payments through US financial institutions.
Upon receipt of the SWIFT message, the Kenyan bank strips key information
from the message fields before routing the payment to a second
correspondent bank in the US. The intent is for the US correspondent to route
the payment to the beneficiary bank. The Kenyan bank successfully facilitates
the payment through the US correspondent. However, the beneficiary bank
rejects the payment.
A legal entity in Sudan requests the Sudanese bank to send US$10,000 to a
beneficiary in Europe, which banks at a global financial institution. To evade
sanctions restrictions, the Sudanese bank relies on the colluding Kenyan bank
to strip information from the SWIFT payment instructions. The complicit
employees replace the Sudanese bank’s details, including its name, business
identifier code, and address, with those of a big and respectable Kenyan bank.
This gives the false impression that the Sudanese bank’s customer has an
account in Kenya. As a result of the wire stripping, the US correspondent does
not detect suspicious activity. As such, the US correspondent settles and
sends the payment to the beneficiary bank.
Upon the SWIFT message’s arrival, the beneficiary bank’s employees conduct
due diligence. The employees discover discrepancies between the payment
information from the US correspondent and the beneficiary customer’s
supporting documents. The supporting documents reveal the involvement of
the Sudanese bank. Based on the evidence of wire stripping attempts, the
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 35
beneficiary bank, which maintains a policy of complying with OFAC sanctions,
refuses to process the payment.
Key takeaways
• Conduct robust know your employee protocols to prevent internal
stripping violations.
• Establish guidelines and policies outlining employee conduct to ensure
compliance with relevant laws and regulations.
• Request supporting payment documents and exercise due diligence. This
will detect collaboration between multiple individuals and entities to
remove information.
Internal versus external stripping
Stripping is the intentional removal or modification of information from a
record to avoid detection against a sanctions list. Stripping can occur
internally or externally. Internal stripping occurs when an organization’s
employee removes information from a payment message or record. External
stripping occurs when a customer or third-party removes information from an
incoming payment message.
Stripping makes it difficult for screening controls and compliance staff to
accurately identify and restrict payments to and from high-risk individuals,
entities, or jurisdictions. Stripping is associated with compliance risks such as
reputational damage and monetary penalties.
Internal stripping for one organization, such as sending stripped payment
messages, might be external stripping for another organization. In this case,
an organization's employee receives a payment message without sanctioned
information. Stripping can happen externally, but when there are stripping
violations at an organization, they are rarely entirely external. There must be an
inside person to commit the crime. Although the number of reported
violations due to internal stripping has decreased in recent years, internal
stripping continues to pose a risk to organizations. Internal stripping could
expose an organization to AFC system weaknesses, data breaches, and data
manipulation. Establishing a strong know your employee framework could
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 36
help a financial institution identify employees who might have a higher risk of
engaging in internal stripping.
External stripping remains a serious threat to organizations. Financial criminals
could use advanced technology and techniques to remove information from
transactions, such as wire transfers, and create complex structures to evade
detection. They might also use other individuals’ accounts to funnel funds.
Both internal and external stripping could put an organization at risk for
regulatory fines and enforcement actions. To mitigate the evolving risk of
stripping, organizations should collaborate and share information with financial
institutions and regulatory bodies. Providing ongoing training to employees
and implementing robust CDD, transaction monitoring, and screening controls
could also be helpful.
U-turn payments
A U-turn payment is a transaction a bank performs in one country for the
benefit of a bank in another country. A bank from Country A sends a
transaction through a bank in Country B using an offshore bank. In most cases,
U-turn payments involve the US financial system and have both their origin
and destination outside of the US, but the payments are in US dollars.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 37
Banks can use U-turn payments to route funds to sanctioned entities and
jurisdictions. For example, U-turn payments are commonly associated with US
sanctions, particularly those imposed on Iran. However, due to increased
regulations and restrictions, this is mostly a historical technique that is no
longer in use.
Before 2008, the US Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) allowed a limited
exemption under a general license from restrictions that prevented US banks
from processing transactions involving Iran or the Iranian government. OFAC
permitted US dollar transactions under the condition that a US bank was not
directly processing a payment for an Iranian entity. To do this, the originating
and beneficiary banks would use offshore banks. This meant that any money
that passed through the US clearing system exclusively came from, or was
sent to, the offshore banks.
In 2008, the US revoked the U-turn exemption related to Iran. However, some
banks continued to process U-turn payments, in violation of sanctions. By
using U-turn payments, they could defy the targeted sanctions against
individuals, entities, and jurisdictions on the OFAC Specially Designated
Nationals list. Those banks were able to do so because they stripped, or
removed, specific terms that would cause automated screening tools to block
a payment.
To mitigate the risks, compliant US correspondent banks implemented a
standardized payment message format. This format enhanced transparency
and reduced errors in cross-border payments because it required additional
payment details. As a result, it became more difficult for U-turn payments to
flow through the US financial system undetected.
U-turn payments (Case example: Société
Générale SA)
Société Générale SA (SG) is an international banking and financial services
company headquartered in Paris with a licensed branch in New York. In 2018,
the New York Department of Financial Services (DFS) fined SG US$325 million
for violating US economic sanctions. During the investigation, the DFS
determined that SG had used U-turn payments from 2003 to 2013 through its
US-based branch to execute billions of US dollars in illegal and nontransparent
transactions. The U-turn payments were made on behalf of parties located in
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 38
jurisdictions subject to US embargoes or sanctions, including Iran, Sudan,
Cuba, and Libya.
U-turn payments were allowed under laws governing economic sanctions
until November 2008. However, the execution of U-turn transactions often
triggered alerts at US clearing institutions, which resulted in longer processing
times than the customers expected. Elevating customer service over
compliance, SG employees in Paris developed a procedure specifically for
payments in US dollars. This procedure involved dividing payment instructions
into two message streams. The first SWIFT message, or MT103, included all
details about the transaction and was sent directly to the sanctioned
beneficiary’s bank. The second SWIFT message, or MT202, did not include
details about the underlying parties to the transaction and was sent to the
bank’s US-based branch. If identifying information was included in the MT202
payment message, SG removed information from the message that could
trigger an alert, including the addresses and names of Iranian and Sudanese
parties.
The DFS determined that a principal factor that allowed these unlawful
practices was the inadequacy of SG’s sanctions-related internal controls. In
2003, the bank had no centralized sanctions compliance function and lacked
employee training. Its policies were not centralized for all relevant employees.
Despite SG’s operations staff highlighting issues with US dollar payments, the
compliance function failed to provide the required clarification or appropriate
guidance to ensure compliance with US sanctions. Written group policies
were primarily focused on French and European sanctions, with a brief note
on US sanctions compliance.
Key takeaways
• Implement adequate internal controls, including centralized sanctions
policies and employee training.
• Provide enhanced oversight of subsidiaries, including their compliance
with applicable sanctions laws and regulations.
• Scrutinize payment messages for possible illegal and nontransparent
transactions, including U-turn payments.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 39
Separating SWIFT messages
SWIFT messages
SWIFT stands for Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial
Telecommunications. SWIFT is a system that organizations use in wire transfer
payments. SWIFT messages have predefined fields that serve to standardize
and secure the exchange of financial information. Additionally, there might be
narrative fields that allow for descriptions or additional information to provide
context about a transaction.
SWIFT messages are categorized into nine Message Type (MT) categories.
The most used MTs within those categories are the MT103, MT202, and MT202
COV. To comply with changing international standards, SWIFT introduced
another message format, MX, which replaces the MT format.
SWIFT message formats vary based on the type of transaction activity. The
MT103 is for single-customer credit transfers and has specific fields necessary
for the accurate and efficient processing of these payments. It includes
multiple fields dedicated to information such as the ordering customer,
beneficiary customer, amount, currency, value date, and correspondent
banks. Field 50A identifies the originator of the payment. Field 59A contains
details about the beneficiary customer, such as their name and address. Field
72 provides a narrative message about the transaction.
MT202 and MT202 COV are for bank-to-bank transfers and have similar
predefined fields. Field 52A identifies the financial institution initiating the
payment. Fields 53A and 54A indicate the originator’s and beneficiary’s
respective correspondent banks. Field 57A identifies the financial institution
with which the beneficiary party holds an account. Unlike MT202, MT202 COV
has additional information related to the cover payment in the "sequence B"
section. This section includes the names of the originator and beneficiary
parties involved in the underlying payment transactions.
With the ISO 20022 or the MX format, the MT format is no longer allowed after
November 2025 and the MX format is the only acceptable standard for cross-
border payment instructions. The MX format allows for more transparency
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 40
and more data in a simplified format. It also improves communication and
cooperation between financial institutions.
SWIFT messages for international payments
The SWIFT messaging system is the world’s most widely used messaging
network for facilitating international payments. This is due to the network’s
robust levels of consistency, transparency, and security. Correspondent
banking services use the SWIFT network to process payments between
respondent banks and third-party banks, execute transactions in foreign
currencies, and exchange valuable account information.
A benefit of SWIFT messaging is that it provides a standard for processing
international payments. Without standardization, international transaction
processing could become slower, less reliable, and less secure.
SWIFT messaging
Financial institutions often need to process international, cross-border
payments. If the originator and beneficiary are in different parts of the world,
they can use SWIFT messaging to engage in financial transactions. For
example, Banks A and B are, respectively, the originator’s and beneficiary’s
banks in their home countries. Banks C and D have correspondent
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 41
relationships with Banks A and B, respectively. The originator approaches Bank
A in Frankfurt and arranges to send a payment to the beneficiary's account in
Bank B in Hong Kong. Bank A sends an MT103 payment message to Bank B. If
Bank A has an account with Bank B in the currency of the transaction, then no
other SWIFT message is necessary.
However, if the transaction needs to be in a foreign currency and Bank A does
not hold an account in this currency with Bank B, then Bank A has two options.
Under ISO 15022, it could use a method called the MT103 serial payment. Bank
A sends the MT103 message to its correspondent bank, Bank C. Bank C will
then send the message to Bank B's correspondent bank, Bank D. Afterwards,
the funds will reach Bank B.
Alternatively, Bank A can use the cover method with ISO 15022. Bank A will
send an MT103 message to Bank B. At the same time, it will send an MT202
COV message to its correspondent, which is Bank C. The MT202 COV
message must have the originator and beneficiary information for banks to
conduct proper screening. Bank C then sends another MT202 COV to the
other correspondent bank, in this case, Bank D. Upon receiving the MT202
COV message, Bank D would send an MT910 Credit Advice to Bank B to
complete the transaction.
How a SWIFT message gets separated
When financial criminals separate SWIFT messages, the world’s most widely
used messaging network for facilitating international payments, they expose
financial institutions to the risk of sanctions evasion. Separating messages
means sending two different types of messages for the same payment with
incomplete or different information. This type of sanctions evasion happens
when more than two banks engage in the process.
When the MT202 was the only payment technique to facilitate bank-to-bank
transfers, financial criminals could separate SWIFT messages to avoid
detection. This is because the limited scope of the MT202 allows for the
removal of key payment information that would alert the sanctions screening
systems. However, with the implementation of the MT202 COV, attempts at
evasion became increasingly difficult.
To decrease processing time and ensure transparency, it is common for an
originating bank to use the cover method, whereby the originating bank sends
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 42
the MT103 directly to the beneficiary bank while sending the MT202 COV
indirectly to the involved correspondent banks. Before the creation of the
cover method, this situation would leave the intermediary bank with only a
partial view of the overall transaction. The intermediary would receive an
MT202 payment, but the underlying MT103 did not contain the required
information that is in an MT202 COV. Because the transfer appeared as a
bank-to-bank payment, the intermediary bank could not identify the
originator and ultimate beneficiary. Additionally, due to the limited amount of
information the MT202’s format could accommodate, the intermediary could
not effectively filter or screen payment information.
SWIFT’s introduction of the MT202 COV marks an improvement in the
situation. With the MT202 COV, the payment message contains a “sequence
B” field that must be identical to fields 59 and 59A of the underlying MT103. The
sequence B information allows for the identification and screening of the
underlying parties.
Additionally, ISO 20022 or the MX format replaces the MT standards and can
carry more data to allow for robust sanctions screening. The MX format
exchanges rich, structured, and high-quality data that could eliminate
payment separation schemes.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 43
Payments and transactions
Using nested accounts to evade sanctions
Correspondent banking is necessary for the global financial system to
function smoothly. Regulators understand and accept this, provided there is
transparency and banks identify the originator and ultimate beneficiary of
transactions. If correspondent banking relationships include nested accounts,
however, the correspondent cannot effectively comply with KYC policies,
suspicious activity reporting, ongoing monitoring regulations, and sanctions
compliance. This is because the correspondent might be unaware that it is
collaborating with a customer’s customer or second respondent.
Due to their lack of transparency, nested accounts are challenging to regulate
and could allow the correspondent to unknowingly facilitate illicit transactions.
This could expose the correspondent to regulatory penalties and reputational
damage.
If transactions involve nested accounts, the correspondent is unable to follow
KYC policies and ensure strong due diligence. This is because nested
accounts could provide access to third-party financial institutions that are not
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 44
the direct customers of the correspondent bank. In turn, third-party financial
institutions could unknowingly facilitate a transaction involving a sanctioned
individual, entity, or jurisdiction. Sanction evaders could use this lack of
transparency in nested account transactions to conceal ownership structure.
As a result, the correspondent would be unable to identify the originator and
ultimate beneficiary of a transaction.
Sanction evaders could use nested accounts to often layer funds and pass
them through several financial institutions. As such, they might use nested
accounts to prevent the tracing of funds. Additionally, transactions within
nested accounts might lack proper documentation or contain falsified
documentation. This might complicate identification efforts and enable
sanctioned individuals and entities to use nested accounts.
Before establishing correspondent accounts, a bank should identify the
respondent bank’s owners, consider its jurisdiction, and understand the nature
of its regulatory oversight. Additionally, a bank should require the disclosure of
nested account relationships as part of account opening and ongoing risk
profile reviews. If nested accounts are involved, the bank should warrant a
higher level of due diligence and screening on the respondent and third-party
financial institutions.
Using nested accounts to evade sanctions
(Case example: Halkbank)
In 2019, US federal prosecutors charged Halkbank, Turkey’s second-largest
state-owned bank, with fraud, money laundering, and sanctions violations.
According to prosecutors, Halkbank’s offenses were related to the bank’s
participation in a multibillion-dollar scheme to evade sanctions on Iran.
In this scheme, Halkbank's senior executives used nested accounts to
facilitate the transfer of funds to Iran and deceive US regulators. The nested
accounts were under the names of Iranian state-owned entities and used in
international transactions in connection with Iran’s oil and gas exports. To
avoid detection of illicit nested account activity, the senior executives
facilitated transactions through multiple corporate entities, forged
documents, and obscured wire payment instructions. In return for their
complicity, they received kickbacks.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 45
Regulators reported that billions of dollars’ worth of proceeds from the sale of
Iran’s oil and gas were kept in accounts at Halkbank under the names of the
Central Bank of Iran, National Iranian Oil Company, and National Iranian Gas
Company. Because of US comprehensive sanctions on Iran, it was initially
difficult for the Iranian government to access the funds in those accounts.
Using nested accounts, however, Halkbank’s senior executives helped the
Iranian authorities bypass sanctions restrictions for four years.
The nested accounts made it easier for the proceeds from the export of
Iranian hydrocarbons to be used to buy gold, for the ultimate benefit of the
government of Iran. They also facilitated transactions that had been
deliberately falsified. In fact, the senior executives made the transactions
appear as purchases of food and medicine by Iranian customers. This
falsification purposely fell within OFAC’s “humanitarian exception” from
certain Iranian sanctions. However, Halkbank’s senior management knew that
the purchases were not for food or medicine. The use of the nested accounts
allowed for the transfer of approximately US$20 billion worth of restricted
funds to Iran.
Key takeaways
To mitigate the misuse of nested accounts:
• Consider the respondent’s regulatory oversight.
• Identify the ultimate originator and beneficiary of a transaction.
• Require respondents to disclose nested account relationships.
• Conduct enhanced due diligence and screening before entering
correspondent banking relationships.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 46
Offering downstream services
Correspondent banking arrangements carry particular risks, one being
downstream services. Often correspondent banks expect to process
transactions on behalf of their respondent banks and their respondent banks’
customers. But if the respondent bank itself offers services to other banks that
offer services to their customers and other banks, then the original
correspondent has a much greater risk than it foresaw when it opened an
account for its respondent.
Because of this, most jurisdictions’ AFC regulations require that an increased
level of due diligence be applied, particularly when legitimate services are
provided to a respondent bank in another jurisdiction. The correspondent
bank will provide clearing services or products to its downstream
correspondent that the originating bank would not be able to access on its
own.
Under ordinary circumstances, the downstream correspondent will supply the
identifying information that is required, and legitimate transactions will be
processed. However, if the downstream correspondent has a motive to evade
sanctions for one of its customers, or its customer conceals information
before presenting the transaction for processing, then the upstream
correspondent bank is exposed. That bank will process a transaction for which
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 47
it has incomplete or incorrect information, and it could unwittingly participate
in sanctions evasion.
To protect itself, the correspondent bank should develop an effective due
diligence process for its customers to adequately assess the risk presented by
each one. This risk profile could be a combination of the following factors:
• The size of the downstream bank customer
• The anticipated transaction volume
• The products being accessed
• The type of customer profile the downstream bank has
• The strength of the downstream correspondent’s AFC programs
• The regulatory jurisdiction the downstream correspondent is under
• The geographic scope of their business—both the downstream
correspondent’s location and where its customers do business
By quantifying these factors upfront and reviewing them regularly, the
upstream correspondent will be far more likely to foresee a problem or
identify unusual activity early on.
Cryptoasset sanctions indirect risk
Internationally, enforcement agencies are paying attention to sanctioning
cryptocurrency addresses, with the US Office of Foreign Assets Control
leading the way since 2018 when it first added virtual currency addresses to its
Specially Designated Nationals List. Virtual asset service providers (VASPs)
must recognize the related customer risks and red flags and understand how
to mitigate them.
Many tools can screen cryptocurrency transactions and identify exposure to
sanctioned entities. It can be difficult to determine whether a customer is at
fault for being linked to a sanctioned entity. Many customers use VASPs and
other nested exchange services. This method combines the transactions of
multiple people at once, which means a customer's funds could be indirectly
linked to a sanctioned entity without the customer’s knowledge. Nested
exchanges act as intermediaries in cryptocurrency. A nested exchange holds
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 48
accounts with established, well-known cryptocurrency platforms and allows
its customers to trade with those accounts through its service.
Large VASPs often act as intermediaries for other nested cryptocurrency
exchanges that do not carry out the trading themselves. These nested
exchanges offer services to their customers, but they trade on another
centralized VASP. Nested exchanges carry a significantly higher risk for VASPs
because they often do not know who is sending and who is receiving the
funds. Additionally, nested exchanges often have weak AML and KYC
requirements. They might also allow high-risk payment methods, such as in-
person cash payments.
Regarding sanctions risk, the following indirect risks and red flags should be
considered:
• A VASP doing business with a nested exchange might not know what AML
and KYC measures the nested exchange is applying and who their
customers are.
• Weak KYC measures could allow the onboarding of companies with
sanctioned individuals.
• Some nested cryptocurrency services could be established for the sole
purpose of evading sanctions.
• Transaction monitoring rules might not be set up correctly to identify
potentially sanctioned payments.
• Certain cryptocurrencies, such as privacy coins can have built-in privacy
features, making it difficult to ensure compliance with sanctions screening.
• The potential involvement of cryptocurrency mixing services, or tumblers,
which serve to blend cryptocurrency funds from multiple sources to
obscure their origin, poses additional compliance challenges.
To mitigate indirect sanctions risk, VASPs that conduct business with nested
exchanges need to understand their target customers and the AML and KYC
requirements in place at the nested exchange. They must act on transaction
monitoring alerts quickly and effectively.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 49
Cryptoasset sanctions indirect risk (Case
example: BitCab)
The US Department of Justice filed a criminal complaint charging a US citizen,
John Anon, with conspiring to violate US sanctions against Iran. In fact, there
was probable cause to believe that Anon used an IP address in the US to
conspire to operate an online payments and remittances platform, BitCab,
based in Iran. The operation involved establishing a US-based front company
to facilitate the purchase of BitCab’s domains, using US financial accounts to
conduct transactions on behalf of BitCab and its customers, and transferring
cryptocurrency to BitCab-linked accounts.
BitCab advertised its services as designed to evade US sanctions by
facilitating seemingly untraceable cryptocurrency transactions. However, by
tracking the cryptocurrency, the US government found that Anon was
operating BitCab.
Anon failed to hide BitCab’s illegal activity, having been misled by the
perceived anonymity of cryptocurrency. He registered various domain names
for BitCab, which were paid for by the front company. Anon’s identifying
information, including his US residence IP address, was also linked to a US-
based online financial institution account tied to BitCab. This account received
and sent thousands of dollars to Iran on behalf of BitCab’s customers. Anon
also opened an account with a US-based virtual asset service provider (VASP).
This account was registered using one of Anon's personal email accounts and
funded with fiat, or government-regulated, currency from a US financial
institution. He used this VASP account to send thousands of dollars to two
accounts at foreign-based VASPs. These foreign VASP accounts were
accessed from IP addresses that were identified to be from Iran shortly after
the funds were sent. Anon used these two accounts to transfer over US$10
million worth of cryptocurrency between the US and Iran.
The KYC information collected from the VASPs and the US financial institution
attributed Anon as the source of illicit activity. For this reason, the US
government established probable cause that Anon violated its Iran sanctions
and prosecuted him accordingly.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 50
Key takeaways
• Cryptocurrency can be traceable.
• Failure to comply with sanctions regulations, including those related to
cryptocurrency, could lead to prosecution.
• Forged IP addresses can obscure the involvement of sanctioned
jurisdictions in cryptocurrency transactions.
• Front companies can be used to obtain cryptocurrency domains.

Investigating a
Sanctions Breach
Introduction
Setup: A potential violation
You are a lead investigator for a German multinational corporation that
manufactures and sells sensitive medical equipment. Some of this equipment
is classified as dual use, meaning it can serve both civilian and military
purposes. The corporation’s customers are based all over the world.
The corporation has a robust compliance framework for managing sensitive
exports. This includes strict adherence to international sanctions regimes and
thorough customer screening. However, the organization is also committed to
social responsibility and contributes significantly to global humanitarian
initiatives.
Today, your boss comes to you looking very concerned. He explains that a
journalist reached out to him asking about potential sanctions violations
involving an Iranian military organization. Allegedly, your corporation provided
resources to an entity that is designated under the EU Iran Nuclear Regime. If
the allegations are confirmed, the violations could result in substantial financial
penalties, the loss of business licenses, and reputational damage. You must
immediately begin investigating the situation.
You begin your investigation by reviewing export documentation. You
discover that your corporation supplied medical devices to several public
hospitals in Iran under a humanitarian exemption. The objective of the
exemption was to improve healthcare access for underprivileged populations.
Further analysis indicates that one of the beneficiary hospitals is controlled by
Iran’s Ministry of Defense. The hospital operates a laboratory which is a
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 52
separate legal entity. The laboratory has been sanctioned for conducting
scientific research linked to nuclear weapons development. The involvement
of this entity potentially constitutes a breach of international sanctions.
As the lead investigator, you must advise on next steps to mitigate regulatory
and reputational risks while ensuring the integrity of the investigative process.
There is increasing pressure from senior management to act swiftly.
What would you do?
What should you do next?
• Interrogate the employees involved.
• Seek support from senior management.
• Hand over the investigation to regulators.
• Issue a press release.
Feedback
The best response is to seek support from senior management. This will help
ensure that you have adequate resources for the investigation. You should
then work with senior management to allocate these resources and formulate
a plan that ensures compliance with laws and regulations. You must then
document findings, preserve evidence, and communicate with your team to
efficiently conduct the investigation and ensure the integrity of the process.
Interrogating the employees involved is not the best response. While taking
swift action in this way could be potentially helpful, it is risky to conduct
interrogations without first securing senior management support and
developing a strategic plan. This approach runs the risk of missing critical
steps, overlooking legal considerations, or creating unnecessary confusion.
Handing over the investigation to regulators is not the best response. While
involving authorities might eventually become necessary, it would be
premature at this time. Your team could lose control over the process, and
your company might not have a clear picture of the situation before the
authorities intervene. Instead, a comprehensive internal investigation should
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 53
come before any engagement with authorities to ensure that the company is
well prepared and aware of all relevant facts.
Issuing a press release is not the best response. Public disclosure of a
sanctions violation without thoroughly understanding the facts could lead to
significant reputational damage, unnecessary panic, and legal implications.
Transparency is important, but this should be carefully balanced with strategic
communication that protects an organization’s reputation and interests.
Premature disclosure may not only harm the company but also hinder the
investigation.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Discuss how an alert leads to an investigation.
• Describe how to research a possible sanctions breach.
• Identify the factors that contribute to concluding an investigation.
• Describe the appropriate steps following an investigation.

You are accountable
Controls across the customer life cycle
Organizations manage financial crime risk by implementing several controls
throughout the customer’s life cycle. This includes implementing controls at
onboarding, on an ongoing basis throughout the customer’s relationship with
the organization, and during offboarding. The key controls that organizations
implement include:
• Know your customer (KYC)
• Transaction monitoring
• Investigations
KYC is one of the first controls executed. At pre-onboarding, it includes the
creation of a customer profile and concludes when a decision is made to
onboard the customer. KYC is also an ongoing control. Organizations are
expected to keep the customer’s records updated throughout the customer
relationship. Transaction monitoring and investigations are both ongoing
controls.
KYC at onboarding includes common types of due diligence. These include
identification and verification, simple, standard, and enhanced due diligence,
and screening for sanctions, political exposure, and adverse media. Your
organization will designate processes and thresholds.
Almost every organization creates a customer profile with a risk score. This is
used to decide whether a customer should be onboarded. Ongoing KYC
controls, also known as a KYC refresh, involve reviewing customer records
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 55
periodically and during trigger events. Ongoing KYC controls use a risk-based
approach to screen customers and capture any changes in customers’
records. Perpetual KYC is the practice of maintaining accurate and up-to-date
customer data. This is done through real-time updates based on changes in
customer behavior and circumstances.
Transaction monitoring is an ongoing effort that looks at the customer’s past
activity over an extended period. It detects complex patterns that could
indicate money laundering, tax evasion, or other financial crimes. Transaction
monitoring focuses on unusual transactions, the circumstances leading up to
them, and the counterparties involved. When an alert is generated, an analyst
reviews the alert and either clears it or escalates it.
Investigations start when a customer’s profile or behavior raises questions
during ongoing KYC or transaction monitoring. If the investigation concludes
that a suspicion has occurred, a suspicious activity report (SAR) must be filed.
How a case is handed off to an investigator
Once an alert reaches a case level, there is a presumption that it has the
potential for a suspicious activity report (SAR) filing or a sanctions violation.
Rather than scramble for all the documents needed to start a case,
investigators will often receive a standard package of information to begin an
investigation. Handing off a case to an investigator marks the beginning of the
investigation. The investigator must review and analyze the information to
decide whether to file a SAR or disclose a sanctions violation—and what to
include.
That information becomes the case file of record, so it must have correct
information—and as much information as one might need to get the case
going. This should include information about the alert or escalation, why and
how it made its way to the investigator’s desk, and context for what steps to
take next.
At the beginning of the investigation, the clock has already begun ticking. The
investigator must determine whether to file a SAR within the regulatory time
frame. The investigator must receive and understand the brief narrative that
describes what triggered an alert. Examples include a high-risk country, the
nature of an information-sharing request, or what caused the manual
escalation. These sources help the investigator put whatever information they
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 56
see next into context. The handoff can also include additional supporting
documents, such as account opening forms, bank statements, sanctions lists,
screening tools and identifiers, and even surveillance footage. This
documentation is provided during the handoff to help provide direction for
the investigation while relying on the investigator’s skills to determine whether
a sanction violation has occurred, or whether to file a SAR.
Complex investigations require clear guiding procedures, which might have
several steps and requirements. As the investigation moves forward, the
investigator will be able to rely on those instructions to provide increasing
structure to their case narrative so that all the steps continue to the next
reviewer. This could be the sanctions compliance officer, SAR approver, or law
enforcement, who will then rely on that documentation and supporting
narrative to better understand what the suspect customer did, and why it is
suspicious.
Sources of investigations
Sources of investigations provide you with the initial direction on how to craft
your hypothesis and plan your investigation. Internal sources of investigations
come to you in the form of escalations from teams such as KYC and
transaction monitoring, internal hotlines, and referrals from customer-facing
employees. Internal referrals may come in the form of a suspicious activity
referral form of the financial institution’s own design.
External sources of investigation are typically received by a dedicated team in
your organization. If there is a result that cannot be discounted, it will be
referred to you for further investigation.
External sources of investigation can arise from negative media searches,
which are often performed by relationship managers. Other external sources
of investigation can come from your customers, the filing of a civil lawsuit
against the organization, government subpoena or search warrant,
production orders from law enforcement agencies, information requests
from a financial intelligence unit, and recommendations from bank regulators.
Once you receive an external regulatory request, you need to screen it
against your customer database to see if there are any true positive matches.
If there is a true positive match, you can begin planning your investigation.
First, start by cross-referencing the true positive alert with any open-source
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 57
intelligence that your organization subscribes to, or try using common search
engines. Second, determine the internal sources of investigation you will
require. These include the type of documents you need to locate and review,
the employees you need to interview, and the internal legal counsel you may
have to consult in situations of attorney-client privilege. Third, look for any
other independent external sources of information that will help you in your
investigation.
Sources of investigations (Case example:
Faisal Nadeem)
A bank’s sanctions screening system raised an alert for a payment received
by one of its customers. The alert was for a potential match to an individual on
a sanctions list. The analyst, Omer, analyzed the alert and gathered
information from open-source intelligence, including list references. Next, he
reviewed and analyzed the beneficiary’s KYC files and transaction history.
Based on his findings, Omer decided to escalate the alert to a case. Once the
investigator received the case, she reviewed Omer’s findings and
documentation.
Next, she requested additional information from an external source and
internally from the relationship manager. She required this additional
information to decide whether to close the case or file a suspicious activity
report (SAR).
The sender of the incoming payment was identified as a potential match to a
person on OFAC’s Specially Designated Global Terrorist list. Omer attempted
to gather information from the sanctions list to verify the sender’s full name,
date of birth, nationality, and residency. Additionally, he reviewed the
transaction history and the bank’s KYC files for information on the relationship
between the sender and the beneficiary, the purpose of the transaction, and
any other relevant details regarding the payment. By reviewing the payment
message, Omer discovered that the sender’s name was Faisal Nadeem.
However, the other data that he found through search engines was
inaccurate or could not be validated. Omer could not confirm the match. He
escalated the alert to Sana, an investigator.
After the case was handed off to Sana, she reviewed the sources and
immediately requested additional information about Mr. Nadeem from the
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 58
ordering financial institution. It verified his name and nationality, but not his
date of birth or residency status. Since this was the first payment the bank
received from Mr. Nadeem, Sana requested further information from her
bank’s relationship manager to clarify the customer’s relationship with the
sender and provide evidence of the purpose of the payment. This would assist
Sana’s investigation and help her make a final decision on whether to close
the case, self-disclose to OFAC, or recommend a SAR.
Key takeaways
• Investigators review any available source of information to assess whether
there is a potential sanctions violation.
• Sources of investigations include screening system data, sanctions lists,
and transaction history.
• Investigators could request additional information from relevant internal or
external parties who can provide insight into a customer’s activity.
Leading an investigation
When leading an investigation, ensure the key elements of success are in
place. Seek commitment from senior management to support the
investigation. Secure adequate resources. Allocate resources strategically for
maximum efficiency. Be knowledgeable about the law and ensure that your
team is working within the parameters of the law. Document your findings in
detail and keep all supporting documents evidencing your findings. Form a
plan for how you will preserve your findings and be ready to undertake post-
investigation follow-up.
As a lead investigator, you should be experienced and maintain high standards
of ethics and integrity. You should understand the types of resources required
to undertake the investigation and whether external assistance is needed. You
should be sufficiently experienced to be able to create a plan for the
investigation and allocate resources according to your areas of expertise. You
should have a solid understanding of the skills and expertise needed during
various aspects of the investigation, be able to identify the members of your
team who have the specific skills, and then allocate those resources
accordingly. You should be able to communicate effectively in giving
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 59
instructions and directions to your team. You should maintain confidentiality
and be able to preserve the integrity of documents and evidence throughout
the investigative process.
You should be able to identify patterns in the investigation and develop
theories based on those patterns. You should be a good manager of time,
know when the investigation is not on schedule, and take the appropriate
action to bring the investigation back on course. You should be able to build
strategic relationships with various stakeholders and be a negotiator, which
will assist in getting the cooperation needed when undertaking an
investigation.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 60
Research considerations
Four research steps applied to an
investigation
Conducting research is an important step prior to deciding to escalate to
investigation. You can improve the quality of your investigation by applying the
four research steps: Assess, Explore, Organize, and Present. First, you assess
all of the information you have gathered to identify any gaps. Second, you
explore the external sources available for your research and their relevance.
Third, you organize all the sources of information, both internal and external.
Your last step is to prepare a report and present your findings.
When you assess the information gathered, you identify gaps and decide how
you will close those gaps. For example, you can gather information from
internal sources by interviewing employees, or from external sources by
requesting that the relationship manager interview the customer. Then you
explore the information from external sources to help in your research.
When sourcing external information, you must ensure that it is from a
reputable source, current, independent, unbiased, and reliable. Ensure that
the information obtained from external sources is consistent with that of your
internal sources. After gathering all the information required, reassess the
information and identify any gaps.
Next, organize your sources of information gathered from research.
Determine if it is relevant and whether there are additional insights you can
gain from similarities or differences between your internal and external
sources of information. Clarify with your sources as needed. For example, you
could accompany the relationship manager to a site visit for a follow-up
interview with the customer. As you are organizing your information, you
might determine that it is incomplete. In that situation, you would need to
return to the explore step to find the information you need. Finally, you will
prepare a report to present your findings. Ensure that you document your
findings, supported by evidence, and report any information you have
requested but is not available.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 61
Primary sources of information
Primary sources of information provide direct, important KYC data during a
review or an investigation. These primary sources encompass various types of
documents issued by government bodies and regulators, including key legal
documents, key regulatory and law enforcement watchlists and guidelines,
and key trade activity lists. Additionally, primary sources include the internal
customer records maintained by organizations, such as KYC documentation.
This includes customer outreach.
Analysts and investigators should ensure that all primary sources of
information are up-to-date and come directly from official sources.
Primary sources might vary depending on the jurisdiction. In the case of key
legal documents or lists, each jurisdiction generally issues its own rules,
regulations, and lists. They also determine the scope of enforcement. These
documents are readily available online through the respective government or
agency website.
Key legal documents can include:
• UN Security Council Resolutions
• Financial Action Task Force Recommendations
• EU Council Regulations and EU Council Decisions
• EU AML Directives
• US Executive Orders
Key regulatory and law enforcement watchlists can include:
• Politically Exposed Persons List
• Office of Foreign Assets Control Specially Designated Nationals and
Blocked Persons List
Key trade activity lists can include:
• Common Military List of the EU
• US Department of Commerce Bureau of Industry and Security Denied
Persons List
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 62
• International Trade Administration Consolidated Screening List
• France’s Directorate General of the Treasury List
Internal customer records consist of information an organization has on each
customer from its KYC, standard due diligence, and transaction activity.
Examples include:
• Government-issued forms of identification, such as a passport
• Certified legal documentation verifying the customer’s legal and trading
names, places of operation, and key individuals within the business
• Purchases made online
• Cross-border payments
• Transactions involving money services businesses
Secondary sources of information
Secondary sources contain information that has generally already appeared in
primary documents. They can provide additional, valuable information about
customers. Commonly used secondary sources include corporate registers,
third-party databases, and media publications.
It is important to note that not all secondary sources are equally reliable. Just
as news stories from different media channels and outlets vary in their
accuracy, some secondary sources are more reliable than others.
Inquiries for gathering primary sources and gathering secondary sources are
conducted separately. If primary and secondary sources show a discrepancy,
further investigation is warranted to clarify the inconsistency.
Secondary sources of information can vary in type and value. Therefore, you
should follow your organization’s policies.
Corporate registers include information about when a corporation was
formed and who its owners and directors are. Corporate registers are often
publicly available. Two examples of these registers are the Accounting and
Corporate Regulatory Authority in Singapore and annual or quarterly reports
for publicly traded companies.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 63
Third-party databases are usually provided on a subscription basis. Specialized
companies can provide tailored, comprehensive solutions that include the
coverage of close associates, financial crime lists, and vessel information. The
information they provide should not be used on its own. These databases
include Dow Jones Risk & Compliance, and Refinitiv World-Check, among
others.
Some media publications might make accurate reporting a top priority, while
others might place a higher value on sensational stories that boost traffic to
the publisher’s web page. For this reason, many organizations maintain lists of
media publications that either can or cannot be consulted in an investigation.
When necessary, specialists such as advisory teams, law firms, or both can be
called in for additional support. Many organizations have internal advisory
teams made up of specialists in regulations and related laws.
Social media can reveal useful information on connections between parties
and provide leads for further investigation. However, social media are not
reliable sources as the information could be easily manipulated by the party
under investigation.
Communicating with law enforcement and
regulators for an investigation
Certain investigations require you to engage with law enforcement agencies
or regulators to seek guidance or support to progress an investigation or
reporting. Your organization might have policies and procedures for
interacting with law enforcement agencies or regulators. Most organizations
have a designated law enforcement/regulatory engagement nominated
person such as a chief compliance officer or money laundering reporting
officer (MLRO).
It is important that any communication be clear, concise, and fully
documented, with all records maintained in accordance with your
organization's record retention policies.
Engagement with law enforcement agencies or regulators can occur when
you are seeking guidance, support, or direction on formal reporting, most
often at the conclusion of an investigation.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 64
Depending on the complexity or length of your investigation, you might have
to communicate periodically to provide updates or seek further guidance to
determine the direction of your investigation. You might be running your own
investigation alongside a wider investigation being conducted by a law
enforcement agency or regulator and need to share information. Or an
investigation is so serious that you have to arrange to hand it over.
It is important to be aware of the engagement protocols within your
organization to ensure you do not inadvertently breach internal policies.
Understanding this will also ensure that you engage with the appropriate
authority or department, saving time and ensuring that your investigation is
progressing accordingly, and reporting is being done correctly.
Determine an appropriate method of communication; it is not advisable to
have an informal discussion with an agency or regulator. All communication
must be formally documented and retained with your investigation case file.
For example, a telephone call might not be recorded, but notes should be
taken on the points discussed and retained in accordance with your
organization's record retention policy.
Communicating with law enforcement and
regulators for an investigation (Case
example: Partial match)
Carole is the money laundering reporting officer (MLRO) for a bank in the UK.
She investigated a confirmed partial match of a new customer with a person
on the UK Sanctions List. She was unable to obtain a valid photo, nor could she
verify the date of birth of the listed person. As the designated MLRO, and
based on her bank’s internal policies, Carole filed a partial matching report
with the UK’s financial intelligence unit (FIU). Additionally, she followed the
FIU’s regulatory guidance for cases of partial matches.
According to her bank’s internal policy, the MLRO is required to communicate
with the FIU and file suspicious activity reports (SAR) through the goAML
system. This is a fully integrated software system developed to meet the data
collection and management requirements of FIUs. The bank policy also states
that partial matching reports must be filed within 24 hours, and SARs within 48
hours.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 65
As per the bank’s record retention policy, Carole needed to document and
maintain instances of FIU communications, partial matching report filings, SAR
filings, and any updates. Carole completed the appropriate fields in the goAML
system except for the listed person’s missing data. She filed a partial matching
report and attached all the validated evidence she had used throughout the
investigative process. Finally, she requested further guidance from the FIU
regarding frozen funds for these types of cases.
The FIU confirmed receipt of Carole’s SAR and provided the known age of the
listed person for the bank’s records. The FIU and Carole did not engage in any
further communication. Carole proceeded with freezing, or blocking, the
customer's account and blocked their transactions until further notice from
the FIU.
A week later, the FIU requested the release of the account and the unblocking
of transactions. Carole executed the instructions and retained the
communication notes in the appropriate investigation case file for audit
purposes.
Key takeaways
• Seek guidance for an investigation from law enforcement or regulators to
ensure compliance.
• Follow engagement protocols within your organization.
• Establish an appropriate method of communication with law enforcement
or regulators.
• Document and retain all communication in accordance with your
organization’s record retention policy.
If more information is needed
During your research, if more information is needed, sometimes the best
source is people close to the customer, transaction, or situation. The second
line of defense can create a request for information—sometimes called an RFI.
There are billions of customers doing billions of transactions each day. Your
organization will have only a narrow view of the information that you will need
to understand the bigger picture. Start internally, with colleagues such as a
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 66
relationship manager. If appropriate, request information from other
organizations. In some cases, the first line of defense might even contact the
customer—always strictly following your organization’s policies.
There is no set template for an RFI, but there are steps to follow before raising
a request.
• Exhaust all standard internal and external resource options before raising
an RFI. These requests are typically sent by a compliance department to
frontline staff. Don’t waste others’ time by not doing your work first.
• Provide accurate and specific details, including the customer account
number and transaction reference details, data, amount, and currency (if
relevant). Make it easy for the person responding.
• Request only information appropriate from the source you’re asking. It is
acceptable to ask an internal relationship director if they have any previous
financial crime risk concerns, but not appropriate to ask another
organization the same question. Although jurisdictions vary, there is often
no obligation on another organization to disclose whether they have
reported concerns about your customer.
• Request only information pertinent to your investigation. Avoid broad
questions such as, “Could you describe the type of counterparties you
expect your customer to transact with?” Instead, be specific. Say, “Your
customer has transacted more than 10,000 British pounds with a customer
in the catering industry for the first time. Is this transaction consistent with
the customer profile you hold and your understanding of this customer’s
expected activity?” Being specific helps the respondent of the RFI
understand why the information is needed and helps them respond in a
clear, unambiguous way.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 67
If more information is needed (Case
example: Golden Sun)
Summit Bank uses a subscription-based, third-party database to identify
matches with sanctions lists during the customer onboarding process. The
database generates an alert for a prospective customer, Golden Sun, as a
potential match. The alert triggers further review.
After reviewing the alert, the analyst concludes that there is a high likelihood
that Golden Sun matches an entry on OFAC’s Specially Designated Nationals
(SDN) List. However, further analysis is required, including a close examination
of KYC documentation. The analyst escalates the alert to an investigator on
the compliance team.
Having failed to clarify the issue using all available information, the investigator
ultimately initiates a request for information (RFI).
The investigator closely reviews the KYC documentation and customer profile
details. She concludes that the customer’s:
• Legal name and country of registration match the SDN entry
• Date of incorporation is March 3, 2003, whereas the SDN entry just
references the year 2003
• Company registration number is not provided, just the tax identification
number, in line with business convention in the country of the customer
Using available sources of information, the senior investigator attempts to
confirm the customer’s company registration number. She separately
acquires various corporate records from a third-party database for which
Summit Bank has no active subscription. However, these records do not
contain the information she is looking for. As a result, the investigator initiates
an RFI to the relationship manager.
In the RFI, the investigator specifies the data the relationship manager should
request from the customer as well as the time frame within which a response
is expected. She needs the customer to confirm their company registration
number and provide detailed disclosure of their ownership and management
structures. This information had previously been determined to be only
partially reflected in the KYC documentation on file.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 68
Key takeaways
A request for information:
• Could be sent to a customer via a customer-facing employee to clarify
specific information, seek extra disclosure, or challenge them on identified
discrepancies
• Could gather valuable data that helps a bank create a more
comprehensive customer profile
Data security and privacy
Financial institutions have a high duty of care, and often legal obligation, to
ensure the security and privacy of data they hold about their customers. Data
you hold about a customer should be shared only with others who need to
know and have the requisite permission and authority to view it. It must be
stored securely. In many jurisdictions, it is prohibited by law for data collected
for one purpose to be used for another purpose, such as marketing. Once it
has served its purpose, data must be securely destroyed. Many countries have
rules and regulations about how long data should be retained. Your
organization will have a policy on how long data should be stored and when
data should be destroyed.
Many countries have specific national privacy or data security laws, including
laws and regulations that apply to financial institutions. One of the strictest is
the EU General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) for how organizations
protect EU citizens' personal data and privacy.
These laws place challenging requirements on companies that collect data.
They may require the consent of the subject individual to process their data. In
some instances, data may not be transferred across national borders, which
present specific requirements for global financial services organizations.
These laws also have strict requirements for notifying the authorities and the
individuals affected if a data breach occurs.
The data that is collected is comprehensive and often purposeful. Care must
be taken to ensure physical data is properly protected. It should not be left on
an employee’s desk overnight. When stored electronically, data must be
entered into your organization’s approved databases in accordance with
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 69
policy. Some organizations prohibit the use of desktop folders. Most
organizations also prohibit the use of USB sticks for storing data as these are
easily lost. Data should not be retained indefinitely and must be destroyed in
accordance with your organization’s destruction policy.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 70
Making a final decision
Deciding to conclude an investigation
Your goal in investigating a financial crime is to see whether the activity meets
the conditions for a self-disclosure or filing a SAR after gathering sufficient
supporting evidence. Once you gather sufficient evidence, analyze the
evidence, develop a theory, and validate your theory based on reasonable
grounds for belief, you should decide whether you need to make a disclosure
or file a SAR. Carefully preserve all witness statements along with all
documents and information gathered during the investigation that will be
used as evidence. Follow the guidelines for preserving the integrity of your
evidence by preventing tampering, unlawful access, or unauthorized
disclosure of information.
Once you are satisfied that sufficient evidence has been gathered and a
determination can be made on the next course of action, conclude your
investigation. If there is insufficient evidence to warrant further action, the
investigation may be concluded prematurely.
Before you decide to conclude an investigation, consider the following:
• Have you gathered sufficient evidence to warrant an arrest, prosecution, or
other legal action?
• Have you explored all possible sources of information and can find nothing
substantial?
• Has the investigation failed to produce sufficient evidence to pursue legal
action?
When you have determined that there is sufficient evidence to take legal
action, identify the key supporting information including witnesses and data.
Depending on the complexity and sensitivity of the investigation, you might
need to provide for the protection of these witnesses. You would also need to
consider whether follow-up investigations would be required or if any other
government or regulatory agencies would need to be apprised of the findings
of the investigation. Once substantial evidence has been gathered, and key
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 71
supporting information such as witness statements, and information is
sufficient to proceed with the appropriate next course of action, you can
conclude your investigation.
Factors that can influence your decision to conclude an investigation
prematurely include:
• The absence of proper records that can serve as evidence
• The absence of key witnesses
• Inaccurate lead
• Customer has exited the institution
• Suspicious activity ceases
Deciding to conclude an investigation (Case
example: AgriTech)
Banco Vizcaya is a bank based in Spain. A long-term customer is a local
professional football club, Barcelona Esmeralda (BE). According to the
customer profile and transaction history, BE frequently buys, sells, or lends
players to and from football clubs around the world. This is a common industry
practice in football, so these high-value transactions are not considered to be
unusual behavior for this customer.
Recently, an incoming payment was sent to BE from a company called
AgriTech. The company is a customer of a non-sanctioned bank in Belarus.
However, Banco Vizcaya classifies Belarus as a high-risk jurisdiction for
sanctions evasion due to its proximity to Russia. Therefore, the payment
triggers an alert and is put on hold pending further investigation.
The compliance analyst uses several sources of information, including open
sources, to conduct the investigation. On AgriTech’s official company website,
he discovers that the company is the leading provider of agricultural
machinery in Belarus. He finds it unusual that AgriTech sent a payment to a
football club in Spain.
He finds media publications that indicate AgriTech owns a football club in
Belarus that receives sponsorship from the Belarusian government. There are
photos of AgriTech senior officials, including its chairman, with the President

Key takeaways
When investigating:
• Research the parties involved in the transaction
• Evaluate your findings to determine whether a violation might have
occurred
• Conclude an investigation:
o If there is a lack of sufficient evidence
o After you confirm that no violation has occurred
Documenting your decision-making process
As you begin your investigation and assess your line of inquiry, your
investigation plan will guide your initial approach. However, as the investigation
progresses, you could uncover new information that leads the investigation in
a different direction and a need for you to shift your strategy.
Throughout the investigation, it is important to document your decision-
making process. This gives you a clear outline of your initial thinking, any
changes in direction, and possible next steps. In addition, it serves as an audit
trail for review as you bring the investigation to a close.
At the beginning of your investigation, you might outline your thinking on the
different lines of inquiry you are or are not going to take. As the investigation
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 73
progresses, you might refer back to your initial decisions and assess your
progress, and whether you need to make different decisions to achieve your
goal. Recording each of your decisions builds an audit trail of your thoughts.
During your investigation, you will make a number of decisions that will have an
impact on its direction. Recording your decision-making is important as it
allows you to affirm or reassess your current position at any point in the
investigation. A record of your decision-making also serves as a tool that can
be used to brief other investigators who could raise a line of inquiry that you
have not yet thought of.
Where you record your decision-making will depend on how you are
compiling your case and how complex it is. In complex investigations, you
have a decision-making log that all investigators contribute to. A shared log
might reveal lines of inquiry that you had considered but decided not to
pursue. This is another opportunity to affirm or reassess your position.
As you conclude your investigation, the record of your decision-making might
include notes in a case management solution that can act as a memory aid
when writing your conclusion.
