Next steps
Self-reporting a potential breach of
sanctions
When an organization determines that it violated a sanctions regulation, it may
need to file a report with the relevant authority. It may also be necessary to
submit a suspicious activity report (SAR). This is particularly true for
organizations that suspect they have been handling criminal property. A
formal letter to the authority can also be beneficial, providing additional detail
on the breach and your sanctions compliance program.
Once a sanctions breach is confirmed, your organization needs to determine
whether a self-report to the relevant authority is appropriate. In some
circumstances, there may be valid reasons not to submit a report. It is good
practice to engage legal counsel in this process. Importantly, key sanctions
authorities do place significant emphasis on self-reporting and will consider
this within their approach to enforcement.
When reporting a sanctions breach, it must be in line with relevant legislation
and procedures set out by the relevant authority. In some scenarios, multiple
reports might need to be made to multiple authorities in multiple jurisdictions.
When a report is made, it should include all information related to the breach
and all actions taken. If the potential breach is in relation to a transaction, the
action might involve freezing, blocking, or rejecting it.
Different jurisdictions require reports to be made in a specific format. For
example:
• The Office of Foreign Assets Control in the US has an online reporting form.
If this is unavailable, there are several PDF forms that can be completed
and submitted, or you can write a letter. You may also be required to
prepare a SAR.
• The Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation in the UK has a
Compliance Reporting Form that is available online. It should be completed
with details of the breach. Similarly, you are also able to write a letter.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 75
Depending on wider regulatory obligations, you may also be required to
submit a SAR.
• Hong Kong requires any transactions with designated individuals or entities
to be reported to the Joint Financial Intelligence Unit through a suspicious
transaction report.
• Australia requires you to submit all information about a potential breach to
the Department of Foreign Affairs and Trade using the online form.
Self-reporting a potential breach of
sanctions (Case example: Loan application)
An offshore-registered entity requests a loan from a US-based bank. The bank
identifies that the offshore entity has two beneficial owners. Person A is on
OFAC’s Specially Designated Nationals and Blocked Persons (SDN) List and
owns 30% of the entity’s shares. Person B is not subject to sanctions and owns
the remaining 70%. Due to the presence of an SDN in the company’s
ownership structure, the bank categorizes the customer as high-risk, yet
decides to approve the loan.
In accordance with the bank’s policies on high-risk customers, a compliance
analyst manually reviews the customer’s account three months later. She
discovers that there was a transfer of ownership approximately a month after
its onboarding, which directly exposed the bank to sanctions risk. The bank
self-reports a breach of sanctions to OFAC.
The bank lent the offshore entity US$100,000 for agricultural expansion in
Africa. While reviewing the customer’s account, the compliance analyst
discovers that shares were transferred from the non-designated shareholder,
Person B, to the designated shareholder, Person A. She immediately escalates
the case. An investigator who takes charge of the case finds out that Person A
already owned 53% of the borrower, versus 30%, when the bank released the
second portion of the loan worth US$32,000.
The investigator suspects that the customer intentionally changed its
ownership structure post-onboarding, without the bank’s knowledge, to
bypass sanctions controls. The customer is registered in an offshore
jurisdiction where beneficial ownership information is not available online. As
such, the bank relies on a third-party vendor to manually retrieve information
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 76
at the bank's request every quarter. The bank also conducts regular adverse
media screening on the customer. However, the customer has a very low
profile.
The bank immediately self-reports to OFAC. In its voluntary self-disclosure,
the bank includes a detailed description of the breach and the involved
parties. Supporting documentation, such as the loan agreement and three
funds disbursement notices, are attached to the report. The bank
simultaneously blocks the offshore entity’s account as it is now subject to
OFAC’s blocking sanctions as being 50% or more owned by an SDN.
Key takeaways
• Notify your sanctions regulator of a breach within the required time frame.
• A timely voluntary self-disclosure demonstrates your organization’s
commitment to compliance.
• Implement immediate remediation measures to prevent future sanctions
breaches. Remediation can include enhanced ongoing monitoring and
enhanced due diligence.
Self-reporting a potential breach of
sanctions (Case example: US real estate
purchase)
A US bank receives a mortgage application from a prospective customer,
GlobalNexus. They are an entity outside of the US looking to acquire prime real
estate in California. GlobalNexus is making a 60% down payment and offering
the property as collateral. The bank approves the loan application.
Six months later, the bank receives a 314(b) request from another bank whose
customer previously made repeated high-value transfers to GlobalNexus. The
other bank suspects that a sanctioned Russian oligarch owns GlobalNexus and
that their customer is part of a network to move money from Russia to the US.
FinCEN issues an alert to all financial institutions. The alert warns them of
potential investments in the US commercial real estate sector by sanctioned
Russian elites, oligarchs, their family members, and other proxies. After
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 77
receiving the 314(b) request, the US bank conducts additional research to
verify the other bank’s suspicions. They find circumstantial evidence of their
customer's connection to Russia, yet they cannot verify that any sanctioned
individual owns the entity. The US bank identifies the customer’s address for
its headquarters is tied to several Russian entities but there is limited available
information on the entities. Additionally, the customer’s representative, who is
the main point of contact for communications with the bank, is in multiple
social media photos and videos with wealthy Russian officials. The US bank
discovers that the representative holds managerial positions in multiple
offshore entities and trusts.
The US bank decides that there is a lack of information to confirm a breach.
They file a suspicious activity report (SAR) with FinCEN anyway, alleging
suspected money laundering and potential sanctions evasion.
The SAR includes:
• Detailed information on the customer, including their name, address, and
account number
• A description and narrative of the suspicious activity, including:
o Dates, amounts, the involved parties, and financial institutions
o The key term FIN-2023-RUSSIACRE, a special tag advised by FinCEN
• Supporting documents that confirm the suspicious activity, including the
transaction logs depicting the high-value transfers to GlobalNexus
Key takeaways
• File a suspicious activity report (SAR) even if there is insufficient evidence
to confirm a sanctions breach or money laundering.
• A SAR should include comprehensive details of the suspicious activity.
• Stay vigilant for FinCEN alerts. Integrating insights from FinCEN alerts into
SAR reports enhances the effectiveness of AFC efforts.

Asset freeze and making funds available
Asset freezing, or asset blocking, is a common financial sanction imposed against
named individuals, entities, and bodies. This involves freezing any assets, most
commonly bank accounts, owned or controlled by the sanctions target. Its
purpose is to restrict access to funds and economic resources.
The freezing of assets includes a general prohibition against making funds
available to a sanctioned person, entity, or body, whether directly or indirectly. This
type of sanction most often takes the form of freezing or blocking bank accounts.
Asset freezes do not involve a change of ownership of the funds or economic
resources, nor do they involve the confiscation of assets or economic resources.
The assets still belong to the sanctioned person or entity.
When an asset freeze is in place, it is generally prohibited to:
• Make funds or economic resources available to, or for the benefit of, a
designated person, whether directly or indirectly
• Engage in actions that circumvent the financial sanctions prohibitions, whether
directly or indirectly
• Deal with frozen funds or economic resources that belong to or are owned,
held, or controlled by, a designated person
Failing to freeze or block assets and making them available to benefit a designated
person, entity, or body, can violate sanctions and result in criminal and civil
penalties.
When assets are frozen, you cannot “deal with” funds and economic resources in
such a way as to allow the assets to be used. “Dealing with funds” refers to moving,
altering, using, transferring, accessing, or otherwise managing funds in a way that
results in a change to their volume, location, ownership, possession, character,
destination, or other change that allows the funds to be used.
“Dealing with economic resources” refers to the use of economic resources to
obtain funds, goods, or services in any way. Exemptions do exist for the use of
frozen funds. For example, they can be used to cover the basic needs of a
sanctioned person, pay legal fees, and satisfy contractual obligations.
Asset freezes must be implemented without delay. Any frozen assets or bank
accounts may need to be reported to the relevant authority.

Introduction
Setup: Ms. Krylova
You are a newly hired relationship manager in the New York office of a global
bank. You have successfully completed an extensive onboarding program. So,
you feel confident that you will be able to provide excellent customer service.
You receive a call from a customer by the name of Alessandra Krylova. She
has been a customer of the bank for several years. Ms. Krylova is trying to
access the funds in her account, which have been, in her words, “randomly
locked.” During the call, you ask her a few questions and request additional
documentation. She must provide proof of identity so you can take the next
course of action. She is upset and refuses to give you the information. The
customer expresses frustration. She believes that no one at your bank ever
helps her and that you will not help either. You apologize and assure her you
are committed to addressing her concerns.
After you end the call, you confirm that Ms. Krylova’s account has been frozen
due to your bank’s sanctions policy. Based on a note in her file, the sanctions
compliance team has determined that her date of birth, country of origin, and
last name are on a sanctions list. It is the Office of Foreign Assets Control
(OFAC) Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list.
You recall from your training that sanctions lists are in the public domain. Your
bank has a regulatory obligation to freeze assets when they suspect or know
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 80
that a customer is on the SDN list. You review Ms. Krylova’s account and note
that the frozen funds are held in an interest-bearing account. Your bank
reported the frozen assets to OFAC four days after the name match was
initially detected. Your bank continues to reference the customer’s account in
its annual blocked property report to OFAC. As a relationship manager, you
want to provide good service to your customers, but you must follow your
bank’s policies.
What would you do?
What is your next step?
• Explain why her assets are frozen.
• Suggest that she appeal her listing.
• Ask compliance to close her account.
• Unfreeze her account for 30 days.
Feedback
The best step would be to explain to the customer why her assets are frozen.
You can tell her that she is on a sanctions list. You know that sanctions lists are
publicly available, unlike suspicious activity reports which you are not allowed
to disclose. So, disclosing that she is on a sanctions list would not be a violation
of applicable laws or your bank’s policies.
Suggesting that she appeal her listing is not appropriate. Your bank should not
be advising the customer on how to address her current sanctions situation.
The relationship manager must first explain to the customer why she cannot
access the funds in her account. If the customer would like to formally appeal
her sanctions listing, or reach out to OFAC to pursue a license, she may do so
on her own.
Asking the compliance team to close her account is not a good option.
Generally, there is no requirement to close an OFAC-blocked account if there
are funds in it. The funds can remain frozen in an interest-bearing account,
assuming they will continue to be reviewed by your bank, and reported to
OFAC as needed.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 81
Unfreezing her account for 30 days is not allowed under OFAC regulations.
Your bank has a requirement to freeze assets when they find that a customer
is on the OFAC SDN list. Unfreezing the funds would cause the bank to violate
OFAC regulations and could result in penalties, fines, and other regulatory
actions.
Learning objectives
After completing this learning experience, you will be able to:
• Explain how to identify and freeze assets.
• Describe the requirements for handling frozen assets.
• Discuss the controls for ongoing account management.

Identifying and freezing assets
Identification of assets
Financial institutions often need to identify and verify assets. This might occur
during account opening, before conducting high-value wire transfers, prior to
conducting complex financial transactions, or when determining beneficial
ownership. Broadly defined, the term “asset” refers to any funds or economic
resources owned by a customer. Although the details vary slightly by
jurisdiction, the definition is relatively constant around the world. This ensures
that all types of assets intended to be covered by AFC regulations are, in fact,
covered.
Funds can include any financial assets, such as cash, securities, and stocks.
Economic resources can include any nonfinancial assets that could be used to
obtain funds, goods, or services. For example, real estate is considered an
economic resource because it has the potential to generate income for the
owner.
To effectively identify customer assets, financial investigators, relevant
authorities, and professionals might collaborate to ensure accurate asset
discovery. Asset identification and discovery can involve a thorough review of
various records and documents. For example, when a customer opens a new
account, the financial institution is required to identify and verify the source of
funds. Mergers and acquisitions can involve the transfer of substantial assets.
As a result, financial institutions are obligated to identify the parties involved
and ensure the transfer does not involve high-risk individuals or entities.
Financial criminals might use complex ownership structures to conceal illicit
activities and assets. As such, financial institutions must collect and verify
beneficial ownership information for legal entity customers to mitigate risks
and prevent financial crimes.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 83
To identify funds or financial assets, you can review financial statements, bank
records, retirement plan documentation, brokerage accounts, or investment
portfolios. These types of assets can include:
• Cash
• Checks
• Bank Deposits
• Debts and debt obligations
• Money orders
• Securities
• Bonds
• Notes
• Warrants
• Derivative contracts
• Insurance policies
• Dividends
• Letters of credit
• Bills of lading
• Safety deposit boxes and their contents
To identify economic resources or nonfinancial assets, you can review
ownership documents, property records, business contracts, legal
documentation, and registrations, or conduct physical inspections. These
types of assets can include:
• Cars, planes, and boats
• Works of art and other valuable objects
• Real estate properties
• Computers, software, tools, and machinery
• Firearms and related material
• Inventories of goods
• Commodities including oil, minerals, or timber
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 84
• Patents and other forms of intellectual property
• Virtual assets
Freezing or blocking assets
Asset freezing, which is similar to asset blocking, is a way of controlling the
customer’s assets. International organizations, such as the UN and EU, along
with national authorities might impose asset-freezing measures on individuals
involved in, or suspected of, committing crimes.
Legal requirements in a given jurisdiction govern which assets must be frozen
or blocked when a financial crime occurs and the process an organization
must follow. The process of freezing assets must occur quickly and
effectively. This is necessary to prevent the risk of asset flight and asset
dissipation. Asset flight occurs when a customer attempts to move assets into
a different jurisdiction. Asset dissipation occurs when a customer attempts to
spend or misallocate their assets to avoid financial obligations.
Asset freezing should take place immediately after a court order or a
legislative act comes into force. The goal is to prevent the use, alteration,
movement, transfer, and access to the targeted assets. It also prevents any
other change to the nature of the targeted assets such as a change in
location, ownership, or destination.
The process of freezing assets has certain built-in operational challenges.
Locating assets and funds belonging directly or indirectly to the customer can
be difficult, especially if there are accounts in multiple branches of the
organization or accounts held through complex ownership structures.
Additionally, freezing requirements vary across jurisdictions.
The UN and EU provide distinct procedures to their respective member states
to address various scenarios that might require asset freezing. In the US,
organizations primarily follow the Office of Foreign Asset Control’s (OFAC)
requirements. In jurisdictions such as the UK and Switzerland, the processes
for asset freezing are primarily governed by their respective domestic laws
and regulations. In the UK, for example, an organization must either know with
certainty, or have “reasonable cause to suspect,” that it is in possession or
control of the assets of a criminal, or that it is somehow otherwise dealing in
these assets. If it is certain or has reasonable cause, it must freeze the assets.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 85
Then, it makes the assets unavailable to the person or for that person’s
benefit. Lastly, the organization should not deal with the assets. This means
that they should not engage in any financial activities involving the assets that
would violate the freeze order.
Freezing or blocking assets (Case example:
REPO Task Force)
In response to Russia’s invasion of Ukraine, international partners imposed
severe, far-reaching sanctions on individuals, entities, and financial institutions
in Russia. This was accomplished through the multilateral Russian Elites,
Proxies, and Oligarchs (REPO) Task Force, which is comprised of the Group of
Seven (G-7) members, the European Commission, and Australia.
In coordination with its international partners, the US implemented restrictions
on transactions with Russia’s Central Bank, National Wealth Fund, and Ministry
of Finance. The effective result was that any assets of these entities that were
held in US financial institutions were immediately frozen or blocked. In
addition, financial institutions outside the US that held US dollars for these
entities were unable to disburse those funds.
Between 2022 and 2023, as a result of the Russian invasion and these
restrictions, the US added over 2,500 Russia-related targets to its OFAC
Specially Designated Nationals list. Out of those targets, there were 2,400
individuals and entities, 115 vessels, and 19 aircraft. The sanctions targets
spanned across Europe, Africa, and Asia, and included key trade jurisdictions in
the Middle East and East Asia. The sanctions targets included Russian officials
and their family members to prevent them from moving or hiding assets. The
REPO Task Force froze luxury assets, such as the yachts and the real estate of
Russian billionaires. Official reports stated that the REPO Task Force
successfully tracked sanctioned assets around the globe and froze
approximately US$300 billion worth of Russian Central Bank assets.
The EU reported in February 2023 that it had frozen Russian assets worth
US$23 billion belonging to individuals and entities across multiple member
jurisdictions. As of March 2022, Australia froze at least US$45 million worth of
assets belonging to Russian government officials and oligarchs, and as of
February 2023, Canada froze US$90 million worth of assets. In March 2023, the
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 86
REPO Task Force said that its members had collectively frozen approximately
US$58 billion worth of Russian assets.
Key takeaways
• Family members could move or hide the assets of sanctioned individuals
and entities.
• Freezing, or blocking, assets could involve multilateral efforts to prevent
the movement, use, change, or transfer of assets.
• Sanctions require authorities to freeze or block assets based on the
jurisdiction in which they are held.
Confiscation, seizure, and forfeiture of
assets
The confiscation, also known as forfeiture, of assets is different from the
freezing or blocking of assets. When assets are confiscated or forfeited, the
customer or target loses ownership of the assets. This loss of ownership can
be temporary or permanent. When assets are frozen, they still belong to the
customer or target, but cannot be accessed or controlled until the sanctions
are lifted.
In many cases, the confiscation or forfeiture of assets will follow the freezing
of assets. Their processes vary depending on the specific circumstances, laws
and regulations, and objectives of governments and authorities.
The confiscation of assets can take place on AML and sanctions grounds.
These processes could operate separately. However, in cases where a person
or entity is engaging in money laundering and violating sanctions, authorities
can pursue both actions in parallel and invoke the confiscation of assets.
The confiscation of assets occurs after a court order is issued. It is generally
intended to permanently take possession of assets that have been generated
through criminal activity. In one example, the US confiscated frozen assets
from Iranian state bank accounts. The purpose was to compensate the
American families of victims of the 1983 bombing attack in Lebanon which
was believed to have been linked to the Iranian government. In another
example, the US and Canada confiscated assets belonging to several Russian
Page 87
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
oligarchs to fund Ukraine’s reconstruction in response to the 2022 Russian
invasion. The assets confiscated included cash held in bank accounts, luxury
items, and corporate assets.
The seizure of assets usually takes place before a court order is issued. Asset
seizures occur when law enforcement agencies take control of assets they
identify as being the proceeds of criminal activity.
The forfeiture of assets tends to follow the seizure of assets and results in the
permanent loss of assets. It is done by law enforcement agencies to defund
organized crime and prevent the commission of new crimes. In asset
forfeiture, assets unrelated to the commission of a crime can be taken from
the individual accused of committing a crime.
Laws about contacting the customer (Case
example: Sanctions and AML connection)
Viktor, a current customer at US-headquartered XBank, is sanctioned by
OFAC. Earlier, he appointed a woman named Elena to pay taxes and utility bills
for the maintenance of his property in the US. Elena sends a copy of a power
of attorney (POA) and an official letter to XBank requesting to transfer money
from Viktor’s account to a third-party account to pay bills on Viktor’s behalf. A
relationship manager (RM) denies the request and notifies Elena that Viktor’s
account is blocked due to OFAC sanctions.
After the rejection, Elena tries to pay the bills on Viktor’s behalf with funds
from her personal account at XBank. The transaction triggers an alert. The
compliance department investigates the alert, finds a connection to money
laundering, and blocks her account. The RM does not tell Elena that her
account is blocked due to a money laundering suspicion because that would
be tipping off.
XBank cannot process any payments to or from Viktor’s account. As a result,
when Elena presents the POA and requests the bank to transfer US$100,000
from his account to “pay taxes and utility bills,” the RM denies the request. The
RM notifies Elena that, due to sanctions restrictions, the bank requires a
specific license to release funds from Viktor’s account.
After the transfer request is denied and Elena uses her personal account,
XBank’s transaction monitoring system flags the transaction as suspicious,
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 88
based on several red flags. First, it is unusually large. Second, it does not
reference any property management expenses. Third, Elena’s account
frequently receives payments from shell companies in unrelated industries.
XBank’s compliance department suspects potential money laundering and
blocks Elena’s account. However, the bank is not allowed to tell her why her
account has been blocked nor can it tell her that a suspicious activity report
(SAR) has been or could be filed. Tipping off, a federal crime in the US, might
allow the customer to move potentially illicit funds, compromising an
investigation. Upon blocking Elena’s account, the bank files a SAR linking the
two individuals and their transactions.
Key takeaways
• Check for possible connections between sanctions and other financial
crimes, such as money laundering.
• Know the relevant laws related to tipping off in your jurisdiction.
• Follow compliance procedures to mitigate tipping off risks.

Managing frozen assets
Managing frozen assets
After an organization has frozen or blocked assets, it must manage those
assets according to regulatory requirements. Asset-freezing restrictions
require that the organization holding frozen assets not “deal in funds.” Dealing
in funds includes moving, transferring, altering, using, or accessing funds. It
also includes dealing with funds in any way that would result in any change to
their volume, amount, location, ownership, possession, character, destination,
or other change that would enable the funds to be used, including portfolio
management.
An organization must evaluate each management process depending on the
jurisdiction and type of asset. Depending on the jurisdiction, regulations might
require frozen funds to be held in interest-bearing accounts. Regulations
might also allow an organization to manage routine fees such as service fees.
Assets, such as money in bank accounts, may be frozen without the need for
active management from an organization. Meanwhile, physical, or moveable
assets, such as real estate properties or cars, might require more active
intervention from an organization. In cases where only a portion of a
customer’s assets is subject to freezing, an organization must segregate
those assets from the rest of the customer’s assets.
Organizations must research applicable laws in each case to manage frozen
assets properly. For example, most jurisdictions allow organizations holding
frozen assets to add interest to a frozen, interest-bearing account. Others
might require a license to charge interest to a frozen account that would
normally have interest charged, such as a loan or credit card. When managing
routine fees, most organizations are legally allowed to debit frozen accounts
for normal business charges. In the EU, charges may often be deducted
without first obtaining a license, if the bank can demonstrate that the charges
are commercially reasonable.
The cost of managing and maintaining frozen assets can be significant. For
example, these expenses could include additional monitoring and reporting
staff, technical controls to flag any attempts to deal in funds, and the time
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 90
required by the compliance team to identify assets, locate assets, and
properly report its findings to the relevant authorities.
Managing frozen assets (Case example:
OFSI)
A UK bank’s sanctions screening system generates an alert on a customer
that could be a match to a designated person in the United Nations Security
Council’s (UNSC) Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC) program. The
compliance analyst reviews the alert to determine its legitimacy. After further
confirmation, the bank determines that the customer is newly sanctioned by
the UNSC and decides to freeze their assets.
The reporting officer immediately notifies the Office of Financial Sanctions
Implementation (OFSI) about the true match and the decision to freeze the
assets.
The bank discovers that the customer is subject to direct UNSC sanctions due
to his position as a political and military leader of armed groups operating in
the DRC. In compliance with regulatory guidelines, the bank:
• Places the customer’s funds in a designated account
• Refrains from dealing with and providing any financial services related to
the customer’s frozen assets
• Informs the customer about the freeze, referencing specific UN and UK
sanctions lists
• Conducts enhanced due diligence on its customers to identify any
connections to the newly sanctioned customer and other sanctioned
parties
The bank submits the report to OFSI. The report includes comprehensive
information on the customer, evidence confirming the match, transactional
activities, and details of the frozen assets. The bank follows its policy to
maintain records of all actions taken with the frozen assets and their
communications with OFSI for five years.
As part of OFSI’s Frozen Asset In-Year reporting, the bank conducts an annual
review of all frozen assets and submits a return within one month of OFSI’s
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 91
notice. The return is sent in the form of completed templates from the
government website to OFSI’s official e-mail address. The return includes the
descriptions and values of all the frozen assets held or controlled by the bank
in the UK and overseas where assets are subject to UK sanctions. The bank
notes that it applies account maintenance fees to the UNSC-sanctioned
customer’s funds.
Key takeaways
• Banks might be required to perform annual reviews on frozen assets and
report to their sanctions regulator.
• Banks may apply standard fees to frozen accounts.
• Managing frozen assets should focus on restricting certain transactions or
dealings with sanctioned parties.
Reporting requirements for frozen assets
Reporting requirements for frozen assets vary by jurisdiction. There are
variations in requirements such as reporting timelines, which could range
from immediate to several business days. There are various reasons why
assets might be frozen, including sanctions matches, court orders, or other
legal processing. Generally, reporting requirements for sanctioned assets
subject to a freeze are stricter.
In general, financial institutions must keep all documents regarding frozen
assets for five years from the date the funds were released. The regulations
could require detailed reporting of the frozen assets. This typically includes
the identification of assets, which provides a description of the assets in terms
of their type, amount, and location. The regulations might also demand the
timely reporting of frozen assets and annual reporting thereafter.
Financial institutions should be aware of their reporting timelines and
procedures. In general, the sooner financial institutions report the events to
the proper authorities, the better.
In the EU, frozen assets must be reported without delay. Reports often include
all the information that guided the financial institution in its decision to freeze
and all identifying information about the individual subject to asset freezing.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 92
Financial institutions should include a description of the nature and quantity of
any assets and their value.
The UK, like the EU, has its own regulatory authority and its own set of
reporting rules. Frozen assets must be reported as soon as reasonably
possible. You should only submit reports if you have reasonable cause to
suspect a violation. The report must include a description of all the assets and
details regarding who owns the assets or has any other ownership or
signatory rights.
In the US, financial institutions follow the Office of Foreign Asset Control’s
(OFAC) reporting requirements. Frozen assets must be reported within 10
business days of their blocking. Reports can include identifying information
about the individual or entity subject to asset freezing, the value of the assets,
and their asset and account types. OFAC also requires financial institutions to
submit an annual report of any blocked property they hold.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 93
Managing controls
Customer exit
Financial institutions will likely exit a customer when the relationship falls
outside of their risk appetite. This is known as de-risking. Factors for de-risking
must be considered on a case-by-case basis. In some cases, the financial
institution could conduct a partial de-risking. This implies that specific
accounts are marked for closure and the financial institution will manage the
risks.
In full de-risking, it closes all customer accounts and terminates the customer
relationship. Although regulators do not define a clear process for financial
institutions to follow when managing AFC-related decisions, there are specific
expectations. Regulators, such as the European Banking Authority in the EU,
and the Financial Crimes Enforcement Network in the US, require financial
institutions to terminate customer relationships when the financial crime risks
cannot be reasonably explained.
An account closure strategy should determine the types of risk, accounts,
relationships, or customer behaviors that warrant closure. The process for
determining which accounts and relationships will be referred for closure
varies by the financial institution and jurisdiction. Once closure has been
agreed upon, the next steps include:
• Submitting a closure form with details that have been reviewed for
evidence
• Acquiring AFC or equivalent officer or team review and approval
• Taking steps so that the customer is added to a prohibited list
A prohibited list or blacklist is an internal list of customers with whom the
financial institution will no longer have a relationship.
Behavior that could result in an account closure recommendation includes
multiple suspicious activity reports (SAR), negative news, or high-risk
international transactions that lack legitimacy. A risk rating could be applied to
ensure the exit of high-risk customers as soon as possible. For low to
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 94
moderate-risk customers, the financial institution could conduct a partial de-
risking and perform increased due diligence and enhanced monitoring of
their accounts. For example, after SAR filings, customer accounts would
undergo ongoing scrutiny and proactive risk-reduction measures.
Ending a customer relationship is often complicated and involves multiple
stakeholders. In some financial institutions, an oversight committee reviews
accounts, and some circumstances might require legal advice.
Customer exit (Case example: Sanctions-
risk exposure)
Bank customer Krandlehoff Supply is in the maritime supplies business. It sells
shipping-related products, including tools to repair fixtures and hardware on
long-haul vessels. The same bank also holds accounts for Krandlehoff Supply’s
United Arab Emirates (UAE)-based subsidiary Krandlehoff ME.
A recent transaction cleared for Krandlehoff ME involves the sale of
crosshatch adhesion tape to a buyer from the UAE. The local relationship
manager suspects the UAE buyer of being a reseller. Various media articles
and blog posts suggest that this company has previously engaged in business
with sanctioned entities, including the Iranian Navy.
After careful consideration, the bank decides to engage in partial de-risking
by maintaining its relationship with Krandlehoff Supply while fully de-risking
from Krandlehoff ME.
The bank is doubly concerned about Krandlehoff ME’s sales of crosshatch
adhesion tape. First, this specific tape is an export-controlled item, being a
dual-use good. Second, Krandlehoff ME has potentially dealt with sanctioned
entities via a reseller. Both factors create significant indirect sanctions
exposure for the bank. To mitigate sanctions risk, the bank conducts an in-
depth review of its customer relationships. The bank’s relationship with
Krandlehoff Supply is longstanding and profitable. There have been only minor
compliance-related issues so far. It would prefer not to exit this strategic
customer.
The sanctions compliance review team meets with the relationship
management team to determine whether there is sanctions exposure and if
there is an apparent sanctions violation to report. Based on joint findings, the
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 95
bank’s senior management is presented with three options. It could de-risk
either fully or partially. Alternatively, it might choose to keep both its
relationships with Krandlehoff Supply and Krandlehoff ME while keeping the
latter or both under enhanced monitoring.
The bank opts for a mixed approach. It retains its longstanding and
commercially important relationship with Krandlehoff Supply but places the
parent company on an internal enhanced monitoring list. Conversely, the bank
de-risks from Krandlehoff ME by terminating its account with immediate
effect.
Key takeaways
When considering customer exit, a bank may:
• Monitor for red flags without taking any immediate action.
• Implement partial de-risking by restricting customer access to high-risk
geographies or products.
• Implement full de-risking if it wants to remove exposure to sanctions risk.
Maintaining an account after unusual
activity
After filing a suspicious activity report (SAR), financial institutions must decide
whether to maintain or close the impacted account. The decision is made
based on the financial institution’s risk appetite and tolerance, and in
accordance with its own standards and guidelines. However, a law
enforcement directive may request that an account remain open for further
investigation.
In addition to complying with law enforcement, it is critical to perform
customer due diligence after a SAR has been filed. If the customer is identified
as a high-risk customer, it is imperative for the financial institution to perform
enhanced customer due diligence and review its existing transaction
monitoring process to reduce additional risks. If the financial institution
chooses to continue the business relationship, it is required to comply with all
applicable legal and reporting requirements.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 96
After a SAR filing, next steps for the financial institution include actions related
to regular review and enhanced monitoring of the account, legal restrictions,
and changes in the relationship with the customer. If an account remains
open after submitting a SAR, the financial institution should monitor the
account for any additional suspicious activity by performing a regular review
of its transactions. It should also ensure it is taking appropriate enhanced
measures to manage the risks of other accounts being used for illegal
activities by the same customer. The financial institution may need to perform
enhanced due diligence by updating account information related to purpose
and expected activity. In some circumstances, extra measures may be
introduced, such as approval from senior management before executing
transactions. You also need to be aware of and adhere to any restrictions
placed by law enforcement agencies.
Some relationships are difficult to terminate, such as credit lines, mortgage
loans, automobile loans, and large business loans. In some jurisdictions, you
cannot terminate a customer relationship or certain products, such as open-
ended guarantees, without approval from the customer. In many instances,
financial institutions have no other option but to maintain the account and
care for the lending relationship. However, they can still prevent the customer
from opening new accounts and ensure that any principal and interest
payments are not proceeds of crime.
Maintaining an account after unusual
activity (Case example: Industrial
Corporation)
Manuela is a sanctions officer for a multinational bank. In a quarterly
presentation to the AML and sanctions compliance committee, Manuela
points to an increase in the number of suspicious transactions. This is partly
due to increased suspicious activity from a single customer, Industrial
Corporation. The customer has recently been making large wire transfers to
entities within geographies known for sanctions evasion risk.
An investigation into the customer does not reveal clear signs of sanctions
evasion. The customer’s recent transactions align with their historical volume
of activity. The goods they buy are also consistent with known transaction
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 97
history. Manuela notifies the compliance committee that the bank is retaining
the customer but downgrading, or increasing, their risk rating.
Manuela informs the compliance committee that the customer generated a
sanctions alert due to high-risk transactions potentially indicative of
Democratic People’s Republic of Korea (DPRK) sanctions evasion. The alert
disposition notes previous industry guidance about entities using fictitious
transactions as a sanctions evasion tactic for the benefit of the DPRK.
The case investigator reviews and requests additional customer information
from the business such as supporting customer records. The investigator
concludes there are no indications of either past or ongoing sanctions evasion
in the customer’s account. In addition, the customer remains cooperative and
made detailed disclosures about the recent transactions to demonstrate that
no high-risk counterparties were involved.
Manuela explains to the compliance committee that, despite the thorough
investigation, the nature of the transactions was unusual. Therefore, the bank
decides to maintain the customer’s account but also mitigate risk. The bank
changes the customer’s risk rating from medium to high and places them on
the internal watchlist for enhanced monitoring. This means additional
transaction monitoring and sanctions screening going forward. The customer
must also meet with their relationship manager quarterly instead of annually to
evaluate their bilateral relationship.
Key takeaways
• A bank can contact the customer to verify unusual activity before deciding
whether to maintain an account.
• Typically, a bank conducts an internal investigation to understand unusual
account activity to determine whether it will maintain the account.
• Maintaining an account after unusual activity can include more frequent
monitoring, screening, and contact.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 98
Prevent the customer from opening a new
account
When a customer has multiple suspicious activity reports (SARs), a financial
institution may decide to end the banking relationship and prevent the
customer from opening a new account. However, terminating the relationship
does not eliminate the risk, as the customer may attempt to use alternate
identities to open new accounts.
To prevent this, financial institutions must make sure to gather sufficient
onboarding information, implement robust monitoring systems, and know the
customer’s related parties and businesses. Doing so can help ensure there are
adequate controls, processes, and systems in place to detect and prevent a
former customer from opening new accounts of any kind. Having a complete
view of all parties and businesses related to the customer is important since
these individuals and entities can be used to attempt to open new accounts in
different jurisdictions.
Financial institutions need adequate controls and processes to prevent a
customer from opening another account after a SAR has been filed. Because
a customer with a SAR on his record might try to hide his true identity, it is
important to review the black or grey list of sanctioned customers, remain
watchful of attempts to change contact information, and stay alert to efforts
to use others’ information for misleading purposes. A comprehensive review
of customer information identifies all products, services, and related
information associated with the financial institution where the SAR was filed.
However, a major challenge is that most institutions do not have adequate
technology or visibility into customer accounts across the entirety of their own
organization, or into other organizations. To reduce the risk posed by this
difficulty, institutions can perform enhanced customer due diligence.
Institutions can also establish specific filters in the transaction monitoring
system to further reduce risk.
While it is important to retain the customer’s profile details in case the
customer attempts to open a new account, this will most likely only capture
exact information matches. The customer might apply variations to her
information to conceal her true identity information, thereby bypassing
transaction monitoring system filters. The customer’s name might remain the
same, but she might use a different address or telephone number. Customers
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 99
might also use the information of close family members, such as a spouse or
child, to open new accounts for their own use. Be aware of the various ways
terminated customers might attempt to open new accounts.
Prevent the customer from opening a new
account (Case example: Allstone Ventures)
The compliance audit team of a global bank has concluded its annual
sanctions examination. This includes a test on the bank’s “blacklist.” The
blacklist is the bank’s watchlist of high-risk customers that the bank exited, or
terminated, due to compliance concerns.
The compliance audit team selected Allstone Ventures (Allstone) as part of
the audit sample. Allstone had previously relied on the bank for trade finance
deals. Then, the bank exited the customer, advised them that they could not
conduct further transactions, and added them to the blacklist. Allstone was
chosen specifically because it appeared on a list of customers who were
exited and attempted to regain account access. Using that list, the auditors
determined that the bank had successfully prevented the company from
opening a new account.
The auditors reviewed the circumstances that led to Allstone’s second
blacklisting. Two years after Allstone’s account closure, a prospective
customer approached the bank for the same products as the ones previously
used by Allstone. It soon became clear that the prospective customer was
registered to the same office suite and managed by the same individual as
Allstone. This finding led the bank to add Allstone to its list of customers who
had been exited due to compliance concerns and tried to regain account
access.
During the audit period, the bank screened a new prospective customer, Swirl
Time, against its internal blacklist and official sanctions lists. Although the two
businesses initially appeared unrelated based on their name and operating
structure, further examination of other details revealed a connection to
Allstone. According to KYC records, Swirl Time appeared to be under the
control of Allstone’s beneficial owner. Based on this information, the bank
suspected Allstone of using a different but related company to try to open a
new account for itself. As a result, the bank decided to turn down Swirl Time’s
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 100
application and also added it to the blacklist. Based on the sample analysis, the
auditors determined that the bank’s controls had proved effective.
Key takeaways
• Add existing noncompliant customers to the blacklist to prevent them
from opening new accounts.
• Verify the beneficial owners and other key information to prevent
blacklisted customers from opening new accounts.
• Conduct regular audits to help identify and resolve weaknesses in the
account opening process.
Transaction monitoring and ongoing
reviews
Transaction monitoring is the scanning and analysis of transactional data for
potential money laundering, or efforts to breach economic sanctions. This is a
largely automated process with a system that generates alerts based on rules
or customer behavior changes. Artificial intelligence and machine learning are
increasingly used to ensure that alerts generated are true alerts with minimal
false positives. A false positive is an alert which is considered acceptable and
not a true flag. Analysts review these alerts and either escalate them or close
them as false positives.
Customer profiles need to be maintained and updated over time. Your
organization’s risk-based approach will determine how often. However, some
events trigger a refresh even sooner than scheduled. For example, if your
organization files a suspicious activity report, or SAR, related to the customer.
Common approaches to transaction monitoring include the creation of in-
house, customized transaction monitoring systems or engaging a third-party
vendor to assist with the development and implementation of automated
systems. These systems work on the basis of defining what normal behavior is
for the customer segment and flag any transactions that do not fit with
normal behavior. Financial institutions using a risk-based approach to
transaction monitoring should consider the size of the institution, the products
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 101
offered, and the features of those products when designing transaction
monitoring rules.
Your organization should have policies and procedures for monitoring
transactions for suspicious activity. These should specify the parameters and
thresholds that are in place to trigger an investigation. These policies should
be regularly reviewed and updated to account for changes and
enhancements to the monitoring rules program.
Customer profiles need to be refreshed and updated over time. Your
organization’s risk-based approach will determine how often. If your
jurisdiction allows, low-risk customers might be reviewed only every three to
four years or never unless otherwise triggered by outside information. Higher-
risk customers might be reviewed annually. This periodic update happens
even if the customer does not change its type or volume of business with your
organization. Certain events might trigger a refresh of the customer profile
even sooner than scheduled, such as a customer expanding their activities or
introducing a new way of using their existing products.
Transaction monitoring and ongoing
reviews (Case example: Sanctions-related
intermediary)
Peter is an AFC compliance analyst in a US-based financial institution. He
reviews transaction alerts based on various typologies. From experience,
Peter knows that transaction alerts involving high-risk jurisdictions can
sometimes be complex.
As Peter reviews a new alert, he notices that it involves a pending purchase of
crude oil worth US$20 million. The originator is a US-based customer, while the
intermediary is a noncustomer trading firm based in Hong Kong and the
supplier is a noncustomer entity in the United Arab Emirates (UAE). Peter
decides to conduct additional research and detects the hidden involvement
of an OFAC-designated entity.
Peter recalls from his training that crude oil is a high-risk product. In fact,
multiple OFAC advisories discuss the secret trading of Iranian oil. However, the
US$20 million oil import transaction is common for the industry. The buyer is
based in the US and is a well-known customer of the bank. Both the
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 102
intermediary and the supplier appear to be legitimate businesses with non-
sanctioned ownership and management.
As Peter continues his research, an unrelated overseas customer of the
bank’s Middle Eastern branch is designated by OFAC for its involvement in Iran
sanctions evasion. The bank freezes the customer’s accounts and initiates an
in-depth lookback review of its previous transactions. As a result, Peter quickly
finds out that over the past year the customer, which is allegedly controlled by
Iran, repeatedly made sizable money transfers, declaring them as revolving
credit, to the Hong Kong trading firm. Peter then starts to question the legality
of the US-based customer’s US$20 million transaction.
Peter discovers that while the Hong Kong trader occasionally takes on
cargoes of UAE oil for resale, it appears to be a front company of the National
Iranian Oil Co, which is a Specially Designated National. Peter rejects the
payment request and sends a report to OFAC.
Key takeaways
• Transaction monitoring systems can categorize alerts per typology, such
as high-risk jurisdictions known for sanctions evasion.
• Transaction monitoring and ongoing reviews must be aligned with
applicable regulations, guidelines, and policies.
• Transaction monitoring alerts should be analyzed against customer
behavior and regulatory alerts to identify unusual activity.
Detecting and Investigating Sanctions Evasion Techniques
Version 2.01
Page 103
Conclusion
Congratulations! You have completed this learning experience.
You should be able to:
• Identify sanctions evasion through ownership and trade.
• Describe sanctions evasion through payments.
• Explain the process for investigating a sanctions breach.
• Explain how to identify, freeze, and manage assets.

Conducting a Sanctions Risk
Assessment
Introduction
This case study illustrates important concepts from the courses. Explore the
cases to solve real-world problems. The related activities are not scored and
include feedback to help you learn. You will be able to apply and retain the key
concepts.
Correspondent banking sanctions risk
(Case example: Iris Bank)
Orchid FinCorp is a global financial institution that has been making strategic
acquisitions in order to offer its customers a greater variety of products and
services. Its latest acquisition, Iris Bank, is based in Greece. Iris Bank is known
for its correspondent banking services. Orchid FinCorp hoped that this
acquisition would bolster its capacity for international transactions and solidify
its status as a premier financial services provider worldwide. Unfortunately,
shortly after the acquisition, Orchid FinCorp was fined for a sanctions violation
committed by one of Iris Bank’s respondent banks, Violet Bank.
Orchid FinCorp has operations in the US and Europe. Its recent acquisition, Iris
Bank, provides correspondent banking services that serve as a vital
connection between regional banks in Cyprus, Lebanon, and Egypt, and major
financial institutions in the US, UK, and Europe.
A few months after the acquisition, Orchid FinCorp received a fine from the
Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) due to a violation by one of Iris Bank’s
respondent banks in Cyprus, Violet Bank.
Violet Bank had a history of serving Russian customers. Neither Iris Bank nor
Orchid FinCorp were aware of this. In a number of instances, transactions
Violet Bank facilitated breached EU, US, and UK sanctions related to Russia.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 1
When it acquired Iris Bank, Orchid FinCorp had not adequately evaluated the
risks posed by Violet Bank’s operations.
To address future risks in its correspondent banking services, Orchid FinCorp
has since updated its sanctions risk assessment (SRA) by implementing the
following measures:
• Conducting enhanced due diligence on the respondent banks in Cyprus,
Lebanon, and Egypt to ensure compliance with sanctions regulations
• Establishing ongoing monitoring and review mechanisms
• Conducting periodic reviews of correspondent banking activities
Correspondent banking sanctions risk (Activity:
Matching risks)
1. Orchid FinCorp’s sanctions risk profile has evolved with the acquisition of Iris
Bank. Match the correspondent banking services offered through Iris Bank
on the left with the applicable type of sanctions risk on the right.
Match each option on the left to its corresponding option on the right to
complete the matching assignment.
A. Check clearing correspondent banking
services
Jurisdiction
B. Services offered to a respondent bank
in Lebanon with alleged Hezbollah ties
Channel
C. Services offered to a respondent bank
in Cyprus that has had Russian
customers
Product
D. Services offered to FinTech companies
that onboard customers digitally
through online platforms

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 2
Correspondent banking sanctions risk (Activity: SRA
Update)
2. Which factor might be relevant for updating Orchid FinCorp’s
correspondent banking sanctions risk assessment after the acquisition of Iris
Bank?
o Iris Bank has a 9% minority shareholder whose majority owner is
designated by OFAC. This shareholding remains frozen and the rest of
the shareholders are well-known Greek businessmen.
o Iris Bank acquired a small retail bank in Greece one year ago.
o Iris Bank has respondent banking customers that previously served
high-risk jurisdictions.
o Iris Bank had one minor sanctions breach, which didn’t result in a
penalty, in the past seven years.
Key takeaways
When assessing correspondent banking sanctions risk, consider:
• The risks posed by respondent banks
• Applicable sanctions based on the jurisdictions and currencies involved
• The geographical operations to understand what sanctions regimes and
programs are most likely to be violated
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 3
Wealth management and private banking
sanctions risk (Case example: Compliance
crisis)
Orchid FinCorp recently acquired a boutique firm, Rosebud Private Wealth
(RPW), specializing in wealth management and private banking. Shortly after
the acquisition, Orchid FinCorp’s compliance team received an anonymous
alert from a person claiming to be a former employee. He alleged that RPW
had a long-standing practice of secretly serving customers ordinarily resident
in, or closely connected to, Iran.
The compliance team immediately launched an investigation and found the
allegations to be true. Orchid FinCorp had not updated its sanctions risk
assessment (SRA) after acquiring the firm, which is why the company did not
discover customers with connections to Iran.
RPW, now under Orchid FinCorp’s ownership, usually operates in low-risk
jurisdictions. The firm serves legal entity customers, offering them structured
investment products.
Prompted by the external alert, Orchid FinCorp’s compliance team made a
shocking discovery. Some of RPW’s key customers, that accounted for over
30% of its total fees in various years, had used front men to conceal the
identities of their ultimate beneficial owners (UBO). Many of these UBOs were
located in Iran and had ties to the government of Iran, in particular Iran’s
armed forces. Additionally, certain customers had set up complex legal
arrangements to hide assets linked to Iran.
Orchid FinCorp’s compliance team promptly informed the relevant authorities
and froze the accounts associated with the Iran-linked customers. They
awaited further instructions and evaluated potential penalties.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 4
To prevent a similar situation from happening again, Orchid FinCorp adjusted
its SRA methodology. Some updates made to the SRA process included:
• Scrutinizing ownership structures to identify sanctions exposure from
ultimate beneficial owners
• Scrutinizing customers’ sources of wealth to mitigate risks posed by front
men
• Conducting negative news searches to ensure the timely discovery of
connections to sanctioned jurisdictions or persons
• Monitoring transactions in alignment with customers’ investment and
financial crime risk profiles

Insurance sanctions risk (Case example:
Dahlia Insurance)
Orchid FinCorp was recently found to be noncompliant with sanctions
prohibitions on the provision of goods and services, namely the sale of
insurance products, within Russian-occupied areas of Ukraine. These
violations were identified through an internal audit, which indicated
weaknesses in the KYC process.
Orchid FinCorp had recently acquired Dahlia Insurance, a life insurance
business located in the US. Dahlia Insurance relies heavily on third-party
distributors for the marketing and sale of insurance products. This differs from
Orchid FinCorp’s existing insurance business model, which utilizes an internal
sales force.
Upon discovery of the violation, Orchid FinCorp’s leadership team recognized
the need for immediate action. They conducted a thorough sanctions risk
assessment (SRA) and significantly enhanced KYC policies and procedures.
The sanctions violation discovery came as a surprise to Orchid FinCorp’s
leadership. The illegal sale of insurance products to customers located in
Russian-occupied areas of Ukraine showed weaknesses in the existing KYC
process. The acquisition of Dahlia Insurance, which relies heavily on third
parties, created new sanctions risks. Third parties might compromise the
robustness of the KYC process when securing new customers. This increases
the risk of selling insurance products to:
• Residents in sanctioned areas
• Persons subject to asset freezes
• Persons subject to targeted financial prohibitions, such as investment bans
Orchid FinCorp failed to appropriately update its KYC processes to consider
these new risks. After a thorough SRA, Orchid FinCorp developed a plan to
significantly improve its internal controls.
First, the organization mandated thorough verifications of its customers’
addresses, including the use of geolocation. Second, it implemented a
rigorous sanctions screening process for all customers secured by third-party
distributors. This step helped mitigate the risk of unintentionally serving
persons subject to asset freezes or who reside in restricted areas. Finally,
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 7
Orchid FinCorp decided to require third-party distributors to attest that they
will strictly adhere to its KYC policies.
Insurance sanctions risk (Activity: Product risk)
5. Orchid FinCorp discovers that the product portfolio of its newly acquired
insurance subsidiary, Dahlia Insurance, includes investment-based insurance
“wrappers.” This means that multiple bankable assets, such as stocks and
hedge funds, are blended into one product.
When revising its sanctions risk assessment (SRA), what type of sanctions
prohibitions should Orchid FinCorp consider in order to analyze the risks
stemming specifically from its wrapper offerings, as opposed to its life
insurance policies?
o Restrictions on the facilitation of fundraising
o Prohibitions on the sale of insurance products in sanctioned
jurisdictions
o Asset-freeze designations
o Restrictions on investments in certain overseas legal entities
Key takeaways
• The use of third-party distributors could result in increased delivery-
channel sanctions risks.
• Risks include conducting business with individuals subject to asset freezes
or residents of sanctioned areas.
• Agents might not adhere to their principals’ KYC policies.

Corporate and investment banking
sanctions risk (Case example: A fortunate
divestment)
Orchid FinCorp has been actively acquiring new businesses as part of its
strategy to diversify product offerings. However, it recently faced regulatory
penalties for unintentional sanctions violations made through some of these
newly acquired companies. In response, Orchid FinCorp has made sanctions
compliance a priority, conducting thorough sanctions risk assessments (SRA)
for each line of business.
This heightened focus on sanctions risk prompted Orchid FinCorp to divest its
corporate and investment banking division, Azalea InvestPro, due to its high-
risk profile. This was the right decision, as shortly after the sale, Azalea
InvestPro breached US sanctions related to Iran, resulting in hefty penalties for
its new owner.
Azalea InvestPro serves multinational corporations, including those operating
in high-risk industries such as defense manufacturing, natural resources, and
dual-use goods. Many of its customers have complex ownership structures
and frequently engage in cross-border wire transfers. Customers are often
introduced through intermediaries.
Previously, Orchid FinCorp had contemplated divesting Azalea InvestPro to
cut operational expenses. Due to the competitive nature of the industry and
the need for cost efficiency, the prospect of a sale became increasingly
appealing.
The most recent SRA conducted by the bank’s corporate and investment
banking line of business validated Orchid FinCorp’s decision to divest.
Numerous sanctions risks were identified, primarily deriving from Azalea
InvestPro’s customer base in high-risk industries. These customers posed a
significant risk of engaging in transactions that could violate EU, US, and UK
sanctions restrictions.
In addition, serving customers with complex ownership structures exposed
Orchid FinCorp to the possibility of unintentionally processing transactions on
behalf of hidden designated beneficial owners. By selling Azalea InvestPro,
Orchid FinCorp was able to reduce risks and eliminate some of the controls
that were specifically related to corporate and investment banking.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 9
Several months after the sale, a news article revealed that the new owner had
been fined by the US Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC). Azalea
InvestPro had processed transactions in violation of US sanctions on the
Iranian oil sector. By selling Azalea InvestPro, Orchid FinCorp managed to
refocus its resources while avoiding the risk of penalties.

Framework for a robust sanctions
compliance program (Case example: Orchid
FinCorp)
Orchid FinCorp recently conducted sanctions risk assessments (SRA) for
each of its core lines of business. Due to numerous acquisitions in different
sectors, and a few unintentional sanctions breaches, the organization set out
to enhance its group-wide sanctions compliance program (SCP).
Not all of Orchid FinCorp’s lines of business had an increase in sanctions
exposure. This was because the organization recently made decisions to de-
risk and divest from business lines and activities that previously posed
significant sanctions risks.
The compliance team compiled the SRAs for each core business line,
conducted an enterprise-wide SRA to assess its overall sanctions exposure,
and ultimately adjusted its group-level sanctions compliance framework.
Orchid FinCorp’s compliance team conducted an enterprise-wide SRA that
considered the findings from the recent SRAs conducted for all core business
lines. They determined that the risk exposure increased in the following areas:
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 11
• Correspondent banking, due to an acquisition that included a portfolio of
high-risk customers from the Middle East
• Wealth management, due to the connection of certain customers of a
newly acquired business with Iran or with the Iranian regime
• Insurance, due to the acquisition of a company that relied heavily on third-
party distributors
The team further determined that Orchid FinCorp’s sanctions risk exposure
decreased in its corporate banking business line after the divestment of a
franchise that catered to complex legal entities in high-risk industries.
Considering these factors, Orchid FinCorp’s compliance team revised its SCP
to reflect the organizational changes. They revised the policies, procedures,
and controls for each business line. Next, they established a global sanctions
compliance standard to be implemented worldwide, applying the internal
controls relating to the business line with the highest level of risk to all other
business lines.
Then, Orchid FinCorp established a training program to educate staff on the
newly assessed risks. The compliance team also drafted an assurance
program to test if the new policies and procedures are being implemented
and followed as expected.
As a final step, the chairman of the board sent a message to employees to
explain the new standard and encourage a culture of compliance. The
chairman used that occasion to assure employees that the organization was
committed to a well-resourced, efficient, and effective SCP.

Key takeaways
• An enterprise-wide SRA should analyze the risks for each business line to
reach a conclusion about overall risk exposure.
• Line-of-business SCPs should address the risks identified in the enterprise-
wide SRA.
• A revised enterprise-wide SCP should set a common compliance standard
and encourage a culture of compliance.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 13
Conclusion
Each business line poses distinct risks to an organization. Consider risks posed
by factors such as respondent bank operations, complex structures, and
delivery channels. In cases of considerable risk, divestment might be the best
course of action. An enterprise-wide sanctions risk assessment helps adjust
individual line-of-business sanctions compliance, establishing organizational
standards and nurturing a culture of compliance.

Sanctions Licensing and
Permissible Activities
Introduction
This case study illustrates important concepts from the courses. Explore the
cases to solve real-world problems. The related activities are not scored and
include feedback to help you learn. You will be able to apply and retain the key
concepts.
General licenses or exemptions (Case
example: Water Relief)
Bettany’s Bank is a prominent global financial institution headquartered in the
UK. It serves many customers within the charity sector and facilitates
international aid efforts. One of these customers is Water Relief, a German-
based non-governmental organization (NGO) led by an American director.
Recently, Water Relief approached Bettany’s Bank seeking approval to
provide humanitarian relief to Afghanistan.
In response, Bettany’s Bank asks Water Relief to submit a comprehensive
proposal outlining the logistics. This includes information on the recipients,
distribution process, and intended purpose. Bettany’s Bank finds that OFAC
has a general license that will cover this activity, while the EU and the Office of
Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI) have applicable exemptions. After
thoroughly checking the conditions, Bettany’s Bank allows the project.
After receiving the humanitarian aid proposal from Water Relief, Bettany’s
Bank checks the details and considers the conditions for the relevant general
license and exemptions. Because Bettany’s Bank operates in the UK and
Water Relief is a European organization with an American director, it needs to
consider UK, EU, and US sanctions.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 15
Considering all these factors, Bettany’s Bank determines that the project
meets the relevant requirements for the OFAC general license and EU and
OFSI exemptions. Water Relief will be allowed to use a bank operating in
Afghanistan for its transactions related to humanitarian aid.
Next, Bettany’s Bank checks the duration of the general license and
exemptions. It discovers that the OFSI and EU exemptions expire in 12 months.
Bettany’s Bank decides to allow the project but will check the status one
month prior to the expiration. If an extension exists at that time, the bank will
allow the project to continue. If not, the charity may be able to apply for
specific licenses from the UK and Germany.
Although there are no relevant reporting requirements, Bettany’s Bank uses
enhanced due diligence to monitor the project because it is in a high-risk area.
This includes manual screening of all transactions within the project, screening
of any potential partners in Afghanistan, and regularly checking for any
amendments to the licenses.
General licenses or exemptions (Activity: A French
charity)
8. A French nongovernmental organization (NGO) specializes in
reconstructing villages that have been damaged by war. The NGO has
launched a new project in Syria and approaches its US bank asking it to fund
the project through a Syrian state-owned bank. The US bank would like to
help its client. What should it do?
o File a suspicious activity report (SAR).
o Refuse to undertake the project because it cannot send funds into
Syria.
o Check whether any exemptions and general licenses could apply to
the project.
o Tell the NGO to apply to OFAC and the French regulator for the
appropriate licenses or exemptions.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 16
Key takeaways
• Humanitarian aid for sanctioned jurisdictions might be permitted by a
general license or an exemption.
• You might need more than one license if you operate in multiple
jurisdictions.
• Conduct enhanced due diligence for projects in high-risk areas.
• Check reporting requirements and the expiration date of any license or
exemption.
Specific licenses or exceptions (Case
example: H&H law firm)
Jones Bank, a growing financial institution in the UK, encounters a unique
scenario with one of its esteemed customers, Hawkins & Hawkins (H&H). H&H
is a prominent law firm, also located in the UK. It is tasked with representing a
sanctioned entity involved in a lawsuit, which presents a variety of regulatory
complexities. Before it can take on this client, H&H must secure a specific
license from the UK regulator, the Office of Financial Sanctions
Implementation (OFSI), to facilitate the payment of reasonable legal fees.
Jones Bank must thoroughly analyze the license before it is submitted and
after its approval. It must then establish procedures to appropriately monitor
the funds and confirm that they are within the scope of the license.
Before H&H submits its application to OFSI, Jones Bank’s compliance team
carefully reviews it to ensure that every clause is thoroughly understood and
encompasses the scope of the request. The stakes are high, and one misstep
could result in significant repercussions. The license, once approved, would
allow the sanctioned entity to send money to H&H’s account at Jones Bank.
After extensive review, Jones Bank allows H&H to proceed with the
application.
Upon OFSI’s approval of the license, Jones Bank takes further action to protect
itself. The license will allow the payment of legal fees to H&H’s account at
Jones Bank, and payment for other necessary expenses, such as counsel or
expert witnesses. Recognizing the gravity of the situation, the compliance
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 17
team implements enhanced monitoring protocols for H&H’s account. Every
transaction is subject to manual screening, ensuring strict adherence to the
conditions of the license.
As required by the license, the compliance team provides regular reports and
bank statements to OFSI. These documents help ensure transparency and
accountability. By strictly adhering to the conditions of the license to ensure
compliance, Jones Bank successfully helps its respected client and protects
itself from potential regulatory penalties.

transactions to cover the basic needs of the individual and his dependents.
These include the payment of insurance premiums, taxes, and mortgage
payments. Felicity Bank must determine its next steps. Place the following
steps in the most logical sequential order.

Sequence # Description

Report to the regulator.
Check the reporting conditions of the license.
Understand the scope of the activity covered.
Check that the administrative details are correct.
Use manual screening for payments.
