Key takeaways
• Only the customer, not the financial institution, can apply for specific
licenses.
• Applicants should seek a license before the activity takes place.
• Financial institutions should check the conditions for the use of licenses,
and establish policies and processes to monitor their use.
• Financial institutions must provide reports to the regulator if required.
Effective application of humanitarian
licenses (Case example: Judea First)
Jarvis Bank is a US-based financial institution. Its customer, Judea First, is a
non-governmental organization (NGO) that is involved in the preservation and
restoration of archaeological and historic sites in the Middle East. Judea First
recently approached Jarvis Bank seeking approval for its latest project to
protect the ancient city of Palmyra, Syria.
Jarvis Bank asks for the details of the project and checks whether it is
permissible under OFAC’s licensing grounds. It finds that Judea First can use
the OFAC Syria NGO general license. However, there are specific conditions
that it must meet to proceed with the project.
Jarvis Bank begins reviewing Judea First’s project details. At first, it seems that
the project activities do not fall under the category of humanitarian aid or
assistance. However, Jarvis Bank realizes that these activities are covered, but
they have an additional reporting requirement. Judea First is required to file
quarterly reports with OFAC within 30 days of the end of each quarter. This
reporting requirement applies only to preservation activities and not to other
humanitarian activities. Jarvis Bank will need to monitor the transactions and
file these reports along with any reports from its other customers each
quarter.
Jarvis Bank’s compliance team confirms that the license allows Judea First to
use a Syrian state-owned bank as its partner in Syria. Before approving the
project, the compliance team conducts enhanced due diligence (EDD) on the
other project details. This includes finding information on Judea First’s
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 19
partners in Syria, the payment route that will be used, and the materials that it
will need to export.
The compliance team discovers that the project will involve specific
equipment that requires an additional export license. Before the project can
begin, Judea First must apply for the appropriate license from the US Bureau
of Industry and Security (BIS). If the compliance team had not conducted EDD,
this export licensing requirement might have been missed. Jarvis Bank could
have suffered fines and penalties.

Delisting impact on KYC and ongoing
monitoring (Case example: Xavia Trading)
Xavia Trading, an international commerce company, was previously placed on
the OFAC Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list due to unintentional
violations of sanctions on Iran. After a recent change in ownership, Xavia
Trading has created stricter compliance measures and requests to be
delisted. The request is granted by OFAC.
Xavia Trading reaches out to its bank, Wren Bank, to inform them that it has
been delisted by OFAC. The company requests that its accounts be unfrozen.
Wren Bank must confirm that this information is accurate. Then, the bank
must ensure that Xavia Trading’s KYC information is up to date. It must also
establish procedures for managing the risks posed by this delisted customer.
The compliance team at Wren Bank knows that it must approach this delisting
situation carefully, because frozen accounts are governed by specific
regulations. Wren Bank does not have the power to unfreeze the account
without specific license authorization from OFAC. Xavia Trading has applied
for the appropriate license from OFAC to unfreeze its account. However,
Wren Bank cannot restore the account until the license is granted.
After several months, OFAC grants the license and Wren Bank begins the
process of unfreezing Xavia Trading’s accounts. The compliance department
seeks updated information to verify the customer’s sanctioned status and
identity. It conducts new KYC checks on Xavia Trading’s business activities and
reassesses its risk rating.
Wren Bank includes Xavia Trading’s delisted status in its records. Due to this
status as well as risk factors caused by its business activities, Wren Bank
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 21
decides that Xavia Trading should remain a high-risk customer. The bank
decides to maintain enhanced due diligence (EDD) on Xavia Trading, which
includes transaction monitoring and an annual status review.
Finally, the compliance team informs internal stakeholders of the change to
Xavia Trading’s status. These include the senior management team, the risk
management team, and the client relationship team.
Delisting impact on KYC and ongoing monitoring
(Activity: Denis Kormonov)
11. Denis Kormonov is a member of the Belarus government who was
previously on the OFAC Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list. He
contacts his US bank to tell them that he is being delisted. He asks that the
bank unfreeze his accounts and property and allow funds to be transferred
to his bank account in Germany. Which action should the US bank take first?
o Transfer funds to the German bank account.
o Apply for a license from OFAC to unfreeze the account.
o Check with the regulator to confirm that the customer has been
delisted.
o Refresh the customer’s KYC and assess his risk rating.
Key takeaways
• Obtain delisting authorization from the regulator before unfreezing a
customer account.
• Perform KYC and assess risk rating after a customer is delisted.
• Perform enhanced due diligence for recently delisted customers, if
needed.
• Communicate the delisted customer status to all relevant internal
departments.

Licensing challenges (Case example:
AstroNav Solutions)
AstroNav Solutions is a French communications company that specializes in
navigational satellite equipment. It is planning to export equipment to a
tourism company, ExpeditionXperience Travels, in the Democratic Republic of
Congo (DRC). AstroNav Solutions has applied for an export license and has
contacted its bank, Orion Bank, for help with the project.
The compliance team at Orion Bank takes steps to understand the scope of
the project and checks whether AstroNav Solutions has undertaken the
appropriate due diligence. They request more information about the goods,
the customer, and the ultimate end-user of the equipment. AstroNav
Solutions provides the necessary information and presents the appropriate
export license to the bank. Only then does Orion Bank transfer the funds.
Some satellite equipment is considered a dual-use good, as it can be used for
both civilian and military applications. Because of this, Orion Bank checks for
the product codes, known as HS codes, within the sanctions regulations. The
codes reveal that the navigational satellites fall under the category of dual-use
goods.
After screening the customer and conducting open-source checks, Orion
Bank does not find any red flags. ExpeditionXperience Travels, the company
that AstroNav Solutions is exporting to, conducts adventure trips in the DRC
and has a valid use for the equipment. ExpeditionXperience Travels is not
subject to any sanctions, and the goods will go directly from AstroNav
Solutions’s warehouse in France to the DRC. AstroNav Solutions will only need
a license from the French authorities.
However, Orion Bank follows a risk-based approach and considers whether
the project is likely to breach sanctions regulations. The DRC is a high-risk
jurisdiction which is subject to both UN and EU sanctions for the purpose of
resolving armed conflict and promoting democracy. Orion Bank takes
additional steps to confirm that AstroNav Solutions’s payment channels are
appropriate and checks the terms and conditions of the license once it is
received.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 23
When the compliance team feels confident, they accept the project. As a final
precaution, Orion Bank establishes enhanced due diligence (EDD) and
ongoing transaction monitoring to mitigate any remaining risks.
Licensing challenges (Activity: Dynamic Systems)
12. Dynamic Systems is a US-based company that manufactures ball bearings.
These items are commonly used in food processors, other household
goods, and bicycles. However, ball bearings can also be used in missiles and
projectiles. Dynamic Systems asks its bank to finance its exports to Somalia.
Which steps should the bank take next? Place the following steps in the
most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
Manually monitor all transactions.
Screen Dynamic Systems and other companies in the
payment chain.
Check the HS code for the goods.
Check whether multiple countries are involved.
Check whether an export license is in place.
Key takeaways
When applying for an export license:
• Consider all parts of the supply chain.
• Recognize the possible need for different licenses if goods are sent to
more than one country.
• Check for financial sanctions as well as export requirements.
• Exercise enhanced due diligence for any military goods or dual-use items.

Scope of licensing (Case example: Chief
Builders)
Chief Builders, a construction company based in the UK, completed a
construction project in Russia just before the invasion of Ukraine in 2022. Chief
Builders had not yet been paid £150,000 for the final stage of the project when
the Russian counterparty and its bank were sanctioned.
After seeking legal advice, Chief Builders applied for a specific license from
the UK regulator, the Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI). It
could apply under the prior obligation category which allows payments to be
fulfilled if the contract was signed before the person or entity was sanctioned.
While Chief Builders was waiting for its specific license to be approved, OFSI
released a general license. Chief Builders decided to use this instead and
asked its bank, Ignatius Bank, to process the transfer.
A new general license was released, which covered prior obligations related
to the Russia sanctions regime shortly after Chief Builders applied for the
specific license. Chief Builders promptly informed Ignatius Bank about the
general license, and the bank checked the license requirements.
To qualify, Ignatius Bank determined that the payment must be for the benefit
of a UK person or entity. This would allow Chief Builders, as a UK company, to
use the general license. The requirements also specified that the total
remaining amount under the contractual obligation could not exceed
£200,000. Fortunately, Chief Builders was owed less than that. The bank
confirmed that the general license would allow the payment to come from a
Russian-sanctioned entity through a Russian-sanctioned bank.
Next, Ignatius Bank ensured that Chief Builders fully understood the reporting
requirements under the general license. Chief Builders needed to send
certain documentation to the regulator within 30 days of receiving payment
under the license. This included the legally enforceable contractual obligation,
any relevant invoices, and details of the payment.
According to this general license, the reporting requirements would be the
responsibility of the user, Chief Builders, rather than the financial institution.
Chief Builders was also required to keep records for six years of all activity
under the general license.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 25
Scope of licensing (Activity: License scenarios)
13. Licenses and exemptions generally allow activity that would otherwise be
prohibited under sanctions regulations. There are different types of licenses
and exemptions. For each of the scenarios on the left, choose which type of
license or exemption applies. Only one type of license or exemption applies

Key takeaways
• Sometimes general licenses and specific licenses can cover the same
activities.
• Always check the conditions, as they vary for each license.
• Many general licenses have a transaction amount limit.
• Most licenses have recordkeeping and reporting requirements.
Conclusion
General licenses, or exemptions, might authorize the delivery of humanitarian
aid for sanctioned areas. Sometimes, more than one license is needed.
Financial institutions cannot apply for a specific license on behalf of a
customer. Some general licenses and specific licenses might cover the same
activities. Financial institutions should always check the conditions for the use
of licenses, set up appropriate monitoring, and conduct EDD when necessary.
If a customer is delisted, a financial institution must wait to receive a license
from the regulator before unfreezing the customer’s account.

Sanctions Screening
Introduction
This case study illustrates important concepts from the courses. Explore the
cases to solve real-world problems. The related activities are not scored and
include feedback to help you learn. You will be able to apply and retain the key
concepts.
Fuzzy logic, AST, and reading sanctions lists
(Case example: Sapphire Bank)
Sapphire Bank discovered an issue with its new automated screening tool
(AST) during a testing round. Due to date and name variances between a
sanctions listing and transaction information, the AST failed to flag a
transaction of a sanctioned individual. Luckily, the transaction was fake for the
purpose of testing and no error was made. After adjusting the AST’s
thresholds and fuzzy logic parameters, the sanctions compliance team was
able to resolve this issue successfully. If Sapphire Bank had not conducted
testing, it could have suffered fines and penalties if a sanctions violation had
occurred.
Sanctions listing
Name: Maria Kircelenko
Location: Sergey Street, Minsk, Belarus
Date of birth: 03/08/1975
Nationality: Belarus
Sapphire Bank recently implemented a new AST to enhance its sanctions
screening processes. The bank’s independent audit team conducted a series
of tests on the new tool to verify its effectiveness. The tests identified gaps in
the AST’s configuration that could have had serious implications if not
remedied.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 28
During a test transaction involving a known sanctioned individual, the AST
failed to identify the connection due to a variety of factors. The date of birth
was misinterpreted by the system as March 08, 1975. The date of birth was in
US format, with the month, day, and year. The date of birth should have been
recognized as August 03, 1975, which is the European format, with day, month,
and year. In addition, the test transaction listed the beneficiary under the
name Maria Kircelenko-Minsk, which was not recognized as a potential true
match for Maria Kircelenko.
To prevent this issue from happening again, Sapphire Bank lowered the
threshold of the AST to reduce the risk of false negatives. The bank also
updated its fuzzy logic matching algorithm to accommodate date and name
variances. After further testing, the enhancements proved their effectiveness.

was able to match “DPRK” to “Korea,” meaning North Korea, as well as the

SWIFT message screening (Case example:
Pearl Bank)
Pearl Bank, located in Germany, recently conducted a review of a SWIFT MX
message that was flagged by the bank’s filtering system. SWIFT is a system
that organizations use in wire transfer payments. Keywords found in the
SWIFT message triggered an alert related to a payment for a potential
shipment of prohibited goods. After obtaining additional information from the
customer, the sanctions compliance team at Pearl Bank ultimately
determined that the transaction did not violate any applicable sanctions
restrictions. The transaction was therefore fulfilled by the bank.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies

Pearl Bank received an international funds transfer request from its customer
IcePlant, the owner of a crude oil tanker.
The transaction was flagged by the bank’s filtering system because of certain
keywords, such as “Crude Oil” and “Ceyhan Port,” which were found in the
“Remittance information” field of the SWIFT transfer.
"Ceyhan Port" was flagged due to being included in an internal customs list.
The list captures ports with proximity to sanctioned countries, ensuring they
are not used as intermediary stations for shipping goods to or from these
countries. “Crude Oil” was flagged because it is a restricted good in certain
jurisdictions.
Following the alert, Pearl Bank conducted a detailed review of the transaction.
It was able to obtain a certificate of origin stating that the oil was from
Azerbaijan and had been transferred to Ceyhan Port through a pipeline. The
bill of lading indicated that the crude oil had been shipped to Tunisia. Pearl
Bank authorized the transaction to proceed, because it demonstrated
compliance with sanctions regulations and did not involve shipping restricted
goods to or from a sanctioned area.

Trade activity screening (Case example:
Jade Bank)
Jade Bank recently received a request from its customer, Marida Limited, for
an import-related letter of credit. Upon receiving the request, Jade Bank’s
sanctions compliance officer screened the customer’s shipping information
against sanctions lists. While no matches were found for the counterparties,
ports, or vessels involved, a match was found for the code used to classify the
goods.
Jade Bank conducted further investigation and uncovered a possible attempt
by the customer to evade EU sanctions. The bank ultimately decided not to
grant the customer’s request. This decision possibly saved Jade Bank from the
penalties and fines that it might have been subject to if the transaction had
been a part of a sanctions evasion scheme.
Shipping documentation
Seller: Vladiviking Company
Consignee: Marida Limited
Shipping
Vrachas Limited, owner of the vessel Marina
company:
Marida Limited, a fish-trading company, is a customer of Jade Bank in
Germany. Marida Limited applied for a letter of credit related to the import of
frozen crustaceans that it was expected to receive in Finland.
Jade Bank obtained all the necessary shipping documents. During manual
screening of the shipping information against various sanctions lists, the
compliance team discovered a match to a customs code indicating that the
product is prohibited from importation into the EU from Russia.
The port of loading was in Estonia, according to the shipping documents.
Because Estonia borders Russia, the compliance team conducted further
investigation on the shipping route by using vessel tracking technology. The
vessel stopped at the Port of St. Petersburg. It arrived there empty, but left
fully loaded just days before docking at the Port of Tallinn in Estonia. This led
the bank to conclude that the product had been likely purchased from Russia.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 38
When asked to provide a certificate of origin, the customer refused. Because
of that, along with the suspicious stop in St. Petersburg, Jade Bank determined
that the risk was too high and refused to proceed with the transaction.
Trade activity screening (Activity: Ruby Bank)
22. Ruby Bank is performing due diligence on a trade finance transaction for
their customer, Chipmaster, that is registered in Malta. A Spanish company
named Hawiwi purchased computer chips from Chipmaster. The
transaction is in Euros and all banks involved are in Europe. After screening
relevant information, Ruby Bank determines that Hawiwi is included on the
US Department of Commerce’s Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS) Entity
List. What next steps should Ruby Bank take? Place the following steps in the
most logical sequential order.

Key takeaways
For trade finance transactions, obtain:
• Shipping documents, including bills of exchange, bills of lading, invoices,
and certificates of origin
• Information related to counterparties, ports, and vessels
Use vessel tracking to identify recent stops in or near sanctioned jurisdictions.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 39
Conclusion
There are various techniques and tools available to assist financial institutions
with sanctions screening. Automated screening tool (AST) threshold
calibration determines which and how many alerts will be generated. AST
configurations should be regularly tested for accuracy. The fuzzy logic
algorithm increases the effectiveness of the screening process by
recognizing name variations and partial matches. SWIFT messages contain
keywords that should be captured by screening technology. Important trade-
related information, such as the origin of goods, counterparties, ports, and
vessels, can be captured by screening shipping documents.

Identifying Beneficial Ownership
and Applying the 50% Rule
Introduction
This case study illustrates important concepts from the courses. Explore the
cases to solve real-world problems. The related activities are not scored and
include feedback to help you learn. You will be able to apply and retain the key
concepts.
The OFAC 50% rule (Case example: Pelican
LLC)
Pelican LLC ownership
A US financial institution is preparing to onboard a new client, Pelican LLC,
registered in the state of Delaware. For AML purposes, the financial institution
identifies ultimate beneficial owners (UBO) as those who hold 25% or more of
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 41
the equity interests. For sanctions purposes, the financial institution adheres to
the US Office of Foreign Assets Control’s (OFAC) 50% rule.
An initial analysis finds that Hummingbird Ltd., which is a publicly-listed
company, is 20% owned by two Indian businessmen, while the rest is free float.
An internet search for the other Pelican LLC shareholders determines that
Eagle Ltd. is wholly-owned by a Russian businessman. The financial institution
decides to perform due diligence to identify the owners behind all the
shareholders of Pelican LLC. The investigation reveals an aggregate
ownership connection to a designated Russian politically exposed person
(PEP) that prompts the financial institution to file a suspicious activity report
(SAR).
The investigation reveals that Pelican LLC has no identifiable UBOs for AML
purposes under the ownership standard. At the same time, the Russian
businessman who owns Eagle Ltd. is a close associate of a high-ranking
political figure, Maxim Goldenman, who has been designated by OFAC. Maxim
Goldenman owns Owl Ltd., while his wife owns Swan Ltd. and his son owns
Parrot Ltd. Goldenman’s wife and son are not sanctioned.
In total, 55% of Pelican’s shares are owned by a sanctioned individual, his wife,
his son, and his close associate. There is also a risk of additional related
holdings hidden in Hummingbird Ltd.’s publicly held shares.
According to OFAC’s 50% rule, only Owl Ltd. is directly owned by the
sanctioned individual. Owl Ltd. only has 12% ownership in Pelican LLC, so
Pelican LLC is not considered a designated entity. However, because it is 55%
owned in the aggregate by a sanctioned person and several closely related
individuals, the sanctions risk is high. The financial institution decides not to
onboard Pelican LLC, while also filing a SAR with the US Financial Crimes
Enforcement Network.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02

The OFAC 50% rule (Activity: H LLC)

o H LLC is not subject to an asset freeze because C Ltd., G Ltd., C SA,
and G SA each hold less than a 50% ownership stake.
o H LLC is subject to an asset freeze because C Ltd. and G SA are
owned by two SDNs and their aggregate stake is equal to 50%.
o H LLC is subject to an asset freeze because G Ltd. and C SA are
owned by two SDNs and their aggregate stake is equal to 50%.

Key takeaways
• Different thresholds may apply for the identification of UBOs under
sanctions versus AML compliance requirements.
• Stopping due diligence after reaching the lower AML threshold may fail to

Ownership versus control and the OFAC,
EU, and UK 50% rules (Case example: SDNs
X and Y)
A sanctions compliance officer at a US-based financial institution is
performing due diligence on two customers, X and Y. X and Y were both
recently designated by the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC) and the
Council of the EU, due to connections each had to two different Russian
designated persons. X was also designated by the UK.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 45
The financial institution has two branches, one is in Portugal and one is in the
UK. The two branches share a common customer, Company F, that is
connected to the designated customers by a complex ownership structure.
In considering whether Company F’s accounts should be frozen, the
compliance officer applies the appropriate beneficial ownership rules for
each branch location. They determine that only the accounts in Portugal
should be frozen.
The financial institution is in the US, so OFAC’s 50% rule must be considered.
Only Company D’s 20% stake is considered, as it is indirectly 55% owned by Y.
Therefore, none of Company F’s accounts are subject to an asset freeze
under OFAC.
For the Portugal branch, the EU’s 50% rule must be considered. The stakes of
Company C, Company D, and Company E should be considered as they are
either controlled or 50% or more owned by sanctioned entities. Company C is
controlled by X as the settlor of the trust, Company D is 55% owned by Y, and
Company E is controlled by Y as chairman of the board. The stakes must be
aggregated even if the underlying sanctioned individuals are not the same.
The aggregated total is 70%, so Company F’s accounts in Portugal must be
frozen.
Under the UK 50% rule, only Company C’s 20% ownership stake should be
considered. This is because only X has been sanctioned in the UK and only
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 46
Company C is more than 50% owned or controlled by an SDN. Company F’s
accounts in the UK are not subject to an asset freeze, because Company C
does not own more than 50% of Company F.

Key takeaways
• To calculate relevant ownership stakes, determine which jurisdictions’
ownership rules apply.
• To calculate beneficial ownership in the EU or UK, you must consider
control in addition to ownership. In the EU you might also have to consider
aggregate ownership.
• Financial institutions with a branch in a different jurisdiction than its country
of registration must consider ownership rules in both jurisdictions.
Restructuring ownership interests and front
companies (Case example: Oxana Ltd.)
A sanctions compliance analyst at a US financial institution is considering
onboarding a new client, Oxana Ltd., registered in the British Virgin Islands.
The beneficial owner of Oxana Ltd. is Alex Winter, who worked for 10 years as
the finance director of Samare Ltd., a company that was recently designated
by the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC).
Oxana Ltd.’s balance sheet shows that it was primarily funded by a loan of
US$50 million. This loan came from a company called Irongate Ltd. Oxana
Ltd.’s main source of funds is a US$48 million investment in the share capital of
Maryland Ltd., which is also a Specially Designated National (SDN) under OFAC.
When the compliance analyst reviews the ownership structure more carefully,
he discovers that Alex Winter has several connections to a Russian oligarch.
Based on the evidence, the compliance officer concludes that Alex Winter is
acting as a front man to help the Russian oligarch avoid sanctions.
The additional research reveals the following:
• Alex Winter has a limited source of wealth, so it is not reasonable that he
would own a company with assets of US$48 million. The assets were fully
funded by the loan from Irongate Ltd.
• Alex Winter has been a close associate of Maxim Goldenman, a Russian
oligarch, recently designated by OFAC. Samare Ltd., which was Winter’s
former employer, and Maryland Ltd. are both owned by Goldenman.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 50
• The ownership structure of Irongate Ltd. indicates that it is controlled by
Maxim Goldenman, though it is not directly designated.
• The loan granted to the company by Irongate Ltd. and the investment in
the shares of Maryland Ltd. occurred a week before Maxim Goldenman
was designated by OFAC.
Given the red flags, the compliance analyst forms a reasonable suspicion that
the prospective client is owned in disguise or controlled by Maxim Goldenman
and that Alex Winter is acting as a front man. They might have entered into
this arrangement to safeguard Maxim Goldenman’s assets from being frozen.
The compliance analyst decides not to onboard the client and proceeds with
reporting to the authorities.
Restructuring ownership interests (Activity: Saso Ltd.)
28. Saso Ltd. is a company owned by two individuals: 75% by Alexander Osman
and 25% by Kolsoman Abramich. Saso Ltd. has accounts with an EU financial
institution.
On January 3, Mr. Osman is included on the EU sanctions list, which is published
in the Official Journal of the European Union in the evening of the same day.
The chief executive officer for Saso Ltd. contacts the financial institution to
report that Mr. Osman sold 26% of his holdings to Mr. Abramich on the morning
of January 3. She states that Mr. Osman now owns only 49% of the company,
which, in theory, means that the accounts of Saso Ltd. should not be frozen.
An investigation into the sale reveals that the payment for the shares was
funded by a loan from Mr. Osman to Mr. Abramich.
Which indicates that the sale might not be legitimate?
o The sales agreement was drafted on January 3.
o The payment for the sale was funded by a loan from Mr. Osman to Mr.
Abramich.
o Mr. Osman and Mr. Abramich are business partners.

Front companies (Activity: Red Rock Limited)
29. A UK financial institution is reviewing a new customer. It is an oil trading
company called Red Rock Limited, which is considered moderate risk. The
shareholders all hold UK passports. The ownership structure of the company
is as follows:
• 53% owned by Ahmed Amin
• 33% owned by Mohamed Asiz
• 14% owned by others
During the review, a blog post is identified that mentions that Red Rock has
purchased oil from Syria. An internet search shows that the shareholders
appear to be distant relatives of Syria’s president, who is sanctioned by the
UK.
Which is the best next step the financial institution should take?
o Decide whether to onboard the customer.
o Decide whether to file a suspicious activity report (SAR).
o Conduct enhanced due diligence on the ownership structure and
source of capital.
o Obtain sanctions attestations from current shareholders.
Key takeaways
Designated individuals might try to hide their ownership using front men. Red
flags that could indicate this are:
• Declared beneficial owners with undetermined sources of wealth
• Beneficial owners previously employed by SDNs
• Funding by companies owned by sanctioned entities
• Restructuring of assets shortly before designations are made

Disguised ownership (Case example: Machi
Ltd.)
A sanctions compliance officer at a financial institution headquartered in
Cyprus is performing a review of an existing customer, Machi Ltd. The
company, which was incorporated a year ago, is registered in Cyprus. It was
recently reclassified as high-risk due to an increase in wire transfers to or from
countries neighboring Russia.
The sanctions compliance officer identifies several red flags that need to be
investigated. Machi Ltd.’s beneficial owner is a Russian national, though she
resides in Cyprus. The company purchases electronic products from a US
supplier. The products are considered to be dual-use and are primarily sold to
a buyer in Israel.
The red flags indicate that Machi Ltd. could be engaged in sanctions evasion.
The compliance officer ultimately decides to terminate the relationship with
the customer.
Upon further review, the compliance officer discovers that the beneficial
owner of Machi Ltd. has only recently moved to Cyprus and obtained a
residency permit. Previously, she lived in Russia and worked for 15 years at a
defense company, which has now been sanctioned by the Office of Foreign
Assets Control (OFAC) and the EU.
In addition, the primary buyer of the dual-use goods is Mini Ltd., which is
registered in Israel. The bills of lading indicate that the end-user of the traded
products is also Mini Ltd. The company claims to sell electronic products as
spare parts for fire alarms, but their website is poorly designed with many
broken hyperlinks. This indicates that Mini Ltd. could be a front company and
that the goods it buys are used for illicit purposes.
An investigation into the flow of the funds credited to Machi Ltd.’s account
from Mini Ltd. reveals that Mini Ltd. has limited activity in its home market of
Israel. Its main buyer is a company registered in Turkey. A negative news
search finds an article that connects the company in Turkey with the sale of
prohibited dual-use goods to Russia.
The additional information indicates that Machi Ltd. might be controlled by the
sanctioned defense company in Russia and that the dual-use goods are
illegally shipped to Russia. The trade documents provided by the client could
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 53
be fake, making the transactions look legitimate. The compliance officer
decides to terminate the business relationship and submits a suspicious
activity report (SAR).
Disguised ownership (Activity: Helmet LLP)

registered in the UK, with the following findings. Please review the findings

• Front companies might be used for illegal trade in prohibited goods with

• Financial institutions must look for red flags in the supply chain and the

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 54
Conclusion
Sanctioned entities might try to evade sanctions by using complex ownership
structures or by hiding behind front men. When calculating beneficial
ownership, examine both ownership and control, and know the jurisdictional
requirements of each. Adjust due diligence controls for sanctions. It might be
necessary to reduce ownership thresholds to ensure that hidden aggregate
ownerships are identified.

A complex investigation (Case example:
Goldman Group AST alert)
A German financial institution has recently onboarded Goldman Group, a
German company. Established three years ago, the company manufactures
and trades electronic devices. Its business has expanded into Europe and Asia,
including Ukraine and Russia. Sales in Russia were stopped after the full-scale
invasion of Ukraine began in early 2022.
Six months after the customer was onboarded, an alert is generated by the
financial institution’s automated screening tool (AST) for an incoming
payment from an individual on the US Bureau of Industry and Security’s (BIS)
Denied Persons List. The financial institution decides to start an investigation
into the customer. The investigation uncovers a new major shareholder and
two new customers of Goldman Group that raise red flags for sanctions
evasion.
The AST alert generated a true match against an incoming payment from a
counterparty included on the US BIS Denied Persons List. Because Goldman
Group trades dual-use goods and has previously done business in Russia, the
compliance department decides to open a sanctions investigation.
The compliance investigation team reviews several sources of information
about Goldman Group. This includes information from the customer’s file on
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 56
the company’s ownership structure, the past transaction history, the main
counterparties, and the major shareholders. Next, the investigators screen all
related parties and counterparties against sanctions lists and adverse media
lists. Though the customer’s file includes the detailed corporate structure of
Goldman Group, the documents are from a year ago, so the team utilizes the
registrar of companies to confirm the current structure.
Shipping documents prove that the transaction with the US BIS Denied Person
did not involve US-controlled goods, so the true match did not implicate a
potential breach of US export control regulations. However, the investigation
team’s findings have also revealed that Goldman Group has a new majority
shareholder, a man named Alex Dovalni. He was arrested in the US five years
ago on charges of exporting dual-use goods without a license. The charges
were later dropped. The team also finds that Goldman Group’s sales
increased 50% in the last six months, largely due to two new customers in
Kazakhstan and the United Arab Emirates.
Mr. Dovalni’s criminal history and the new customers’ proximity to sanctioned
countries are red flags. The team decides to expand their investigation into
Goldman Group.

Identifying red flags of trade-related
evasion methods by KYC (Case example:
Investigating counterparties)
A sanctions investigation into Goldman Group uncovers two new customers
who are responsible for most of the company’s sales in the last six months. Silver
Hand Ltd. is a wholesale electronics trading company established two years ago
in Kazakhstan. Bronze Hand Ltd. is a forwarding company established 10 years
ago in the Dubai Free Trade Zone. The fact that one is a forwarding company,
and the other is located close to Russia are red flags that need to be investigated.
The sanctions investigation team gathers more information on each customer
from corporate registers and internet research. Ultimately, the team cannot
determine the beneficial owners of the companies and they deepen the
investigation into Goldman Group.
The investigation team discovers that the website for Silver Hand Ltd. provides
limited information on the operations of the company. They tested the links to
order products and found that they did not work. When they search the
company’s address on Google Maps, it shows a small house in a village near
Astana, the capital of Kazakhstan. Because Bronze Hand Ltd. is a forwarding
company, the ultimate destination of goods cannot be determined.
Corporate documents for each company do not reveal beneficial owners.
Instead, the majority shareholder of each company is a nominee legal
representative. Goldman Group’s shipping documents for the customers also do
not reveal end-users. Instead, the final destinations are the customers’ corporate
addresses.
Because the investigation team cannot confirm the beneficial owners of the two
customers or the end-users of the goods, they work with the relationship
manager to obtain this information from Goldman Group. The relationship
manager contacts the finance director of Goldman Group, who states she has
no information about where the products end up once they are bought by
customers. These additional red flags generate further suspicion. Silver Hand Ltd.
appears to be a front company and is possibly being used to evade Russia
sanctions. Bronze Hand Ltd. is a forwarding company in Dubai, so they could be
violating sanctions by forwarding the products into sanctioned jurisdictions such
as Iran or Russia.

from an intermediary destination.

Conducting more research (Case example:
Alex Dovalni)
An investigation team at a German bank has found several red flags related to
their customer, Goldman Group, who trades electronic devices. The team
does not yet have evidence of any sanctions violations. They decide to further
investigate Alex Dovalni, Goldman Group’s largest shareholder.
In addition to an arrest record in the US, they find that Mr. Dovalni is 34 years
old. He was born in Russia, and previously worked as an architect in a little-
known firm based in Siberia, before investing in Goldman Group. He has
multiple bank accounts for holding companies he owns. The investigation
finds that Mr. Dovalni might be acting as a front man. The assets currently held
in his companies were purchased via a loan from a Russian politically exposed
person (PEP) who is currently designated by the US Office of Foreign Assets
Control (OFAC).
Alex Dovalni’s background as an architect is not relevant to the electronics
trade, so it is unusual that he would be the primary shareholder of Goldman
Group. Additionally, Mr. Dovalni’s probable salary as an architect in a small
Siberian firm is a red flag. It would not have provided him with enough money
to purchase the majority of shares in Goldman Group.
To research Mr. Dovalni more thoroughly, the investigation team submits a
314(b) request, on behalf of the financial institution’s US banking subsidiary, to
another financial institution where Mr. Dovalni is a customer. They learn he has
accounts for three different holding companies. The account statements
reveal that the companies hold several works of expensive art, real estate in
the south of France, and a yacht. The assets were primarily financed through
loans from a Panama-based company called Diamond 345 Ltd. When the
team screens Diamond 345 Ltd. against adverse media lists, they get a true
match against documents from the Panama Papers leak. The true match
shows that Diamond 345 Ltd. is controlled by a Russian PEP who has recently
been designated by OFAC as a Specially Designated National (SDN).
These findings indicate that Mr. Dovalni might not be the true owner of these
assets and could be acting as a front man for the designated PEP. It also
points to the possibility of Goldman Group being controlled by the SDN. Mr.
Dovalni’s background explains neither his interest in Goldman Group nor his
source of wealth or funds used to purchase the majority ownership interest.

• An in-depth sanctions investigation might need to be expanded to cover

Deciding to conclude an investigation (Case
example: Goldman Group sanctions
violation)
An investigation team at a German bank has gathered evidence indicating
that their customer, Goldman Group, is exporting electronic goods in violation
of applicable export prohibitions. The evidence also suggests a man named
Alex Dovalni is acting as a front man for a Russian Specially Designated
National (SDN) and is holding the majority of shares in Goldman Group on the
SDN’s behalf.
The team decides they have enough evidence to conclude their investigation.
They document the investigation according to their policy. Next, they draft a
suspicious activity report (SAR) and submit it to the financial intelligence unit
(FIU). They confer with their legal team about what voluntary disclosures need
to be made regarding possible sanctions violations, and what to do with
Goldman Group’s assets. Finally, they conduct a root cause analysis to learn
how to prevent a similar situation from occurring in the future.
The investigation team gathers the evidence and organizes it, ensuring each
step of the investigation is properly documented and all information sources
are filed appropriately. They use this evidence as supporting documentation
to draft the SAR, which describes in detail the initial suspicions, the red flags
that were uncovered, and the ultimate findings. They submit their report to
the German FIU.
Next, they confer with their legal team, to assess whether a voluntary
disclosure of a sanctions breach is necessary. The legal team also advises
them on what to do with Goldman Group’s accounts. Deciding whether to
freeze assets or close the accounts is complicated by the fact that the
financial institution is based in Germany, while the legal and regulatory issue at
stake has to do with US sanctions and export control restrictions.
Finally, the financial institution must assess the root cause of the sanctions
violation. When Goldman Group was onboarded, Alex Dovalni was not a
majority shareholder, so initial due diligence might not have identified the
sanctions risk. However, ongoing monitoring should have caught the
beneficial ownership change, and proper transaction monitoring could have
caught increasingly larger transactions with suspicious customers. Preventive
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 65
actions could involve targeted training and a refinement of onboarding and
transaction monitoring procedures.

cause analysis)

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 67
Key takeaways
• Ensure sufficient evidence has been collected and analyzed before
concluding an investigation.
• Concluding an investigation might require seeking legal advice. Financial
institutions should understand the legal consequences of disclosing
possible sanctions breaches and freezing or closing accounts.
• When a sanctions breach occurs, identify root causes and take mitigating
action.
Conclusion
When conducting a sanctions investigation, gather evidence from various
sources to assess red flags. The more thorough your investigation, and the
more evidence you collect, the stronger your findings will be. An in-depth
investigation might require you to expand your initial scope to cover
counterparties so you can find all connections to sanctioned parties. When
you conclude your investigation, consider the legal requirements for
disclosing any breaches and freezing accounts. If a breach occurred, conduct
a root cause analysis and take mitigating actions to prevent future sanctions
breaches.

Asset Identification, Asset
Freezing, and Regulatory
Reporting
Introduction
This case study illustrates important concepts from the courses. Explore the
cases to solve real-world problems. The related activities are not scored and
include feedback to help you learn. You will be able to apply and retain the key
concepts.
Identifying, freezing, and blocking assets
(Case example: Hyperion Bank)
Hyperion Bank, a UK financial institution, recently concluded a complex
sanctions review. They have discovered that one of their customers, Russian
businessman Alexey Rostov, has been designated in the latest round of Russia
sanctions.
Mr. Rostov holds several different accounts with Hyperion Bank. The bank’s
sanctions compliance team, with the help of relationship managers, needs to
identify which assets are owned by Mr. Rostov and freeze these assets as
quickly as possible to prevent the risk of asset flight. The team considers the
personal accounts Mr. Rostov holds and also determines whether he has any
business accounts. They also investigate whether he owns other assets, such
as artwork in the bank’s custody or securities, as they need to block access to
these economic resources, too.
The sanctions compliance team must ensure that no funds or economic
resources are made available to Mr. Rostov, either directly or indirectly.
To ensure this, the team needs to thoroughly review the records and
documents associated with Mr. Rostov. This includes financial statements,
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 69
bank records, and investment portfolios. Doing this will help the team locate all
the assets belonging to Mr. Rostov, though the process becomes more
difficult if the assets are located in different branches or business lines of the
bank.
Mr. Rostov holds a large investment portfolio including stocks, bonds, and
cryptoassets. The team tracks the bank’s records and Mr. Rostov’s brokerage
accounts to find these and place blocks on them.
The team must also identify the nonfinancial assets that Mr. Rostov owns, such
as artwork for which the bank might be serving as a custodian. To do this, it
reviews ownership documents, property records, company contracts, and
business registrations. Luxury art is often owned by a chain of shell companies,
which complicates the task of establishing ownership of and control over such
high-value assets.
In parallel with its interaction with the UK Office of Financial Sanctions
Implementation, the team collaborates with Mr. Rostov’s law firm and
insurance company to ensure that it has identified everything that needs to be
frozen in accordance with the law.
Identifying, freezing, and blocking assets (Activity: UK
sanctions)
39. Alexey Rostov is sanctioned by the UK government. It is discovered that he
is the majority owner of Gulf Gold, a publicly listed oil company. He also owns
38% of another company, Persepolis Inc. He does not have any involvement
in either company beyond owning his shares. Both companies have various
subsidiaries and assets. Mr. Rostov has a large investment portfolio that
includes stocks, derivatives, and bonds. He also owns a yacht, several luxury
automobiles, and real estate in the UK and Germany.
Hyperion Bank must block all of the assets that it holds for Mr. Rostov. Which
of the following should be frozen?
o Real estate in Germany
o Large investment portfolio
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 70
o Shares in Gulf Gold owned by third-party investors
o Assets held by Persepolis Inc.
Key takeaways
• When a sanctions designation has been made against your customer, you
must act quickly to identify relevant assets to prevent asset flight.
• In addition to reviewing documents and records pertaining to financial
assets, you should conduct reviews for any nonfinancial assets that the
bank might have an obligation to freeze.
Freezing assets and reporting frozen assets
(Case example: Peterhouse Holdings)
The sanctions compliance team at Hyperion Bank reviewed the assets of Mr.
Rostov, a customer who was sanctioned by the UK. The team discovered a
business account for a company by the name of Peterhouse Holdings. Mr.
Rostov has majority ownership of Peterhouse Holdings, which in turn holds
ownership stakes in several other companies.
Because Mr. Rostov is a sanctioned individual, the team needs to identify
which of the companies are majority-owned by Peterhouse Holdings, and
which companies Mr. Rostov might not majority-own but control via
Peterhouse Holdings. They use external databases and internal sources to
determine company ownership structures, and freeze accounts for several
companies. They then file a report with the UK Office of Financial Sanctions
Implementation (OFSI).
The sanctions compliance team begins investigating Mr. Rostov’s companies.
They use third-party databases and internal documentation to determine the
ultimate beneficial owners. The first step is to identify for which companies
Peterhouse Holdings is the majority shareholder.
As the bank is based in the UK, the second step is to consider whether Mr.
Rostov controls any of the companies in which he does not have majority
ownership, directly or indirectly. They must determine if Mr. Rostov has the
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 71
power to remove or appoint the board of directors or whether the
companies’ affairs are conducted according to his wishes. The team
examines the make-up of the board and any meeting minutes that are
available. When it is not clear whether Mr. Rostov controls the company, the
team takes a risk-based approach to make freezing decisions.
Once the team is confident that they have identified all companies that
Peterhouse Holdings owns or that Mr. Rostov otherwise controls, it freezes all
relevant funds and economic resources, ensuring that no activity is permitted
on the blocked accounts. The team then files a report to OFSI, informing it
which assets it holds and has frozen. The bank must file subsequent reports if
any changes are made to the frozen assets.
Freezing assets and reporting frozen assets (Activity:
Asset freezing steps)
40. Hyperion Bank is investigating Alexey Rostov, an individual sanctioned by
the UK. They must follow the proper procedure for identifying and freezing
his assets. Place the following steps in the most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
Identify any nonfinancial assets Mr. Rostov holds.
Block all accounts associated with Mr. Rostov or his
companies.
Establish which companies Mr. Rostov owns or controls.
Report the frozen assets to the Office of Financial
Sanctions Implementation (OFSI).
Review Mr. Rostov’s financial statements, bank records,
and investment portfolios.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 72
Key takeaways
When identifying assets to freeze you should:
• Investigate whether the sanctioned entity owns holding companies.
• Consider control in addition to ownership for the EU and the UK.
• Use a risk-based approach if you cannot determine control.
Managing frozen assets (Case example:
Bank branches)
Hyperion Bank’s sanctions compliance team has identified, to the best of its
ability, all UK-based assets owned or controlled by its sanctioned customer,
Alexey Rostov. It has also taken action to freeze these assets. Now, the team
must decide on the next steps to manage the frozen funds.
The bank has branches in the US and France, in addition to its primary place of
business in the UK. The bank notifies the US and French branches of its initial
findings. To the extent that UK sanctions apply to foreign branches as well, the
two branches also take steps to block Mr. Rostov’s accounts and report
information on frozen assets to the bank in the UK. The UK bank then files a
consolidated report with the Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation
(OFSI).
When the UK-based sanctions compliance team notifies the US and French
branches of its initial findings, they undertake their own investigations into Mr.
Rostov. As these investigations progress, Mr. Rostov becomes a designated
person in both the US and EU, in addition to the UK. In this context, each bank
branch must consider their local jurisdiction’s requirements in developing
plans for both reporting on and managing frozen assets.
In the UK, OFSI requires financial institutions to submit a report at the time of
freezing. OFSI also runs an annual asset freeze reporting activity that requires
all entities that hold frozen assets to report them, regardless of whether there
have been any changes to the assets.
In the US, frozen funds must be placed into an interest-bearing account from
which only debits authorized by the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC)
may be made. The funds must earn interest at a commercially reasonable
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 73
rate. Hyperion Bank decides to create segregated accounts for Mr. Rostov’s

Key takeaways
You must adhere to your jurisdiction’s requirements for managing frozen

• In the EU and UK, you must report frozen assets without delay.

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 75
Specific licenses or exceptions (Case
example: Rental property)
Hyperion Bank’s customer, Alexey Rostov, is sanctioned by the EU, US, and UK,
and his assets have been frozen. Because he is sanctioned, his holding
company, Peterhouse Holdings, is also subject to sanctions. Peterhouse
Holdings manages several rental properties, but the residents of these
properties are unable to pay their rent to the company, due to the asset
freeze. The company is also unable to make necessary payments, such as
building service charges, utility bills, and insurance.
Mr. Rostov decides to apply for a specific license from the UK’s Office of
Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI), which grants the license following
a careful review. Hyperion Bank establishes strict controls to ensure all
transactions involving the frozen accounts adhere to the license’s terms and
conditions.
Mr. Rostov applies for the specific license on the grounds of prior obligation for
the rental payments and on the grounds of routine maintenance for the rental
properties his holding company owns. He uses a law firm to make the
application in his behalf. The application submitted to OFSI identifies all parties
involved in the proposed transactions, the details of the bank accounts to be
used, and the amount of the proposed transactions.
Four weeks after his application was submitted, OFSI approves his request. Mr.
Rostov receives a specific license allowing the rental payments and routine
maintenance payments for Peterhouse Holding’s rental properties.
Hyperion Bank carefully checks the terms and conditions of the license to
understand exactly what payments are allowed. It shares details of the license
with correspondent banks to enable the payments to be made. It sets up an
enhanced due diligence (EDD) process, including manual screening, to ensure
that only payments allowed by the terms of the licenses are entering or
leaving Peterhouse Holding’s frozen accounts. It also conducts KYC checks on
all parties making or receiving payments under the license.
As the license involves regular payments, rather than just a single payment,
Hyperion Bank is required to provide quarterly reports to OFSI on the use of
the license.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02

Key takeaways
• Specific licenses allow sanctioned entities to engage in activities that
would otherwise be prohibited, but they often have complex terms and
conditions applied.
• Financial institutions must establish strict controls to ensure payments
adhere to specific licenses’ terms and conditions.

General licenses or exemptions (Case
example: Insurance, utilities, and legal fees)
Alexey Rostov, Hyperion Bank’s recently sanctioned customer, has applied for
a specific license from the UK Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation
(OFSI), requesting the release of blocked funds. His legal team has realized
that some of the activities outlined in the specific license application are
already covered by general licenses in the UK. Mr. Rostov could use these
licenses immediately to access his funds without going through the
application process.
His lawyers write to Hyperion Bank asking it to release funds under the terms
of general licenses for building insurance, utilities, and legal fees. The bank
must consider the terms and conditions of each general license and ensure
they comply with them.
The general license for insurance payments allows sanctioned entities to
make payments for building and engineering insurance to a UK insurer for
properties based in the UK. Mr. Rostov’s holding company, Peterhouse
Holdings, is also subject to sanctions. For this license to apply, the bank must
confirm whether the holding company, which manages Mr. Rostov’s rental
properties, has UK-based insurers. There are no reporting requirements for
the bank under this license and no limits to the payments. However, the
sanctioned entity must report to OFSI within 10 days of using the license.
The general license for utilities allows UK-sanctioned entities to make
payments for gas and electricity for UK-owned properties to UK-registered
energy companies. There is a reporting obligation for both the UK-sanctioned
entity and the energy company for this license. However, there are no
payment limits or expiration dates under it. Despite there being no payment
limits, Hyperion Bank decides to use enhanced ongoing monitoring to ensure
that all payments meet the terms of this license.
The general license for legal fees allows payments of up to £500,000 and has
an expiration date. Hyperion Bank notes the limit on the payment and the
expiration date and puts manual transaction monitoring in place to ensure it
complies with the terms.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 78

Specially Designated National (SDN) List under the Russia sanctions regime,
and many of his companies are subject to sanctions. The US businessman

using a general license. Place the following steps in the most logical

General and specific licenses (Activity: Shard
Enterprises)
45. Alexey Rostov’s company, Peterhouse Holdings, has a minority 5%
ownership stake in Shard Enterprises, a large, diversified business which
happens to be a client of Hyperion Bank. Recently two of Shard Enterprises’
directors were sanctioned by the EU and the UK under the Libya sanctions
regime. The two directors also have joint majority ownership of the
company.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 79
The bank is considering which of Shard Enterprises’ activities can be
licensed. Select which activities can be undertaken with a general license
and which need a specific license.
Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares.
General Specific
Paying fees to the UK’s register of companies ☐ ☐
Paying for flood damage to its warehouse ☐ ☐
Paying the damages in a court case ☐ ☐
Paying property tax ☐ ☐
Paying building insurance ☐ ☐
Key takeaways
• General licenses can be used instead of specific licenses, under certain
conditions, to allow sanctioned entities access to frozen funds.
• You must understand the full terms and conditions of a general license
before allowing payments to and from frozen accounts.
• When using a general license, pay particular attention to reporting
requirements, payment limits, and expiration dates.
Conclusion
After your customer has been made subject to sanctions, you will need to
identify and locate all economic resources that are owned by the sanctioned
person, to prevent asset flight. This includes assets across all divisions of your
financial institution. When freezing the assets, follow a risk-based approach
and adhere to your jurisdiction’s requirements. If a license applies, ensure that
you understand its terms and conditions, and that your organization
implements and follows proper controls for managing frozen funds and
processing payments from frozen accounts.

Iris Bank. Match the correspondent banking services offered through Iris

C Jurisdiction

bank in Lebanon with alleged

bank in Cyprus that has had Russian

D. Services offered to FinTech

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 81
Check-clearing correspondent banking services: These pose a type of product
sanctions risk. Checks are not automatically screened against sanctions lists,
so the bank could accidentally clear checks in violation of sanctions.
Services offered to a respondent bank in Lebanon with alleged Hezbollah ties:
These pose a type of customer sanctions risk. Hezbollah has been designated
as a terrorist organization in several dozen jurisdictions, including the US, UK,
and many European countries. It also has close historical ties to Iran.
Services offered to a respondent bank in Cyprus that has had Russian
customers: These pose a type of jurisdiction sanctions risk. Cyprus is a high-
risk jurisdiction from a sanctions perspective due to its historical connections
with Russia. The fact that the respondent bank has a history of serving Russian
customers is especially concerning.
Services offered to FinTech companies that onboard customers digitally
through online platforms: These pose a type of delivery channel risk. Because
customers are onboarded online, there is a risk of accepting customers who
are residents of, or who operate from, sanctioned jurisdictions.
Correspondent banking sanctions risk (Activity: SRA
Update)
2. Which factor might be relevant for updating Orchid FinCorp’s
correspondent banking sanctions risk assessment after the acquisition of
Iris Bank?
o Iris Bank has a 9% minority shareholder whose majority owner is
designated by OFAC. This shareholding remains frozen and the rest of
the shareholders are well-known Greek businessmen.
o Iris Bank acquired a small retail bank in Greece one year ago.
o Iris Bank has respondent banking customers that previously served
high -risk jurisdictions.
o Iris Bank had one minor sanctions breach, which didn’t result in a
penalty, in the past seven years.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 82
Iris Bank had one minor sanctions breach, which didn’t result in a penalty, in the
past seven years: This is not the best response. This indicates that Iris Bank
more likely than not has strong internal controls and has managed to properly
monitor customer activity, thus avoiding material sanction breaches.
Iris Bank acquired a small retail bank in Greece one year ago: This is not the best
response. This indicates that the customers are local to Greece, a jurisdiction
with low sanctions risk.
Iris Bank has a 9% minority shareholder whose majority owner is designated by
OFAC: This is not the best response. Because this is a minority shareholder and
the rest of the shareholders are all legitimate businessmen and because this
shareholding is frozen, the designated shareholder exercises no control over
the bank’s decisions.
Iris Bank has respondent banking customers that previously served high-risk
jurisdictions: This is the best response. This is especially true if these high-risk
jurisdictions include countries subject to sanctions. A possible risk of this
scenario is that a government official or businessperson who is subject to
sanctions might operate in disguise behind complex structures.

Wealth management and private banking
sanctions risk (Case example: Compliance
crisis)
Wealth management and private banking sanctions
risk (Activity: Identifying risks)
3. The following is a list of steps that should be taken to identify red flags
related to specific wealth management and private banking sanctions
risks. Place the following steps in the most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
1. Obtain documentation for the ownership structure.
4. Conduct negative news searches on beneficial owners,
directors, and signatories.
3. Screen beneficial owners, directors, and signatories against
sanctions lists.
5. Monitor transactions to ensure that the nature of the
business and the counterparties are aligned with the
customer’s profile.
2. Determine the identity of each beneficial owner.
To identify red flags related to specific to wealth management and private
banking sanctions risks, you should:
1. Obtain documentation for the ownership structure. Confirm that all layers of
ownership leading up to each beneficial owner are available for analysis.
Red flags include registration in tax havens and other high-risk jurisdictions,
the use of opaque legal arrangements such as trusts and foundations, and
so on.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 84
2. Determine the identity of each beneficial owner. If the structure cannot be
supported by corporate documents, this is a red flag.
3. Screen beneficial owners, directors, and signatories against sanctions lists.
4. Conduct negative news searches on the beneficial owners, directors, and
signatories. Any suggestion of a sanctions connection, whether through
activities, jurisdiction, or counterparties, should be a red flag.
5. Monitor transactions to ensure that the nature of the business and the
counterparties are aligned with the customer’s profile. Any discrepancies
should be considered a red flag.
Wealth management and private banking sanctions
risk (Activity: Assessing risk profile)
4. Which statement is true about the impact of the recent wealth
management boutique acquisition on Orchid FinCorp’s risk profile?
o Customers: The boutique’s legal entity customers with multi -layered
structures and past sanctions evasion allegations increase sanctions
risk.
o Channels: The boutique markets its products through well-
established, third-party investment advisors, which lowers its
sanctions exposure.
o Jurisdiction: The boutique is in a low-risk jurisdiction, which means
there is no material sanctions risk.
o Products: The boutique’s complex investment products with income
that is difficult to predict increases sanctions risk.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 85
Products: The boutique’s complex investment products with income that is
difficult to predict increases sanctions risk: This statement is not true. The
complexity of investment products does not affect sanctions risk. It does,
however, create other types of risks for the organization, such as high-income
volatility.
Jurisdiction: The boutique is in a low-risk jurisdiction, which means there is no
material sanctions risk: This statement is not true. Jurisdictional risk is not
limited to the country of registration. The organization’s overseas operations
and the locations of its clients should also be considered when assessing
sanctions risk.
Channels: The boutique markets its products through well-established, third-
party investment advisors, which lowers its sanctions exposure: This statement
is not true. Although the advisors might be well-established within the
markets, the use of third-party distributors and agents in foreign markets
actually increases, rather than lowers, sanctions risk.
Customers: The boutique’s legal entity customers with multi-layered structures
and past sanctions evasion allegations increase sanctions risk: This statement is
true. Whenever legal entities have complex, multi-layered ownership
structures, this should be a red flag. There is a risk that the entities may be
used to obscure true beneficial owners. This, along with the allegations of
sanctions evasion, increases sanctions risk.

Insurance sanctions risk (Activity: Product risk)
5. Orchid FinCorp discovers that the product portfolio of its newly acquired
insurance subsidiary, Dahlia Insurance, includes investment-based
insurance “wrappers.” This means that multiple bankable assets, such as
stocks and hedge funds, are blended into one product.
When revising its sanctions risk assessment (SRA), what type of sanctions
prohibitions should Orchid FinCorp consider in order to analyze the risks
stemming specifically from its wrapper offerings, as opposed to its life
insurance policies?
o Restrictions on the facilitation of fundraising
o Prohibitions on the sale of insurance products in sanctioned
jurisdictions
o Asset-freeze designations
o Restrictions on investments in certain overseas legal entities
Prohibitions on the sale of insurance products in sanctioned jurisdictions: This is
not the best response. These prohibitions are equally relevant for Orchid
FinCorp’s offerings of investment-based and life cover-only insurance
products.
Asset-freeze designations: This is not the best response. The risk of providing
services to customers subject to asset freeze designations has equal
importance for both categories. Under no circumstance can Orchid FinCorp
sell its insurance products of whatever nature to individuals under blocking
sanctions by jurisdictions whose regulations the organization must comply
with.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 87
Restrictions on the facilitation of fundraising: This is not the best response.
Fundraising is not applicable to the organization’s insurance division.
Restrictions on investments in certain overseas legal entities: This is the best
response. In the course of its SRA, Orchid FinCorp must determine whether it
is at risk of violating any applicable restrictions on investments in specific
overseas legal entities, for example those imposed by the United States on
companies incorporated in jurisdictions identified as natural security threats.

select the appropriate category of risk.

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 89
Where are the customers and suppliers located?: This addresses jurisdictional
risk. Determining the origin of goods helps identify any applicable export
prohibitions. Establishing the destination will determine if any sanctioned
countries for which import prohibitions apply are involved.
Where will funds be sent and received from?: This addresses jurisdictional risk.
This question will determine whether the customer is involved with any high-
risk jurisdictions and help decide whether it makes sense to engage, on the
customer’s behalf, with banks in those locations.
Which currencies will be used for cross-border wire transfers?: This addresses
product risk. This question concerns the customer’s use of the bank’s wire
transfer services in specific currencies. Certain currencies, such as US dollars,
entail higher regulatory risk due to their widespread use in international trade
and aggressive regulatory enforcement for sanctions violations.
What types of goods will be traded?: This addresses customer risk. This will
establish the industry in which the customer operates, and any relevant
prohibitions.
What is the company’s ownership structure?: This addresses customer risk.
Dealing with a legal entity that has a complex ownership structure poses
heightened sanctions risks.

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 91
Providing correspondent banking services to banks in Cyprus that have Russian
clientele: This risk can be addressed by enhancing the effectiveness of the
screening tool by lowering the filtering threshold. Transactions routed through
Cyprus are particularly susceptible to Russian connections, and lowering the
threshold will increase the chances of identifying counterparties subject to
Russia sanctions.
Providing wealth management services to multi-layered entities: This risk can
be addressed by enhancing customer due diligence policies and procedures.
This measure will help identify connections to sanctioned persons within a
complex corporate setup.
Selling insurance products through third-party distributors: This risk can be
addressed by expanding screening to cover vendors and outsourcing agents.
This broader approach aids in identifying any connections that third parties
might have to sanctioned parties.
Providing wire transfer services to companies selling dual-use goods: This risk
can be addressed by enhancing the effectiveness of the screening tool by
integrating US Department of Commerce-controlled customs codes and
goods descriptions. Any alerts generated can then be further investigated to
ensure compliance with licensing requirements and to prevent any violations
of trade controls.

General licenses or exemptions (Case
example: Water Relief)
General licenses or exemptions (Activity: A French
charity)
8. A French nongovernmental organization (NGO) specializes in
reconstructing villages that have been damaged by war. The NGO has
launched a new project in Syria and approaches its US bank asking it to
fund the project through a Syrian state-owned bank. The US bank would
like to help its client. What should it do?
o File a suspicious activity report (SAR).
o Refuse to undertake the project because it cannot send funds into
Syria.
o Check whether any exemptions and general licenses could apply to
the project.
o Tell the NGO to apply to OFAC and the French regulator for the
appropriate licenses or exemptions.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 93
Tell the NGO to apply to OFAC and the French regulator for the appropriate
licenses or exemptions: This is not the best response. An applicable EU
exemption and an OFAC license already exist, so there is no need to apply.
Refuse to undertake the project because the bank cannot send funds to Syria:
This is not the best response. Although it is usually not possible to make funds
available to a state-owned bank in Syria, there are general licenses and
exemptions for humanitarian purposes that would apply in this scenario.
However, the bank could still choose to take a risk-based approach and
decide not to undertake this activity.
File a suspicious activity report (SAR): This is not the best response. Unless the
bank discovers red flags for financial crime, there is no need to file a SAR. The
NGO’s request appears to be legitimate.
Check whether any exemptions and general licenses could apply to the project:
This is the best response. This will help determine the legitimacy of the
request and the best way to proceed, while ensuring compliance with
regulatory requirements.
Specific licenses or exceptions (Case
example: H&H law firm)
Specific licenses or exceptions (Activity: Felicity Bank)
9. Felicity Bank, which is located in the UK, has a customer who is a Russian
oligarch sanctioned by the UK. The customer has a specific license from
the Office of Financial Sanctions Implementation (OFSI). The license allows
transactions to cover the basic needs of the individual and his dependents.
These include the payment of insurance premiums, taxes, and mortgage
payments. Felicity Bank must determine its next steps. Place the following
steps in the most logical sequential order.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 94
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
4. Establish enhanced transaction monitoring.
6. Report to the regulator.
2. Check the reporting conditions of the license.
1. Understand the scope of the activity covered.
3. Check that the administrative details are correct.
5. Use manual screening for payments.
The next steps that Felicity Bank should take are:
1. Understand the scope of the activity covered. Many specific licenses allow
unique activities and have specific payment caps. It is essential for a bank
to understand what is permissible under each specific license.
2. Check the reporting conditions of the license. Each license has different
reporting conditions. If a bank fails to adhere to these conditions, they
violate the regulations and could receive a monetary penalty.
3. Check that the administrative details are correct. If the name, address, or
other administrative details do not match, the license could be invalid.
4. Establish enhanced transaction monitoring. This license will have several
different transactions attached to it, so it is essential to check that each
transaction is permitted.
5. Use manual screening for payments. This is part of the enhanced
monitoring. Manual screening increases the likelihood that any issues will
be uncovered. Many licenses for basic needs cap payments at a certain
amount. Manual screening will ensure that these conditions are met.
6. Report to the regulator. Most specific licenses require regular reports to the
regulator. These are often monthly or quarterly.

UK General License for Syria Humanitarian Activity: This is applicable. Because
Hope Relief is based in the UK, it will need UK permission to send funds to Syria.
UK General Trade License: Earthquake Relief Efforts in Syria and Turkey: This is
