Delisting impact on KYC and ongoing
monitoring (Case example: Xavia Trading)
Delisting impact on KYC and ongoing monitoring
(Activity: Denis Kormonov)
11. Denis Kormonov is a member of the Belarus government who was
previously on the OFAC Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list. He
contacts his US bank to tell them that he is being delisted. He asks that the
bank unfreeze his accounts and property and allow funds to be transferred
to his bank account in Germany. Which action should the US bank take first?
o Transfer funds to the German bank account.
o Apply for a license from OFAC to unfreeze the account.
o Check with the regulator to confirm that the customer has been
delisted.
o Refresh the customer’s KYC and assess his risk rating.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 97
Apply for a license from OFAC to unfreeze the account: This is not the best
response. It is generally the responsibility of the client to apply for a license
and then to show the license to the bank to confirm the activity is permissible.
In this case, Denis Kormonov must apply for a license from OFAC to unblock
his accounts.
Transfer the funds to the German bank account: This is not the best response.
Until the US bank has seen a license from OFAC, it cannot make any changes
to the frozen accounts and would be prohibited from making a transfer. Even
after the bank receives the license and unfreezes the account, it must still
check whether Kormonov is subject to EU sanctions. It is possible that he
might be delisted by OFAC but listed by the EU.
Refresh the customer's KYC and assess his risk rating: This is not the best
response. If the bank has confirmation that a customer has been delisted, it
should perform a KYC refresh. However, it should not do this until it has
received the license from the customer.
Check with the regulator to confirm that the customer has been delisted: This is
the best response. Kormonov told the bank that he is being delisted, but that
could mean that he has only applied to be delisted. Until his name is removed
from the SDN list, the bank should not take any further action.
Licensing challenges (Case example:
AstroNav Solutions)
Licensing challenges (Activity: Dynamic Systems)
12. Dynamic Systems is a US-based company that manufactures ball bearings.
These items are commonly used in food processors, other household
goods, and bicycles. However, ball bearings can also be used in missiles
and projectiles. Dynamic Systems asks its bank to finance its exports to
Somalia. Which steps should the bank take next? Place the following steps
in the most logical sequential order.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 98
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
5. Manually monitor all transactions.
1. Screen Dynamic Systems and other companies in the
payment chain.
2. Check the HS code for the goods.
3. Check whether multiple countries are involved.
4. Check whether an export license is in place.
The next steps that the bank should take are:
1. Screen Dynamic Systems and other companies in the payment chain. If any
parties in the payment chain are subject to an asset freeze, the trade will
likely not be allowed to progress.
2. Check the HS code for the goods. The HS code will give exact information
on the type of product. You should check for the HS code within the
sanctions regulations to determine whether the trade needs a license or is
not permitted.
3. Check whether multiple countries are involved. Trade often crosses
borders, so you need to consider the relevant sanctions legislation for each
country that is involved.
4. Check whether an export license is in place. If you discover that the trade
needs a license, the license needs to be in place before the exports can
begin. Check the terms and conditions of the license to understand
exactly what is permitted.
5. Manually monitor all transactions. If transactions are going to a high-risk
environment, such as a sanctioned country, it is sensible to use enhanced
transaction monitoring. Most licenses contain terms and conditions,
including keeping records of the transactions undertaken with the license.
The bank might have to report the use of the license to the regulator.

prohibited under sanctions regulations. There are different types of

which type of license or exemption applies. Only one type of license or
exemption applies to each scenario.

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 100
A UK company exporting computers to Sudan: This would require a dual-use
goods export license. Computers are on the list of potential dual-use items
and might require a dual-use license, depending on the country to which they
are being exported. This would apply to the US, UK, and the EU, as well as other
countries.
A French NGO rebuilding villages in Iraq: This would require an exemption. The
EU uses exemptions rather than general licenses for delivering humanitarian
assistance to sanctioned countries.
A US citizen visiting family in Cuba: This would require a general license. US
citizens are allowed to travel to Cuba if they fall within 12 categories of the
Cuba general license. These include visiting family, educational activities, and
athletic competitions.
A US citizen, who is a delisted SDN, who asks that their account be unfrozen:
This would require a specific license. When someone is delisted by OFAC, they
need to apply for a specific license to allow their bank to unblock their frozen
accounts and property.
A German lawyer representing a sanctioned person: This would require a
specific license. Under European and UK regulations, you can apply for a
specific license for the payment of legal fees. These allow lawyers to
represent sanctioned people and entities.
A UK commercial flight to Moscow: This would require a general license. Such a
license would allow vessels and aircraft to travel to Russia from the UK for
certain permitted activity. A company needs to register with the UK
government to use this license, as well as report activity under it.

Fuzzy logic, AST, and reading sanctions lists

Fuzzy logic (Activity: OFAC designation)
14. Garnet Bank discovers that one of its customers was recently designated
by OFAC. Garnet Bank’s automated screening tool (AST) did not catch the
connection during batch screening due to differing information in the
bank’s records and the OFAC Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) listing.
The AST was able to match “DPRK” to “Korea,” meaning North Korea, as
well as the two dates of birth written in different styles. However, it failed to
match the customer’s name to that of the SDN.
Garnet Bank's records: OFAC listing:
Name: Chang, Mionk Hol Name: Jang, Myong Khol
DOB: 12/03/1960 DOB: 03/12/1960
Nationality: DPRK Nationality: Korea
What should Garnet Bank do to enhance the fuzzy logic of its AST based on
the screening failure?
o Introduce a phonetic algorithm.
o Reduce the fuzzy logic threshold to 80% to capture more possible
matches.
o Introduce screening in the Korean script.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 102
Introduce a phonetic algorithm: This is not the best response. While this would
enhance the fuzzy logic, it is not a priority. Because the customer’s name
originates from a non-Latin script, a phonetic algorithm cannot be
implemented before screening in the original script.
Reduce the fuzzy logic threshold to 80% to capture more possible matches: This
is not the best response. This threshold of 80% is too low and would
significantly increase the number of false positives. The exact percentage
should be set based on several rounds of testing.
Introduce screening in the Korean script: This is the best response. The bank
needs to introduce screening in a number of non-Latin scripts corresponding
to the key geographies of its customers. After screening in the appropriate
non-Latin scripts, the bank should then introduce a phonetic algorithm to
enhance the fuzzy logic.
Automated screening tool (Activity: False negatives)
15. Moonstone Bank is performing an annual testing of its automated
screening tool (AST). The bank has identified four false negatives, or true
matches that were missed. Match each of the false negatives on the left
with the appropriate AST enhancements on the right.
Match each option on the left to its corresponding option on the right to
complete the matching assignment.
A. Mary Anne Alison Johnson not
recognized as Mary Ann Johnson
C Phonetic algorithm
B. MacDonald not recognized as
McDonald
D Applying non-Latin
screening
C. Katherine Mabal not recognized as
Catherine Mampal
B Lowering the fuzzy
matching threshold
D. Vhkh Xaralambous not recognized
as Viki Charalambous
A Applying less weight to
first names
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 103
Mary Anne Alison Johnson not recognized as Mary Ann Johnson: The best
enhancement for this scenario is applying less weight to first names. In this
example, the AST did not recognize the match when one of the individual’s
first names was removed. The best way to ensure that these manipulations
are identified is to assign less weight to first names. When calculating the
overall percentage match, the weight attributed to last names during the
scoring should be higher.
MacDonald not recognized as McDonald: The best enhancement for this
scenario is lowering the fuzzy matching threshold. In this situation, MacDonald
was changed to McDonald by removing a single letter. The system did not
capture this similarity, most likely because the threshold was set too high.
Katherine Mabal not recognized as Catherine Mampal: The best enhancement
for this scenario is a phonetic algorithm. The names Katherine Mabal and
Catherine Mampal sound almost the same but are spelled differently. To
capture these discrepancies, the phonetic algorithm should be incorporated
into the fuzzy logic.
Vhkh Xaralambous not recognized as Viki Charalambous: The best
enhancement for Vhkh Xaralambous is to apply non-Latin screening. This is
especially important if a large percentage of the bank’s customers are located
in a region where a non-Latin script is used. In this case, the bank would need
to screen the name in Greek.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 104
Automated screening tool (Activity: False positive)
16. Onyx Bank recently flagged a customer account as a match against
OFAC’s Specially Designated Nationals (SDN) list. After further investigation,
the bank determined that it was a false positive. The customer had a similar
name to that of a sanctioned individual. Which likely caused the false
positive alert?
o The customer was included on the screening system's inequalities or
"good guys" list.
o Fuzzy logic did not incorporate a phonetic algorithm.
o The matching criteria were too sensitive.
o The threshold was set too high.
The threshold was set too high: This is not the best response. High thresholds
reduce the risk of false positives, while increasing the risk of false negatives.
Fuzzy logic did not incorporate a phonetic algorithm: This is not the best
response. This would have prevented the screening system from recognizing
names that sound the same but are spelled differently, and an alert would not
have been created at all.
The customer was included on the screening system’s inequalities or "good
guys" list: This is not the best response. This would have helped limit the
number of parties that might be identified as possible matches to names on
sanctions lists. If the specific customer was included on an inequalities list, an
alert would not have been created.
The matching criteria were too sensitive: This is the best response. The bank
should enhance its screening tool by incorporating additional data such as
date of birth, nationality, and address. These additional details could help
improve the accuracy of screening.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 105
Reading sanctions lists (Activity: Screening alert)
17. During the daily batch screening, Topaz Bank’s automated screening tool
generated an alert against a customer named Feliksovich Vekselberg. The
alert matched the following OFAC listing:
Name: Viktor Feliksovich Vekselberg
a.k.a. Feliks Vekselberg
Date of birth: 03/13/73
Place of birth: Crimea, Ukraine
Citizenships: Russian, Israeli
Country location: Ukraine
Other information: Chairman of Rem Group
PEP: Major of Sviyazhsk, Russia (2016-2020)
Match the documents in the customer’s file on the left with the appropriate
OFAC listing fields to check against on the right.
Match each option on the left to its corresponding option on the right to
complete the matching assignment.
A. Passport(s) B Country, address
B. Utility bill C Remarks
C. Customer’s curriculum vitae (CV) A Citizenship, nationality, date
of birth, place of birth, aliases
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 106
Passport(s): These should be checked against the “citizenship,” “nationality,”
“date of birth,” “place of birth,” and "aliases" fields in the OFAC listing. This will
help establish if the customer has the same citizenship, date of birth, and
place of birth as the designated individual. Additionally, if the customer has
more than one passport, the name could be written differently on each
passport and could help identify possible matches against the alias identifier,
commonly referred to as “a.k.a.”
Utility bill: This should be checked against the “country” and “address” fields in
the OFAC listing. The address on the utility bill requested from and provided
by the customer should ideally indicate the customer’s current place of
residence.
Customer’s curriculum vitae (CV): This should be checked against the
“remarks” field. This is because the CV, also known as a resume, can contain
pertinent information, such as businesses owned, board positions held, and
job history.

SWIFT message screening (Activity: Casemar
Electronics)
18. Diamond Bank’s automated screening tool (AST) failed to identify a
possible sanctions match for a SWIFT transfer requested by its customer,
Casemar Electronics. The SWIFT message included the following
information:
Beneficiary: Fidias Komalenko
Address: 14 Square Street, Dubai
Particulars: Salary for services provided in Kersch
Beneficiary Bank: National Bank of Mima
Amount: US$500
The AST failed to determine that the word “Kersch” referred to Kerch, a
city in Crimea which is subject to comprehensive US sanctions. What
should Diamond Bank do to prevent this error in the future?
o Incorporate a word order swapping algorithm into the AST.
o Program the algorithm to ignore common words such as “salary, ”
“for, ” and “in. ”
o Raise the AST’s threshold.
o Map the bank’s custom list of cities in Crimea to the “address” field.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 108
Raise the AST's threshold: This is not the best response. Higher thresholds
increase the likelihood of false negatives. Diamond bank should instead
decrease the likelihood of false negatives, and one way to do this is by
lowering the threshold.
Incorporate a word order swapping algorithm into the AST: This is not the best
response. This enhancement would not help in this case because the city of
Kerch is a single-word name.
Map the bank’s custom list of cities in Crimea to the “address” field: This is not
the best response. In this case, reference to Kerch is in the “particulars” field.
Program the algorithm to ignore common words such as “salary,” “for,” and “in”:
This is the best response. Diamond Bank’s AST likely uses an algorithm to
measure linguistic differences. If the threshold was set too high, the incorrect
spelling of “Kersch” was probably not recognized as “Kerch” because it
appears in a long string of text.

Originator and Beneficiary: Both of these fields should be screened against
designated entities and individuals. Vessels, ports, banks, and countries cannot
act as originators or beneficiaries.

be screened against sanctioned countries.

screened against designated banks—BICs. Additionally, the AST could be
programmed to stop transactions with banks in sanctioned countries by using
BIC codes to indicate the country in which the bank is located.

designated vessels, designated ports, and sanctioned countries. This is

of the payment. It can include information about a designated entity,
individual, vessel, port, or a sanctioned country. Additionally, extra keywords
can be incorporated, which could trigger red flags, such as prohibited goods.
By correctly mapping sanctions lists against SWIFT message fields, the AST
avoids generating unnecessary, false alerts, such as screening the originator’s
or the beneficiary’s name against the name of a vessel.

SWIFT message screening (Activity: ISO 20022)
21. Emerald Bank is re-calibrating its screening system in accordance with the
ISO 20022 standard. The fuzzy logic algorithm for field 59, which includes
the beneficiary name and address, uses the Levenshtein algorithm and has
the threshold set to 91%. This field is mapped against entity and country
lists. The new message fields include the following:
• Creditor Name
• Postal Address:
o Street Name
o Town Name
o Country
How should fuzzy logic be adjusted to the new SWIFT message fields listed
above?
o Remove the algorithm and entity list mapping from the “Country" field.
o Remove the algorithm and country list mapping from all fields under
“Postal Address.”
o Keep the algorithm set for all fields and keep the current mapping.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 113
Keep the algorithm set for all fields and keep the current mapping: This is not
the best response. Under the ISO 15022 standard, it was mandatory to use
fuzzy logic for both the name and address of the beneficiary customer. This
was because all information was included in one field. The successor ISO
20022 format breaks down this information into several discrete fields.
Keeping the current algorithm and mapping for the “Country” field would
cause an increase in false positives.
Remove the algorithm and country list mapping from all fields under “Postal
Address”: This is not the best response. While the fuzzy logic and mapping are
not needed for the “Country” field, they are necessary for the “Street Name”
and “Town Name” fields in order to handle possible variations and data
inconsistencies. Without these mechanisms in place, there is a higher risk of
false negatives.
Remove the algorithm and entity list mapping from the "Country" field: This is
the best response. Under the ISO 20022 standard, the “Country” field accepts
only specific country codes. Thus, the fuzzy logic algorithm for this field will no
longer be relevant because the algorithm’s purpose is to track potential
matches as opposed to complete matches. The threshold works in parallel
with the fuzzy logic and no longer applies once the algorithm is removed.
Because the “Country” field is now a standalone field, it also makes sense to
remove the mapping of listed entities against it.

Version 2.02

22. Ruby Bank is performing due diligence on a trade finance transaction for

relevant information, Ruby Bank determines that Hawiwi is included on the
US Department of Commerce’s Bureau of Industry and Security (BIS)

steps in the most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
1. Check the exact content of Hawiwi’s BIS listing.
4. Check if a BIS license is available from Hawiwi.
3. Check if any license exceptions apply.
5. Proceed with or reject the transaction.
2. Check whether the goods supplied by Chipmaster are of US
origin.
The next steps that Ruby Bank should take are:
1. Check the exact content of the Hawiwi’s BIS listing. The bank must
determine which restrictions apply, such as restrictions on all items subject
to the US Export Administration Regulations.
2. Check whether the goods supplied by Chipmaster are of US origin. It should
determine any additional restrictions that may apply, for example, if the
goods are destined for a specific country or have a specific restricted end-
use or end-user.

The OFAC 50% rule (Activity: H LLC)
23. A sanctions compliance officer of a US financial institution is attempting to
determine whether H LLC is subject to OFAC sanctions.
Person X has been designated by OFAC under the Russia sanctions
regime.
regime.
Person Y has been designated by OFAC under the Venezuela sanctions
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 117
Calculate the relevant ownership stakes for H LLC. Based on your
calculations, which is correct?
o H LLC is not subject to an asset freeze because C Ltd., G Ltd., C SA,
and G SA each hold less than a 50% ownership stake.
o H LLC is subject to an asset freeze because C Ltd. and G SA are
owned by two SDNs and their aggregate stake is equal to 50%.
o H LLC is subject to an asset freeze because G Ltd. and C SA are
owned by two SDNs and their aggregate stake is equal to 50%.
The holdings of the four shareholders of H LLC should be analyzed as follows:
• A Ltd. is subject to sanctions as being 50% or more owned by an SDN.
However, as A Ltd. holds only 45% ownership in B Ltd., the cascading stops
there and C Ltd.’s holding in H LLC should not be considered.
• D Ltd. is also subject to sanctions as being 50% or more owned by an SDN.
Given that both F Ltd. and G Ltd. are similarly subject to sanctions, G Ltd.’s
25% holding in H LLC should be considered in a final analysis.
• The 25% holding in H LLC by C SA should be considered, as Person Y’s
holdings from A SA cascaded down to C SA are equal to or greater than
50%.
• The 20% holding in H LLC by G SA should not be considered, as Person Y’s
holding in D SA is only 25%, so no further cascading can be made.
• The two SDNs, Person X and Person Y, hold an aggregate stake in H LLC of
50%. The aggregate rule applies to SDNs, regardless of the regimes under
which they are designated, so the two stakes must be added together.
Therefore, H LLC is subject to an asset freeze because G Ltd. and C SA are
owned by two SDNs and their aggregate stake is equal to 50%. If the US
financial institution maintains bank accounts for H LLC, these should be
immediately frozen.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02

The OFAC 50% rule (Activity: Complex structure)
24. The sanctions compliance officer of a US financial institution is analyzing a
complex ownership structure. Entity A is 100% owned by Person X, who is
on OFAC’s SDN list under the Syria sanctions regime. Entity B is 100%
owned by Person Y, who is on the SDN list under the Russia sanctions
regime. The compliance officer needs to make a series of calculations to
determine if entities F, D, C, or E are subject to an asset freeze.
Please match each entity to the description.
Match each option on the left to its corresponding option on the right to
complete the matching assignment.
A. Entity C D Subject to an asset freeze (50%)
B. Entity D C Subject to an asset freeze (60%)
C. Entity E A Not subject to an asset freeze
D. Entity F B Subject to an asset freeze (100%)
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 119
Entity C: Entity C is not subject to an asset freeze. Although Entity B is subject
to an asset freeze because it is 100% owned by Person Y, Entity C is only 40%
owned by Entity B.
Entity D: Entity D is subject to an asset freeze. Entity D is 20% owned by Entity
B, which is subject to an asset freeze, and 80% owned by Entity A, which is
similarly subject to an asset freeze. The aggregate rule applies even if the
holdings come from SDNs designated under different sanctions regimes.
Therefore, Entity D is subject to an asset freeze as being 50% or more owned
in the aggregate by SDNs
Entity E: Entity D owns 60% of Entity E, so Entity E is subject to an asset freeze.
This is because Entity D is itself a sanctioned entity, as it is 50% or more owned
by Entity A, a sanctioned person.
Entity F: Entity F is subject to an asset freeze due to 50% or higher ownership,
in the aggregate, by SDNs:
• Entity E is subject to an asset freeze and holds 25%.
• Entity B is subject to an asset freeze and holds 25%.
• Entity C, which holds the remaining 50%, is not subject to an asset freeze.

EU and UK 50% rules (Activity: EU financial institution)
25. A financial institution based in the EU maintains accounts for the following
clients:
Customer 1: Company listed on the London Stock Exchange
• 51% owned by a legal entity sanctioned by the EU.
• 49% owned by the wider public

Customer 1: The accounts should be frozen. Even if the company is listed on a
reputable stock exchange, it is more than 50% owned by a sanctioned legal
entity. Due to the sanctions, it is highly likely that trading in the shares of the
company on the London Stock Exchange has been suspended.
Customer 2: The accounts should be frozen. Even though the beneficiary of
the trust is not sanctioned, she is directly related to the sanctioned trust
settlor. It is highly likely that the sanctioned settlor of the trust exercises
control over the administration of the trust’s assets.

Version 2.02
Page 121

shareholder is not sanctioned, they are a nominee shareholder, meaning they
have no beneficial ownership of the company. The law firm is likely holding
shares for the sanctioned beneficial owner as a sanctions evasion strategy
while the beneficial owner continues to make significant decisions about how
the entity operates.

49% of the shares, and the EU 50% rule applies to holdings of 50% or more.

Scenario 4 ☐ ⊠ ☐ ☐
Scenario 1: The customer’s accounts are held at a subsidiary in Brazil, which is a
separate legal entity. Generally, OFAC sanctions do not apply to foreign
subsidiaries of US financial institutions, unless they are covered by a specific
sanctions program, such as Iran or Cuba. It is a US financial institution, so EU
and UK sanctions are not relevant. Therefore, the customer’s accounts are not
subject to an asset freeze under the US, EU, or UK regimes.
Scenario 2: The branch is located in the US, so OFAC sanctions apply. EU
sanctions also apply to the EU financial institution’s foreign branches. UK
sanctions are not relevant.
Scenario 3: The US subsidiary is subject to OFAC sanctions. It is not subject to
EU sanctions, as it is a separate legal entity, not a branch. UK sanctions are not
relevant.
Scenario 4: OFAC sanctions are not relevant. The subsidiary in Italy is subject to
EU sanctions. It is not subject to UK sanctions, as it is a separate legal entity, not
a branch.

Restructuring ownership interests (Activity: Saso Ltd.)
28. Saso Ltd. is a company owned by two individuals: 75% by Alexander Osman
and 25% by Kolsoman Abramich. Saso Ltd. has accounts with an EU
financial institution.
On January 3, Mr. Osman is included on the EU sanctions list, which is
published in the Official Journal of the European Union in the evening of the
same day.
The chief executive officer for Saso Ltd. contacts the financial institution to
report that Mr. Osman sold 26% of his holdings to Mr. Abramich on the
morning of January 3. She states that Mr. Osman now owns only 49% of the
company, which, in theory, means that the accounts of Saso Ltd. should
not be frozen.
An investigation into the sale reveals that the payment for the shares was
funded by a loan from Mr. Osman to Mr. Abramich.
Which indicates that the sale might not be legitimate?
o The sales agreement was drafted on January 3.
o The payment for the sale was funded by a loan from Mr. Osman to Mr.
Abramich.
o Mr. Osman and Mr. Abramich are business partners.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 126
The sales agreement was drafted on January 3: This is not the best response.
The fact that the sales agreement was drafted on January 3 does not indicate
the sale was not legitimate. However, it might indicate that the sale was a last-
minute decision, prompted by the imminent sanctions designation.
Mr. Osman and Mr. Abramich are business partners: This is not the best
response. The fact that Mr. Osman and Mr. Abramich are business partners is
not sufficient to suggest an illegitimate sale. Co-owning a company does not
necessarily mean that the individuals colluded in a sham sale to evade
sanctions.
The payment for the sale was funded by a loan from Mr. Osman to Mr. Abramich:
This is the best response. The fact that the payment for the sale was funded
by a loan from Mr. Osman to Mr. Abramich is a strong indication that the sale is
not legitimate. It is unusual for the seller to loan money to the buyer for a
purchase; it suggests that the sale was done on paper to create a misleading
ownership structure. It is a red flag for sanctions evasion by restructuring
ownership.
Front companies (Activity: Red Rock Limited)
29. A UK financial institution is reviewing a new customer. It is an oil trading
company called Red Rock Limited, which is considered moderate risk. The
shareholders all hold UK passports. The ownership structure of the
company is as follows:
• 53% owned by Ahmed Amin
• 33% owned by Mohamed Asiz
• 14% owned by others
During the review, a blog post is identified that mentions that Red Rock has
purchased oil from Syria. An internet search shows that the shareholders
appear to be distant relatives of Syria’s president, who is sanctioned by the
UK.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 127
Which is the best next step the financial institution should take?
o Decide whether to onboard the customer.
o Decide whether to file a suspicious activity report (SAR).
o Conduct enhanced due diligence on the ownership structure and
source of capital.
o Obtain sanctions attestations from current shareholders.
Decide whether to onboard the customer: This is not the best next step. It is too
soon to make that determination without additional research.
Decide whether to file a suspicious activity report (SAR): This is not the best
next step. There is not enough information to make that decision at this time.
The financial institution might decide to file a SAR after they have collected
and analyzed more information.
Obtain sanctions attestations from current shareholders: This is not the best
next step at this stage. The financial institution could choose to obtain
attestations from the current shareholders, stating that they fully comply with
UK sanctions. However, attestations would not be enough for the financial
institution to decide about onboarding the client.
Conduct enhanced due diligence on the ownership structure and source of
capital: This is the best next step. The financial institution should conduct
enhanced due diligence on the company’s ownership structure and source of
capital to establish:
• Whether the company is funded by its declared owners
• Whether the owners have sufficient funds that match the company’s
capital
• Whether there is any indication that the company has been funded by the
Syrian government

Disguised ownership (Activity: Helmet LLP)
30. A US financial institution is performing due diligence on Helmet LLP,
registered in the UK, with the following findings. Please review the findings
and specify which findings represent red flags of disguised ownership.
Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares.
Red flag Not a red flag
Helmet LLP has no customers in the US. ☐ ⊠
Helmet LLP's main supplier is a US defense
company.
⊠ ☐
Helmet LLP's main customers are two recently
registered companies in Hong Kong.
⊠ ☐
Helmet LLP bills mostly in Euros. ☐ ⊠
Helmet LLP is funded by customers through
prepaid orders.
⊠ ☐
The owner of Helmet LLP is a 25-year-old with
no background in the industry.
⊠ ☐
The findings that are not red flags of disguised ownership:
• Helmet LLP has no customers in the US: Having a US bank account but no
customers in the US is not suspicious. The company’s main supplier is in the
US—this is probably why it needs an account there.
• Helmet LLP bills mostly in Euros: The fact that Helmet LLP bills mostly in
Euros is not suspicious. This could be because a majority of its customers
are in the EU.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 129
The findings that could be red flags of disguised ownership:
• Helmet LLP's main supplier is a US defense company: US defense companies'
products are export-controlled.
• Helmet LLP's main customers are two recently registered companies in Hong
Kong: Being recently registered in Hong Kong indicates that the companies
might have been established as part of a sanctions evasion scheme.
• Helmet LLP is funded by customers through prepaid orders: Being funded by
prepaid orders is an unusual business practice for a typical company. It
could indicate that the customers are closely connected with Helmet LLP.
• The owner of Helmet LLP is a 25-year-old with no background in the industry:
This indicates that he might be a front man.
The red flags indicate there is a possibility that the real owner is not the
declared one, but rather someone attempting to violate sanctions. The
company is likely to be involved in the circumvention of US export controls.

Leading a Complex Sanctions

A complex investigation (Case example:
Goldman Group AST alert)
Research steps applied to an investigation (Activity:
Investigating AST alert)
31. In the Goldman Group case, the investigation team focuses its initial
assessment on the inbound payment for which the counterparty is a true
match against the US BIS Denied Persons List. Place the next steps in the
most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
5. Record and document the investigation.
1. Determine if there is an applicable sanctions restriction.
3. Establish whether a sanctions restriction has been violated.
2. Confirm the scope of the sanctions restriction.
4. Determine how the sanctions violation occurred.
The investigation team should:
1. Determine if there is an applicable sanctions restriction. You must
determine this first before your investigation can proceed. For Goldman
Group, the US Export Administration Regulations (EAR) are the applicable
restriction.
2. Confirm the scope of the sanctions restriction. You need to confirm if the
applicable restriction applies to your customer. In this situation, the
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 131
restriction prohibits exporting dual-use goods of US origin to entities on the
BIS Denied Persons List. Given that your customer is involved in trading
electronics, which could be considered dual-use goods, the product type
and origin should be investigated further.
3. Establish whether a sanctions restriction has been violated. Once you have
determined the relevancy of the sanctions restriction, you must establish
whether your customer has violated it. The inbound payment to Goldman
Group was related to the sale of electronics to an entity on the BIS Denied
Persons List. The shipping documents, which could include invoices or a bill
of lading, will reveal the product type, which can then be compared to the
BIS Commerce Control List (CCL). If there is a match to the CCL, the origin
of the goods must be investigated to determine if a US manufacturer or US
content is involved. The financial institution can also obtain attestations
from the client that the sold goods were not of US origin.
4. Determine how the sanctions violation occurred. If you find there was a
sanctions violation, you should examine if there were past violations by the
same customer that were not detected and, if so, how this may have
happened.
5. Record and document the investigation. Your final step is to document your
entire investigation, so you have a record of your analysis and decisions.
Primary and secondary sources of information
(Activity: Information sorting)

will need to review various sources of information. Please indicate which of
the following information sources are primary and secondary.

Primary sources of information provide direct evidence about sanctions or
sanctions targets. They can include official sanctions lists published by
regulatory authorities and information obtained directly from the customer.
Examples include:
• The OFAC Specially Designated Nationals and Blocked Persons List
• The customer’s payment history
• The Common Military List of the EU
Secondary sources of information provide information that has generally
already appeared in primary documents. They can include internet searches,
corporate registers, media publications, and third-party databases. Examples
include:
• An internet search of the main parties
• Corporate documents of a legal entity found through a corporate register
• An adverse media search of the main parties
• A beneficial ownership registry
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 133
If discrepancies are identified between primary and secondary sources, this
should be seen as a red flag for the purposes of the investigation.
Identifying red flags of trade-related
evasion methods by KYC (Case example:
Investigating counterparties)
Trade-related evasion methods (Activity: Unusual
trade route)
33. The investigation into Goldman Group is expanded to cover its major
customers. An Israeli customer is flagged, because a documented
shipping route Goldman Group uses to transport some goods is unusual.
The goods are shipped by Goldman Group from the US through Central
Asia before reaching their final destination in Israel.
Which is the most probable explanation for the unusual route?
o The final destination of the goods is not Israel but somewhere along
the route.
o Some of the goods do not originate in the US and will be picked up
from an intermediary destination.
o The unusual route is not the actual route, but has been falsified on
official documents to justify elevated shipping costs.
The unusual route is not the actual route, but has been falsified on official
documents to justify elevated shipping costs: This is not the most probable
explanation. It is possible that the unusual route is not the actual route but has
been falsified. A longer route would require a higher shipping cost, and falsely
inflating the shipping cost on the invoice could allow them to hide the higher
cost associated with illicit goods. This would be an example of over-invoicing,
a common trade-based money laundering technique. However, without any
evidence of over-invoicing, this is not the most likely explanation.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 134
Some of the goods do not originate in the US and will be picked up from an
intermediary destination: This is not the most probable explanation. Making it
appear that the goods originate from the US, when, in reality, they originate
from a third country, would not be helpful. Export restrictions apply to dual-
use goods originating from the US, meaning this type of falsification would add
additional export restrictions, not reduce them. A more common evasion
technique would be making it appear that the US goods originated from a
third country that either does not impose export restrictions or has a weak
export controls regime.
The final destination of the goods is not Israel but somewhere along the route:
This is the most probable explanation. The customer is using the unusual route
to falsify the final destination of the goods. The shipping documents might
indicate the destination is Israel, when in reality the goods are delivered to an
in-between destination, such as Russia and Syria, both of which are subject to
stringent US export restrictions.
Identifying red flags of sanctions breaches (Activity:
Red flag matching)
34. During the investigation of Goldman Group, the compliance team
uncovers several red flags that could indicate attempted sanctions
evasion.
Match each red flag on the left to its corresponding sanctions evasion
technique on the right.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 135
Match each option on the left to its corresponding option on the right to
complete the matching assignment.
A. The insurance cost for the transfer of
electronic goods is set 150% above market
price.
A Over-invoicing
B. The phrase “Port of St. Petersburg” was
removed from a wire transfer.
D Sham divestment
C. Vessel tracking reveals that a vessel
carrying electronic goods stopped
transmitting its automatic identification
system (AIS) signal for 30 hours.
C Loitering
D. A sanctioned individual transfers the
ownership of a company to a business
associate, who happens to be Goldman
Group’s beneficial owner.
B Stripping
The insurance cost for the transfer of electronic goods is set 150% above
market price: This is an example of over-invoicing. This technique is used when
the actual goods being traded are not what is listed on the invoice. The
difference in price between the real, illicit, goods and the listed goods is
accounted for in the high insurance cost. This way, the final price on the
invoice appears logical, and the illicit goods remain hidden.
The phrase “Port of St. Petersburg” was removed from a wire transfer: This is
an example of stripping. It is done to prevent an automated screening tool
from detecting that the transaction relates to a shipment to Russia, which
could trigger an investigation.
Vessel tracking reveals that a vessel carrying electronic goods stopped
transmitting its automatic identification system (AIS) signal for 30 hours: This is
an example of loitering. It is done to prevent vessel tracking systems from
detecting stops at sanctioned ports or stops in the middle of the sea for ship-
to-ship transfers.
A sanctioned individual transfers the ownership of a company to a business
associate: This is an example of a sham divestment. It is done to make it
appear that the company no longer belongs to the sanctioned individual.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 136
Conducting more research (Case example:
Alex Dovalni)

A legal entity counterparty receiving funds from Company A: The investigation
team should refer to the beneficial ownership registry in the registration
country of the legal entity. This will provide information on the owners and
controllers of the counterparty and help the investigators understand the
relationship between Dovalni and this counterparty.
The purpose of an inward transfer sent to Company A in the form of a loan: The
investigation team could send a request for information (RFI) or a 314(b)
request to the ordering bank, which should have conducted its own due
diligence on the transfer and will be able to share information with the
financial institution.
The minority holding of Company A held by a trust: The investigation team may
refer to the trust agreement, whose provisions should provide information on
the identities of the settlor and the beneficiaries.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 137
A recent high-value sale of property by Company A: The investigation team
could obtain the valuation report on which the sale was based. This could help
determine whether the price is comparable to market prices in the area, or
whether it was set too high in order to move illicit funds.
Disguised ownership (Activity: Metal Ltd.)
36. During the Goldman Group investigation, the team discovers several
suspicious findings for Metal Ltd., a holding company registered in Malta.
Which finding requires the highest attention and further investigation?
o The beneficial owner declared to the bank is Mr. Dovalni, while Malta’s
corporate registry lists a Goldman Group employee as its sole owner.
o The company holds three different types of assets in three different
jurisdictions.
o The company’s secretary and directors are all nominees.
o The company was registered nine months prior to the start of the war
in Ukraine.
The company’s secretary and directors are all nominees: This is not the best
response. The fact that the company’s secretary and directors are all
nominees is not a red flag by itself, provided other aspects of the company
structure are sufficiently transparent, such as its ownership structure.
The company was registered nine months prior to the start of the war in
Ukraine: This is not the best response. The fact that the company was
registered nine months prior to the start of the war could be an indication it
was created to hide assets that might be frozen once the war commenced.
However, this would be more likely if the company had been registered a
month or two before the war. It is unlikely the war in Ukraine could have been
predicted nine months out.
The company holds three different types of assets in three different
jurisdictions: This is not the best response. This is very common for holding
companies.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 138
The beneficial owner declared to the bank is Mr. Dovalni, while Malta’s corporate
registry lists a Goldman Group employee as its sole owner: This is the best
response. This finding requires the highest attention and further investigation,
as it indicates disguised ownership.
Deciding to conclude an investigation (Case
example: Goldman Group sanctions
violation)
Deciding to conclude an investigation (Activity: Root
cause analysis)
37. The investigation team is conducting a root cause analysis of the Goldman
Group case, to help them identify future potential sanctions evasion cases
sooner. Match each root cause on the left to the correct mitigating
measure on the right.
Match each option on the left to its corresponding option on the right to
complete the matching assignment.
A. Failure to identify a new shareholder
with a sanctions nexus
B Implement enhanced due
diligence (EDD) procedures
regarding the background of
beneficial owners and their
sources of wealth or funds.
B. Failure to identify that Mr. Dovalni is
acting as a front man for an SDN
C Enhance procedures for
reviewing new
counterparties.
C. Failure to identify that two new
buyers have sanctions red flags
A Upgrade a client’s sanctions
risk rating to ensure more
frequent reviews.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 139
Failure to identify a new shareholder with a sanctions nexus: The financial
institution could upgrade a client’s sanctions risk rating to ensure the client is
marked for high sanctions risk. Once classified as high-risk, the customer will
be reviewed more frequently, and changes in corporate structure could be
picked up sooner.
Failure to identify that Mr. Dovalni is acting as a front man for an SDN: The
financial institution could implement enhanced due diligence (EDD)
procedures regarding the background of beneficial owners and their sources
of wealth or funds. EDD could reveal that the background of a beneficial
owner might not justify their source of funds, indicating they could be acting
as a front man.
Failure to identify that two new buyers have sanctions red flags: The financial
institution could enhance procedures for reviewing new counterparties,
namely to ensure that their economic profile is aligned with the customer’s
established profile, and that there are no adverse media findings.
Freezing or blocking assets (Activity: Goldman Group’s
assets)
38. In the investigation of Goldman Group, the team determines that Alex
Dovalni is acting as a front man for a Russian Specially Designated National
(SDN). As the investigation proceeds, the Russian SDN is added to the EU’s
Consolidated Financial Sanctions List. The team has also confirmed that Mr.
Dovalni has accounts for several holding companies at another German
financial institution.
Based on the above information, select which assets held by either
Goldman Group or by Mr. Dovalni’s holding companies should be frozen by
both German financial institutions.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02

Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares.
Freeze Do not freeze
Private jet stationed at an airport in the US ☐ ⊠
Savings account held with a financial
institution’s Dutch branch
⊠ ☐

holding company

☐ ⊠

Private jet stationed at an airport in the US: This does not need to be frozen.
The jet can be considered as being under the control of the SDN. Although
OFAC does not mandate blocking on the basis of control only, the jet is still at
risk of freezing under US law. However, as it is US-based, it is outside the
jurisdiction and control of the German financial institutions.
Savings account held with a financial institution’s Dutch branch: This should be
frozen immediately and reported to the authorities in the Netherlands.
House in France held by Metal Ltd., a Dovalni holding company: This does not
need to be frozen. This is subject to EU sanctions, but it is outside the
jurisdiction and control of the German financial institutions.
Listed securities held under custody by a financial institution’s subsidiary in the
UK: This does not need to be frozen. The listed securities held under custody
by either German financial institution’s subsidiary in the UK are outside the
jurisdiction of both institutions. No freezing is necessary to the extent that a
UK-based subsidiary is not considered to be an EU person and the Russian
SDN is not sanctioned by the UK.

Pending proceeds from a letter of credit in favor of Goldman Group: This should
be frozen. The pending proceeds must be credited, frozen, and then reported
to the authorities.

Identifying, freezing, and blocking assets
(Case example: Hyperion Bank)
Identifying, freezing, and blocking assets (Activity: UK
sanctions)
39. Alexey Rostov is sanctioned by the UK government. It is discovered that he
is the majority owner of Gulf Gold, a publicly listed oil company. He also
owns 38% of another company, Persepolis Inc. He does not have any
involvement in either company beyond owning his shares. Both companies
have various subsidiaries and assets. Mr. Rostov has a large investment
portfolio that includes stocks, derivatives, and bonds. He also owns a yacht,
several luxury automobiles, and real estate in the UK and Germany.
Hyperion Bank must block all of the assets that it holds for Mr. Rostov.
Which of the following should be frozen?
o Real estate in Germany
o Large investment portfolio
o Shares in Gulf Gold owned by third-party investors
o Assets held by Persepolis Inc.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 143
Real estate in Germany: This is not subject to freezing. Alexey Rostov is
sanctioned by the UK government, which has no jurisdiction over his property
outside the UK.
Assets held by Persepolis Inc.: This is not subject to freezing. Mr. Rostov is a
minority owner of Persepolis Inc. As he does not own more than 50% of the
company, it is not subject to the asset freeze under UK regulations. The bank
does not need to freeze any companies that Persepolis majority-owns or
controls or any assets it holds. However, the bank might decide to subject
Persepolis’ accounts to enhanced ongoing monitoring, as it is known to be
associated with a sanctioned person.
Shares in Gulf Gold owned by third-party investors: This is not subject to
freezing. Because Gulf Gold is a publicly listed company, a certain percentage
of the shares in the company is owned by non-sanctioned private investors.
These third-party shares are not subject to an asset freeze. However, Gulf
Gold is majority-owned by Mr. Rostov, so his majority stake as well as all assets
of the company are subject to the asset freeze. This includes any entities of
which Gulf Gold owns more than 50% and any minority-owned entities that it
controls.
Large investment portfolio: This is subject to freezing. Mr. Rostov’s investment
portfolio and any other assets owned by him that are in the UK would need to
be frozen. This includes any cryptoassets, jewelry, property, artwork, or
anything else that could be exchanged for an economic benefit.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 144
Freezing assets and reporting frozen assets
(Case example: Peterhouse Holdings)
Freezing assets and reporting frozen assets (Activity:
Asset freezing steps)
40. Hyperion Bank is investigating Alexey Rostov, an individual sanctioned by
the UK. They must follow the proper procedure for identifying and freezing
his assets. Place the following steps in the most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
2. Identify any nonfinancial assets Mr. Rostov holds.
4. Block all accounts associated with Mr. Rostov or his companies.
3. Establish which companies Mr. Rostov owns or controls.
5. Report the frozen assets to the Office of Financial Sanctions
Implementation (OFSI).
1. Review Mr. Rostov’s financial statements, bank records, and
investment portfolios.
To properly identify and freeze Alexey Rostov's assets, Hyperion Bank should:
1. Review Mr. Rostov’s financial statements, bank records, and investment
portfolios. This information should be readily available to the bank and is a
sensible place to begin the investigation.
2. Identify any nonfinancial assets Mr. Rostov holds. This includes anything that
could be exchanged for economic value such as vehicles, commodities,
property, and artwork. These assets are more difficult for the bank to
identify than the financial assets and may require a physical inspection or
collaboration with other relevant parties.
3. Establish which companies Mr. Rostov owns or controls. This would require a
complex investigation, considering the percentage of each company that
Mr. Rostov owns. Under UK regulations, the bank must also consider
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 145
whether Mr. Rostov can be said to exercise control over any of the
companies where he does not hold a majority ownership stake.
4. Block all accounts associated with Mr. Rostov or his companies. The assets
must be blocked or frozen as soon as possible to prevent the risk of asset
flight or sanctions evasion.
5. Report the frozen assets to OFSI. The bank must check OFSI’s reporting
requirements to ensure what information it needs to include.

Managing frozen assets (Activity: Gulf Gold)
41. Alexey Rostov’s oil company, Gulf Gold, is subject to an asset freeze under
sanctions regimes in the EU, US, and UK. After freezing Gulf Gold’s
accounts, each branch of Hyperion Bank must take additional actions to
manage the frozen assets. Select which actions apply to the jurisdiction(s).
Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares. More than one
response might apply per row.
EU US (OFAC) UK

account.
☐ ⊠ ☐
Report frozen funds without delay. ⊠ ☐ ⊠
Report frozen funds within 10 days. ☐ ⊠ ☐
Credit interest to a frozen account. ⊠ ⊠ ⊠
Debit bank charges from a frozen

Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 146
Place frozen funds in an interest-bearing account: The US Office of Foreign
Assets Control (OFAC) requires that all frozen funds be placed in an interest-
bearing account from which only OFAC-authorized debits can be made.
Report frozen funds without delay: Both the EU and UK require banks or other
businesses that freeze funds to report to the relevant authority without delay.
Report frozen funds within 10 days: OFAC requires that frozen funds be
reported within 10 days of blocking the funds.
Credit interest to a frozen account: All jurisdictions allow interest to be credited
to a frozen account without the need for a license or for reporting to the
regulator.
Debit bank charges from a frozen account without a license: OFAC regulations
generally allow a bank to debit blocked accounts for normal service charges
without needing a license.
Managing frozen assets (Activity: Necessary steps)
42. Hyperion Bank must put steps in place to manage Alexey Rostov’s frozen
assets effectively. For each listed step, select whether it is necessary or
unnecessary for effective frozen asset management.
Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares.
Necessary Unnecessary
Keep updated on changes to the relevant
sanctions.
⊠ ☐

Monitor the ownership of Mr. Rostov’s
companies.
⊠ ☐
Seek independent legal advice. ☐ ⊠
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 147
Keep updated on changes to the relevant sanctions: This is a necessary step.
New sanctions can come into place and existing sanctions can be lifted.
Licenses could be introduced that allow payments that were previously
prohibited. It is important to ensure you have updated information on
sanctions regulations.
Review any payments in or out of the accounts: This is a necessary step. Some
transactions, such as crediting interest to a frozen account, are permitted
under sanctions legislation. There might also be general or specific licenses in
place that cover other payments from the accounts. It is essential that all
payments are monitored and records are kept to ensure no sanctions
breaches occur.
Monitor the ownership of Mr. Rostov’s companies: This is a necessary step.
Changes in ownership can affect whether sanctions restrictions apply or not.
Seek independent legal advice: This is an unnecessary step. If the bank has an
experienced compliance team, it should not be necessary to take
independent legal advice to manage the accounts. It should have existing
processes in place that it can follow.
Specific licenses or exceptions (Case
example: Rental property)
Specific licenses or exceptions (Activity: Restricted
activities)
43. Alexey Rostov successfully applied for a specific license for his rental
properties and is interested in applying for more licenses. Select which
activities he is allowed to apply for under the UK’s licensing guidelines.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 148
Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares.
Allowed Not allowed
Pay his mortgage ⊠ ☐
Pay for construction on his house that was
completed before he was sanctioned
⊠ ☐
Sell his cryptocurrency on an exchange ☐ ⊠
Pay mooring fees for his yacht ⊠ ☐
Pay his mortgage: This is allowed. UK residents can apply for licenses to cover
their basic needs to ensure sanctioned persons are able to continue to live in
their resident country. Basic needs licenses cover mortgage or rent
payments. They would also cover payments of insurance premiums, tax
payments, and utility charges.
Pay for construction completed before he was sanctioned: This is allowed. You
can apply for a license to cover prior obligations. Provided there was a
contract showing that the work was undertaken before Mr. Rostov was
sanctioned, he would be able to apply for a license to pay his builders.
Sell his cryptocurrency on an exchange: This is not allowed. Cryptocurrency is
an economic resource and is treated in a similar way to other economic funds
like bonds and shares. Selling cryptocurrency would make funds available to
Mr. Rostov. There are no licensing grounds that would allow this.
Pay mooring fees for his yacht: This is allowed. The derogation for routine
holding and maintenance of frozen funds and economic resources could be
used to cover mooring fees for Mr. Rostov’s yacht. Any routine fees such as
building charges for property or costs needed to maintain the condition of
frozen assets could be applied for under this license.

General licenses or exemptions (Activity: Stock
divestment)
44. A US businessman has an account at his bank which holds a portfolio of
investments, including shares in some of Alexey Rostov’s companies. Mr.
Rostov has just been added to the Office of Foreign Assets Control (OFAC)
Specially Designated National (SDN) List under the Russia sanctions
regime, and many of his companies are subject to sanctions. The US
businessman asks the bank to divest all his shares affected by the
sanctions on Mr. Rostov, using a general license. Place the following steps
in the most logical sequential order.
Place the following choices in the most logical order.
Sequence # Description
4. Ensure any transactions meet the terms and conditions of the
license.
2. Review the OFAC Russian Sanctions legislation.
3. Confirm if there is a general license in place.
5. Report the use of the license to OFAC, as appropriate.
1. Establish which parts of the portfolio are majority-owned by
the SDN.
To assist the businessman by using a general license, the bank should:
1. Establish which parts of the portfolio are majority-owned by the SDN. There
might be shares in other companies that are also majority-owned by the
SDN. Before the bank can take any action, it must isolate the relevant parts
of the portfolio.
2. Review the OFAC Russia Sanctions legislation. The bank might need to
consult its legal department or take outside advice to understand exactly
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 150
what actions are permitted under the United States’ Russia sanctions
legislation.
3. Confirm if there is a general license in place. OFAC has issued several
general licenses that allow for the divestment and wind-down of positions
in sanctioned companies listed under the Russia sanctions regime. These
licenses are designed, among other things, to allow US persons to divest
their assets without being penalized by the sanctions.
4. Ensure any transactions meet the terms and conditions of the license. Wind-
down licenses are generally time-limited and have conditions attached to
them allowing specific actions.
5. Report the use of the license to OFAC, as appropriate. Some general
licenses have reporting conditions attached to them.
General and specific licenses (Activity: Shard
Enterprises)
45. Alexey Rostov’s company, Peterhouse Holdings, has a minority 5%
ownership stake in Shard Enterprises, a large, diversified business which
happens to be a client of Hyperion Bank. Recently two of Shard Enterprises’
directors were sanctioned by the EU and the UK under the Libya sanctions
regime. The two directors also have joint majority ownership of the
company.
The bank is considering which of Shard Enterprises’ activities can be
licensed. Select which activities can be undertaken with a general license
and which need a specific license.
Sanctions Compliance Case Studies
Version 2.02
Page 151
Mark your answers by choosing the appropriate squares.
General Specific
Paying fees to the UK’s register of companies ⊠ ☐
Paying for flood damage to its warehouse ☐ ⊠
Paying the damages in a court case ☐ ⊠
Paying property tax ⊠ ☐
Paying building insurance ⊠ ☐
Paying fees to the UK’s register of companies: The UK has put in place a general
license that covers all regimes to allow payments to Companies House, the
UK’s executive agency that registers company information and makes it
available to the public. This license allows companies to pay annual fees and
penalties due.
Paying for flood damage to its warehouse: Shard Enterprises would need to
apply for a specific license. It could apply under extraordinary expenses which
allows for payments that are extraordinary in nature–unexpected,
unavoidable, and not recurring.
Paying the damages in a court case: Shard Enterprises could apply for a
specific prior jurisdiction license. This enables the use of frozen funds to pay a
judicial decision if it was established before the date of designation and is
enforceable in the UK.
Paying property tax: There is a general license in the UK that allows sanctioned
entities to make payments to local authorities for property tax and rates. It also
permits the payment of any penalties. This license applies across all regimes.
Paying building insurance: There is a general license that allows the payment of
building or engineering insurance to brokers or insurance companies. Other
types of insurance are also included such as motor insurance and terrorism
insurance for buildings.
